Chapter 9: Universalizing Hindu Dharma: HemƒÅdri and his Legacy

Figure 1: Photo provided courtesy of Temple Trust: Hatnur NƒÅganƒÅtha before the renovation, side view.

Reinventing the Western Deccan: The Seu·πáa Sea Change

In 2002, in the small town of Hatnur in rural Marathwada, the temple-trust responsible for the management and preservation of the early fourteenth-century NƒÅganƒÅtha temple undertook an ambitious program of renovation and refurbishment. Though the project was meticulously documented, for the historian or even the aficionado of heritage, the end result was something of a mixed blessing. On the one hand, one of the few surviving examples of a completely intact early medieval stone ≈õikhara in the region‚Äîwhich had apparently become structurally unsound‚Äîwas replaced by a colorful slender spire replicating the style preferred by the MarƒÅ·π≠has in the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries.

[[P1258]]

Figure 2: Hatnur, NƒÅganƒÅtha, after the renovation in 2002.

More constructively, in removing many centuries of debris and overgrowth, the committee‚Äôs efforts uncovered the full extent and majesty of Hatnur‚Äôs enormous stepwell (S. pu·π£kari·πáƒ´, M. bƒÅrava). With a tank that could comfortably accommodate three or four NƒÅganƒÅthas, in both [[P1259]] scale and sophistication, its execution overshadows the rather plain and austere temple. Despite being located in a place that even today is scarcely inhabited, barely reachable by road, and somewhat desolate, the sheer scale of Hatnur‚Äôs stepwell echoes back to a time when traditional methods of irrigation, feeding into this vast tank, would have made the desert bloom. Perhaps this would have allowed for the support of a much larger population, comprised of a mixture of locals and pilgrims. While the temple itself was built in the reign of the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava King RƒÅmacandra, during a peculiar moment when he had temporarily reasserted his independence from the Delhi Sultanate, this step well is several centuries older. Even in its diminished condition, one has only to look at the surviving ornamental work on the edges of the stepwell, which introduces elegant and flowing lines into the stone, to say nothing of the remnants of the relief and sculptural components that would have been incorporated therein, to recognize a hand far finer than the one that shaped NƒÅganƒÅtha.

[[P1260]]

Figure 3: L: Fragments of panel with three figures worshipping a ≈õivali·πÖga. R: Details of stonework, Hatnur tank.

The workmanship here is quite clearly in the style of the KalyƒÅ·πái CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas. Compare the subtlety of the lines on these fragments with the much more roughly hewn even somewhat irregular renderings of geometric shapes in evidence on the doorway leading into NƒÅganƒÅtha.

Figure 4: Look at its blocky, almost polygonic Ga·πáe≈õa‚Äî the only figurative work incorporated into this Seu·πáa complex. Clearly, something must have happened in the intervening centuries that precipitated this marked shift in the aesthetic and visual culture.

Just how drastic this transition must have been becomes evident when we recognize that Hatnur‚Äôs NƒÅganƒÅtha lies only fifteen miles to the south of the temples at Charthana that we explored in the preceding chapter. Indeed, despite their vast divergence in focus skill level and style, Hatnur‚Äôs NƒÅganƒÅtha incorporates into its ceilings some visual features reminiscent of the otherwise unattested byzantine abstract geometrical formations one also finds at Charthana in the temple that celebrates MatsyendranƒÅtha in its garbhag·πõha. As we explored in the previous chapter, Charthana‚Äôs temples, all of which incorporate numerous figurative and often narrative representations onto their pillars and most of which also present us with images of a wide range of esoteric deities and yogins, represented both in the round and in relief, were likely to have been constructed around the early portions of the thirteenth century. In other words, within precisely the same geographical region and in less than a hundred years, both what temples represented and how they were built altered suddenly, and‚Äîas the older style and prowess [[P1262]] was never to resurface‚Äî irrevocably.

Figure 5: T: NƒÅganƒÅtha interior. B: Detail of Renuka Devƒ´, Charthana representing very different sort of gods: the pantheon of the ≈öƒÅkta Tantras.

In this regard, Hatnur is hardly unusual. Quite frequently, as I have discovered time and again in his travels within what was once Seu·πáa territory, trusts overseeing rather tame late thirteenth-century Hemadpanthi temples engage in beautification projects that demand the draining of temple tanks only to discover that the bottom is often full of traces of a very different sort of material culture.

[[P1263]]

Figure 6: Thanks to the efforts of the Hatnur Temple Trust, we are in a position to recognize that this rupture in both the visual style and sophistication of technique finds its almost exact counterpart in a change in religious sensibilities. In dredging up the bottom of temple tank, much to their surprise, the work crews under the direction of trustees found the deliberately broken remnants of older, larger, and somewhat ferocious images Hatnur: Top portion of a large standing image of the goddess commonly worshipped as MahƒÅlak·π£mƒ´.

Thus, for example, the mahƒÅrƒÅj who tends the temple at Parde Darda an hour from Lonar‚Äîa Hemadpanthi temple complex dedicated to the joint worship of NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa and a ≈õivali·πÖga also built in the reign of RƒÅmachandra‚Äîrecovered from the temple tank on the edge of the property a treasure trove of older images that now fill his temple‚Äôs courtyard. Perhaps most memorably, these include an immense and highly unusual RƒÅ·π£·π≠rak≈´·π≠a style portrait of a dagger-bearing queen or female ascetic who holds a skull bowl in her right hand. Much as we have seen in our other case studies, Hatnur is of special interest not because it markedly diverges from the dynamics of its day, but because it provides us with a published paper trail associated with a corpus of material cultural evidence. Taken as whole, and read in relation to parallels at other places, this archive tells a surprisingly straightforward and unified story.

[[P1264]]

Figure 7: Parde Darda: R: Portion of large KƒÅpƒÅlika portrait found in tank. L. Top: Early thirteenth-century doorway covered in images of yogins that is all that remains of the older structure. L. Bottom: The Hemadpanthi style temple that replaced it in the mid- to late-thirteenth century, itself subsequently renovated.

In both its form and content, the inscription at Hatnur1 breaks from the chancellery scribal conventions of the medieval Deccan. Not only is it written in old Marathi instead of old Kannada or Sanskrit, but it has been executed by an artisan (S. s≈´tradhƒÅra, M. s≈´tƒÅru) who writes in a rather sloppy hand, did not bother to measure out the space where his inscription was to be located before he began engraving, is seemingly unaware of the existence of the anusvƒÅra, and who somehow managed to misspell the name of his own profession (M. s≈´tƒÅr≈´) as well as the common word for temple (presadu for prƒÅsƒÅdu). Nevertheless, the intention behind the inscription is quite clear. An authority named Puru·π£adevapa·πá·∏çit, a figure who, as this chapter will demonstrate, was infinitely more likely to have been a householder BrƒÅhma·πáa [[P1266]] than a ≈öƒÅkta ascetic, enlisted an artisan with the strikingly Vai·π£·πáava name of Harƒ´deo to both make (kelƒÅ) and ‚Äúrenovate‚Äù (vriddhi keli) a temple dedicated to NƒÅganƒÅtha. This combination of making and renovating is itself highly irregular, and not just because it is redundant. No matter how extensive the changes that are being made, when speaking of a single structure, inscriptional records in the Deccan speak either of the renovation of an existing temple or the construction of a new one. In light of the nature of Harƒ´deo‚Äôs project, one is tempted to see our artisan as employing these dual verbs with the intention of communicating something quite similar to the modern real estate idiom of ‚Äúgut-renovating‚Äù a newly purchased ‚Äútear-me-down‚Äù home where the majority of the value lies in the property underneath the structure itself.

As one enters the temple of NƒÅganƒÅtha, the devotee is confronted with the following Old Marathi inscription, riddled with irregular spellings and scrawled in an indifferent hand, which awkwardly occupies an entire panel on the entry way pillar:

O·πÉ saku. 1223 p·∏∑ava sa·πÉchare. puru·π£udeo pa·πá·∏çiti [sic. pa·πá·∏çiti·πÉ] nƒÅganƒÅthƒÅcƒÅ presadu [sic. prƒÅsƒÅdu] kelƒÅ vridhhi keli sutƒÅr≈´ [sic. s≈´tƒÅr≈´] Harƒ´ deo. O·πÉ nƒÅganƒÅtha namastu.

In the ≈öaka year 1223, [in the reign of RƒÅmachandra YƒÅdava], the temple of NƒÅganƒÅtha was made and renovated by Puru·π£odeopa·πá·∏çita. The artisan was Harƒ´deo. O·πÉ: Benedictions to NƒÅganƒÅtha.

Figure 8: Detail: Hatnur NƒÅganƒÅtha. The elaborate plinth is composed of a different type of stone and executed in a different style than the temple itself.

What our visual [[P1267]] evidence seems to suggest is that under Puru·π£adeva‚Äôs direction, only the old black stone platform, which had rested underneath the prior structure housing the goddess and Bhairava‚ÄîAt a nearby town with the evocative name of Gosai Pimpaleswar, where the MahƒÅnubhƒÅvika sant Cakradhar once dwelt for an extended period of time, though we are lacking in inscriptions, a similar visual dynamic is in evidence. Here again the temple itself is exceptionally plain, though in this case efforts seem to have been made to incorporate some portions of the older ‚Äúoriginal structure.‚Äù

Figure 9: Pimple≈õvara Hemadpanthi temple (mid/late-thirteenth century), front view.

Once again, a monumental but very simple crude structure occupies a space that had until recently been defined by a different aesthetic, grounded in a much more intricate and precise visual language, executed with greater skill, and communicating ≈öƒÅkta-≈öaiva values. The grounds at Gosai Pimpale≈õvara not only preserves beautifully rendered images of Bhairava and [[P1269]] his associates, but we are also lucky to have access to some of the portraits of the ‚ÄúgosƒÅvƒ´s‚Äù‚Äî most likely KƒÅlamukha ƒÅcƒÅryas and sƒÅdhakas‚Äîthat adorned the original structure, looking down from on high upon the devotees. We can be sure that this was the original arrangement because, despite some ideologically inconvenient iconography, the thirteenth-century renovation of the site was intent upon preserving the older temple‚Äôs garbhag·πõha. Perhaps because the end result would have been an eyesore and the engineers lacked the skills necessary to preserve the worship space intact, they preserved the most nondescript of the yogins in situ on its back wall and the door guardians inside.

Figure 10: Top: Pimple≈õvara side view. Top R: Detail, the doorway into the garbhag·πõha preserves older features, such as this skull staff bearing dvƒÅrapƒÅla Bottom: Hemadpanthi interior.

Figure 11: Gosai Pimpale≈õvara: Back exterior of temple preserving features of the older structure: R: Detail of yogin on exterior. [[P1270]]

Figure 12: : Top: GosƒÅvƒ´ ƒÅcƒÅrya. Bottom: SƒÅdhaka. Both panels would have originally adorned the temple. [[P1271]]

Figure 13: Right top: Details of temple decoration recovered by villagers and now reinstalled lining step well. Middle Right: Small panel preserved on back of temple depicting meditating yogi. Bottom: Detail of an ascetic propitiating two squatting female deities with prominent genitals followed by image of ascetic in meditation (possibly a narrative panel). [[P1272]]

Figure 14: B: Select images of m≈´rtis, standing images of the mothers, and a seated form of Sarasvatƒ´. An iconographically unusual and perhaps related standing female deity, heavily eroded from centuries of worship, continues to be worshipped by the dreadlock-wearing jogtƒ´ (female ≈öƒÅkta ascetic) who currently dwells behind the temple.

As the inscription from Hatnur tells us, NƒÅganƒÅtha was constructed in the year 1301 CE, during a brief period when the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava ruler RƒÅmachandra‚Äîin the absence of his ostensible foreign overlord Alauddin Khilji (who had conquered Devgiri in 1296 and then promptly vanished from the Deccan)‚Äîattempted to reassert independent rule. As one of nine significant dated inscriptions issued under the regnal year of the dynasty before Khilji returned from his military conquests in north India in 1308 CE2 and resumed more direct oversight, [[P1273]] it is a testament to the priorities and vision of both the Seu·πáas and the local powers that paid them homage. All of these texts, provided they do not commemorate an untimely death, resume the very same project. They reimagine the sacred landscape of the Deccan, the terms of its administration, as well as the dominant patterns of land ownership and patronage. Often implicitly, but many cases quite explicitly, they set out to dismantle the epistemic frames we have been exploring for the past thousand pages, erasing the KƒÅlamukhas and comparable property-holding ≈öƒÅkta-≈öaiva communities from the pages of history.

No. 463: ‚ÄúMASR (1929)124.no. 57 Kannada A.D. 1294: Belgami inscription, Saka 1216 (or 1218). Records the gift of the office of Heggadike (manager) of the Bherundasvami temple in Balligave (Belgami) made to Vaidyadasanna by Devarasa along with the Pattanasvami (the chief of the city) and the heads of the five mathas, etc., a plot of land of two mattaras situated in the estate belonging to a Jaina temple named Prathamasena basadi was also given away along with the land.‚Äù

No. 468: ‚ÄúMSP .9.2(Jan. 62) Marathi A.D. 1298 ARIE (1958-59)170 Koprad (sopara) stone inscription of Yadava Ramacandra. It refers to one Mahmud Prosrahi a ruler under Kanharadeva-the feudatory and general of Ramacandra Yadava. Some income has been assigned to a mosque. It is the only Yadava inscription referring to a mosque.‚Äù RƒÅmachandra issues a single inscription, in support of a mosque in 1298 CE, the year Devgiri is brought under Khilji‚Äôs control. The inscriptional record only resumes at the very end of 1300 CE, when Khilji has gone north and the Seu·πáas resume acting as quasi-independent agents.

No. 469: ‚ÄúSMHD.1.79-81 (no.8,1) Marathi A.D.1300 Dec.5 Velapur inscription, Malshiras tal.,Sholapur dist. Saka 1222. Mentions Ramacandra Yadava, his Sarvadhikari Jaideva; his subordinate Brahmadeva and his brother Baideva. Baideva is recorded to have repaired the temple of Natesvara Jogesvara in Velapura which was situated in the Mana country.‚Äù Here we see an instance of the ‚Äúrenovation‚Äù of a temple dedicated to a ≈öƒÅkta-≈öaiva form of the dancing ≈öiva into a Hemadpanthi temple.

No. 470: ‚ÄúSMHD.2.8 (no.12,2) Marathi A.D.1300 Velapur inscription, Malshiras tal.,Sholapur dist. Saka 1222. Mentions Ramacandra Yadava, his subordinate Jaideva his subordinate Brahmadeva Rana and his brother Baideva Rana. The last is recorded to have built a monastery and some rooms near Vatesvara at Velapura in the Mana Country. The writer was Maideva, the son of Damodara Pandita.‚Äù Here we see another instance of the ‚Äúrenovation‚Äù and deinstitutionalization of preexisting ≈öƒÅkta-≈öaiva center, in this case resulting in its acquisition by the lineage of the old Marathi yogin/sant Ca·πÖgadeva VƒÅ·π≠e≈õvara, the disciple of MuktƒÅyoginƒ´.

No. 471: ‚ÄúEC.11.58 (Dg.26). Kannada A.D.1300 Harihar inscription, Davanagere tal.,Chitaldurga dist. Mysore State. Saka 1222. Records that in the 32nd year of the reign of the Yadava Ramacandra, Harihara, which was formerly a Brahmana endowment, andwhich[sic] afterwards Krsnaakandhara restored ‚Äì that Harihara, Mummudi Liggaya-Nayaka‚Äôs son Khandeya-Raya, again granted, with pouring water at the feet of Visnu, and placing himself before the Brahmanas of Harihara.‚Äù [[P1274]]

As we will see through cases drawing upon documentary records in old Marathi, Sanskrit and old Kannada, sometimes this is accomplished through direct armed interventions into the previously sacrosanct domain of long established undying land grants, the forcible eviction of the governing ≈öƒÅkta-≈öaiva authorities, the conversion of the grant into a taxable property under the purview of the state, and the installation of a new managerial class, usually comprised of BrƒÅhma·πáas.3 In place of the well-worn formulas identifying the KƒÅlamukhas to which we have grown so accustomed, we find instead emphatic evocations of the unimpeachable orthopraxy and impeccable ethical comportment of the pious BrƒÅhma·πáas. Instead of merely hailing from Vedic gotras, the figures in question are depicted as actively engaged in the practice of ≈örauta ritual. And where we would expect appeals to specific samayas and the doctrines of LƒÅkulas and ƒÅgamas, the text extol the virtues of the study and [[P1275]] teaching of Advaita VedƒÅnta. In the vast majority of cases, however, it is the sudden silences in our documentary record that cry out to us as places that had received unbroken reverence from every sort of ruler in the Deccan for many hundreds of years suddenly and permanently disappear from our records.

Quite specific and eminently recoverable conditions of possibility underwrite the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava reinvention of the preexisting and deeply ≈öƒÅkta sacred landscape. An introduction to this innovative interpretive project as well as the texture of the rewritten religious and institutional dynamics that will become increasingly predominant throughout the Seu·πáa domain throughout the second half of the thirteenth century are compellingly conveyed through a close and contextual reading of an old Marathi inscription embedded in an exceptionally remote place now called Unkeshwar.4 Unkeshwar lies on the far edge of Marathwada near what is now the Telangana border. As late as the nineteenth century, the region was primarily home to ƒÄdivƒÅsis and jogƒ´s, including traditions associated with the rasa siddhas. Apart from Mahur, sacred to the followers of DattƒÅtreya and the MahƒÅnubhƒÅvikƒÅs, which lies an hour to the west, the area even today has no major cities and has yet to be the focus of any significant archaeological, ethnographic, or historical work, even within the modern Marathi academy.

[[P1276]]

Figure 15: Unkeshwar: Entrance to the site. Stairs leading up the hill and past the fort to the hot springs.

Figure 16: Unkeshwar: Behind the gate, S≈´rya Ku·πá·∏ç (left) is one of the sulphur-rich hot springs. The inscription from 1278 CE (center) is on the exterior of the extensively renovated Hemadpanthi temple.

[[P1277]]

For a rather selective demographic of tourists, however, Unkeshwar‚Äôs contemporary significance is not a matter of heritage or piety but a question of chemistry ‚Äîcompelled by utter desperation. The site where our inscription stands is a built on top of the only active hot springs in eastern Maharashtra, and the high sulphur content of the boiling water is seen as having curative potential‚Äîor at the very least providing relief‚Äîto lepers and other people afflicted by diseases of the skin, whose families bring them there in hope of a miracle. Close to crashing waterfalls up among hills that are riddled with caves, for five miles in every direction around Unkeshwar one finds geothermal pockets that heat the water to at least a hundred degrees. The surrounding plains are so overabundant with mercury that the farmers worry about it poisoning the crops and the companies committed to extracting coal and gold out of the ground must take precautions as they dig because it periodically oozes up from the ground. Succinctly, for the siddhas of the early medieval world, the site we call Unkeshwar must have amounted to an alchemist‚Äôs dream. Though a matter of informed conjecture, our inscription from 1278 CE is complicit in the almost complete erasure of this lurid zeitgeist. For precisely the same reasons, it seeks to manicure and domesticate even the classical Sanskrit tradition in the service of promoting a new kind of ‚ÄúHindu‚Äù ethos, actively mediated through the prestige and authority of the state. The inscription reads as follows:

O·πÉ, Salutations to Ga·πáe≈õa! In the auspicious ≈öaka year 1201, known of as Vikrama Sa·πÉvatsara, during the reign of the vigorous mighty one, auspicious RƒÅmacandradeva the Cakravartin, on this very day‚Äîright here‚Äîsubsisting on the feet of him, the master of elephants‚Äî‚ÄúbhƒÅvakadeva‚Äù5‚Äîthe chief minister HemƒÅdipa·πá·∏çita, the officer under his [HemƒÅdri‚Äôs] direction (M. ta·πÉniropƒ´ta·∏•, S. tat-nir≈´pita), the nƒÅeku (S. nƒÅyaka), master scribe (M. kastu, S. kƒÅyastha) Somadeyo Pa·πá·∏çita, at the time did it [i.e., oversaw the implementation of the grant as follows]:

In the tretƒÅ yuga, RƒÅma on the occasion of dwelling in the forest, came to the ƒÅsramƒÅ (S. ƒÅ≈õrama) of Sarabha·πÖga. To please Sarabha·πÖga, he made the spring water become hot (S. u·π£·πáa). Since that time, this tƒ´rtha was made by the god. [[P1278]]

By the grace of Harihara, the one who dwells at MƒÅtƒÅpura, Sara·πáanƒÅeka, belonging to the Ka·πáva sƒÅ·π£ƒÅ (s. ≈õƒÅkhƒÅ) and Kau·πá·∏çinyagotra, the son of Maghadeva, commenced the making of all the temples (M. sakalaprƒÅsƒÅda). By the grace of RƒÅma, he made it complete. His homage to Harihara, to the group of the thirty-three [Vedic] deities who dwell in the temples. Homage to all the tƒ´rthas [gap]. Homage to all the three times [when sandhyƒÅ is performed]. Whoever reads this will be victorious!!

The village of that place is a perpetual (M. nƒ´ti, S. nitya) designated land grant from the state (M. rƒÅjava·π≠ƒ´, S. rƒÅjav·πõtti).6 Thus, the terms of the grant run as follows:

[[P1279]]

On the surface a seemingly simple text, the first portion of the Unkeshwar inscription has much to teach us about the fundamental transformation of the relationship between religion and the state and the role played by BrƒÅhma·πáas in popular worship under the last three Seu·πáa kings, KandharƒÅya, MahƒÅdeva, and RƒÅmacandra. We are witnessing here an extraordinary moment. On the edge of the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava domain, a duly credentialed (Ka·πáva ≈öƒÅkhƒÅ, Kau·πá·∏çinya Gotra) BrƒÅhma·πáa named Sara·πáa decides to undertake a program of massive investment in a sacred field, building a series of temples at a site sacred to RƒÅma. There is no acknowledgement of any preexisting religious institutions or infrastructure. Despite the fact that some of these are temples associated with ≈öiva and RƒÅma, the latter of whom is directly responsible for making the site sacred, ≈öiva is in absentia and RƒÅma himself is only responsible for the successful completion of the project. Instead, rather pointedly, the text twice attributes the capacity of the patron to undertake this project to the grace of Harihara, the distant god at Pandharpur whom our records show was heavily patronized both by the Seu·πáa kings and his chief minister, and whose worship at this time suspiciously resembles a sort of state religion. Similarly, instead of aligning with particularized ‚Äúlocal‚Äù pantheons and concerns or the deities of specific social communities, as had been typical of the inscriptional records in the Deccan for centuries, the text tells us that these new temples, built by a non-resident BrƒÅhma·πáa, will be housing the thirty-three Vedic deities, hardly popular focuses of veneration.

Even in its invocation of its imagined classical pedigree, our inscription employs a certain sleight of hand in its deployment of canonical mythology. In both the VƒÅlmƒ´ki RƒÅmƒÅyƒÅ·πáa and KƒÅlidƒÅsa‚Äôs Raghuva·πÉ≈õa,7 RƒÅma‚Äôs brief encounter with the sage ≈öarabha·πÖga is one of the more bizarre moments in the epic. In the critical edition of the book of the forest, enroute to Citrak≈´·π≠a, the dying gandharva Tumburu encourages RƒÅma to swiftly seek out the ƒÅ≈õrama of the sage ≈öarabha·πÖga. This place lies along the GodƒÅvarƒ´ river, which is to say roughly in the region of Maharashtra under discussion. As RƒÅma makes his way towards the sage‚Äôs ƒÅ≈õrama, he sees radiant Indra and his chariots rushing towards ≈öarabha·πÖga‚Äôs home. After many centuries of fierce austerities (ugre·πáa tapasƒÅ), ≈öarabha·πÖga, a great sage resplendent like the sun (mahar·π£i·∏• s≈´ryasa·πÉnibha·∏•), is getting ready to ascend into heaven. Thankfully, RƒÅma and his brother arrive just in time, right at the moment that ≈öarabha·πÖga is about to step into the agnihotra fire and immolate himself. When he sees the descendants of Raghu, ≈öarabha·πÖga is overjoyed and clasps their feet. The sage delights that he is there to welcome them, declares the moment most auspicious, and then, after offering them the hospitality of his house, he invites them to watch as he kills himself, shedding his body as a snake sloughs off its skin. Overbrimming with radiant tejas (mahƒÅtejas), ≈öarabha·πÖga then steps into the sacrificial fire. It consumes his hair, flesh, and bones. Transformed into an elegant [[P1280]] youth, ≈öarabha·πÖga‚Äôs radiant fiery spirit is seen ascending into heaven. In VƒÅlmƒ´ki, RƒÅma is then welcomed by the VaikhanƒÅsa sages who dwell in that place, who encourage him to go kill some rƒÅk·π£asas that have been troubling the region. For KalidƒÅsa and his ninth-century Kashmiri commentator Vallabhadeva, in contrast, the story of ≈öarabha·πÖga primarily holds a didactic function.8 The moral of the story is that, in much the same way that it is a breach of etiquette to eat without first offering hospitality and food to one‚Äôs guests, it also a ≈õi·π£·π≠a faux pas to be eaten‚Äîin this case by agni kravyƒÅda, consumer of flesh‚Äîwithout first going through all the social niceties.

[[P1281]]

In either case, in the classical reception histories of the story, RƒÅma and ≈öarabha·πÖga share space for approximately five minutes and exchange fewer than eighty words. This is hardly enough time for RƒÅma to perform the miracle of the bubbling hot springs. Adding insult to injury, the very idea in the first place that RƒÅma making spring water become hot (M. udabhi udaka u·π£·∏∑a kele·πÉ) is the sort of thing that would please ≈öarabha·πÖga (M. sarabha·πÖgƒÅprƒ´tyartha·∏•) belies even a passing exposure to the narrative. As we have seen, ≈öarabha·πÖga does not exactly stick around long enough to be pleased by any additions to his ƒÅ≈õrama. Perhaps even more significantly, the last thing in the world that a sage who is described as a miniature walking sun that constantly gives off ambient heat would need is someone else‚Äôs help in making things become hot. Much like VƒÅlmƒ´ki himself, whose portrait of the ‚ÄúVedic sage‚Äù ≈öarabha·πÖga‚Äîelsewhere appearing as an ascetic in Buddhist literature‚Äî reads as an attempt at incorporating and sanitizing an external tradition of violent asceticism, in many ways akin to the proto-Tantroid worship of Rudra through self-immolation practiced by A≈õvatthƒÅman in the Sauptikaparvan. The author of our inscription seems quite intent on forcibly taming‚Äîand Vedicizing‚Äîa much wider, wilder world.

Though it contains some phrases that remain obscure, the purely documentary portion of our inscription9 also seems to be animated by an analogous BrƒÅhma·πáizing ethos. In regard to the main grant within the central village, this establishes funding for food offerings and so forth to be given to the unspecified deity that rules the village (grƒÅmƒÅdhyedevo bonayƒÅ·πÉ sthala), allots two fields so that their earnings will go towards repairing broken buildings (cƒÅkulivƒÅ·πÉci tathƒÅ pa ‚Äì sthala·∏•), and assigns one field to be cultivated by tenant farmers, with the proceeds going to the temple of RƒÅma for the first several years and any remainder in subsequent years being put towards the funding of festivals (adhƒ´k mƒÅse bha·πÉgaleƒÅ kƒ´ rƒÅmƒÅsƒ´ ekado·πái sa·πÉvatsaraparya·πÉta so (mo?)·∏çale te·πÉci·πÉ karƒÅve·πÉ maga su·π£e ure te·πÉ ko·π≠hiƒÅ ca·πáeyƒÅ). In perpetuity, two additional villages are dedicated to providing financial support and material resources to the temples to Unhapakadeva and RƒÅma (tathƒÅ grƒÅmi unhapa[ka]devƒÅ·∏• rƒÅmƒÅ mele : / dasƒ´·πáe p≈´rve boy·πÉthƒ´ye dohi·πÉ devƒÅ·πÉ malƒÅ·∏• / sƒÅ·πÖgavi·πÉye·πÉ dohi·πÉ devƒÅ·πÉ malƒÅ). In this regard, the grant prescribes the installation of at least two more li·πÖgas (li·πÖgƒÅ·πÉ dohi devƒÅ malƒÅ), and, micromanaging the landscape, establishes a storage house for cloth (pa·π≠avade·πÉ sthala·∏•), lamp oil (tale·π£ala), as well as endowments providing jaggery and sugar (kƒÅmatu gu·∏çƒÅ sthala / tale·π£ala kƒÅpa sakƒÅragavƒÅ·πÉ sthala). It rations out which materials from plots of land [[P1282]] containing different types of trees have their products allotted to the temple (ci·πÉcavalƒ´ sthala pi·πÉpalajai sƒÅ·πÉgavi·πÉ sthala) and even specifies which plots of land are to self-cultivated or to be tended by hired labor. Rather distinctively, for this dimension has no earlier known parallels, it also grants in perpetuity a ‚Äúfertile field each to the BrƒÅhma·πáas engaged in the work [lit., the path] of the king,‚Äù (nƒÅva·πÉ mahuseta [S. madhuk·π£etra] teyƒÅ·πÉcƒ´ brƒÅhma·πáƒÅ pratyai rƒÅjamƒÅrgici), a phrase that most likely refers to the main minister HemƒÅ·∏çi and his deputy Somadeva Pa·πá·∏çita.

The most stunning dimension of the Unkeshwar inscription, however, is the circumstances under which it was executed, for the text tells us at this remote place on the edge of Seu·πáa world, the fulfillment of the grant was carried out in the presence of the master of elephants, whose identity we will discuss in a moment, the king‚Äôs most important (pradhƒÅna) minister, HemƒÅ·∏çi (S. HemƒÅdri) Pa·πá·∏çita, and, acting under HemƒÅdri and at his command, Somadeyo Pa·πá·∏çita, the lead administrator of the royal chancellery directing the scribes and record keepers. Now, as we have encountered ever so many time over the course of this study, in the context of the normative paradigm reflected in the documentary records of the early medieval Deccan, the establishment of a land-grant upon the building of a new temple entails both acts of dak·π£i·πáƒÅ on the part of the most prominent figures present as well as the transfer, either in person or in absentia, of the sovereignty of the space from secular to sacerdotal authorities an act marked by the washing of the feet of the local authority or ƒÅcƒÅrya. In [[P1283]] contradistinction, at Unkeshwar, none of the authorities present seem to be contributing anything at all‚Äîneither land nor resources‚Äîto the grant itself, either of their own accord or behalf of the state. Despite this being listed as an undying land-grant, there is no indication that sovereign authority has been transferred from the state to a new agent, or that the recipient of the grant has been endowed with new rights or no longer has to pay taxes. Instead, it seems that these high-ranking agents of the state are simply there to ensure that everything is being done according to the proper procedure, as part of a process that our prescriptive sources will call examination (parƒ´k·π£ƒÅ).

In his edition as well as his earlier partial translation10 of the Unkeshwar inscription, Tulpule has assumed that for the inauguration of Sara·πáanƒÅeka‚Äôs new temple complex, four distinctive social actors were in attendance representing the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava state. In Tulpule‚Äôs reading, these would include King RƒÅmacandra himself, the master of elephants, purportedly a certain BhƒÅvakadeva, the chief minister HemƒÅ·∏çi Pa·πá·∏çita, who as we shall see is none other than the great BrƒÅhma·πáa polymath and author of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, and finally, acting explicitly under HemƒÅ·∏çi‚Äôs authority, Somadeyo Pa·πá·∏çita, the leader of the scribes and record keepers. If, however, one examines the contemporary multilingual Seu·πáa YƒÅdava inscriptional corpus from this precise period, say, from the years 1260‚Äì1280 CE, attending to chancellery formulas and syntactical commonalities, as we shall see, it is evident that Tulpule is mistaken in respect to two rather significant details. Small as they might seem, they will have an enormous impact on the argument of the present chapter. His edition and translation of the second line reads as follows. The matters of contention have been highlighted in bold:

adyeha mƒÅhƒÅprau·∏çhapratƒÅpacakravartƒ´ ≈õrƒ´rƒÅmacandradeva·∏• vijayo tatapƒÅdapadmopajivi hƒÅthisƒÅhƒÅ·πáƒ´ bhƒÅvakadeva pradhƒÅna hemƒÅ·∏çi pa·πá·∏çita ta·πÉniropƒ´ta·∏• nƒÅeku·∏• kastu somadeyo pa·πá·∏çita.

Today, in the auspicious ≈öaka year 1201, known of as Vikrama Sa·πÉvatsara, the paramount sovereign, possessing great valour, ≈örƒ´ RƒÅmacandradeva, with his devoted chief of the elephant division of the army [BhƒÅvakadeva], Minister HemƒÅ·∏çi Pa·πá·∏çita, [[P1285]] and the deputy of the latter, NƒÅyaka KƒÅyastha Somadeva Pa·πá·∏çita, in his time [the present grant is made].11

Tulpule believes that the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava king RƒÅmacandradeva was himself present at Unkeshwar solely because he rather unexpectedly construes the word that follows the king‚Äôs canonical list of titles‚Äîvijayo‚Äîas modifying the noun RƒÅmacandra‚Äîvictorious RƒÅmachandra. This interpretation would place the king as one subject among many in the list of officials that soon follows. What Tulpule has overlooked is that this portion of the text is simply offering a shorthand form of an inherited chancellery formula signifying the date.12 Within this convention, the name of the reigning king and his titles is immediately followed by a term such as vijayodaya, or vijayarƒÅjya, one occasionally even sees just vijaya, and the sense is that this is an expression akin to the Western calendrical notion of measuring time ‚Äúin the year of our Lord.‚Äù In much the same way that if someone in medieval Europe were to speak of something happening in their hometown ‚Äúin the Year of Our Lord 1290,‚Äù he or she would very much not be implying that Jesus Christ was present in the flesh right then in that very village, when a land grant in old Marathi refers to the regnal year, it is not invoking the actual presence of the king onsite. In Sanskrit, we would express this sort of meaning, which postulates a continuing state of affairs co-current with the actual matter under discussion, using an extended locative absolute. More than mere pedantry, this observation‚Äîthat King RƒÅmacandra is present only in name and that other people are carrying out the affairs of the state‚Äîdoes not merely just set the stage for making sense of the other major place in this passage where Tulpule has gone astray. It also communicates something of vital interest about the actual workings of political authority within the YƒÅdava state. Almost without exception, in both our documentary record and the old Marathi and Sanskrit texts, after 1240 CE, the Seu·πáas appear simply as ciphers. In fact, the king rarely if ever leaves his court and instead delegates most if not all of his authority to another person who then acts out in the world ‚Äúas if he were the king himself.‚Äù [[P1286]]

As we have seen in our earlier chapters, apart from when they are discussing a ruling overlord (such as the KalyƒÅ·πái CƒÅ·∏∑ukya emperor) in discussing other strata of authoritative persons, the multilingual inscriptional records of the past five centuries have typically presented us with lists comprised of a diverse range of social actors and offices. Often to our consternation, these figures bear the same or comparable titles and it is very difficult to figure out their relative authority in relation to each other. Though to some degree this is a reflection of our own ignorance of the workings of early medieval courts, it also almost certainly the case that in the social realities we have been exploring, authority is commonly conceptualized as multicentric and diffuse. This is reflected in the proliferation of types of cabinet positions in the royal court, with the CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas, for example, recognizing sixteen distinctive subject and discipline specific advisory positions, each of which held governing authority only within their respective domain.13 We have seen this pluralistic theorization of both knowledge and authority as site and community-specific at work in scholastic, juridical, mythological, theological, political, and social domains throughout our investigation. By now they may well [[P1287]] have become so familiar that one might see them as part and parcel of a Deccani ethos, unchallengeable norms written into the fabric of reality.

One of the defining features of the Seu·πáa imperium in the half-century after the death of Si·πÖgha·πáa II is that at virtually every level of expression, these norms are repudiated, even actively dismantled. This interpretive choice is reflected in the Seu·πáa inscriptions themselves. Suddenly, for the first time in centuries, the chancellery formulas are emended with the aim of clearly delineating levels of dependence and authority among different social actors. Thus, while, in theory, the absent king himself sits at the top of the food chain, in the inscriptions it is the figure who subsists (tatpƒÅdapadmopajƒ´vi)‚Äîone might even say whose livelihood depends‚Äîon the feet of the king who is the actual acting authority. This figure‚Äîalways a single person who is often given a chain of titles specifying what would otherwise seem to be a series of unrelated responsibilities, offices, and specific accomplishments‚Äîis then seen as having at least one active subordinate. This position marked by the phrase, given various spellings, ‚Äúta·πÉniropita,‚Äù followed by that person‚Äôs name and titles. Tulpule observes in his dictionary14 that tan-niropita/niropƒ´ta is a tadbhava form of the Sanskrit phrase tat-nir≈´pita, and while this is certainly true, one might suppose that the identification of this exact parallel entails that its programmatic usage in these old Marathi documentary records is transposed from the Sanskrit sources. Rather unexpectedly, however, digital corpus analysis of a substantial sampling of literature in Sanskrit as well as inscriptions makes it clear that not only is such a usage in this context seemingly unattested, but in fact the terms tannir≈´pita, tatnir≈´pita or tadanur≈´pita up through this time period are virtually never used in reference to [[P1288]] people or within a social setting. Instead, they are almost exclusively attested within high scholastic discourses, especially works of Navya NyƒÅya or discussions of the logic of figuration in Sanskrit poetics. In this context, they are terms of art signifying the absolutely determined subordination and logical dependency of one precisely specified thing over another, with the dominant subject being whatever is the intended referent of the pronoun ‚Äútat.‚Äù In short, what we have here is an example of a synthetic attempt to transpose the ‚Äúartificial‚Äù logical clarity found in certain exalted registers of Sanskrit scholasticism down into the messy and imperfect world of human experience. As we shall see, this is precisely the interpretive project that occupies the majority of textual production in the court of the later Seu·πáas themselves and that practically defines the oeuvre of the iconoclastic poetician, Dharma≈õƒÅstrin, public intellectual, administrator, and de facto ruler of the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava kingdom, HemƒÅdri S≈´ri.

Our examination of the Unkeshwar inscription as the product of a specific chancellery culture with highly regularized formulas also prepares us for wrestling with Tulpule‚Äôs more significant misinterpretation, namely, his posting of a certain ‚ÄúBhƒÅvakadeva‚Äù as the master of elephants (hƒÅthisƒÅhƒÅ·πáƒ´) who subsists on the feet of the king. Were we to accept this reading, this would presuppose the existence of an entirely different, and quite plausibly higher-ranking person than the chief minister, HemƒÅdri. On the basis of textual parallels, however, there is good reason to understand ‚ÄúBhƒÅvakadeva‚Äù not as someone‚Äôs name, but as one of many adjectives qualifying the main subject, HemƒÅ·∏çri, who is himself assigned the office of master of the elephants amidst his many other titles. Here, it is not the old Marathi corpus, but rather a nearly contemporary inscription15 discussing HemƒÅdri and his social role drawn from the [[P1289]] Sanskrit corpus16 that offers our strongest evidence. Though the text is issued on behalf of both RƒÅmacandra and the ‚Äúacting king‚Äù HemƒÅdri in 1289 CE, both figures are discussed solely within distinctive sets of locative absolute constructions set off from the rest of the text. They were not present at the drafting and distribution of these copper plates. Instead, that task was assigned to their subordinate, a certain Acyuta NƒÅyaka, who happens to be the grandson of the famous Jalha·πáa, military commander, master of the elephants, and compiler of the poetic anthology the S≈´ktimuktƒÅvali. In any case, the relevant passage runs as follow:

Now while King RƒÅmacandra, moon to the lotuses of the Yadu race, brilliant with the series of all titles such as: ‚Äúhe who dries up the pools of the hostile monarchs‚Äô glory by his most fierce heat [or, majesty], who adorns the girdles of the ladies of the quarters of space with lines of precious pearls arising his own stainless virtues, who displays the awfulness of a N·πõsimha by tearing open the surface of mighty foemen‚Äôs breasts, whose face-lotus is kissed by the bees which are the restless eye-corners of ≈öambara‚Äôs slayer [KƒÅma], who by his own arm has won the name EkƒÅ·πÖgavƒ´ra, who is a treasury of all virtues, who is a Vƒ´ra-NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa to the demons his enemies, who by his life makes light of Grandsire [Brahman], a Grandsire of monarchs, who is the lord of the city of DvƒÅravatƒ´, a lion shattering the elephants of the G≈´rjara, an elephant in uprooting the tall trees of Tƒìli·πÖga, a blast of the Day of Doom in extinguishing the lamps of the MƒÅlavas, a trees of desire possessing the virtue of liberality,‚Äù is reigning over the whole girdle of the earth;

And, while HemƒÅdri, superintendent of all the elephant riders (hastipakƒÅdhyak·π£e) inspiring men to appreciate the fineness of his virtues (nijagu·πáasubhaga[bhƒÅvuke] bhƒÅvake)17 [emphasis added], conqueror of the province of JhƒÅ·∏çi, crest jewel of ministers, a R≈çha·πáa Mountain of the gem of virtues, is exercising the administration of the whole kingdom which has been obtained by his favor and is controlling the whole treasury.18

[[P1290]]

In the old Marathi inscription, the syntax in the phrase ‚ÄúhƒÅthisƒÅhƒÅ·πáƒ´ bhƒÅvakadeva·∏• pradhƒÅna hemƒÅ·∏çipa·πá·∏çita·∏•‚Äù by itself is somewhat uncertain. As we have seen, it could refer to two distinct subjects, a master of elephants named BhƒÅvakadeva and the minister HemƒÅ·∏çi Pa·πá·∏çita, or, as will turn out to be the case, it could offer a series of adjectives assigning specific roles to the single subject HemƒÅdri. In contrast, the structure of the Sanskrit parallel, in which every compounded phrase placed in the locative must refer back to the single subject, is unambiguous. Here, we find, in precisely the same sequence, albeit with just a bit of further embellishment, that it is HemƒÅdri that is being characterized as the one who holds the office of overseeing the elephants (hastipakƒÅdhyak·π£e) and then, taking the compound literally, setting aside for a time its domain-specific connotations, ‚Äúbeing the one whose good fortune is a product of his own good qualities‚Äù (nijagu·πáasubhaga[bhƒÅvuke]bhƒÅvake).‚Äù While in the case [[P1291]] of the old Marathi, the grammatical work that this term ‚ÄúbhƒÅvaka‚Äù is doing is somewhat obscure19‚Äîone possibility being that the term is again being used in the sense of lord of literary connoisseurs‚Äîwhat is quite clear is that the old Marathi text has drawn upon a preexisting formula for describing HemƒÅdri‚Äôs various titles and that the text it is not in fact referring to two separate people. The Sanskrit copper plate also makes crystal clear that the enormous scope of the authority ascribed to HemƒÅdri‚Äîas we shall see, most often by himself‚Äî‚Äúexercising the administration of the whole kingdom,‚Äù ‚Äúcontrolling the whole treasury,‚Äù as well as having war elephants at his beck and call, was no fanciful literary trope but hard social fact.

Here we would do well to remember that, among the Seu·πáas in particular, the master of the elephants held direct control over the most important component of the YƒÅdava war machine‚Äìits pachyderm shock troops, the nuclear weapons of their day.20 From the time of Bhillama V, this position seems always to have been held by a learned BrƒÅhma·πáa,21 and its conferral seems to have served repeatedly as the fastest track to political advancement, often culminating in assuming the highest office in the land, after that of the king himself. In fact, except in the case of the apparently preternaturally talented BrƒÅhma·πáa general Khole≈õvara, [[P1292]] who appears to have used other tactics,22 it seems that most of the major victories of the YƒÅdavas were dependent on the deploying of huge battalions of elephants in the service of crushing their enemies. Effectively, so long as the king did not object, the office of master of the elephants could decide when, how, and where to wage war, and could also bring the threat of physical violence to bear against those who resisted this authority. As the Thane inscription tells us, this is precisely what HemƒÅdri seems to have done when he conquered the JhƒÅ·∏çima·πá·∏çala, a region to the north of Marathwada, the reincorporation of which into the Seu·πáa domain is never assigned in our inscriptions to king RƒÅmacandra himself. As we shall see in the next chapter, the master of elephants was also entrusted to prosecute the king‚Äôs own wars and, in the absence of the king himself, to receive the homage of subjugated rulers who the Seu·πáa forces has defeated on the battlefield.

As both the Unkeshwar and Thane inscriptions make plain, in the case of HemƒÅdri, sovereign ‚Äúhard power‚Äù over the military found its complement in autonomous economic power through ‚Äúmanaging the entirety of the royal treasury,‚Äù and soft power, in his capacity as chief minister and policy advisor guiding at least two generations of Seu·πáa kings. Perhaps most critically, as ≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara, HemƒÅdri assumed an active and direct oversight not only over the workings of the royal bureaucracy, comprised of scribes, accountants, and record keepers, itself, but ultimately, as he would reimagine the role, of the production, evaluation, and preservation of documentary record keeping throughout the Seu·πáa world.

As we will soon see, HemƒÅdri was at once a brilliant and original theologian and the architect of a new vision for articulating a powerful and pious ‚ÄúHindu‚Äù state. His most immediately evident legacy, however, from an Indological perspective, are four surviving Sanskrit treatises, all of which suffer from poor transmission and inadequate editions. We have the ‚ÄúResurrection of the Ayurveda‚Äù (ƒÄyurvedarasƒÅyana), his commentary on the A·π£·π≠ƒÅ·πÖgah·πõdaya;23 the ‚ÄúDiscrimination Regarding the Play of Hari‚Äù (Harilƒ´lavivekƒÅ), his commentary on what is essentially a thematic index to key narrative episodes in the BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa;24 and, the ‚ÄúIlluminator of Enstasy‚Äù (Kaivalyadƒ´pikƒÅ), which ostensibly comments on his disciple Bopadeva‚Äôs anthologization of verses, the MuktƒÅphala, from the BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa.25 And of course, we have the ‚ÄúWish-Fulfilling Gem of the Four Classes‚Äù (CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái), a voluminous Dharma≈õƒÅstra nibandha.26 And yet, without any exaggeration, in the entirety of the history of Sanskrit thought, there are few figures whose legacy had a greater concrete impact, within the medium of ≈õƒÅstra, in the domain of religion, and upon the wider social world.27

[[P1294]]

Sadly, the remainder of our literature discussing HemƒÅdri simply treats our author as an embodied instantiation of a perennial BrƒÅhma·πáical worldview, often in a manner that directly conflicts with the theology in the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, without consulting his Sanskrit works. Emblematic of this trend is the usually exemplary G. Sontheimer, author of what was until recently the most substantial essay on HemƒÅdri, who relies exclusively on the old Marathi sources of the MahƒÅnubhavikas in representing his subject. G√ºnther-Dietz Sontheimer, ‚ÄúGod, Dharma and Society in the Yadava Kingdom of Devagiri According to the Lilacharitra of Chakradhara.‚Äù In G√ºnther-Dietz Sontheimer: Essays on Religion, Literature, and Law, ed. Heidrum Br√ºckner, et. al., (New Delhi: Manohar, 2004), 305‚Äì326. Sontheimer‚Äôs model has been much expanded upon and refined to good effect in regard to the old Marathi sources in Christian Novetzke‚Äôs The Quotidian Revolution as well as in an additional essay composed for the Oxford History of Hinduism‚Äôs volume on Hindu law. Both of these works offer us vivid portraits of HemƒÅdri as perceived by the MahƒÅnubhƒÅvika communities he persecuted. Novetzke, however, not being a Sanskritist, relies on Sontheimer‚Äôs characterization of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs intellectual project, which alas is entirely uninformed by a study of his texts. Christian Lee Novetzke, The Quotidian Revolution: Vernacularization, Religion, and the Premodern Public Sphere in India (New York: Columbia University Press, 2016); Christian Lee Novetzke, ‚ÄúVernacularization,‚Äù in The Oxford History of Hinduism: Hindu Law: A New History of Dharma≈õƒÅstra, ed. Donald R. Davis (New York: Oxford University Press, 2017), 480‚Äì96.

A similar issue is in evidence in Mary McGee‚Äôs monumental dissertation comparing the representation of vrata practice in the nibandha literature, especially vis-√Ý-vis the role of women, with the author‚Äôs extensive fieldwork among Maharashtrian women performing vratas. McGee is the only author to date who has studied and translated extensive selections from the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, treating whole vrata cycles. However, her representation of the ‚ÄúnibandhakƒÅra‚Äù theology of vrata, against which she contrasts the lived tradition, rests upon a synthetic composite ‚Äúnibandha theology‚Äù that is produced out of an aggregate of her sources without attending to the specific theological positions and procedures of the individual works. In the case of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, in the absence of reading the opening portions of the Vratakha·πá·∏ça or its ParibhƒÅ·π£ƒÅ, she ascribes a theology of vrata to HemƒÅdri that is in many regards antithetical to the one propounded in his text, which we will explore in the next chapter. Mary McGee, Feasting and Fasting: The Vrata Tradition and Its Significance for Hindu Women: A Thesis (PhD diss., Harvard Divinity School, 1987).

Recent careful textual work, though restricted to the DƒÅnakha·πá·∏ça of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, has been carried out by David Brick in the context of producing his edition of the DƒÅnakƒÅ·πá·∏ça of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara. Building upon earlier efforts, such as those of Trautmann, Florinda De Simini has added to our understanding by mapping out the subsequent reception of the text. David Brick, ed. and trans., Brahmanical Theories of the Gift: A Critical Edition and Annotated Translation of the DƒÅnakƒÅ·πá·∏ça of the K·πõtyakalpataru (Cambridge, MA: Harvard Oriental Series, 2015); Thomas R. Trautmann, ‚ÄúThe gift in India,‚Äù HAU: Journal of Ethnographic Theory 7, no. 2 (2017): 485‚Äì496; Florinda De Simini, Of Gods and Books: Ritual and Knowledge Transmission in the Manuscript Cultures of Premodern India (Berlin: De Gruyter, 2016). Brick‚Äôs work builds upon the pioneering efforts of Aiyangar Rangaswami, the K·πõtyakalpataru‚Äôs original editor, each volume of whose work meticulously documents the textual parallels between Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs and HemƒÅdri‚Äôs writings. Though its treatment of HemƒÅdri in its first volume is disappointingly terse, P. V. Kane‚Äôs History of Dharma≈õƒÅstra incorporates references throughout, cross referencing HemƒÅdri‚Äôs CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái within nearly every topic. P. V. Kane, History of Dharma≈õƒÅstra, 8 vols. (Pune: Bhandarkar Oriental Research Institute, 1930‚Äì1962). Though it does not focus on the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, touching upon it only briefly, De Simini has also offered our first substantive study on the technique of the dharmanibandhakƒÅras. Florinda De Simini, ‚ÄúObservations on the Use of Quotations in Sanskrit Dharmanibandhas,‚Äù Journal of Indian Philosophy 43, no. 4‚Äì5 (2015): 601‚Äì624.

In terms of the use of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs work as a repository for PurƒÅ·πáic citations, apart from the collected works of P. K. Gode, as examples of the practice one might fruitfully consults: Albert Henry Allen, ‚ÄúThe Va·π≠a-SƒÅvitrƒ´-Vrata, According to HemƒÅdri and the VratƒÅrka,‚Äù Journal of the American Oriental Society 21 (1900): 53‚Äì66. Rajendra Chandra Hazra, Studies in the PurƒÅ·πáic Records on Hindu Rites and Customs (Dehli: Motilal Banarsidass, 1987). Discussions of the secondary literature on HemƒÅdri‚Äôs in relation to MuktƒÅphala will be reserved for the next chapter. [[P1295]]

As this chapter will make plain, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái reinvented fundamentally and irrevocably the discipline of Dharma≈õƒÅstra and the role of the Dharma≈õƒÅstrin. Under his direction, this knowledge, and as well as its practitioners, who had typically held the religion of ordinary people at best in thinly veiled contempt, is refashioned such that they become conceptually central to the lived practice of Indian religions. Through his intervention in the domain of ≈õƒÅstra, HemƒÅdri takes up arcane hermeneutical strategies from the domain of P≈´rva Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ and the emergent tradition of the new logic, and for first time in the history of Indic thought deploys them in the service of offering a robust intellectual defense of the commonsense conclusions of every day Indian religions and its transactional economies of spirit-based favors. In doing so, the Wish- Fulfilling Gem of the Four Classes effectively made possible much of what is most familiar to us in early modern and modern SmƒÅrta BrƒÅhma·πáical Hinduism.

As we will explore in the final chapter, working in a very different register, with the MuktƒÅphala and the Kaivalyadƒ´pikƒÅ, HemƒÅdri articulates for the first time, seemingly ex nihilo, the affective aestheticized devotional theology of the Vai·π£·πáava rasika.28 As a wealth of scholarly literature, largely oblivious to the source of this discourse, has demonstrated, these values [[P1296]] and conceptual frameworks come to define a plurality of early modern Vai·π£·πáava devotional traditions, especially those found in north and central India and Bengal. Concurrent to his ƒÄla·πÉkƒÅrika-inspired interpretive project, which will set the program for much of the theological writings of the Gau·∏çƒ´ya GosvƒÅmƒ´s, though this influence remains even less recognized, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs bhakti writings also present us with the earliest instance of scholastic reflection in the tradition of theistic Advaita VedƒÅnta philosophy, prefiguring much of what has been assumed to be revolutionary within the writings of figures like Madhus≈´dana Sarasvatƒ´. The grand irony, of course, is that in their early modern reception, what were originally two facets of a single interpretive project‚Äîaffective devotionalism and theistic nondual VedƒÅnta‚Äîwould come to be seen as distinct and opposing schools of thought.

For a general survey of the literature see Raghu Nath Sharma, Bhakti in the Vai·π£·πáava Rasa-≈õƒÅstra (Delhi, Pratibha Prakashan, 1996). For a representative sampling of theorization on bhaktirasa drawn from a range of traditions: Neil Delmonico, ‚ÄúSacred Rapture: The Bhakti-rasa Theory of Rupa Goswamin,‚Äù Journal of Vaishnava Studies 6, no. 1 (1998): 75‚Äì98; David L. Haberman, Acting as a Way of Salvation: A Study of RƒÅgƒÅnugƒÅ Bhakti SƒÅdhana (Delhi: Motilal Banarsidass, 2001); Monika Horstmann, Crossing the Ocean of Existence: Braj BhƒÅ·π£ƒÅ Religious Poetry from Rajasthan; A Reader (Wiesbaden: Harrassowitz, 1983); Barbara A. Holdrege, Bhakti and Embodiment: Fashioning Divine Bodies and Devotional Bodies in K·πõ·π£·πáa Bhakti (New York: Routledge, 2015); Lance Nelson, ‚ÄúBhakti Rasa for the Advaitin Renunciate: Madhus≈´dana Sarasvatƒ´‚Äôs Theory of Devotional Sentiment,‚Äù Religious Traditions: A Journal in the Study of Religion 12 (1989): 1‚Äì16; James D. Redington, ‚ÄúElements of a Vallabhite Bhakti-Synthesis,‚Äù Journal of the American Oriental Society 112, no. 2 (1992): 287‚Äì 294; David Buchta, ‚ÄúEvoking Rasa Through Stotra: R≈´pa GosvƒÅmin‚Äôs Lƒ´lƒÅm·πõta, A List of K·πõ·π£·πáa‚Äôs Names,‚Äù International Journal of Hindu Studies 20, no. 3 (2016): 355‚Äì371.

Despite the undeniable significance of these learned contributions, comprising a neglected legacy that will receive ample attention by the final chapter‚Äôs closure, the present chapter anchors its inquiry not in reflection upon how HemƒÅdri the brilliant scholar sets the agenda for the coming centuries, but rather on how HemƒÅdri the strategist and effective [[P1297]] administrator brings closure to the ethos of the past. Despite his lofty position and self- identification with a BrƒÅhma·πáical ethos, HemƒÅdri does not envision spearheading a conservative restoration of the storied ideals of some imagined BrƒÅhma·πáical golden age that has run afoul of the degeneracies of the kali yuga. Instead, rather candidly, he offers to his readers what amounts to a genuinely revolutionary program for social and religious change. With a degree of self-awareness‚Äîeffectively otherwise unknown to the world of ≈õƒÅstra‚Äîthat what he has to offer is radical and unprecedented, through his writings, HemƒÅdri aims to establish new norms appropriate for a brave new world. Through the state and its bureaucracy, he makes those norms actionable. It is in this spirit that he writes in the introduction to the final book of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, the definitive deliberation concerning ritual timing (kƒÅlanir·πáaya):

Of him [king MahƒÅdeva], the Lord of all the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáas (sarva≈õrƒ´kara·πáaprabhu·∏•) has the name HemƒÅdri. Because of his munificence above all others, he [HemƒÅdri] was capable of producing all forms of auspiciousness/wealth for everyone (sarva≈õrƒ´kara·πáaprabhu·∏•). . . .

On the part of that lord of ≈õrƒ´kara·πáas, there was a certain cleverness when it came to writing. By him, the walls of the directions were quickly imprinted with the praises (pra≈õasti) of [his] glory.

With his power, having wiped clean the flawed script has been written by the creator on the foreheads of the people, he [HemƒÅdri] now pens this auspicious [script], and the creator/fate wondrously makes it authoritative (pramƒÅ·πáƒ´kurute). . . .

I imagine the ocean/treasury is becoming as if it is full of waves‚Äîwith the water that is the charity he has given. Even at the end of the eon, he does not put things out of balance due to [the bends of the serpent ≈öe·π£a/ the flaws that are the remainder (≈õe·π£a) in the balance book]. And so, having discerned the security/surety of the state of the ocean, may [NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa], the enemy of the Daityas, sleep peacefully on the bed that is the body of the bull among serpents [≈öe·π£a].

What can we say of that marvel that is the substance of the deluge of rainwater that is his charity? In contrast to [such charity], the fame of the munificence of others takes on the value of a clod of grass. [[P1298]]

Fallen on the ground, that [rain of charity] erases the mud [on the feet] of the people who absorb it. When it is set down in the hands of the petitioners, it erases the evil script [fate] inscribed on the plank that is the forehead. . . .

Though he bears the guise of a twice-born, HemƒÅdri alone is recognized as the ‚Äúmountain of gold‚Äù (HemƒÅdri). [As he is] possessed of a continuum of nobility, they say that his right arm is the wish-fulfilling tree.

Having seen that the three worlds were impoverished in stories of dharma due to the power of the kali yuga, to offer assistance to them out of compassion, he gracefully manifested the CintƒÅma·πái.

Having uttered its four divisions, known by name as Vrata, DƒÅna, Tƒ´rtha and Mok·π£a, for the purpose of describing matters that supplement that, he composes the remainder (Pari≈õe·π£a) division.29

[[P1299]]

Beneath its wit and mythological allusions, this is poetry about bureaucratic administration, about providing social services, about banking, and most of all, about centrally directed social reforms. HemƒÅdri‚Äîa name that quite literally means ‚Äúthe mountain of gold,‚Äù provides the people‚Äîespecially ascetics and BrƒÅhma·πáas‚Äîwith an unceasing flow of philanthropy. At the same time, so that god and king alike can sleep easefully knowing their kingdom‚Äôs safety and success is secured, he ensures the financial security and social stability of the realm. In the effective absence of the slumbering king, his minister inscribes throughout the realm formalized proclamations (pra≈õasti) praising his own glory. It is not the inactive and distant king, but the engaged minister, who can and has accomplished what more distant powers did not dare.

HemƒÅdri rejects the fatalistic pan-Indo-European notion that each individual person‚Äôs destiny, long before they were born, was secretly inscribed upon their forehead by the creator deity at the moment of their conception. Breaking from the inherited weight of tradition and the claims it lays upon the individual, which he sees as fallible and constricting, our author asserts that he has drafted for humankind a new and better future. He has changed the rules of the game that everyone thought immutable, and so BrahmƒÅ must resign himself to making authoritative (pramƒÅ·πáƒ´kurute) ‚Äîquite literally, to establishing new pramƒÅ·πáas, whole new sets of world-ordering ordinances. In the manner of charismatic leaders everywhere, HemƒÅdri claims that he has ushered in an era of unparalleled fortune and riches. But whereas, especially in early medieval or even early modern South Asia, we would expect such promises to be founded upon appeal to a higher authority, be it special messages from the gods, an esoteric transmission from a lineage, or declarations of one‚Äôs own divinity, instead, in an almost relentlessly ‚Äúsecular‚Äù manner, HemƒÅdri grounds his righteous vision in the power of his office.

As we have already begun to see, our polymath was effectively the acting ruler under several YƒÅdava kings in succession. In this capacity he was awarded a myriad of titles and a wide range of responsibilities. And yet, time and again in his writings, his rhetoric exalts above everything else what is at first glance a rather unassuming position, the office of sarva≈õrƒ´kara·πáaprabhu·∏• or ≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara. The title quite literally means, ‚Äúlord of all the scribal bureaucrats.‚Äù On the surface, this seems to speak to a rather restricted scope of influence. On this basis, the very little that has been written about HemƒÅdri in the past has assumed that whatever his gifts as an intellectual, he was minor functionary. What such [[P1300]] analysis misses is that, as we shall see, while the comprehensive and felicitous use HemƒÅdri would make of this office seems to be sui generis, though it was unknown to the KalyƒÅ·πái CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas and their allies, elevation of the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara to the center of government is not without precedent. Thus, for example in the Lekhapaddhati, a promissory note from 1267 CE bearing the seal of the kings of Valabhi, travelling as a sample document alongside a scribal template, provides us with confirmation that by the middle of the thirteenth century, the previously distinct roles of chief minister of the king (mahƒÅmƒÅtya) and lord of scribes (≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara) had, as a matter of course, become conflated.

VyavahƒÅrapatra from the reign of ≈örƒ´ Sara·πÖgadeva:

When the great minister, the illustrious Dhara·πáƒ´dhara, subsisting as subject to his lotus feet, was conducting the entirety of the business of the royal seal, from the illustrious [office of] ≈õrƒ´kara·πáa onward. . . .

Template for the production of vyavahƒÅrapatras, 1231 CE:

When the great minister, the illustrious Insert Name Here, subsisting as subject to his lotus feet, was conducting the entirety of the business of the royal seal, from the illustrious [office of] ≈õrƒ´kara·πáa onward. . . .30

As a range of epigraphical evidence beginning in the late twelfth century from southern Gujarat and southern Maharashtra attests, this new office of ≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara (lord of the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáas) married the administrative, sacerdotal and apotropaic functions of the king‚Äôs chief councilor and master of mantras with command over a bureaucratic apparatus possessing the type of intimate local knowledge necessary for ensuring the extraction of revenue and the management of debts. Succinctly, were he to choose to make use of it, a ≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara had [[P1301]] both the influence and means for concretely reshaping South Asia‚Äôs cultural and religious landscape. As we saw in chapter two, ≈õrƒ´kara·πáas, called senobova in old Kannada, are a class, not a caste, of professional scribes, responsible for the composition and production of the ephemeral paperwork required of every institution if it wished to be legally legible. If the world, as SkandasvƒÅmin had said, ‚Äúruns on a never-ending transactionality,‚Äù the role with which the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáa was entrusted was at once to document those transactions and to formalize them into a standardized shape. Whether we are talking about personal correspondence, testimony in court, proclamations, contracts, minutes for a meeting, financial records, ledgers of debt and expenditure, or the text of an inscription, the task of the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáa is to rationalize the error-ridden messiness of lived human experience. If we treat the discussions in Dharma≈õƒÅstra as documentary as opposed to aspirational, regardless of its values and normativities, any political power or social body that wanted to ensure that, in case of conflict between communities or within the community, its rights could be adjudicated, would necessarily have had such people on staff producing meticulous records. In a world order defined by juridical and epistemic diversity, the office and praxis of the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáa represented a rare instance of a universal institution operating within a shared framework.

Up until the end of the twelfth century, neither our literary nor our documentary sources offer any suggestions of the scribe as anything other than an independent agent beholden to his specific community or teacher. In the middle of the thirteenth century, most pointedly under the Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas, however, what seems to happen is something with no prior precedent‚Äîthe emergence of a brotherhood of scribes distributed throughout the pluralistically ordered world, but all answering to a centralized state. In other words, a bureaucratic apparatus [[P1302]] possessing intimate local knowledge, transcripts of every meeting, everyone‚Äôs genealogies, if they were invested in a hall of records, all the police records, all the contracts, as well as the ledgers that enabled the extraction of revenue and the management of debts were suddenly no longer beholden to the institutions of which they were a part. Instead, it seems their records, in theory, fell under the jurisdiction of the state, or more properly, under its ≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara and his acting officers. Beyond any ambiguity, we can situate HemƒÅdri in this position by 1260 CE, when King MahƒÅdeva effectively assumes the throne, but there is also some rather suggestive indications in the inscriptional record that HemƒÅdri had already risen rather high within the ranks of the institution of managing the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáas as early as 1254 CE, when he first appears in our inscriptional record, already composing inscriptions.

If one had to identify a year and say ‚Äúthis is when the ≈öaiva age ended,‚Äù a particularly strong case could be made for 1246 CE, for this is when the great warrior, statesman, and ≈öƒÅkta initiate Si·πÖgha·πáa II dies after reigning for nearly half a century. He is replaced on the throne by his son KƒÅnharadeva, who would rule for a bit more than a decade (c. 1245/1247‚Äì 1258/1260, depending on your sources). In our documentary records‚Äîall at once‚Äîeverything changes.31 With the singular exception of Si·πÖgha·πáa II‚Äôs own guru, Sarve≈õvara, whose estate at Munavalli is supported into his dotage, the Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas cease to patronize the KƒÅlamukhas.

[[P1303]]

Figure 17: Munavalli, Bhairava worshipped by Sarve≈õvara (life-size). [[P1304]]

Figure 18: Munavalli, companion to Bhairava with mƒÅtulu·πÖga, (miniature) [[P1305]]

Figure 19: Munavalli: Portrait of Sarve≈õvara, rƒÅjaguru of Si·πÖgha·πáa and the last KƒÅlamukha patronized by the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava court.

Except for a few sites with close ties to the court‚Äîmost notably Ellora and Pandharpur‚Äî the Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas largely give up on issuing traditional undying land grants. In fact, there is also an almost total disappearances of instances in which the king or his representatives formally renew or acknowledge the existence of such institutions. Instead, as early as 1249, the formalized washing of the feet of a sacerdotal authority and acknowledgement of his and his community‚Äôs autonomy is replaced by new approach, which we see illustrated in an edict issued by an officer named Kanaya of behalf of the emperor KƒÅnharadeva, in which ‚Äúthe worshipper of the sovereign‚Äôs feet, entrusted with the collection of taxes in the districts of Kolhapur and the whole of the king‚Äôs domain,‚Äù grants to some BrƒÅhma·πáas in a village limited rights of use over land to be accompanied by regular taxation. From 1248 onward, often by force, the sovereignty of differentiated spaces, even those of great [[P1306]] antiquity, is revoked. Their territory and revenue production capacity is integrated into the apparatus of the state. The management of these now circumscribed land grants is almost invariably placed into new hands.

In the majority of cases, the new custodians, whether we are talking about temples or villages, are BrƒÅhma·πáas who are represented through a rhetoric that extols quite specifically not just their lineage but their knowledge and active practice of Vedic ritual as well as their mastery of VedƒÅnta. While BrƒÅhma·πáas who seem to identify as ≈öaiva continue to form a substantial demographic, we also suddenly see the emergence of a range of types of Vai·π£·πáava communities, now entirely independent from a central focus on the worship of Narasi·πÉha. In particular, there is sudden proliferation of temples dedicated to GopƒÅla or K·πõ·π£·πáa as well as the installation of images of K·πõ·π£·πáa into previously ≈öaiva ritual center centers. Beginning from 1248, where a gƒÅvu·πá·∏ça named Kaluvar Singa in Hebbali, Karnataka makes a donation in support of the ‚ÄúvƒÅrƒ´‚Äù on behalf of Vi·π≠·π≠hal of Pandharpur,32 we also see our first indications of popular Marathi devotional religion emerging on the local level.

[[P1307]]

The case of the Purasiddhƒì≈õvara temple at Haveri, a famous ≈öaiva ritual center south of Dharwad, though in some ways slightly eccentric, offers us a rather elegant illustration of many of these dynamics presented in rapid succession.33 With our earliest, though decidedly unhelpful, inscription dating back to the time of the RƒÅ·π£·π≠rak≈´·π≠as, in something like its present form the temple seems to have been established under VikramƒÅditya VI‚Äôs father ƒÄvahamalla (1042‚Äì68 CE), though the agrahƒÅra itself is plausibly of much greater antiquity, and we have records relating to the CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas, Kalachuri, Hoysalas, and Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas. More specifically, we have a number of inscriptions issued during the reign of the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava King Si·πÖgha·πáadeva II by a range of different types of social actors, one from 1227, two from 1228, and one from 1230 CE. Sadly, and this seems to be true for the whole corpus, each of these has [[P1308]] sustained damage in various places such that in one instance the donor‚Äôs name is illegible. That being said, in the time of Si·πÖghanadeva, in the cases where the donor is known‚Äîin one instance this is JƒÅyidevanƒÅyaka, in one is it is the MahƒÅma·πÉ·∏ça·∏∑ƒìsvara Lak·π£mƒ´pƒÅ·∏∑adƒìva, and in another it is some merchants, Deccese·π≠·π≠i and MƒÅyise·π≠·π≠i, acting with the permission of Lak·π£mƒ´pƒÅ·∏∑adƒìva‚Äîwhen the relevant portion of the inscription is preserved, the figures in question promises to protect (pratipƒÅ·∏∑aka/ pratipƒÅ·∏∑isuvaru) the undying land grant (ƒÅca·πÉdrƒÅrkkatƒÅra·πÉbara/ ca·πÉdrƒÅrkkasthƒÅyiya·πÉbara·πÉ saluttamirƒõ/ dharmma ƒÅca·πÉdrƒÅrkkƒÅ·πÉbara·πÉ).34 Furthermore, when enough of the text is preserved to communicate the recipient‚Äîagain in three cases‚Äîthe chief benefactory acting on behalf of the god Siddhƒì≈õvara is a ≈öaiva ƒÅcƒÅrya. Depending on the nature of his relationship with the donors, this figure is referred to in slightly different terms. Thus, while he is called by the merchants, ‚Äúour auspicious rƒÅjaguru J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õidƒìva‚Äù (≈õrƒ´madrƒÅjaguru j√±ƒÅnarƒÅsidƒìvara), to an unknown [[P1309]] but clearly less intimately connected patron he is simply ‚Äúthe recipient of the donation being the main figure J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õidƒìvara (j√±ƒÅnarƒÅsidƒìvaru mukhyavƒÅgi pƒÅtra),‚Äù and from the perspective of the powerful MahƒÅma·πá·∏çalƒì≈õvara Lak·π£mƒ´pƒÅ·∏∑adƒìva, he is simply to be addressed by name along with the council of ruling authorities whose social origin is here unmarked (j√±ƒÅnarƒÅsidƒìvaru·πÉ mahƒÅjana·πÉga·∏∑u). For our purposes what is most important is that this demonstrates that during the reign of Si·πÖgha·πáadeva II, independent of personal affiliation, donors and most likely visitors to Haveri, whether they were making offerings to the main deity SiddhanƒÅtha or to secondary figure like Ga·πáe≈õa, would have addressed themselves to the resident ≈öaiva authority and only secondarily to a village council of indeterminate social composition.

The contrast between this corpus of documents and the two inscriptions at Haveri issued in the reign of KƒÅnharadeva are stark indeed. In what seems to be the earlier of the texts (though how early is uncertain as the date is illegible), the YƒÅdava king sends his Da·πá·∏çanƒÅyaka, described as the ‚Äúworshipper at the feet of the venerable Kandhara Deva, twice-borns, and gurus, and the subduer of enemy armies,‚Äù Cau·πá·∏ça/Camu·πá·∏çarƒÅya, not to make a donation but to ensure the proper administration of the grant. Cau·πá·∏ça we learn, had been ruling (rƒÅjya·πÉ geyyuttamirƒõ) on behalf of the king from ‚Äúhis palace at Puligere,‚Äù which is to say, he had taken over as his own property the decidedly ≈öƒÅkta-≈öaiva undying land-grant at Lak·π£mƒì≈õvara, with its open links to SomanƒÅtha and the KƒÅpƒÅlikas. As the inscription all but concludes, for we are dealing afterwards with a very damaged text, after honoring the controlling authority with the customary water offering‚Äînoticeably, nobody washes anybody‚Äôs feet‚ÄîCau·πá·∏ça discusses the nature of the community that will manage the grant:

The greatness of the Four Hundred (nƒÅn≈´rvvara), for whom the customary water libation was performed (dhƒÅrƒÅp≈´rvvakam mƒÅ·∏çida), is as follows:

[S.] The dwelling place of BrahmƒÅ/BrƒÅhma·πáas is the abode of the Vedas without remainder. It is encircled by the bearers of wisdom. It is the great oranament to the circle of the earth belonging to the twice-borns who are kings of Nalapurƒ´. The circle of the uplifting assembly (sabhƒÅ) is worthy of praise every day and every moment by those asking questions and the learned epistemologists intent upon its renowned inspiration.

The Four Hundred are devoted to the yamas and niyamas, recitation of sacred texts, mantra repetition, oblations, meditation, conduct, and virtues (svasti yamaniyamasvƒÅdhyƒÅyajapahomasamƒÅdhi≈õƒ´lagu·πáanirataru·πÉ); their actions are always benevolent for everyone; their minds are restrained by knowledge of the self through reflection (manana) and meditation (nididhyƒÅsana) on the Vedas, Upani·π£ads, Sm·πõtis, and PurƒÅ·πáas; . . . . their pastime is the consideration of the composition of NyƒÅya and Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ ≈õƒÅstra, and they amuse themselves with their mistress, the Goddess of Speech.

It was based on these conditions that, in accordance with prior custom (p≈´rvvamƒÅryyƒÅdƒõyi·πÉd(a)), while the Hoysala king BallƒÅ·∏∑a managed (na·∏çasu/na·∏çayisu) the agrahƒÅra, it was handed down within the lineage (para·πÉparƒÅgatam) on the basis of the Four Hundred‚Äôs (nƒÅn≈´rvvara) fitness (yogyatƒÅbaladi·πÉ) and their capacity to curse and bless (≈õƒÅpa-anugraha-samƒÅrtthateyi·πÉ).

Now, [Camu·πá·∏çarƒÅya] became intent (udyatan ƒÅdan) upon making (mƒÅ·∏çal) dharma strong again (punar ≈´rjjitam), for it had disappeared [since the time of the Hoysala king BallƒÅ·∏∑a) because of the deeds of evil men (du·π£·π≠aka·π£·π≠ara dƒõsƒõyi·πÉda).35

[[P1310]]

The most obvious difference between this inscription‚Äôs depiction of Haveri as it was administered in the time of KandharƒÅya and the previous corpus we have just explored is the complete absence of J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õi and the forces he represents. Apart from the usual frozen poetic tropes tying mythological allusions to the power and magnanimity of the class of officer warlords, our inscription has also become denuded of any specifically ≈öaiva references or theology. Somewhat less obviously but of equal significance, all of the technical language affirming Haveri‚Äôs status as a tax-exempt land grant that is the perpetual property of a specific managerial class has also vanished. As we can see, just as has happened to J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õi and his cronies, markedly ≈öaiva social actors and their systems of value have been entirely elided. They have been replaced with a sociality and semiotics that is resolutely, and self-consciously, not merely BrƒÅhma·πáical, but hypertropically evocative of what we would think of as Sanskritic ‚Äúorthodoxy.‚Äù While the so-called ‚ÄúFour Hundred‚Äù have incorporated into their accomplishments a modified canon of the usual ≈öaiva/KƒÅlamukha occupations, these are completely overshadowed by emphatic claims that their conduct does nothing that hurts other people (sarvvajanapar≈çpakƒÅracaritaru·πÉ), that they practice VedƒÅntic modes of inquiry and ‚Äúmeditation‚Äù that takes as its focus the Sm·πõtis and PurƒÅ·πáas as well as the Vedic sources themselves (vƒìdavƒìdƒÅntasm·πõtipurƒÅ·πáamanananididhyƒÅsanƒÅtmaj√±ƒÅna-niyatacittaru·πÉ), and that they preside over what is at once a renowned juridical and scholastic council (sabhƒÅ), answering petitions nearly in the very same breath that they compose works on NyƒÅya and Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ. In short, what we are seeing at play here is a discursive dynamic that explicitly fuses together disciplinary power and the monitoring of decorum with the production and discussion of ≈õƒÅstric knowledge.
