As we shall soon discover, despite its marginality in the classical world of ≈õƒÅstra as a model for what it means to be BrƒÅhma·πáa, it is precisely this imaginary linking of ≈õƒÅstra, especially VedƒÅnta as theology, with NyƒÅya and Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ serving as its disciplinary handmaidens, so different from that of the prior generations, that comes to [[P1311]] dominate the vision of knowledge production in and around the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava court from the reign of KandharƒÅya to the demise of the dynasty.

KandharƒÅya‚Äôs officer Cau·πá·∏ça treats his intervention as a simple restoration of the property to its original owners, the upstanding and properly BrƒÅhma·πáical ‚ÄúFour Hundred.‚Äù Quite pointedly, Cau·πá·∏ça juxtaposes the proper administration of the land-grant according to prior convention as occurring in an unbroken fashion: ‚Äúwhile the Hoysala king BallƒÅ·∏∑a managed the agrahƒÅra, it was handed down within the lineage,‚Äù and that the dharma had only disappeared in the short interim between the end BallƒÅ·∏∑a‚Äôs time and the present ‚Äúbecause of the deeds of evil men (du·π£·π≠aka·π£·π≠ara dƒõsƒõyi·πÉda).‚Äù The subtext here is quite fascinating and unexpected. The good custodian, the Hoysala king Vƒ´ra BallƒÅ·∏∑a II (c. 1173‚Äì1220 CE) was the chief rival and enemy of the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava kings in their ongoing struggles to maintain and expand influence in the Kuntaladƒì≈õa. In contrast, the period during which dharma is said to have declined‚Äî in other words, when J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õi was active at Haveri‚Äîexclusively falls during the decades when KandharƒÅya‚Äôs father Si·πÖgha·πáa II and his officers largely controlled this region of the Kuntaladƒì≈õa and patronized Haveri. [[P1312]]

It is here that our story starts to get complicated in a manner that makes very apparent the plasticity of identities over a couple of centuries, even among a group of people who bear some of the same names and signifiers. On the surface, Cau·πá·∏ça‚Äôs representation of J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õi as a temporary interloper who displaced the long-standing claim of the BrƒÅhma·πáical ‚ÄúFour Hundred,‚Äù whose property was originally bestowed upon them by the mythical hero Nala, looks plausible. Now it is true that the technical term for the BrƒÅhma·πáical Four Hundred, ‚ÄúnƒÅn≈´rvvaru,‚Äù does not appear in the short inscriptions from the time of KandharƒÅya‚Äôs father, though one of them, the most official, does refer briefly but respectively to the mahƒÅjana·πÉga·∏∑u, and these could potentially be the same people. As we shall soon see, it is also the case that as befitting Nalapuri‚Äôs reputation as a center for scholasticism and learning, the nƒÅn≈´rvvaru have a long history of being represented through a certain kind of hyper-BrƒÅhma·πáical rhetoric. Alongside such core continuities, however, as one goes back, what it meant for the Four Hundred to be ‚ÄúBrƒÅhma·πáical‚Äù undergoes substantive shifts.

In the only record36 to survive from the time of the ‚Äúgood‚Äù Hoysala king Vƒ´ra BallƒÅ·∏∑a II, we learn that his main minister went to Nalapuri to help oversee a major renovation and refurbishment of the temple of SiddhanƒÅtha. Assisting him in these efforts were some sƒõ·π≠·π≠is, very much like the ones who appear in Si·πÖgha·πáadeva‚Äôs inscriptions, as well as the BrƒÅhma·πáical Four Hundred ‚ÄúnƒÅn≈´rvvaru,‚Äù all of whom are now described as bhaktas (samasta bhaktaruva·πÉ nƒÅn≈´rvvaruva·πÉ) as well as the leader of the village ≈öri ≈örƒ´ ≈örƒ´ Hƒõgga·∏çƒõ Vaijayyanu, a figure whose exact identity is uncertain, but who seems to occupying the same space later assumed by J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õi, right down to his intimate relationship with the merchants. The grant, needless to say, is to be protected forever (ƒÅcandrƒÅrkkatƒÅra·πÉbara·πÉ rak·π£isuvaru). [[P1314]]

After the single Hoysala grant, we have a single inscription37 issued under the Kalachuris. In this document, an officer named Ke≈õirƒÅja and his Da·πá·∏çanƒÅyaka Basava (tatpƒÅdapadm≈çpajƒ´vi mahƒÅpradhƒÅna·πÉ sƒìnƒÅdhipati basavƒÅsƒïnƒÅ·∏ça purada da·πá·∏çanƒÅyaka·πÉ kƒì≈õirƒÅjananvayam ƒïntƒõ·πÉda·∏çƒõ) both of them acting in the service of King Bijjala, make offerings to the deities Siddhƒì≈õvara and Indrƒì≈õvara (siddhƒì≈õvaradƒìvarggƒõ i·πÉdrƒì≈õvaradƒìvarggƒõ). Given that, as we shall soon see, despite all its hyper-BrƒÅhma·πáical rhetorical trappings, the material culture at Haveri offer us some of the earliest demonstrable instances of i·π£·π≠ali·πÖga worship in the visual record, it is worth noting in passing that these two officers bear the same titles and names of famous Vƒ´ra≈õaiva ≈õara·πáas and are acting on behalf of the very same King Bijjala who features in the Vƒ´ra≈õaiva hagiographical tradition. In any case, these two officers of the Kalachuri king are received by the BrƒÅhma·πáical Four Hundred, but also by a village leader from the line of Rudradƒìva (hƒõgga·∏çƒï rudradƒìvananvayama·πÉ), possibly a fellow referred to as the Brahmabha·π≠·π≠ƒÅraka of MƒÅ·πáikama·π≠ha, who rather surprisingly greets them by subordinating himself to their authority (pƒÅdapadm≈çjƒ´viyƒïnisida). Nalapuri, according to this record, was founded by the mythical king Na·∏∑a who in ancient times assigned Rudradƒìva as the village‚Äôs leading authority (astu na·∏∑acakravartti mƒÅ·∏çida hiriya hƒï·πüi·∏çƒï hƒõgga·∏çƒï rudradƒìvapramukha). Though the record is here broken in a manner that does not permit us to figure out which attributes are just being assigned to Rudradƒìva and successors and which belong to the BrƒÅhma·πáical Four Hundred, one or both of them are seen as singularly intent upon ≈öiva‚Äôs state (≈õivapadaika) at the same time they are ‚Äúdevoted to the yamas and niyamas, recitation of sacred texts, teaching of sacred texts, focused visualizations, vows of [[P1315]] silence, mantra repetition, samƒÅdhi, and conduct (svasti yamaniyamasvƒÅdhyƒÅyadhyƒÅnadhƒÅra·πáam≈çnƒÅnu·π£·π≠hƒÅnajapasamƒÅdhi≈õƒ´lasa·πÉpannaru·πÉ) as well as Vedic ritual and sacrifice, recitation and instruction in Vedic texts (yujanayƒÅjanƒÅdhyayanƒÅdhyƒÅpana).

Curiously, in a display of both ascetic virtues and ≈õƒÅstric knowledge, we are told they are attached to being recipients of a panoply of very specific forms of philanthropy, mostly stemming from the canonical list found in the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa, namely, gifts of cloth, gifts of girls, gifts of female water buffalo, gifts of black antelope skin, the hira·πáyagarbha offering, and the tulƒÅpuru·π£a offering (kanyƒÅdƒÅna-vastradƒÅnatsyatradƒÅna-mahi≈õƒ´dƒÅna- k·πõ·π£·πáƒÅjinahira·πáyagarbha-tulƒÅpura≈õƒÅdyanƒìkadƒÅnapratigrahanirataru). Here again, the stone is damaged, but as many of these titles will resurface, it is worth noting that the text now celebrates their performances of a range of Vedic sacrifices, (the vƒÅjapƒìya, agni·π£·π≠oma, a≈õvamƒìdha) as well as their virtuous worship of guru and BrƒÅhma·πáas (dvijagurup≈´jƒÅtatpara), their purified bodies (pavitrƒ´k·πõta≈õarƒ´raru), and their mastery of two bodies of knowledge, namely, ≈õƒÅstra (Buddhist philosophy, Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ, Vai≈õe·π£ika, logic, grammar, VedƒÅnta, the meaning of the Veda and its a·πÖgas, metrics, poetics, poetry and drama) as well as an underspecified but decidedly Tantric esoterica (yogama·πÉtrata·πÉtraya·πÉtra). In other words, we are at one of the most BrƒÅhma·πáical places in the Kuntaladƒì≈õa that is not purely a BrƒÅhma·πáa agrahƒÅra solely dedicated Vedic ritual, and yet what ‚ÄúBrƒÅhma·πáical knowledge‚Äù looks like, even in the hands of practicing Vaidika ritualists, is a thoroughgoing fusion of ≈õƒÅstra with the MantramƒÅrga. If a person were to trace this story back even further into the properly KalyƒÅ·πái CƒÅ·∏∑ukyan records, a task that will not detain us here, as we approach the time when the temple of Purasiddhƒì≈õvara was built, not all that much changes. The nƒÅn≈´rvvaru, those best of [[P1316]] BrƒÅhma·πáas (dvijottama) are assigned ever longer and more elaborate canons of BrƒÅhma·πáical accolades38 at the very same moment they are characterized as one‚Äôs whose inner subjectivity is devoted to recollecting the feet of ≈öiva (haracara·πáasmara·πáƒÅ·πÉta·∏•kara·πáa).

Even if we decide that J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õi really was an interloper, we still confront the enormous problem posed by the temple itself, for which J√±ƒÅnarƒÅ≈õi is most certainly not responsible. Faced with the obvious issue that its iconography was inappropriate to mid-thirteenth century standards, someone seems to have taken a chisel and chipped out most of the panels. Thankfully for us, they missed some rather pertinent details that will assist in filling in the gaps. [[P1317]]

Figure 20: ≈öikhara of Haveri Purasiddhƒì≈õvara with images chipped off.

First of all, because the image was too high up on the ≈õikhara and can only really been seen with a zoom-in lens, the pious vandals retained a dancing Na·π≠e≈õa suggestively carrying a skull staff. [[P1318]]

Figure 21: A more important indication of the actual nature of the religious tradition observed by our hyper-BrƒÅhma·πáical but not so orthoprax BrƒÅhma·πáical ‚ÄúFour Hundred‚Äù is suggested by the interior ceiling of the temple of Purasiddhƒì≈õvara itself. Here, again, the arrangement and iconography are so subtle that it would be likely to be overlooked by an outsider. Even if it was noticed, its significance would not have been readily apparent, at least if you do not spend much of your time either studying or practicing ≈öƒÅkta Kaula ritual systems. For person so committed or preoccupied, however, the significance of Purasiddhƒì≈õvara‚Äôs representation of a [[P1319]] fierce dancing ≈öiva, having just slain the elephant demon and holding the creature‚Äôs skin aloft in the air while encircled by vividly rendered images of‚Äînot seven‚Äîbut eight mother goddesses is an unmistakable tip of the hat at the Kaula commitments of this temple and its esteemed caretakers.

Confusing the picture even more, as was earlier alluded to, Purasiddhƒì≈õvara also hosts a series of images evocative of the visual register of later Vƒ´ra≈õaiva traditions, where ≈öaiva yogins and deities offer worship to what will later be called i·π£·π≠ali·πÖgas. In article from 1969 published in [[P1320]] Artibus Asiae, the great art historian M. S. Nagaraja Rao39 analyzes and documents a number of images, some of them found scattered throughout Haveri that seem to have once belonged to the temple, and some preserved in situ. The figures in question include a UmƒÅmahe≈õvara, a strange life-size figured holding an i·π£·π≠ali·πÖga which he identifies as Vi·π£·πáu worshipping ≈öiva, a standing figure of the goddess MahƒÅlak·π£mƒ´ of Kolhapur, an image of S≈´rya, and some NƒÅgas. Once again, we have a pantheon that self-evidently speaks to the commitments of the Kaulicized tradition of the worship of MahƒÅlaks·πÉƒ´, one of the dominant forms of ≈öƒÅkta religion in the Early Medieval Deccan.

*Figure 22: * [[P1321]]

The denouement of our story is rather amusing. After investing all that time and effort in restoring Haveri to its rightful BrƒÅhma·πáa owners, KandharƒÅya and its commanders seem to have eventually realized that despite their hyper-BrƒÅhma·πáical affect, something about the NƒÅnvvara and their actual mode of worship was not quite kosher. Granted, this is only a hypothesis, but this would certainly explain why, in the next grant at Haveri issued in 1255 CE during KandharƒÅya‚Äôs reign, not only are the nƒÅn≈´rvvaru nowhere to be found‚Äîindeed they seem never to resurface‚Äîbut the focus of the grant is a temple formerly belonging to the god of blacksmiths (Kalme≈õvara) and its purpose is to record the installation of a ≈örƒ´ Gopƒ´nƒÅtha.40 Most likely, the god in question is a form of K·πõ·π£·πáa VƒÅsudeva, for in place of the nƒÅn≈´rvvaru and the descendants of Rudradƒìva, we find the descendants of a certain Bommarsa, the most important one (bo·πÉmarsaru·πÉ avara putrapautrƒÅdiga·∏∑u·πÉ mukhyavƒÅgi) at the head of community of honored BhƒÅgavatas (bhƒÅgavatajana·πÉga·∏∑u·πÉ), Vai·π£·πáavas following the BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa. As for what happens to Purasiddhƒì≈õvara itself, when it next surfaces under the reign of MahƒÅdeva, the place has been given to the followers of the proto-Vƒ´ra≈õaiva saint SiddharƒÅme≈õvara of Sonnalige, who perhaps were responsible for the commission of the life- size image that now sits in the temple‚Äôs garbhag·πõha, of someone ‚Äîpossibly a six-armed Vi·π£·πáu‚Äî worshipping the ≈õivali·πÖga now addressed as Siddhƒì≈õvara. [[P1322]]

Setting aside such interesting digressions, it is striking how systematically the story of the re-institutionalization of Haveri at the direction of the Seu·πáa state, in its disavowal of the realities of the immediate Seu·πáa past, its tearing down and delegitimization of older ≈öƒÅkta- tinged social forms, its recentering of BrƒÅhma·πáas judged by their sattvic conduct, in its elevation of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ and NyƒÅya as newly indispensable defenders of an institutionalized VedƒÅntic theism, and its embrace of inquiry into the PurƒÅ·πáic corpus as an authoritative source for religious guidance, is emblematic of the driving forces that animate the emerging Seu·πáa episteme. It is this worldview that find its most influential and eloquent expression in the writings of the chief administrator, minister, and de facto ruler of the Seu·πáa kingdom, the polymath HemƒÅdri S≈´ri, whose vast CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äîthe Wish-Fulfilling Gem of the [[P1323]] Four Classes‚Äîsets out transpose such a seemingly eccentric perspective into the dominant lived reality throughout the western Deccan.

Prelude, The CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái: Encountering the Text through Its ‚ÄúLost‚Äù Division on Tƒ´rtha

The great Dharma≈õƒÅstrin K. V. Rangaswami Aiyangar begins his edition of the first volume of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs K·πõtyakalpataru,41 our oldest surviving dharmanibandha, by lamenting the disinterest of nineteenth century Indologists in the study of the later dharma literature. Essentially, once it was discovered that these vast works of jurisprudence did not contain the type of textual record of positive Hindu law that could be readily made use of by colonial administrators, and that in fact the texts were lousy with the type of ‚Äúpurely religious‚Äô‚Äù materials that the colonial powers had a deemed to fall outside of the purview of European regulation, the discipline was largely confined to the ash heap of history. So too, for that matter, were the practitioners of the knowledge system, for they represented the type of autonomous disciplinary mode of power, trespassing on the self-evidently ‚Äúsecular‚Äù domain of the law and often closely linked with the princely states, that posed an obvious challenge to the emerging values of the colonial project. The twentieth and early twenty-first centuries have seen enormous advances in our understanding of both the history of classical Dharma≈õƒÅstra as discipline and of its complicated reception history in the context of the colonial encounter. In this regard, we stand particularly indebted to Ludo and Rosane Rocher and their students, a legacy continued in the writings of Donald Davis, and of Patrick Olivelle, and his disciples. Throughout the current work, I have turned to these resources again and again in his analysis and it would be remiss not acknowledge once again how these writings have made his own [[P1324]] arguments possible. That being said, when it comes to the domain of the dharmanibandha (with the notable exception of the important contribution of a critical edition of the K·πõtyakalpataru‚Äôs DƒÅnakƒÅ·πá·∏ça by David Brick, a work that confines its engagement to its specific topic, as well as an insightful essay on textual reuse by Florinda De Simini), the study of dharmanibandha, either in terms of its history or as an intellectual practice, has seen scarcely any advances since the establishment of India as an independent nation. Where the texts are consulted at all‚Äîthis in and of itself is none too frequently compared to their considerable influence‚Äîthey are mined for citations with scant attention paid to textual methods and modes of argumentation as they are exercised by individual authors. It is simply assumed that nibandha authors, adhering to an ahistorical ‚ÄúBrƒÅhma·πáical orthodoxy,‚Äù share a worldview and a common ideology that the texts presuppose but do not themselves explicate any further. The nibandhas, from this perspective, operate as mere encyclopedias compiling in a single place already well-known principles and practices. As we will soon see, in the case of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái in particular, such assumptions represent egregious misrepresentations of the actual contents of work that is in almost every domain self-consciously innovative, self- evidently constructivist in its approach, and abounding in extended‚Äîsometimes hundred page long‚Äîtheoretical discourses on a whole range of topics, self-reflexively engaging with the very sort of methodological and theological questions that have been presumed to be absent from the discourse.

Apart from the general neglect to which the discipline of dharmanibandha has been undeservedly subjected, part of the reason that the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái itself has not received the attention that its outsized influence on the history of Indian religions warrants is the abominable state of the published text. In comparison with the K·πõtyakalpataru, whose every [[P1325]] division Aiyangar prefaces with a learned English introduction that not only carefully surveys the contents and structure of the text but presents numerous topical essays on history and method, Bharatacandra Siromani‚Äôs (1873‚Äì1911) CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái not only provides its readers with almost no meaningful paratexts in any language, but as we will soon see, prints a highly incomplete version of the root text that nowhere acknowledges its tentative and partial nature. As HemƒÅdri himself tells us repeatedly, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái is envisioned as consisting of four topical divisions (kha·πá·∏ças)‚Äîpresented in sequence‚Äîon vrata, dƒÅna, tƒ´rtha, and mok·π£a. This main portion of the text is then supplemented by a voluminous ‚ÄúRemainder Division,‚Äù or Pari≈õe·π£akha·πá·∏ça. The Pari≈õe·π£akha·πá·∏ça incorporates ‚Äúextra materials‚Äù such as astrological calculations for determining festival dates, the procedures for temple construction and consecration, as well as a multivolume discussion of the different forms of ≈õrƒÅddha rites. These later books, contained within the Pari≈õe·π£a, are explicitly envisioned as kalpa texts, supplementing the main focus of the work as found in the first four kha·πá·∏ças. While the majority of each portion of the text consists of a curated array of textual citations on a given subtopic drawn from across a vast array of source, supplemented by commentary, prayogas, as well as vidhƒÅnas supplied by the author, HemƒÅdri also tells us every division is to include a section on methodological concerns as they apply to the Dharma≈õƒÅstrin practitioner eager to translate textual study into real-world practice. Furthermore, each section also proffers theoretical and apologetic reflections addressing how the topics at hand relate to problematics drawn from the wider world of ≈õƒÅstra.

In this way, quite unlike his immediate predecessors, our author consciously sets out to justify the inclusion within the field of Dharma≈õƒÅstra of a set of ‚Äúreligious‚Äù concerns‚Äînamely, occasional religious vows (vrata), pilgrimage (tƒ´rtha), as well as festival planning and temple [[P1326]] consecration‚Äîthat had until recently fallen entirely outside the purview of the discipline of Dharma≈õƒÅstra. In the same spirit, in an unprecedented fashion, HemƒÅdri has interwoven into his work what are essentially a series of mini-intellectual essays, addressing such matters as the relationship between PurƒÅ·πáic worship and questions of caste as well as the real nature of BrƒÅhma·πáa-hood. In essence, what the text sets out to offer its reader is an entirely new paradigm, at least within the realm of ≈õƒÅstra, for what it means to be a BrƒÅhma·πáa who wields religious authority.

Unfortunately, the published edition of the text hardly does justice to this interpretive program. The CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái has only been published in a single four-volume (with seven parts in total) edition produced by the Asiatic Society of Bengal beginning in 1873.42 The edition in question prints an incomplete version of the Vratakha·πá·∏ça (missing a portion of its introduction), a competent version of the DƒÅnakha·πá·∏ça, entirely overlooks the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça (still extent in several manuscripts), and omits the lost Mok·π£akha·πá·∏ça. Even more problematically, it prints only portions of the Pari≈õe·π£akha·πá·∏ça‚Äîomitting the two parts of the prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ kalpa‚Äîand it does so in the incorrect order, effectively occluding the internal structure of these divisions of the text. By treating HemƒÅdri‚Äôs division on ≈õrƒÅddha as an additional kha·πá·∏ça, and not as a kalpa work as its author intended, the edition misrepresents the place of ≈örƒÅddha in his overall system. And then, as if to add insult to injury, with the exclusion of the original printing, so far as I can determine, every reprint of the text as constituted by [[P1327]] Bharatacandra Siromani have themselves omitted an entire volume‚Äôs worth of material contained within the ≈õrƒÅddhakalpa.

In other words, to be charitable, the text with which the edition presents us is both tentative and incomplete. Even more problematically, given the relatively early date of its production, when a more critical philology had yet to have been integrated into pandit culture, much as you might expect, the edition tells us nothing about the source materials. Though occasionally spare variant readings are given in the lower margin, in the absence of any reflection by the editor Bharatacandra Siromani, we do not even how many manuscripts were used in constituting the printed edition. Every indication suggests, however, that the quality of this source material was relatively poor and incomplete.43

Indeed, the overall impression one has of both the reprints of the text (which reproduce all of the errors uniformly, including blurred text) as well as the original is that a decided lack of care went into their constitution. Facing such an admittedly monumental task, the editor Bharatacandra Siromani, who was responsible for several other Dharma≈õƒÅstra editions of much higher quality, may simply have delegated much of the work to retrained traditional scribes with minimal command of the language. It seems that in some cases they may just have transcribed what they found on the page with little if any reflection on the coherence of what they were printing. This would provide us with a charitable explanation for the sorry state of large swathes of the work, where whole pages are missing case endings and abound in ametrical verses. It would also account for the fact that the edition, which once again contains [[P1328]] no substantive introduction or apparatuses, offers us a number of obviously incomplete kha·πá·∏ças presented in the wrong order. Indeed, despite the fact that HemƒÅdri explains the structure of his project explicitly dozens of times across the divisions, the editor has mistaken the middle of the work, on dƒÅna, for the beginning, which is about vrata. Bharatacandra Siromami also has incorporated an obviously spurious section on prƒÅya≈õcitta, which does not confirm in form, function, or patterns of citation to anything that came before in the work.44

Bharatacandra Siromani‚Äôs treatment of the two thousand, two hundred- and ninety- page Vratakha·πá·∏ça, which he misidentifies as the second division of the work, is particularly egregious. As printed, the text simply abruptly begins: ‚ÄúFirst division: Now, a praise of the author of the text‚Äù (prathamo ‚ÄôadhyƒÅya·∏• / atha granthakarttu·∏• pra≈õasti·∏•). In contrast, the manuscript tradition in DevanƒÅgarƒ´ (for example, Item 1620 from the Indic Manuscripts Collection at the University of Pennsylvania) consistently prefaces the quite extensive self- praise by our author with a verse honoring K·πõ·π£·πáa, followed by a fifty-two verse ‚ÄúrƒÅjapra≈õasti‚Äù celebrating the line of the Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas. The text here is largely identical with the extract first identified and edited by Bhandarkar as clearly belonging to the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, but whose precise provenance that great scholar was unable to determine.45 Reading the CVC‚Äôs Vratakha·πá·∏ça in some approximation of the form that its author intended‚Äîsuch that does not simply begin with HemƒÅdri praising himself for most of the first twenty verses‚Äîa very different rhetorical structure is in evidence, and we suddenly have a substantive answer to the [[P1329]] pressing question of how a work such as this produced in a royal court under Seu·πáa patronage, as printed barely mentions the dynasty at all in its first two-thousand pages.

In a similar spirit, it is quite certain that meticulous philological work making use of the available manuscript evidence would help us clear up many such mysteries. In an ideal world, an examination of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái would be preceded, at the very least, by the compilation and collating of a representative sampling of the manuscript evidence. Done properly, the sheer scale of the work that would be involved, to say nothing of the financial burden a researcher would incur in consulting and procuring in many cases hundreds of five hundred plus folio manuscripts, in and off itself is virtually prohibitive. It would take a team of people decades to simply to go through each exemplum, transcribe it, and collate them all together. In order to critically examine HemƒÅdri‚Äôs use of his sources, and to choose among the variants, many of the PurƒÅ·πáas he references would themselves have to be critically edited. In short, it is a project that could occupy a community of gifted scholars for entire lifetimes. Given the fact that nearly one hundred and fifty years have elapsed without even the slightest indication that any such critical effort might be forthcoming, during which one of the most innovative and influential works in the history of Sanskrit thought has simply been left to languish, it seems better to try our hands at working to make sense what are admittedly quite inadequate texts instead of waiting expectantly for perfect conditions that may never arrive.

Partially inspired by the disjointed condition of Bharatacandra Siromani‚Äôs edition, we will be approaching the vast CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái in very different fashion than its author would have intended. To this effect, we will commence our investigation not primarily by facilitating an encounter with HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own wildly innovative and theologically rich representation of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs interpretive project (that will be the focus of the [[P1330]] next chapter), but by attending to the manner in which HemƒÅdri sets out to reshape, redact, and transform the textual, cultural, and religious resources he inherits from the world of the early medieval Deccan.

In the sections that follow, our primary conceptual focus will be on reading HemƒÅdri as a redactor and reimaginer of pre-existing textual and religious cultures. In these efforts, initially our primary archive will be selections from the unpublished Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça itself, which I read here from manuscript for the first time. After introducing the reader to the evidence recovered so far regarding the status of the unpublished Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, we will begin by exploring portions of the theoretical framework for thinking about and performing pilgrimage that the text proposes in its first chapter. Our primary intention here is to clarify how HemƒÅdri‚Äôs treatment of pilgrimage at once builds upon and repudiates earlier discussions of the topic in dharma literature as well as in relation to extra- Dharma≈õƒÅstra conversations about the nature and function of pilgrimage in broader circulation throughout the early medieval Deccan. Once we have reconstructed HemƒÅdri‚Äôs overall theory of pilgrimage, we will then attend to what his ≈õƒÅstric prose might tell us about the specific intellectual resources that inform his mode of argumentation. Laying the foundations for arguments that will be more fully developed in the next chapter, which tackle head on how HemƒÅdri justifies the use of Sm·πõti and PurƒÅ·πáa as sources for religious guidance independent of the Vedas, we will see that HemƒÅdri‚Äôs theory of dharmanibandha is at once subtly and heavily indebted to cutting edge thinking drawn from the realm of both PrƒÅbhƒÅkara Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ and the emerging tradition of the ‚Äúnew‚Äù and more emphatically theistic NyƒÅya.46 [[P1331]]

Setting theoretical themes aside, in the next section, we will turn to an examination of how HemƒÅdri reworks his sources in the service of denaturalizing the ethos underwriting the Tantric imaginaries of the Deccan. Here, our efforts will be focused on a close reading of a small portion of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça text that draws heavily upon the PrabhƒÅsak·π£etramƒÅhƒÅtmya, found in the vulgate edition of the Skanda PurƒÅ·πáa.47 As we have already begun to see, within the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava imperium during the latter half of the thirteenth century, such efforts were no mere theoretical enterprise, but formed a plan of action for reshaping the actual religious landscape. The religious landscape we will be exploring in particular is the sacred fields surrounding the ≈öaiva tƒ´rtha in Saurashtra called SomanƒÅtha, which at the time remained one of the most important ritual centers on the subcontinent. Retaining throughout this focus on specific ‚Äúlived spaces‚Äù and the theological and juridical frameworks that make them possible, we next turn to selected portions of the DƒÅnakha·πá·∏ça, where we find HemƒÅdri hard at work reinventing the theoretical parameters of philanthropy (dƒÅna) and offering a new standardizing formalization of philanthropic ritual. Through a clever manipulation of his sources, most of them inherited directly from Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, HemƒÅdri excludes the types of social agents that populate the ≈öaiva Age while at the same time dismantling the legal framework that underwrites the institution of the undying land grant upon which Tantric knowledge systems depended for material support. [[P1332]]

Having arrived at some degree of understanding of how HemƒÅdri manages the cultural memory about sacred spaces as well as the harnessing of resources that makes possible their establishment and institutional persistence, we will then turn to understanding the changing relationship within the tradition of Dharma≈õƒÅstra towards the construction and institutionalization of temples complexes by non-BrƒÅhma·πáa agents using non-BrƒÅhma·πáa knowledge systems. First, we will look HemƒÅdri‚Äôs immediate predecessors in the world of Dharma≈õƒÅstra to see what they have to say about the relation of traditions of temple building to the social norms they seek to enforce. As will become apparent, though they share a century and make use of many of the same sources, the twelfth-century writer AparƒÅditya I of the Northern Ko·πÖka·πáa and Lak·π£mƒ´dhara of Varanasi approach the topic from diametrically opposed points of view. From there, stepping back from the prescriptive realm of ≈õƒÅstra and back into the ‚Äúreal world,‚Äù we will see how the Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas in practice marshal the conceptual resources outlined in the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái in the service of destroying the Trika-aligned Siddhak·π£etra at Kukkunur, evicting its KƒÅlamukha preceptors and esoteric ≈öƒÅkta deities and replacing them with pious BrƒÅhma·πáas who conduct worship directed to ‚Äúall the gods.‚Äù In reconstructing HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own position, our analysis will draw heavily on the unpublished Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs, one of the many components of the Pari≈õe·π£akha·πá·∏ça that have not been included in the printed edition. Here, [[P1333]] we will see how HemƒÅdri confronts head-on the epistemic values of the classical prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ tradition, exemplified by the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, wherein space is conceptually differentiated, multivocal ritual addressing disparate imagined audiences are conducted concurrently, and sacrality is defined by sovereignty. At his direction, this episteme is methodically replaced with a new theology for imagining and instantiating the Hindu temple that emphasizes the singular and shared nature of the ritual, utter transparency in its performance that leaves no space for secrets, and the continued dependence of the institution on the state. This will prepare us for the final chapter, where first we will engage with HemƒÅdri‚Äôs intellectual project on its own terms as an emancipatory intervention in the world of ≈õƒÅstra as well as lived religion, and then with our author‚Äôs elevation of a theistic form of Advaita VedƒÅnta aligned with the values of early modernity as the new and lasting core of BrƒÅhma·πáical knowledge.

Placing and Displacing the Dharmas of Place: Pilgrimage in the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça

In his groundbreaking Marathi language monograph ≈örƒ´vi·π≠·π≠hal: Ek MahƒÅsamanvay, which Anne Feldhaus has felicitously rendered into English as The Rise of a Folk God: Vitthal of Pandharpur, the great autodidact Ramachandra Chintamani Dhere sets out to demonstrate the authenticity and antiquity of the PƒÅ·πá·∏çura·πÖgamƒÅhƒÅtmya, a major Sanskrit resource for the worship of Vi·π≠·π≠hal.48 Towards this end, Dhere assembles a considerable body of evidence to convince his reader that HemƒÅdri‚Äôs CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái did in fact once contain a Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça and that the text had incorporated a reflection on the god at Pandharpur. Perhaps most convincingly, Dhere directs our attention to the Vi·π≠·π≠halabh≈´·π£a·πáa, a work by a famously meticulous nineteenth-century pandit by the name of Gopalacarya. As Dhere demonstrates, this text contains numerous citations that the author himself identifies as extracted from the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça of HemƒÅdri. Dhere proceeds to identify numerous shared citations from this work, preserved in the seventeenth-century Vƒ´ramitrodaya of Mitrami≈õra, also identified in situ as derived from HemƒÅdri, as well as in the Tƒ´rthasƒÅra, an early fifteenth-century work of Dharma≈õƒÅstra composed by DalapatirƒÅja a mere hundred years after HemƒÅdri would have died. In short, he proves conclusively that text was not merely composed but that it remained in [[P1334]] circulation, at least among royal libraries, into the colonial period. As Dhere quite helpfully explains:

MahƒÅmahopƒÅdhyƒÅya GopƒÅ·∏∑ƒÅcƒÅrya A·πá·πáƒÅ Gha·∏∑sƒÅsƒ´ KarhƒÅ·∏çkar. . . quotes ten verses, beginning with the words ‚ÄúbhaimyƒÅ≈õ caiva ta·π≠e devi. . . .,*‚Äù that he states are found ‚Äúin HemƒÅdri‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça[kha·πá·∏ça] (tƒ´rthahemƒÅdrau), folio 122.‚Äù GopƒÅ·∏∑ƒÅcƒÅrya also states clearly exactly where he saw the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça: ‚ÄúThis Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs is in the Ra·πÖgamandir in V·πõndƒÅra·πáya. There are 600 pages of it there. The rest of it can be seen elsewhere, in the royal capitals of A·∏∑avara, Jamb≈´, and so on.‚Äù49

Gopalacarya seems to have been implying that even the six hundred folio version of the text then preserved at the Ra·πÖgamandir in Vrindavan, which thus far remains untraceable, was itself hardly complete. This gives us some hint of the vast nature of the original version of the work as well as the inadequacy of the evidence with which we are working. Inadequate evidence, however, is infinitely better than no evidence at all. While neither the text nor even the location of the version preserved at ‚ÄúJamb≈´‚Äù can be determined, I have managed to retrieve images of the work Gopalacarya identifies was preserved at Alwar (A·∏∑avara), for even now the Royal Collection at Alwar does indeed contain an incomplete 349 folio version of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, which in fact transmits the very verses GopƒÅ·∏∑ƒÅcƒÅrya has cited.50

More importantly for our purposes, the manuscript contains virtually verbatim the exact passage cited by the Vi·π≠·π≠halabh≈´·π£a·πáa, which contains slight variants from the published PƒÅ·πá·∏çura·πÖgamƒÅhƒÅtmya. Vi·π≠·π≠halabh≈´·π£a·πáa: ‚Äútƒ´rthahemƒÅdrau‚Äî bhaimyƒÅ≈õ caiva ta·π≠e devi dak·π£i·πáe tƒ´rtham uttamam. . . .‚Äù HemƒÅdri, Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça: bhaimyƒÅ≈õ caiva ta·π≠e devi dak·π£i·πáe tƒ´rtham uttamam / asti k·π£etre daivata·πÉ ca sarvotk·πõ·π£·π≠a·πÉ sudurlabham. . . . pau·πá·∏çarƒ´kam iti khyƒÅta·πÉ tat tƒ´rtha·πÉ k·π£etrap≈´jita·∏• / pƒÅ·πá·∏çurƒÅ·πÖka≈õ ca tatrƒÅste m≈´rtimƒÅn devatottama·∏• / tathƒÅ / pu·π£karƒÅt trigu·πáa·πÉ prokta·πÉ kedƒÅrƒÅt ·π£a·∏çgu·πáa·πÉ bhavet / vƒÅrƒÅ·πáasyƒÅ·πÉ da≈õagu·πáam anantam ≈õrƒ´girer iti /. . . . ·π£a·π£·π≠ivar·π£asahasrƒÅ·πái divyadevatƒÅdar≈õanƒÅt / yat phala·πÉ labhate martya·∏• sak·πõd eva hi tat phala·πÉ / durlabha·πÉ mƒÅnu·π£ajanma tatrƒÅpy acyutasevana·πÉ / Note in particular the retention of the archaic form of the name of the deity as ‚ÄúpƒÅ·πá·∏çurƒÅ·πÖka,‚Äù attested in the inscriptional record, in place of the early modern ‚ÄúpƒÅ·πá·∏çura·πÖga.‚Äù [[P1335]]

Figure 23: The opening folio of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça as preserved at Alwar bearing the seal of the king.

The image you see above offers a part of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça‚Äôs pra≈õasti of the Seu·πáa kings.51 Not only has the first folio, along with much else besides, been lost, but the text from which this was copied itself seems to have been compromised so that the scribe has indiscriminately copied onto single folios what would have formed entirely distinct sections within the original work. This would account for why the fourth folio in our manuscript abruptly transitions from HemƒÅdri praising himself to a discussion, in media res, of MahƒÅkƒÅle≈õvara at Ujjain.52 Quite apart from the fact that in its present form the semantic content of the text is no longer contiguously transmitted and often has to be pieced back together, it would appear we are also missing a good portion of some early chapters as well as the entire end of the treatise.53 Even so, we must be grateful that at nearly 350 double sided [[P1336]] folios containing eleven lines of roughly fifty-two ak·π£aras each, the text as transmitted offers us a substantive encounter with HemƒÅdri‚Äôs chosen textual archives, certainly enough to familiarize ourself with his core methodology and editorial choices.

The sacred geography the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça attempts to cover is vast and pan-Indic. It extends as far south as the Setubandha, RƒÅme≈õvara in Tamil Nadu, as far west as Multan in present day Pakistan, at least as far north as Kashmir, and as far east as Puri in Orissa.54 The CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái treats each of the different regions of the subcontinent in considerable detail, drawing upon a range of sources, mostly PurƒÅ·πáic mƒÅhƒÅtmyas, many of which do not survive or are yet unpublished. Unexpectedly, in curating his canon of sacred sites, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs selections do not in any way privilege either the BrƒÅhma·πáical heartland of ƒÄryavƒÅrta or the particular sacred geographies of the western Deccan contiguous with the Seu·πáa imperium. Such comprehensiveness and impartiality in the realm of sacred geography diverges quite sharply from the only preceding work on tƒ´rtha to emerge from the discourse of BrƒÅhma·πáical Dharma≈õƒÅstra: the comparatively terse Tƒ´rthavivecanakƒÅ·πá·∏ça of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs K·πõtyakalpataru.55 At least among the surviving sources, the Tƒ´rthavivecanakƒÅ·πá·∏ça marks the initial moment in the history of ≈õƒÅstra where pilgrimage (tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ) is incorporated into a self-described common canon of ‚ÄúBrƒÅhma·πáical‚Äù praxis deemed fit for scholastic reflection. This is quite fitting, for as we shall see, the surprisingly short Tƒ´rthavivecanakƒÅ·πá·∏ça forms but a single division of the gargantuan dharmanibandha, the K·πõtyakalpataru, a work of [[P1337]] Dharma≈õƒÅstra that for the first time joins the discrete domains of Hindu law and PurƒÅ·πáic religious practices. We will explore this text and its author, the GƒÅha·∏çavƒÅla chief minister and master of war, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äîwho stands in many ways as the most significant antecedent and counterpart to HemƒÅdri and his interpretive project‚Äîin considerable detail in the next chapter, where we will painstakingly contextualize HemƒÅdri‚Äôs contributions within the history of Dharma≈õƒÅstra. For the moment, however, it is simply worth observing that while HemƒÅdri is transparently indebted to Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äîindeed the raw textual materials that structure the opening division of each of the major chapters of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái are transposed directly from the K·πõtyakalpataru‚Äîin nearly every case, HemƒÅdri makes use of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs sources as a jumping off point for arriving at theoretically distinctive‚Äîeven oppositional‚Äî conceptual ends. Both literally and figuratively, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái sets out to tackle a different terrain.

As a simple but compelling illustration of this dynamic, let us compare HemƒÅdri‚Äôs transregional inclusivism with what we find in the Tƒ´rthavivecanakƒÅ·πá·∏ça itself. In contrast with our text, after nine pages on tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ in the abstract, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara dedicates one hundred and twenty-six pages to discussing a single place, Varanasi.56 None too coincidentally, this is the most important sacred center within the GƒÅha·∏çavƒÅla realm. The text then proceeds in the following manner: PrayƒÅga next receives sixteen pages worth of coverage, the Ga·πÖgƒÅ receives nine, GayƒÅ gets twelve, Kuruk·π£etra gets eight, Pu·π£kƒÅra four, and Mathura nine.57 In other words, the vast majority of Tƒ´rthavivecana takes as its focus a specifically north Indian sacred geography that centers the Indo-Gangetic Plain as the locus mundi in general and the [[P1338]] GƒÅha·∏çavƒÅla realm in particular. With the exception of the NarmadƒÅ river, though not strictly north Indian, also adjacent to GƒÅha·∏çavƒÅla territory, and the eleven other sacred centers that warrant their own chapter, places like DvƒÅraka and KedƒÅra all must make do with two or three pages. In fact, excluding the extensive section on Varanasi, at twenty-six pages in length, the longest division of the Tƒ´rthavivecana is the chapter on the greatness of ‚Äúvarious tƒ´rthas‚Äù (nƒÅnatƒ´rthamƒÅhƒÅtmya).58 While this portion of the text nominally acknowledges a long catalog of sacred centers found on other parts of the subcontinent, each place is allotted at best a few ≈õlokas, and the whole discussion reads as an afterthought.

In contrast to the Tƒ´rthavivecana of the K·πõtyakalpataru, whose sacred geographies, as we have just seen are largely restricted to the Indo-Gangetic Plain and are organized according to geographical proximity to the political territory of its patron, on the basis of surviving colophons, we can be certain that HemƒÅdri originally organized the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça‚Äôs presentation of sacred sites not on account of their location, but rather by type. While much of the theoretical apparatus of the text is not transmitted in our partial and disorderly manuscript, on the basis of the surviving internal colophons we know for certain that, following some initial chapters theorizing pilgrimage, at the very least the text offered in sequence a division on sacred fields (k·π£etraprakara·πáa), on sacred rivers (nadƒ´prakara·πáa), on sacred cities (purƒ´prakara·πáa), and on sacred forests (vanaprakara·πáa). Though the precise definition of some of these categories remains a bit obscure, we also have strong internal textual indications that in its original form, each of the divisions would have been prefaced with sections where our author clearly delineated the exact meaning of the category under discussion and attempted to place them within the wider context of ≈õƒÅstric knowledge. In more abstract terms, HemƒÅdri [[P1339]] breaks from his predecessor in that instead of deriving an organizational program for his ≈õƒÅstric treatments from the parameters provided to him by the world itself‚Äîsuch as the principles of geographical proximity and the exigencies supplied by the political sovereignty of his patron at a specific moment in historical time‚Äîhe sets out to order the world itself so as to bring it under the domain of synthetic ≈õƒÅstric taxonomies.

On the basis of the Alwar manuscript and some of its more important surviving intertexts, especially the K·πõtyakalpataru, I have been able to reconstruct one substantial portion of the introductory prescriptive and theoretical framework that once prefaced the complete Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça.59 As we will see momentarily, though it is currently preserved on non-consecutive pages between folios 230‚Äì240 of our manuscript, this portion of the discourse‚Äîalmost certainly the beginning of our text‚Äîostensibly offers further elucidation on the opening chapter of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs Tƒ´rthavivecanakƒÅ·πá·∏ça. On the surface, what the text appears to be doing is incorporating‚Äîoften verbatim and in sequence‚Äînot only five pages worth of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs curation of citations on the fruits of tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ drawn from the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata as well as a range of PurƒÅ·πáas, but even a substantial portion of his admittedly sparse commentary‚Äîmostly consisting of glosses on some key terms contained in his proof texts. But whereas, beyond the bare fact that he for the first time incorporates ‚ÄúPurƒÅ·πáic‚Äù religion into the realm Hindu law, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara in the Tƒ´rthakƒÅ·πá·∏ça has no discernable theological agenda guiding his selection and presentation of materials, nor does he display a hermeneutical commitment to making his grab bag of citations cohere intellectually, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs intentions are vastly different. Succinctly, our author seeks to deploy the canon of sources he has acquired [[P1340]] from his predecessor with the radically distinctive aim of imposing order upon what he sees as a heterogenous original.

In preparation for our encounter with HemƒÅdri‚Äôs project of building a new theological model out of what at first might seem to be isolated moments of textual exegesis‚Äîmany of which build directly upon verbatim passages drawn from Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äîbefore turning to the text, it is important we highlight the major contours of the argument interspersed throughout the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça‚Äôs opening chapter. First, HemƒÅdri domesticizes and reimagines the type of agent who performs pilgrimage, disassociating the act of pilgrimage from the performance of intense austerities. In blatant opposition to virtually all of his sources, HemƒÅdri erases the understanding that the success or failure of a pilgrimage is largely predicated on how well the virtuoso practitioner has internalized a strident degree of ascetic self-discipline. This interpretive choice refashions the experience of pilgrimage into one that comforts the comfortably well to do. Second, again breaking from everything that precedes him, our author sets out to prove that all pilgrimages, independent of destination, are fundamentally the same sort of activity. On this basis, for the first time in the literature, he presents a standardized paradigm for how and why we do tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ. Invoking the scholastic maxim of ekatra nir·πáƒ´ta,60 HemƒÅdri asserts that just like different places (sthƒÅna) within a text can be ‚Äúharmonized‚Äù using reading strategies derived from Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ exegesis, so too can the ostensibly different modes of practice related to different places (sthƒÅna) in the world.

Though particularly conceptually significant, this is just one of many instances where HemƒÅdri implicitly as well as explicitly makes a broader case‚Äînamely, that the fundamental ritual vocabulary that needs to be deployed during pilgrimage is founded not on local [[P1341]] knowledge and practice but on a set of meta-textual scholastic practices, many of which are derived from BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a and PrƒÅbhƒÅkara Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ exegesis as well as the later tradition of NyƒÅya. When we reach the end of this section, we will thus look both at some of the philosophical and theological conversations that stand behind his discourse and inspire HemƒÅdri as well as the impact of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own intellectual work in the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça on his early modern SmƒÅrta successors.

On account of the close textual dependence of the opening portion of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça on the first chapter of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs Tƒ´rthavivecanakƒÅ·πá·∏ça, it is vital that we read the texts in tandem. Much as we had done earlier in chapters two and four when working with documentary texts produced in accordance with inherited scribal templates, attending carefully to the places where HemƒÅdri has inserted extensive material or has actively sought to rethink or subvert the objectives of his predecessor provides us with a powerful analytical tool for accessing just what facets of our author‚Äôs interpretive project were most important to him and would have been perceived as such by an intended audience familiar with both works. Especially when complemented (where possible) with a careful reading of both authors‚Äô source texts, such an approach also brings into immediate and stark relief the many moments in which our nibandha plays fast and loose with or actively seeks to override the intended meaning of his proof texts so as to advance his own extratextual agenda. By seeking out textual echoes further afield from the expected realm of PurƒÅ·πáa and Dharma≈õƒÅstra, we are also able to familiarize ourselves with the specific ≈õƒÅstric discourses that HemƒÅdri has been attentively reading and that he seeks to establish as key parts of the interpretive context for his reinvention of a newly theistic and devotional non-sectarian Dharma≈õƒÅstra, a discipline now viewed as the primary site for governing and adjudicating a cornucopia of modes of religious practice. [[P1342]]

In the K·πõytakalpataru, book eight, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara introduces the topic of tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ by citing an extended passage from the Tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅparvan found in book three of the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata, where the sage Pulastya teaches Bhƒ´·π£ma about the virtues of pilgrimage.61 This is a quite appropriate choice, as the text in question appears to be the archaic locus classicus legitimizing the practice of pilgrimage within Sanskrit discourse. While the vast majority of the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata‚Äôs chapter offers a detailed itinerary for a network of sacred sites, mostly in north India, with the pilgrimage commencing from Pushkar in Rajasthan, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs opening selection incorporates only seven of the verses that preface this discussion. These offer a rationale for pilgrimage. Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs interpretive choice here, which HemƒÅdri continues, is itself quite interesting, as it, for the moment, removes from our consideration the actual opening of Pulastya‚Äôs discourse, which treats the specific type of person who is eligible to perform the practice. This is quite pointedly someone who has thoroughly disciplined his senses through austerities, a topic that Lak·π£mƒ´dhara will somewhat soften and HemƒÅdri will actively seek to expunge from his discourse. Pulastya then continues by indicating that while Vedic ritual is effective in accomplishing the aims associated with the ancestors as well as reaching heaven, it is not possible (na ≈õakyƒÅ·∏•) for poor people (daridra·πÉ) to ‚Äúobtain‚Äù the sacrifice, as yaj√±as entail a considerable expenditure of wealth and ritual materials (bah≈´pakara·πáƒÅ·∏•, nƒÅnƒÅsa·πÉbhƒÅravistarƒÅ·∏•). While kings and successful men can acquire such resources, they remain out of reach for poorer folks ‚Äúwho have only a single [[P1343]] body‚Äù‚Äîin other words, do not have servants at their beck and call. In this spirit of charity, the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata thus teaches a vidhi, whose fruit is equal to that of yaj√±a, that can be utilized even by poor folks. It calls this practice the supreme secret, proclaiming that it is at least as good if not better than doing Vedic ritual, and the section, as well as Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs selection, then closes by suggesting that ‚Äúreal poverty‚Äù is not going on tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ in accordance with the procedure enjoined by Pulastya. What is missing from this reusage, however, is any indication that in the original source, Pulastya‚Äôs vidhi is not some generic celebration of the concept of pilgrimage, but that the text offers instruction in a very specific linked set of practices, the observance of which is determined by travel to a fixed set of locations, evocative of a largely extinct proto-≈öaiva sacred landscape. As the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata makes clear, though bathing, honoring the ancestors, and feeding BrƒÅhma·πáas are usually part of the package, at each of the different places the pilgrim will have to visit to fulfill the terms of the injunction, the practice engaged in and the rules that govern it are substantively different.

Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, to be sure, discards Pulastya‚Äôs precise itinerary. One can hardly begrudge him this choice, as with the passage of perhaps a thousand years many of the places the text incorporates had disappeared or been thoroughly reimagined. And yet, in many ways he stays true to the spirit of Pulastya‚Äôs discourse to Bhƒ´·π£ma, for in the chapters that follow, the Tƒ´rthavivecanakƒÅ·πá·∏ça will represent pilgrimage to each of the different locations it covers as entailing divergent practices. Thus, while Lak·π£mƒ´dhara dedicates his first chapter‚Äîentitled the tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅvidhi‚Äîto asserting the in many ways rather banal and delimited proposition that going to tƒ´rthas releases one from sin, and then proceeds in the chapters that follow to offer the specific and often quite different practices associated with making pilgrimage to KƒÅ≈õƒ´, PrayƒÅga, GayƒÅ and so forth, as his Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça commences, HemƒÅdri marshals the exact same [[P1344]] materials as his predecessor (only incorporating a few additional verses from Pulastya‚Äôs teaching), in the service of making a very different sort of argument.

Reading his source against the grain, HemƒÅdri turns the inclusivism of the original text on its head. While not denying that people of little of means and who are intent on purging themselves of sin should undertake pilgrimage, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs real focus is advocating for the substantial incorporation of well-heeled men of property and paragons of moral and ritual purity in the pilgrimage process, and for the participation onsite of specialist BrƒÅhma·πáa ritualists as the overseers of the sacred fields. The flipside of this framework is that exemplars of ascetic virtues, who in the source texts are singled out as the pilgrim par- excellence, are denigrated or sidelined, and their values are treated as basically irrelevant to the ethos of pilgrimage. As much of this work is happening at the level of subversive commentarial glosses, it is worthwhile to begin by citing a bit of Pulastya‚Äôs discourse as found in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs text.

MBh: Making sacrifices with agni·π£·π≠oma sacrifice and so forth, which involves a whole lot of money, a person does not obtain that same result as he does from going to the tƒ´rthas. . . . Even having not burned the fires [through performing the Agnihotra], even by those who have just their own body [lit., with a single body] (ekƒÅtmabhi·∏•) and are separated from their companions and who are devoid of assistance (asamhatai·∏•), the ancestors are made to cross over and the grandfathers [are extracted] from the hells.62

HemƒÅdri: Where the texts says ‚Äúwho have just their own body‚Äù (ekƒÅtmabhi·∏•), [what is meant is] who is without a wife (patnƒ´rahitai·∏•). Where the text says, ‚Äúwho are not well [[P1345]] equipped,‚Äù [what is meant is those] who lack a ·πögvedic and SƒÅmavedic priest and so forth. For this reason, the text says, ‚Äúhaving not ignited the Agnihotra and forth.‚Äù Therefore, the fruit of each one of the four [rewards mentioned in the verses], namely, [from] the lighting of the fire for three nights, the going to the tƒ´rthas, the giving of gold and cows is the absence of poverty. This is the sense.63

Through a series of sly recastings of key terms, HemƒÅdri has already begun to adjust our expectations for what constitutes an ideal pilgrim. Displacing the root text‚Äôs conception of pilgrimage as a boon for the genuinely impoverished, who have neither servants nor access to the materials needed in performing ritual worship, he inscribes in their place a very different sort of person, namely, someone who is traveling without his wife and who does not have access to the complete retinue of Vedic ritualists that might assist him in his performances of Soma sacrifices.

Having shifted the terms of the discussion so that this new context will alter the meaning of the materials he is about to import, HemƒÅdri then briefly brings in a few verses from Pulastya‚Äôs original delineation of the characteristics of a pilgrim, once again at every step undercutting the simple denotative meaning of his sources where they do not suit his agenda.

Likewise, MBh: The one of whom the two hands, the two feet and likewise the mind are restrained (susa·πÉyatam), and who has knowledge, austerity, and fame, enjoys the fruit of pilgrimage.64

HemƒÅdri: Fame is in regard to good deeds. ‚ÄúRestraint of the hand‚Äù comes from leaving off of taking other people‚Äôs property (hastasamyama·∏• parasvagraha·πáaniv·πõtyƒÅ). ‚ÄúRestraint of the feet‚Äù means leaving off from going to countries/places that should not be gone to. Restraint of the mind means leaving off from contemptible desires. VidyƒÅ [[P1346]] means knowledge of the qualities of the tƒ´rthas and so forth. Tapas is characterized by dwelling and fasting at the tƒ´rthas and so forth. . . .65

MBh: One who has left off from holding property, who is satisfied with whatever he has, is devoid of ego, and he enjoys the fruit of the tƒ´rthas. He has no preferences, he is without support (nirƒÅlambha), he has not obtained food, he has conquered the senses. Freed from all social contact, he enjoys the fruit of the tƒ´rthas. O king, one who is free from anger, who always speaks truth, is firm in vows, and who is similar to the self in all beings [who sees the same self in all beings], he enjoys the fruits of all tƒ´rthas.66

HemƒÅdri: Being devoid of holding property means immediately after one pilgrimage, then he proceeds directly to another pilgrimage. . . . Akalpaka means without fraud. ‚ÄúWithout support‚Äù means being devoid of the conduct of acquiring money and so forth. ‚ÄúFreed from all social contact‚Äù (ni·∏•sa·πÖga) means one who is not governed by attachment to people of his own gotra. . . . ‚ÄúSimilar to the self means‚Äù he is passionate about the welfare of all downtrodden people. This is the meaning.67

In the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata, Pulastya begins his discourse to Bhƒ´·π£ma by calling upon the great warrior ascetic to listen with a one-pointed mind (ekƒÅgramanasƒÅ), a rhetorical choice that prepares us for his definition of the sort of agent who will find success on pilgrimage. That this person‚Äî whether a householder or a professional renunciate‚Äîis a yogin in all but name is self-evident in the root text, for he has restrained his body and his mind, has performed austerities, does not own property, has cultivated neither attraction nor aversion to any particular thing, sees the self in all, has mastered his emotions and has no ego, dwells alone, consumes limited food, and observes vratas. For Pulastya, then, the pilgrim must embody a set of ascetic virtues to a very high degree precisely because the elaborate vidhi he prescribes will [[P1347]] demand that he performs harrowing austerities at some of the key stopping points in its very involved itinerary.

In the service of advocating for a very different vision of the spirit of pilgrimage and what makes it efficacious, HemƒÅdri systematically sets out to at once domesticate and ethicalize the defining features of a pilgrim. The figure that emerges out of his pen no longer has to have achieved mastery over the workings of the body and mind. He simply must meet a much lower set of prerequisites, being someone who does not steal, who does not stray into forbidden areas, and is not a prisoner of destructive obsessions. Instead of having acquired great learning and already performed intense austerities before attempting pilgrimage, he simply needs to observe such practices in situ as part of his performance of PurƒÅ·πáic rituals, and to listen to and follow the right authorities. In place of abjuring the acquisition of property, having equanimity of vision, and withdrawing from the transactionalities of the social world‚Äî standard requirements for an ascetic‚ÄîHemƒÅdri simply requires the pilgrim to not hang around the pilgrimage site on holiday after he completes his observances and that he not commit fraud, engage in business dealings, or seek to advance the social position of his kin network while he is executing his religious duty. Finally, undercutting the famed teachings of the Bhagavad Gƒ´tƒÅ in a manner eerily akin to modern rereadings of scripture that substitute social justice concerns in place of spiritual attainment, HemƒÅdri sets out to ‚Äúsecularize‚Äù the act of seeing the self in all beings, transmuting this into the pilgrim simply being ‚Äúpassionate about the welfare of all downtrodden people.‚Äù In essence, though from a position of privilege and wielding an almost unimaginable degree of institutional power what HemƒÅdri is instigating is his very own sort of ‚Äúquotidian revolution.‚Äù [[P1348]]

As we will see throughout this chapter, a highly selective curation of sources materials is a ubiquitous feature of the discourse in the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, which through exegetical sleight of hand imagines into being an ideological consensus not evident in the sources. Through such efforts, our author systematically reinvents the nature, context, and function of religious practice and the place which it occupies in the social world. At once one of the most tellingly egregious and relatively straightforward examples of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs preferred sort of scholastic alchemy that transmutes the simple denotative sense of texts into something diametrically opposed to the obvious meaning makes it appearance just a few passages onward from where we have just left off in our readings. HemƒÅdri has already delineated the traits of the pilgrim and he has begun to explain the universal procedure (sƒÅmƒÅnyavidhi) for performing a pilgrimage‚Äîa subject to which we will return momentarily‚Äîwhen he is temporarily detained from fleshing out his major argument by a related tangent, the obviously pertinent question of ‚Äúhow should you travel when on going on pilgrimage?‚Äù On this matter, all of the sources he invokes are quite clear. For example, the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa says:

But, for that man who goes by a vehicle (yƒÅna), out of lordliness, greed, or delusion, all of [his efforts] will be fruitless. Therefore, one should avoid a vehicle.68

This is about as transparent a statement as one is likely to find in the PurƒÅ·πáas. To any reasonable person, its message is self-evident. Traveling to pilgrimage sites by means of a vehicle is strictly prohibited‚Äî no exceptions. Or, so it would seem. Much in the manner of a VedƒÅntin reading their specific theology out from the Upani·π£ads by singling out specific statements in the body of the text as the opening (upakrama) and closing (upasa·πÉhƒÅra) of connected piece of discourse, the significance of which is elevated above the surrounding [[P1349]] materials, HemƒÅdri resolves this apparent clash between the vision of pilgrimage he wishes to promote and the message conveyed by his sources by directing his reader‚Äôs attention to the larger rhetorical framework in Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa within which this apparent prohibition is embedded. Several verses beforehand, the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa introduces this wider topic by offering us as a cautionary tale a certain man (nara·∏• kvacit) who is intent on making pilgrimage to PrayƒÅga (prayƒÅgatƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅrthƒ´) while mounted on a bull (balƒ´vardasamƒÅr≈´·∏çha·∏•). This unfortunate soul, we soon learn, after his own demise for his misdeeds will reap the fruit of having to dwell in the terrible hells (narake vasate ghore). Such a scoundrel is then contrasted with the right sort of person, who feeds the ancestors and gives philanthropy, especially to BrƒÅhma·πáas. Through his actions, in the next life he will dwell in the celestial realms. It is only at this point, when the grammatical subject being referred to has already changed and the seeker after PrayƒÅga is no longer relevant to the discussion, that the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa presents the verse offering a general prohibition on vehicle travel that we have just encountered. For HemƒÅdri, however, this brief mention of PrayƒÅga provides him with an expedient means for extracting himself out of a hermeneutical dead end.

HemƒÅdri: But this prohibition of the vehicle is only relevant to [the pilgrimage] to PrayƒÅga, because such was stated in the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa. . . . Thus, the Kalpataru relates:

‚ÄúTravelling by cow, he is said to be a cow killer. If one goes on a horse, it is fruitless. Going by means of a man, then there is half the fruit, and it is four-fold if one does it on foot.‚Äù

HemƒÅdri: In this quote, ‚Äúby feet and so forth,‚Äù on account of the non-concomitance of the ablative case [padbhyƒÅm] and so forth, there is [for the person going by foot] the fruit of the tƒ´rthas along with extra merit; there is no fault in the usage [of these means of conveyance.]

Thus, when [we recognize that] the prohibition on the vehicle is blocked (bƒÅdhita), [one realizes that] in the case of rain and heat, an umbrella bearer [is to be used] and in the [[P1350]] case of the forest at night, a staff bearer would be the protector of the body. [Therefore], one should always go about with a vehicle.69

Through hermeneutical feats that run roughshod over the transparent denotative meaning of simple declarative statements, our author‚Äôs logic runs as follows. Having first demonstrated that simple prohibitions on the use of vehicles apply only to pilgrimage to PrayƒÅga, our author repurposes the narrow possibility of permitting ‚Äúa vehicle‚Äù under some circumstances, found only in one source, as providing sanction for a whole host of pilgrimage related perks. Umbrella bearers we are told, should accompany the pilgrim in the rainy season, armed guards should escort him when travelling through forest. Since all of these things entail the protection of the body‚Äîand what protects the body best is a vehicle‚ÄîHemƒÅdri arrives at the entirely counter intuitive conclusion that one must always go by vehicle when going on pilgrimage. Even better, as he will observe later on, instead of going through all this bother of travelling to sacred sites in the first place, the savvy entrepreneurial pilgrim might just as well pay someone else to go on his pilgrimage for him‚Äîfor all of the merit apart from what your employee gains from washing himself will be transferred to the patron.

‚ÄúThe one who goes for the sake of another obtains the sixteenth [part] of the merit.‚Äù

H: The meaning is‚Äîthis is the fruit of the tƒ´rtha that belongs to him who goes by proxy. ‚ÄúOn behalf of another‚Äù means by a proxy taking wages [to do his pilgrimage] and so forth.70

The hidden iconoclasm of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs interpretive project‚Äîthat under the guise of [[P1351]] theorizing Hindu pilgrimage he is surreptitiously reinventing Hindu pilgrimage‚Äîcomes into sharp relief when we briefly juxtapose his mid-thirteenth century prescriptions with representations of Hindu pilgrimage from the early twelfth century composed within a similar geographical and social milieu. Indeed, the counterpoint provided here emerged within the court of the KalyƒÅ·πáa CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas, whose sponsoring of the previously marginal Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas formed the necessary preconditions that made possible the emergence of the very institutional space in which HemƒÅdri is operating. The text in question, you may have already realized, is the VikramƒÅ·πÖkƒÅbhyudaya of Some≈õvara III, already explored at some length back in chapter 4. We have covered a lot of ground‚Äîconceptually as well quite literally‚Äîin the interim, so its perhaps worthwhile to highlight of few of the salient points from the earlier discussion. The KalyƒÅ·πáa CƒÅ·∏∑ukya king Some≈õvara I is desperate to conceive a child. So, under the direction of a panel of learned BrƒÅhma·πáas, he is directed to sponsor a series of ritual performances of the sixteen acts of mahƒÅdƒÅna prescribed by the tradition of Dharma≈õƒÅstra, following the procedures outlined in the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa. The king does not perform these rituals himself, but has other people do the ritual acts for him by proxy. In part, it is precisely this decision, the text tells us, that causes the king to sire not a proper heir, but a sociopathic monster whose consciousness is dominated by rajas and tƒÅmas. As you may remember, Some≈õvara I redeems himself and ensures the flourishing of his dynasty by going on pilgrimage. This is an activity that the kƒÅvya seems to understand as falling outside the realm of BrƒÅhma·πáical Dharma≈õƒÅstra and animated by a fundamentally antithetical ethos. Both rhetorically and conceptually, the text repeatedly orients its intended readers to this fundamental difference between the two domains of religious praxis by framing key moments in Some≈õvara I‚Äôs pilgrimage as repudiations of types of choices and values that were reflected in the king‚Äôs behavior during [[P1352]] the mahƒÅdƒÅna rites.71 It emphatically insists on two dichotomies in particular. First, that while during the disastrous philanthropic rite ostensibly honoring ≈öiva, the king ‚Äúworshipped‚Äù through proxy amidst a great retinue, he had to perform the far more effective pilgrimage with his very own two bare feet, bereft of retinue as well resources, in simple solitude. Secondly, while the first rituals were not merely elaborate but genuinely enjoyable affairs where the king was repeatedly indulged and his senses satisfied, in contrast, both enroute to Srisailam and while keeping the company of the god, Some≈õvara I observed intense physical austerities to an extent virtually unknown in the life of a householder.72 Through deliberately inflicting suffering and pain on his own body, the CƒÅ·∏∑ukya king purified his body and his mind, completely one-pointed, and as the text quite plainly states, it was precisely this sort of practice [[P1353]] that caused the Lord, speaking directly to his devotee, to grant him a boon in the form of the child that would become VikramƒÅditya VI. This is of course the very regent whose fifty-year reign did so much to define the discursive and institutional worlds into which HemƒÅdri was born and that he would do so much to deconstruct.

At ≈örƒ´≈õaila, Some≈õvara I worships the Lord himself (Some≈õvara, VikramƒÅ·πÖkƒÅbhyudaya, pg. 43‚Äì44): tata·∏• prabh·πõti pratidina·πÉ pƒÅtƒÅlaga·πÖgƒÅsalilaparip≈´ritai≈õ cƒÅmƒ´karacƒÅrukala≈õair bhagavanta·πÉ trilocana·πÉ snapayƒÅmbabh≈´va / tadanu haricandanacandanaghanasƒÅramƒÅrga-dravair anulilimpa / tato vividhavihitasugandhikusumaviracitavicitra-mƒÅlyapa√±copacƒÅrasubhagƒÅ·πÉ p≈´jƒÅ·πÉ cakƒÅra / tathƒÅ pratibha·π≠anarapati≈õira·∏•kirƒ´·π≠ako·π≠itƒÅ·∏çananiratƒÅbhyƒÅm api svabhƒÅvakomalƒÅbhyƒÅ·πÉ cara·πáƒÅbhyƒÅm eva santata·πÉ vir≈´pƒÅk·π£a·πÉ pradak·π£inƒ´cakre / ‚ÄúFrom that moment on, every day, he bathed the three eyed lord with beautiful gold kala≈õas, which were filled with water from the subterranean Ga·πÖgƒÅ. After that, he smeared the Lord with the essences of deer musk, camphor, sandal paste, and yellow sandal paste. And then, he performed a p≈´jƒÅ that was very auspicious with the five facilitating practices, with many types of flowers garlands that were fashioned with fragrant flowers of many sorts, as was enjoined. Likewise, he performed ceaseless circumambulation of Vir≈´pƒÅk·π£a with his own two feet which, even though they had had been engaged in the striking of crores of the crowns on the heads of adversarial kings, were tender by their very nature.‚Äù

Reconsidering the narrative illustrations of pilgrimage practice in the twelfth-century VikramƒÅ·πÖkƒÅbhyudaya in light of prescriptions in the thirteenth-century CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, we are met with a curious irony. In spite of the fact that Some≈õvara III was skeptical if not downright hostile, at least as it related to the realm of religious ritual, towards the tradition of Dharma≈õƒÅstra as well as its PurƒÅ·πáic imitators of the sort we find in the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa, and while HemƒÅdri is the discourse‚Äôs most strident champion, it is actually the CƒÅ·∏∑ukya king and not our Dharma≈õƒÅstrin polymath whose vision of pilgrimage proves to be most faithful to the ethos we find outlined in the very PurƒÅ·πáic proof texts on top of which the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça assembles its own arguments. Amusingly, this consonance is particularly striking when it comes to the passages that HemƒÅdri has extracted from the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa. In any case, a plurality of the exact passages that HemƒÅdri invokes, to say nothing of the wider context from which those passages were extracted, perceive pilgrimage as a domain animated by precisely the sort of ascetic ethos and sensibility, emphasizing clarity of intention, one-pointed focus, and above all else, and sustained individual effort, that HemƒÅdri sets out to elide through his commentarial practice. HemƒÅdri‚Äôs explicit project of consolidating and synthesizing a preexisting body of knowledge provides him with cover for what in actuality seems to be a radical‚Äîand at the time quite eccentric‚Äî wholesale reinvention of tradition. [[P1354]]

Indeed, the more thoroughly and carefully one reads the methodological portions of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça in particular, the more evident it becomes that the virtual absence of textual precedents for the type of interpretive work on display in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs text within our surviving sources is not in fact a reflection of ignorance on the part of modern scholars of Dharma≈õƒÅstra. Nor can it simply be accounted for by assuming that, apart from Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, we simply lack access to an earlier lost tradition of dharmanibandha writers‚Äîwell known to HemƒÅdri‚Äîwho come down to us only in form of a list names embedded in Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs pra≈õastis honoring his predecessors.73 Such assumptions simply do not stand in light of how HemƒÅdri handles his sources. With great consistency, when HemƒÅdri asserts a doctrinal stance and then invokes the authority of ‚Äúall the nibandhakƒÅras‚Äù (sometimes specifically mentioning the MitƒÅk·π£ara author and Lak·π£mƒ´dhara), or when he engages polemically with the ‚Äúposition‚Äù of a specific Dharma≈õƒÅstra author, the sources he invokes are only tangentially related to the specific topic at hand. Moreover, we have strong indications that the older discussions in question were not actually found in texts that were invested in exploring the topic of tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ.

Making sense of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs argument requires us to proceed slowly and methodically. Let us see how he starts to develop his case: [[P1355]]

[Identified by Vi≈õvƒÅsadevƒ´ as BrahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça PurƒÅ·πáa]: ‚ÄúWhoever should go to the tƒ´rtha pilgrimage, his fasts completed, being previously restrained (susa·πÉyata) to his own house, being zealous for a long time, he should worship Ga·πáe≈õa, genuflecting with bhakti. The intelligent one should worship the gods, the ancestors, the BrƒÅhma·πáas and also the sƒÅdhus. He should propitiate the fierce ancestors/the BrƒÅhma·πáas, with effort, and with wealth, according to his ability. Having returned again and again, then again he should worship the gods, ancestors, and BrƒÅhma·πáas. One who is acting in this way obtains that fruit stated to be from the tirtha. Of that there is no doubt.‚Äù74

HemƒÅdri: On the part beginning susa·πÉyata‚ÄîOn the previous day, the one who has performed the niyama that is exclusive devotion and so forth, who fasted on the day after that, then, having worshipped Ga·πáe≈õa, the grahas, and one‚Äôs own chosen deity, having done a pƒÅrva·πáa offering, one should worship the BrƒÅhma·πáas.

And this pƒÅrva·πáa has ghee as its principal substance (gh·πõtamukhyadravyaka) based on this authoritative statement from the Bhavi·π£ya PurƒÅ·πáa.75

Up until this point in the chapter, though his interpretations have often been quite different, HemƒÅdri has been dutifully reproducing Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs main citations, for the most part in the exact same order. This passage from the BrahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça PurƒÅ·πáa, which replaces some citations from the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa in Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs original, offers us his first extended choice of a new proof text. Taken in isolation, all that the passage seems to offer on its own is an extremely generic invocation of the practice of tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ. It suggests that when you go on pilgrimage, especially if you have not been on one for a long time, you should make a point of honoring Ga·πáe≈õa when you first get there and then eventually get around to feeding the ancestors, the sƒÅdhus, and the BrƒÅhma·πáas. What HemƒÅdri does with this rather banal material, for what seems to be the first time in the scholastic discourse, is he derives from it a formalized [[P1356]] ritual injunction and procedure. This is to say, he treats the PurƒÅ·πáic passage as if it contains not merely a ritual injunction, but a nested series of dependent procedures. As we will see, as he continues to elaborate, this begins with the observation of the niyama that is bhakti, and ends on the last day with a veneration in sequence of Ga·πáe≈õa, the grahas, and one‚Äôs own chosen deity, the making of a pƒÅrva·πáa offering (a type of ≈õrƒÅddha sacrifice) and the feeding of BrƒÅhma·πáas. We should note that of these activities, only the initial display of bhakti, the veneration of Ga·πáe≈õa, and the feeding BrƒÅhma·πáas has actually been mentioned by the root text. Not only has HemƒÅdri‚Äôs list interpolated in these other elements, but it is noticeably silent about elements actually present in his sources, such as the separate veneration of sƒÅdhus. What‚Äôs more, it has imposed from without a logical, sequential and hierarchical relationship between these elements nowhere in evidence in the BrahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça PurƒÅ·πáa itself, even supplying in from an entirely unrelated source the specification‚Äîentirely absent from his proof text‚Äîthat the performance of the pƒÅrva·πáa ritual ‚Äúhas ghee as its principal substance (gh·πõtamukhyadravyaka).‚Äù

It is our author‚Äôs use of this technical language, ‚Äúthe main ingredient‚Äù (mukhyadravyaka) embedded within an awkward unnatural collocation, that provides us with our first indication what exactly HemƒÅdri is really up to, for this term of art and style of discourse is unmistakably derived from the tradition of the Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ exegetes.
