As will soon become apparent, HemƒÅdri is going to ‚Äúderive‚Äù a universal procedure for performing pilgrimage from an intertextual reading of a range of previously logically discrete PurƒÅ·πáic sources, a task he will accomplish by reading the text of these PurƒÅ·πáas as if they were emerging from different ≈õƒÅkhƒÅs of the Veda, whose procedures have to be reconciled. Succinctly, within [[P1357]] totally new textual environs, HemƒÅdri is acting as if he were a PrƒÅbhƒÅkara Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsaka interpreting the Veda.

What exactly does this entail? In the next chapter, we will explore in some detail HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own articulation of this model as presented in the ≈örƒÅddhakalpa, which will prove integral to his defense of the authoritativeness of Sm·πõti and PurƒÅ·πáa. For the moment, however, since it is basically impossible to understand what HemƒÅdri is up to as an internally coherent intellectual project without some background, a bit of orientation is needed.76 To offer a simple introduction to this very complex issue, following Jaimini‚Äôs second s≈´tra on codanƒÅ, all Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsakas read through the Veda in search of injunctive statements‚Äîcommands that there is something that has to be done. They identify these statements on the basis of the presence or absence of certain verbal forms (optative li·πÖ, imperative lo·π≠, Vedic subjunctive le·π≠, and gerundive tavya as well as the present indicative under some special cases) that have an injunctive force. The iconic example, first mentioned by ≈öabara in his commentary on Jaimini but invoked throughout the literature is ‚ÄúsvargakƒÅmo yajetƒÅ,‚Äù which literally means the ‚Äúone desiring heaven should sacrifice.‚Äù In his linguistic analysis of this statement, ≈öabara summarizes this injunctive phrase as yƒÅgena svargo bhavati, literally ‚Äúheaven comes into being by means of the sacrifice.‚Äù This reframing‚Äîwhich becomes standard practice for thinking with an injunction‚Äîenables the exegete to query every ritual injunction by means of a grammatical analysis so that it provides answers to three questions‚Äî1) what do you have to do, 2) what is the instrument you use to do your task, and 3) how, specifically, can the task be accomplished. [[P1358]] In this case, from a BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ perspective, ‚Äúwhat you are doing‚Äù is bringing heaven into being, the ritual you are performing is the instrument, and the instructions for doing the task are the procedures that make up that ritual. This mode of analysis requires introducing a new term‚Äîthe verb of being, bhavati‚Äîand with it a new concept, the idea of bhƒÅvanƒÅ‚Äîbringing into being. For the BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a school of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ, the idea is then that the injunctive verbal root in the verb conveys that the sacrifice somehow brings into being‚Äîby means of bhƒÅvanƒÅ‚Äîa unprecedented (ap≈´rva) state of affairs, which in this case is heaven. Succinctly, the one who desired heaven would not have attained heaven before he performed the ritual. By performing the ritual, at some point in the future, heaven will be attained. KumƒÅrila, the founding figure of the BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a school, refines this model, proposing that bhƒÅvanƒÅ actually works to bring two things into being, on the one hand causing ‚Äúbringing into being‚Äù itself to happen (linguistic bhƒÅvanƒÅ) and on the other hand bringing into being specific objects or elements in the ritual (arthabhƒÅvanƒÅ), in this case heaven.

What is important for our purposes is that the PrƒÅbhƒÅkara Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsakas, figures like ≈öƒÅlikanƒÅtha Mi≈õra, object to this whole ‚ÄúbhƒÅvanƒÅ‚Äù model. Asserting an even more staunchly empiricist stance than their BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a rivals, they reject linguistic bhƒÅvanƒÅ as a fantasy with no empirical referent that needlessly complicates our understanding. Instead, they argue, just as HemƒÅdri is about to do, that normative verbal suffixes just provide us with information about the grammatical number (singular, plural, etc.) and temporal status of the agent and objects in a textual passage. Effectively their position is that, unlike with the BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠as, Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ does not need to consider the intentionality or subjectivity at all of the ritual agent, especially not to the extent of rethinking our philosophy of language (in this regard they are very much not like HemƒÅdri, who will take his inspiration in this domain from the emergent tradition of the new [[P1359]] logic). They suggest we should shift our focus from trying to understand how the injunction impels the sacrificer to act and instead center our investigation on the bare fact that the agent is compelled to act and then investigate the nature of the action that is to be done. In short, what the PrƒÅbhƒÅkara tradition does is re-extend what Francis Clooney has called the decentering of the human.77 When this is done, Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ becomes once again primarily about thinking through the local relationships between the discrete material elements in a ritual, especially theorizing the transference of ritual details from the archetype (prak·πõti) for a ritual to its ectype (vik·πõti), as well as context-specific attempts at deploying those rules in particular ritual settings. And as it turns out, these are precisely the two intellectual practices that dominate the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái.

The PrƒÅbhƒÅkaras propose that what is ap≈´rva in any given moment, in the sense of expected and not yet manifest, is not some transcendent result but the specific ritual detail that is needed in that given moment. This is why when the PrƒÅbhƒÅkara tradition glosses ‚ÄúsvargakƒÅmo yajetƒÅ,‚Äù it does so as ‚Äúdar≈õap≈´r·πáamƒÅsyayƒÅgena svargam bhƒÅvayet.‚Äù Whereas a BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a would understand this as bringing into being heaven by means of the specified sacrifice and fixating on thinking about how that bringing into being works, the PrƒÅbhƒÅkara instead provides us with an analytical matrix that, through proper reading, enables one to eventually locate that for performing this particular sacrifice, rice is the ingredient of the moment (mukhyadravya). In the metalanguage of the PrƒÅbhƒÅkaras, because rice will ‚Äúaccomplish the role of what is desired‚Äù it is the ≈õe·π£a, and thus we need to produce a new sentence, through viniyoga, that preliminarily reframes the above as ‚Äúone should bring the X sacrifice into being by means of rice,‚Äù and ultimately as ‚Äúhe should bring about the result through six rites that [[P1360]] make up the full and new moon sacrifices using specific ingredients in particular relationship with various mantras, and so forth.‚Äù Dharma, in this formulation, entails the grasping (grahƒÅka) of the ≈õe·π£a, constituting a heap of ritual items and steps situated in the proper context. It is this bundle of purely empirical elements that the tradition defines as ‚Äúwhat you have to do,‚Äù the itikartavyatƒÅ.

Now, as we have just started to see, this sort of exegetical work is precisely one of the main projects that HemƒÅdri keeps returning to throughout the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái. As HemƒÅdri will ultimately argue, because they find no explicit mention in the Vedic texts, all of the rituals discussed in the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái fall under the category of ectypal (vik·πõti) rituals. Like all such rituals in the Vedas, this means that we cannot simply consult a single source that discusses them in full‚Äîor even a single ritual canon‚Äîbecause such resources do not exist. Instead, just as HemƒÅdri does throughout the CVC, the ‚Äústandard‚Äù or universal form of a ritual, nowhere in evidence in the source texts, must be arrived at analytically through the application of metarules, reconciling apparent contradictions within and among texts, ordering the relative priority of different statements within a corpus, and then documenting some of the transformations applied to the common ritual procedure in different contexts and circumstances.

In this regard at least, much of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs project would seem to be iconically Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsaka. Where our author diverges enormously from his predecessors is in his choice of materials, for nearly all of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ had proposed that their mode of reading, beyond a theory of the sentence, explicitly does not apply to non-Vedic texts‚ÄîVedƒÅnta even suggests it is inadmissible when it comes to the Upani·π£ads‚Äîand that it was particularly inappropriate in treating the PurƒÅ·πáas. There are many reasons why this would have been the case, starting from [[P1361]] the fact that most of the PurƒÅ·πáas offer what Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ would label arthavƒÅda, things such as stories that neither offer an injunction nor clarify ritual details. Certainly, one significant factor is that there was considerable doubt among intellectuals and theologians, even among those who valued PurƒÅ·πáas, about whether or not they were all equally authoritative as well as if they formed a unitary textual corpus with a singular intention, essential preconditions for reading them intertextually. The dissonance between Vedic and PurƒÅ·πáic textuality and thus between the proper hermeneutics for treating the texts for most readers seemed self-evident. After all, Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ had famously grounded the authority of the Veda on its apauru·π£eyatva‚Äîits being a text without an author‚Äîwhereas PurƒÅ·πáas are explicitly compiled works, different portions of which are ‚Äúauthored‚Äù or spoken by distinctive gods.

For HemƒÅdri, however, for reasons that he will make plain in the next chapter of this thesis, these discrepancies seem not to have been problematic. Thus, he habitually reads the PurƒÅ·πáic corpus as a whole as if the details not found in passages in one work‚Äîsuch as the BrahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça‚Äîcan be readily supplied from another that seemingly addresses similar subjects. Much to the modern reader‚Äôs annoyance, he even does this when the connections between the two sources are hardly self-evident. Take for example, the following passage, which resumes immediately from the place where we left off in his discourse: [[P1362]]

HemƒÅdri: And this pƒÅrva·πáa has ghee as its principal substance (gh·πõtamukhyadravyaka) based on this authoritative statement from the Bhavi·π£ya PurƒÅ·πáa:

Bhavi·π£ya PurƒÅ·πáa: The king, having done the ≈õrƒÅddha [rite] with ghee (sarpi·π£ƒÅ), should go to another country; this is stated to be for the sake of yƒÅtrƒÅ in regard to ‚Äúentry,‚Äù there is no doubt.

HemƒÅdri: Some people say this [passage] is related to bestowing military victory, but, that is wrong. Because, there is a greater hermeneutical probability [lit. interpretive lightness] of construing [the word] upavƒÅsa [in the verse] in the sense of a fast (upavƒÅsa), because of this usage being relevant to the present context.78

Since the BrahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça PurƒÅ·πáa‚Äôs bland and basically detail poor summary of tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ does not supply us with enough information to actually perform the key acts involved in tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ, HemƒÅdri next goes searching elsewhere in the PurƒÅ·πáas. Ultimately, he locates a statement in the Bhavi·π£ya PurƒÅ·πáa that also contains a brief reference to yƒÅtrƒÅ, which specifies that in ≈õrƒÅddha rites, ghee should be used as the main element. This statement, however, is of dubious relevance, for the agent it specifies is not a pilgrim but a king, and the yƒÅtrƒÅ of which it speaks offers no indication that it is fact a pilgrimage. Sanskrit is of course a polysemantic language in general, and yƒÅtrƒÅ can refer to many types of journeys. The fact that the king is the probable agent and that the ≈õrƒÅddha rite precedes ‚Äúgoing to another country,‚Äù however, suggest that the Bhavi·π£ya PurƒÅ·πáa has in mind a very specific and conceptually unrelated context‚Äîthat in fact the yƒÅtrƒÅ under discussion is the war processional. The division on yƒÅtrƒÅbheda, for example, in the MƒÅnasollƒÅsa of Some≈õvara III offers us a taxonomy of different types of yƒÅtrƒÅs, all of them in fact preparations for invading enemy kingdoms, organized according to astrological and prognosticatory factors and prefaced by a range of rituals, some of which do in fact involve the king offering ghee.

HemƒÅdri is quick to try to rebut such suggestions, a task he carries out by invoking Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ-derived reading strategies pertaining to the relative authoritativeness and priority of different approaches for determining the precise meaning of key terms of art in the text and their logical relationship to each other. His effort here however reads as particularly forced. His invocation of the term ‚ÄúupavƒÅsa‚Äù in the sense of fast as clarifying the intended context for [[P1363]] the text of the Bhavi·π£ya PurƒÅ·πáa‚Äôs use of yƒÅtrƒÅ, runs aground of the simple fact that the word upavƒÅsa does not actually appear anywhere in the portion of the Bhavi·π£ya which he has cited. It is in fact being supplied from the earlier quotation in the BrahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça PurƒÅ·πáa, which had characterized the prospective pilgrim as one whose fast has been completed (k·πõtopavƒÅsa·∏•). In essence, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs reading is only meaningful at all if we come to the table having already presupposed the intertextual dependence of all of the PurƒÅ·πáas, that they are a unified corpus with a singular focus that can be read as if they are all providing us with different complementary parts to the same puzzle.

That HemƒÅdri‚Äôs strained readings and usage of his sources are a sui generis phenomenon, predicated on the fact that he lacks any substantive antecedents in the tradition who are trying to make his same arguments, is perhaps most compellingly exemplified in a series of connected portions of his discourse embedded within the same delineation of the universal procedure for pilgrimage and its theoretical foundations that we have just exploring. As we will see momentarily, HemƒÅdri frames much of this section of his text as a reply to a series of stances ascribed to a certain Dharma≈õƒÅstrin named Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra. This is a figure of considerable obscurity, but considering that determining the actual the nature of his contributions is quite pertinent to evaluating HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own use of his materials, it is worth pausing for a moment to survey what little we can know about him.

In a magisterial survey of the early history of eastern Dharma≈õƒÅstra traditions completed in 1915, Rai Monmohan Chakravarti Bahadur dedicates four sentences to Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra, suggesting his contributions are known to us only on the basis of citations found in four later works of eastern Dharma≈õƒÅstra.79 On the basis of the earliest of these‚Äî [[P1364]] the Sm·πõtisƒÅra‚ÄîBahadur proposes that Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra probably lived in Mithila prior to the fourteenth century and that he wrote a work on ƒÅcƒÅra. In his History of Dharma≈õƒÅstra, without acknowledging the extent of his debt to his Bengali predecessor, Panduranga Kane helpfully informs us while summarizing the Sm·πõtisƒÅra of HarinƒÅtha‚Äîin which Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra is cited several times‚Äîthat the work in question dealt with the sixteen sa·πÉskƒÅras, death rites, ≈õrƒÅddha and purification.80 Kane (1962, pg. 372) also mentions another possibly related manuscript that covered many of the titles of law. A number of decades later, Ludo Rocher81 also ascribes to Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra a text on jurisprudence‚Äîentitled the VyavahƒÅratara·πÖga‚Äîquite possibly the same source noticed in passing by Kane, briefly reconstructing Ga·πáe≈õvara‚Äôs position on the subject of vƒÅkpƒÅru·π£ya‚Äîabusive speech‚Äîas it is remembered by later commentators. While Rocher‚Äôs parenthetical note has seemingly been ignored, it is Kane‚Äôs position that has been taken as canonical by later historians of the discipline. This is unfortunate, as Kane in his analysis rather unhelpfully proceeds to assert, seemingly based on the shared name Ga·πáe≈õvara, that ‚Äúit appears extremely probable‚Äù that the Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra under discussion is the same figure as Ga·πáe≈õvara ·π¨hakkura, the uncle of the famous Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara ·π¨hakkura, author of the Sm·πõtiratnƒÅkara, and that he must have been writing between 1275‚Äì1310 CE. Kane‚Äôs supposition is of course unambiguously refuted by the presence of numerous references to Mi≈õra within the circa 1260 CE CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça.

Debunking this association has enormous relevance for our own investigation, precisely because the fourteenth-century Ga·πáe≈õvara ·π¨hakkura is remembered primarily for his [[P1365]] now fragmentary SugatisopƒÅna, a work that combs through the PurƒÅ·πáas in the service of crafting a soteriologically-oriented Dharma≈õƒÅstra. The work is unpublished, but on the basis of what has been cited from it in manuscript, Ga·πáe≈õvara ·π¨hakkura‚Äôs work may well represent one of the earliest surviving scholastic responses to the intellectual project of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái. Had the two Ga·πáe≈õvaras been identical, as Kane had assumed, this may well have been indicative that HemƒÅdri‚Äôs treatment of Tƒ´rtha (and much else besides) was indeed dependent on an earlier lost tradition of Dharma≈õƒÅstra commentary. As we are about to see though, based on the internal evidence from within HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own text, in contrast, far from combing through the PurƒÅ·πáas in search of ‚Äúa bridge to heaven,‚Äù within his own writings, the elder Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra‚Äôs incidental engagement with the theology of place was confined to some stray passages that discuss the relevance of PrayƒÅga and GayƒÅ to the performance of ≈õrƒÅddha rituals. Thus, there is every indication that it is HemƒÅdri himself, appropriating Ga·πáe≈õvara‚Äôs materials, and not some lost predecessor, who for the first time in the scholastic tradition is systematically theorizing tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ in universal terms. Making sense of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs argument requires us to proceed slowly and methodically. Let us see how he starts to develop his case:

HemƒÅdri: Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra and the other say thus: on the day of the fast, also there is the [making] of a bald head. In PrayƒÅga, during the tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ, due to the separation from the mother and the father, one should perform the shaving such that he becomes bald.82

According to the statements of the Sm·πõtisamuccaya, ≈öa·πÖkhalikhita and Vi·π£·πáu, when one is intent on going to the tƒ´rtha (tƒ´rthagamane cikƒ´r·π£ite)‚Äîwearing the garb of a renunciate‚Äîin the beginning there is shaving, this is the meaning. And immediately after the ≈õrƒÅddha, there is the resolution to go. Then, there is the bearing of the garb [of the renunciate].

That is stated in the VƒÅyu PurƒÅ·πáa: [[P1366]]

VƒÅyu PurƒÅ·πáa: If one is prepared to go to GayƒÅ, having done the ≈õrƒÅddha according to injunction, having donned the garb of an ascetic, having performed the circumambulation of the village, having then gone to another village, then, there is the eating of the remains from the ≈õrƒÅddha. Then, every day that he should go, without receiving alms, at each step along the way there would be the fruit of the A≈õvamedha on the part of the one going to GayƒÅ.83

Essentially, what HemƒÅdri has accomplished here is a conflation of a whole range of previously discrete, context-specific practices that have their origins in distinctive sources. By presuming that these materials have a shared conceptual context‚Äîprakara·πáa, in the language of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ, which is indicated, as we will see in the next chapter, by the presence of shared terms of art‚Äîhaving knit them altogether, our author then applies himself to the herculean task of trying to iron out all of the apparent contradictions so that this becomes a single, noncontradictory, and connected discourse. What such an approach elides is that the referents in situ in each of these discussions were originally quite different.

Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra it seems was not really talking about pilgrimage at all, at least as we understand it. Instead, the actual subject of his inquiry is into the precise procedure to be followed by the eldest son in a family immediately following the cremation of his parents. This is a process that famously begins, just as it does today, with the mourner having his head shaved, and generally entails the making of a ≈õrƒÅddha offering at either PrayƒÅga or GayƒÅ. These features of the discourse explain why, even in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs summary, Ga·πáe≈õvara speaks of becoming bald as being motivated by ‚Äúseparation from the mother and father‚Äù (pit·πõmƒÅtraviyogata·∏•). And while we do not know precisely what was in the recension of [[P1367]] ≈öa·πÖkha available to our author, elsewhere in the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái HemƒÅdri draws upon a version of Likhitasm·πõti whose readings are very close to that of the published edition.84 At least as printed, Likhita contains zero references to tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ, or any other euphemism to that effect. Its only references to the shaving of the head appear in verses fifteen to twenty, where the subject under discussion once again is the specific ≈õrƒÅddha one is to perform following the death of one‚Äôs mother or father, a procedure that here as well entails the complete shaving of the head even before one offers the three-fold pi·πá·∏ça. For our purposes, what is important is that in each of these cases, what is under discussion is a highly particularized naimittika ritual. In this exact form it is to be observed only one or two times in a single lifetime. In other words, this is hardly the sort of activity that would be likely to form the template for ‚Äúthe general ritual procedure‚Äù governing any sort of pilgrim venturing towards any sacred place. In fact, even within the archive available to us roughly corresponding to HemƒÅdri‚Äôs chosen sources, most of the highly specific details invoked by our author are uniquely associated with this kind of ≈õrƒÅddha rite. In Likhita, for instance, the other forms of ≈õrƒÅddha do not entail any act of head shaving at all.

The source that HemƒÅdri invokes to complement this evidence‚Äîa passage from the VƒÅyu PurƒÅ·πáa, adds even further complications. HemƒÅdri is invested in this proof text because unlike the other parts of his archive, which were either maddeningly vague or, while outlining a highly specific practice inconveniently were not explicitly related to tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ, the VƒÅyu PurƒÅ·πáa explicitly mentions an injunction and then actually offers us some of the concrete steps that make up the procedure. Even this text, however, requires some tweaking if one is to make it appear to be perfectly on topic. Instead of taking PrayƒÅga as its focus, it is concerned with [[P1368]] GayƒÅ. Just as significantly, while it mentions the donning of the guise of an ascetic (kƒÅrpatƒ´ve·π£a), nowhere does it allude to the shaving of the head. Again, these are not mere oversights, but instead reflect the fact that the ritual deployment of each of these elements prior to their reimagining in our nibandha was highly context, place, and time specific. In order to overcome this host of apparent shortcomings in his sources, which stubbornly seem to keep resisting perfect alignment with his intentions, rather than locating additional proof texts more favorable to his approach, HemƒÅdri resorts instead to the strategic application of a series of further exegetical strategies that find their points of origin in the discursive worlds of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ and late classical NyƒÅya. [[P1369]]

HemƒÅdri:

Even if it is the case that this bearing of the garb of ascetic, having [first] worshipped Ga·πáe≈õa, the grahas, and i·π£·π≠adevatƒÅ,and the [specific] fruit derived from going [on pilgrimage] are known in scripture to refer only [to pilgrimage to] GayƒÅ‚Äîeven so‚Äîit [this vidhƒÅna] is also to be understood with regard to going to another Tƒ´rtha.

[This follows:] According to the maxim that what applies to one place also applies to another place [if the meaning of the ≈õƒÅstra is ascertained in regard to one place in the same text, it would also apply to another location] (ekatra nir·πáƒ´ta), when there is the semantic expectancy (ƒÅka·πÖk·π£ƒÅ) of a statement of the ‚Äúfruit‚Äù/result, because it is revealed as ‚Äúhaving the fruit of going on pilgrimage,‚Äù it is reasonable that in another location as well, it would ‚Äúhave the fruit of going on pilgrimage,‚Äù because it is included as a proximate component [within the same system]. But it is not, however, that the fruit is to be conceptualized according to the vi≈õvajit maxim.85

This is a dense passage, full of elliptical invocations of a meta-≈õƒÅstric background in which HemƒÅdri assumes his reader would have fluency, but which is utterly foreign even to most scholars of Indian religions for whom the mechanics of SmƒÅrta paddhatis remains an undiscovered country. Making it accessible will take some effort. Essentially, as we alluded to [[P1370]] earlier, HemƒÅdri has been proceeding as if the sƒÅmƒÅnyatƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅ vidhi‚Äîin the language of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ‚Äîis a vik·πõti or ectypal ritual. This means that unlike the archetype ritual‚Äîsay the dar≈õap≈´r·πáamƒÅsa i·π£·π≠i, discussed in detail systematically in a range of Vedic sources‚Äîthis is a rite for which scripture does not provide us with direct instruction (upade≈õa) regarding what is to be done (itikartavyatƒÅ), the materials used in ritual (dravya), or even the deity (devatƒÅ) governing the ritual. Indeed, all that scripture might give us directly is a cursory statement‚Äî‚Äúx ritual is to be done.‚Äù It is up to the ritualist to reconstruct the whole rite in a systematic fashion out of stray statements scattered throughout his sources. This is done primarily through the judicious application of the principle of substitution (atide≈õa). To simplify matters significantly, one of the many ways of determining whether or not two practices or elements can serve as appropriate substitutes is whether or not they serve the same purpose and are said to yield the same result. Another complementary principle in Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ exegesis is that if the passages delineating a particular ritual never specify the result of that ritual, rather than assuming the ritual bears no fruit, we can simply assume that its intended result is the obtaining of heaven at some point in the future. In fact, this principle is the very vi≈õvajit-nyƒÅya that HemƒÅdri, intent on making it clear that the maxim does not apply in the case under discussion, explicitly invokes at the end of our passage. [[P1371]]

In short, part of what HemƒÅdri is arguing is that even though in the sources he has cited, the specified ritual details of bearing the kƒÅrpatƒ´ve·π£a and following this particular procedure of worshipping in sequence Ga·πáe≈õa, the grahas, and one‚Äôs own chosen deity have been discussed solely in the context of pilgrimage to GayƒÅ, in fact they apply to all sacred sites that have same designated fruit of ‚Äútƒ´rthagamanaphala‚Äù‚Äî‚Äúthe result you yield from going on pilgrimage.‚Äù In practice, what this means is that every time a PurƒÅ·πáic text describes something as, ‚Äúa fruit you get from going on pilgrimage,‚Äù HemƒÅdri is saying the statements in that passage falls under this category and can be used to supply additional details for ritual. With any luck, this part of our author‚Äôs argument has been rendered a bit more lucidly, setting aside the fact that, unlike good health, a spotted cow, or even heaven, ‚Äúthe fruit you get from pilgrimage‚Äù is not a specific result, but originally simply a place holder for whatever results the text will go on to supply in detail. In other words, this systemization is based on nominalizing and reifying a mere tautology.

Rather unexpectedly, however, HemƒÅdri then supplements what‚Äîapart from the non- Vedic nature of the texts he is analyzing‚Äîis pretty standard Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ-style reasoning with something genuinely arcane, an obscure exegetical rule‚Äîthe ekatra nir·πáƒ´ta nyƒÅya, drawn from an even more gnomic discourse. This principle is not found within the standard Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ tool kit, but first seems to surface in the writings of the eleventh-century Kashmiri NaiyƒÅyika Udayana, a radically innovator thinker in his own right responsible for the consolidation of the previously separate schools of NyƒÅya and Vai≈õe·π£ika into a single discipline and often perceived as the fore-father of the new logic.86 Rather remarkably, HemƒÅdri seems to have derived this principle from Udayana‚Äôs famous treatises that sets out to prove the existence of god, ‚ÄúThe Bouquet of Reasons‚Äù (NyƒÅyakusuma√±jali), where the principle is invoked and explained at [[P1372]] some length in chapter five.87 Chapter five of the NyƒÅyakusuma√±jali is mostly dedicated to offering a series of what at the time were thoroughly novel but to us are still nonetheless relatively straightforward proofs of the existence of God arrived at by means of inferential reasoning (anumƒÅna). Sadly for us, what HemƒÅdri actually decides to take up as an integral part of his intellectual apparatus is not this comparatively accessible material, but rather the argument with which the NyƒÅyakusuma√±jali closes, one of the most difficult parts of the text, the analysis of which has been scrupulously avoided to this date by Western scholars.88 Though this is very difficult material, it is worth taking some time and wrestling with the sort of intellectual resources that are inspiring HemƒÅdri, for as we will see they deeply inform his rethinking of the very nature of ritual and religion.

This last part of ‚ÄúThe Bouquet of Reasons‚Äù has as its focus nothing less than the refutation of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsaka ritual mechanism and atheism, and Udayana sets out to accomplish this objective by deploying the very same tools and reading strategies that Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ had made use of in theorizing the injunctive force of Vedic statements. The position Udayana will ultimately arrive at‚Äîthis is incidentally the very stance that Romila Thapar has retrojected back into the origins of Hindu thought89 but is in fact first in evidence in the eleventh century‚Äîis that just as all injunctive statements in the optative that tell you what you should do presume the intentionality of an authoritative person (ƒÅpta), when it comes to scripture, the authority that enjoins specific ritual actions is in fact God. In Udayana‚Äôs language, injunctions (vidhi) signify the intention (abhiprƒÅya) of the speaker (vƒÅkt·πõ). An injunction enjoins a specific [[P1373]] activity (prav·πõtti) or cessation from activity (niv·πõtti) conveyed to the listener, and it does this by means of the bare presence of the optative verbal formation (li·πÖ and so forth). When the sound of the optative is heard by the listener‚Äîeven before the exact content of that command is cognized‚Äîit instantly tells you that there is something that needs to be done or not done. In order to actually accomplish this task, however, the agent will have to first properly cognize its contents‚Äîthis is his or her responsibility‚Äîand then they will have to make use of a procedure for accomplishing the specified task that actually works‚Äîwhich is the responsibility of the one giving the directions. In other words, in contrast to Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ, Udayana proposes that ritual injunctions (vidhi), and by extension ritual systems, are not a value neutral technology that is made effective by the proper execution of an inherited or derived ritual procedure. Instead, as we are about to see, he argues injunctions are always value-laden‚Äîthis is what he calls vidhipratyaya‚Äîand expressions of a particular worldview, often one that is provided by the supreme creator.

Against the Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsakas, who viewed all action as motivated and made possible by desire (kƒÅma), Udayana argues that what structures activity (prav·πõtti) is not primarily the desires of the ritual actor himself‚Äîthese do not cause anything to happen directly‚Äîbut rather the degree of effort (prayatna) that this actor consciously invests in executing what, if anything meaningful is to result, must be an efficacious procedure, the efficaciousness of which is judged by the agent responsible for its creation. A simple example will help us see what Udayana is really saying. Imagine a child whose parents yell from the other room‚Äî‚ÄúGo Clean your room!‚Äù The child first hears their parents say the word ‚ÄúGo!‚Äù Their body, mostly unconsciously, responds with nervous physical energy, causing them for example to shift posture (Udayana calls this the kƒÅyaparispandamƒÅtra). Only if and when child actually gets [[P1374]] up does any actual ‚Äúgoing‚Äù take place. In other words, the child has to apply conscious deliberate effort if any action whatsoever is going to occur. If all the child hears is that initial ‚ÄúGo!‚Äù‚Äîthis is of course a vidhi of sorts‚Äîall sorts of ‚Äúactions‚Äù might well take place‚Äîout of fear, for example, the kid may take off and run out the front door‚Äîbut it is very unlikely that the intended relationship between a command‚ÄîGo!‚Äî and a specific outcome, the cleaning of the room, will emerge of its own accord. In order for that to happen, the child will have to experience the command acting upon his body as well as engage in a deliberate effort, but he or she will also have to realize that the statement is applicable to them specifically and then to listen to and understand the semantic content of the rest of the statement ‚ÄúGo clean your room!‚Äù

So far this is pretty straightforward. Udayana, however, then adds in one more very interesting observation. It is still not enough to recognize that the agent recognizes a command is given, that it applies to him, and then to cognize it in general terms. In order for the activity to be a success, action does not just have to be undertaken, but it has to be done in accordance with the standards assumed by the speaker doing the enjoining. In other words, if a child wants the act of ‚Äúcleaning the room‚Äù to result in a positive response from their parent, they have to figure out what this particular authority figure really means when they say ‚Äúclean.‚Äù While, for some parents, ‚Äúclean‚Äù might mean simply that nothing should be on the floor in the room, or even just that specific items‚Äîjust as dirty clothing‚Äîbe removed, others might anticipate that everything will be put away in its proper place, the whole room has been vacuumed, or even that the base boards have been scrubbed by hand. What determines which of these requirements is applicable and will result in ‚Äúcleaning the room‚Äù actually being a means towards the accomplishing of the desired goal (i·π£·π≠asƒÅdhƒÅnatƒÅ) is not some abstracted logical relationship [[P1375]] or mechanistic activation of an arrangement of principles intrinsic to the architectonics that order our reality. It is nothing more or less than the inferred intentionality of the original speaker. For this reason, we must speak of any discourse‚Äîincluding the Veda‚Äîas comprised not simply of injunctions (vidhi)‚Äîbut of a specific mental content that travels paired with that injunction (vidhipratyaya), the proper understanding of which determines the success or failure of the act or ritual. In other words, knowledge of an injunction does not really arise separate from knowledge about the object or focus of that injunction.

In the fifth chapter, Udayana arrives at the positions we have just been outlining through a very intricate, systematic, and sophisticated engagement with the philosophy of the Sanskrit grammarians as well Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ analyses of the working of linguistic philosophy.90 In doing so he explores a whole host of possibilities, using the technical idiom of analyzing logical relationships in terms of their kƒÅraka relationships, with the aim of examining whether or not injunctions are 1) attributes of the agent (kart·πõdharma·∏•); (2) the attribute of the action (karmadharma·∏•); (3) attributes of the instrument (kƒÅra·πáadharma·∏•), before finally arriving at his preferred conclusion that the injunction is an attribute of the person that enjoins (niyokt·πõdharma·∏•). We will take up very briefly just a few of these possibilities, namely, the stance that the injunction is an attribute of the action or the ritual sacrifice itself, which lies at the heart of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ.

First, we need to have in hand a bit of his philosophy of grammar. Responding to KumƒÅrila Bha·π≠·π≠a‚Äôs theorization of injunctive statements as having two dimensions of bhƒÅvanƒÅ, the bare capacity for bringing something into being and the targeted capacity of bringing into being a specific thing or state of affairs (arthabhƒÅvanƒÅ), Udayana proposes as an alternative that every verbal action can be parsed into at least two components, so that, for example, when [[P1376]] we say ‚Äúhe cooks (pacati),‚Äù what we are really saying is ‚Äúhe does the cooking (pƒÅka·πÉ karoti). In other words, all verbs can be analyzed so as to be made to take what in English we would call a direct object and what in Sanskrit grammatical thinking at the very least is an implied object. Udayana insists it is this hidden verbal ‚Äúdoing‚Äù‚Äîwhether mentioned explicitly the way you would with a compound verb in Hindi or hidden in the suffix applied to the verbal root‚Äî that in the case of injunctive forms of the Verb communicate to the listener the inseparability of effort (prayatna) to the successful completion of ritual action. In other words, he proposes that the entire Vedic theory about the nature of the impelling force (codanƒÅ) of injunctions as lying at the very center of understanding dharma is completely wrong.

A key part of the reason why he goes to all this trouble is that having established all of this within a framework legible to the grammarians as well as to BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a and PrƒÅbhƒÅkara Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ enables our author to offer from within the conventions of these disciplines a devastating critique of the standard Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ accounts, both BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a and PrƒÅbhƒÅkara, of the iconic injunction ‚ÄúsvargakƒÅmo yajetƒÅ‚Äù‚Äîthe one desirous of heaven should sacrifice. This, we must remember, is grounded in the conception that the real focus of ‚ÄúVedic‚Äù religion is the identification and proper analysis of injunctive statements found in the Veda where the verb possesses certain formal properties. What Udayana demonstrates is that once you revise your thinking about how injunctions work, the whole picture offered by traditional Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ collapses upon itself into a heap of contradictions that violate other presuppositions central to the system‚Äôs theory of reading or generally accepted by Indian logic. Thus, for example, if [[P1377]] what injunctions conveyed to us were the desire for a specific result, rather than being the archetype for Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ style injunctive sentences, ‚ÄúsvargakƒÅmo yajetƒÅ‚Äù would bear the fault of inherent redundancy as svargakƒÅmo and the verbal suffix would serve the exact same function, a major no-no in ≈õƒÅstra.

The real problem arrives, however, when we take this new theory of how injunctions work and then need to account for the role assigned to ‚Äúap≈´rva‚Äù‚Äîthe unprecedented‚Äîwhich for the BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠as is what makes ritual work causally and for the PrƒÅbhƒÅkaras is what makes recovering specific ritual procedures possible. Unlike other human activities, emically speaking, from Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ‚Äôs perspective, ritual tells us to do something ‚Äúunprecedented‚Äù that serves no human purpose. What Udayana asserts is that for some very good reasons, we cannot accept that the injunctive suffix in the verb communicates to us something about or is even logically related to the ‚Äúunprecedented.‚Äù When we read sentences, regardless of whether we accept the BhƒÅ·π≠·π≠a model that the sentence meaning is comprehended word by word or the PrƒÅbhƒÅkara notion that we mentally grasp whole sentences as syntactic units together, we are engaged in a cognitive process that takes words and their meanings as conceptual objects. In other words, our mind relates to the sentence in much the same way that it would when we see a table or a tiger. But the whole point of something being ap≈´rva is that it brings into being some state of affairs in the future that does not and cannot exist in the present. As Udayana says, it is the absence of all possibility of previous perceptions. So here is the problem: if we cannot by definition know what a thing is or have any access to that thing, there is no way within the rules of Indian philosophy that we logically demonstrate that it has any relation to any of the things we do know. [[P1378]]

For example, how we know what words mean is that either we, or someone else, already knew what those words meant beforehand. Recall, if you will, the child who heard the statement ‚Äúgo [[P1379]] clean your room.‚Äù In order for any action to take place that is logically connected to the meaning of either those words or the sentence that contains them, both the speaker and the observer needed to know what all the words mean. This is why if you were to yell ‚Äúgo clean your room,‚Äù and the sentence is overheard by a salamander, an infant, or a person from Equatorial Guinea who has never heard English before, the room is probably not going to get cleaned. What Udayana points out, is that even if by some miracle said statement was made and then the amphibian or person who was being spoken to proceeded to crawl or walk away and go and clean the room, there is no logical grounds for establishing that a causal relationship existed between the two activities‚Äîin other words, that one thing followed necessarily from the other and will do so again in the future given certain circumstances. This is especially true as in several of the above cases, a key component in the sentence‚Äîthe very idea of ‚Äúyour room‚Äù‚Äîespecially as applied to a salamander or a culturally remote stranger, is about as unthinkable as the notion of ap≈´rva. Were the cleaning of a room to happen, we would simply assume it was pure chance, or there is another actual causal relationship unrelated to the one we have been exploring that we have missed.

Expanding on this observation and addressing much more pointedly the objections of PrƒÅbhƒÅkara Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ, Udayana next goes on to demonstrate a further parallel problem. A statement like ‚ÄúsvargakƒÅmo yajeta‚Äù as interpreted by followers of PrabhƒÅkara implies a statement about what is to be done (niyoga), a specific sacrifice, and well as a specified result (niyojya), in this case, heaven. Since at the end of a yajna people do not rise up into the sky, however, we know that the sacrificial act does not produce its desired result at the moment of [[P1380]] its completion, but, we are assured, sometime in the future. But if ritual is the cause of heaven, we have a problem, because the ritual has completely ceased to exist by the time its apparent effect manifests. It is for this reason then the ap≈´rva is posited as a subtle and lasting bridge linking cause and effect. The idea is that its existence must be implied by the injunction if we are to assume any causal relationship between sacrifice and heaven. Udayana‚Äôs response is once again that, if you accept standard Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ accounts about how we acquire authoritative knowledge, there is no logical reason to associate being unprecedented (ap≈´rvatva) with being a cause at all. The reason this is the case is because there are only two ways we could connect them: an act of recollection or an act of inferential reasoning. The problem is that you can only recollect something you have already seen or known before, and ap≈´rva, by definition, is a thing that has never been seen or known before. In a similar spirit, inference demands that we already know the relationship and nature of two things being connected, and that is precisely what we are trying to find out. Finally, since we do not know what ap≈´rva is nor any of its properties, logically speaking, we cannot even determine if it is the same sort of thing or a different sort of thing than being a cause, any more than one could compare and contrast a camel and a ‚Äújsiozjgieha.‚Äù This is because, in any case, either the grasping of the differences between things (bhedagraha) or the non-grasping of a difference (bhedƒÅgraha) always involves act of comparison, and you cannot compare and contrast two things when you know absolutely nothing at all about one of them. Udayana‚Äôs point is that ap≈´rva is not just a philosophically questionable premise, but it really does not even amount to a form of nonsense, since we cannot even determine whether it does or does not possess any specific properties or has or does not have any particular meaning. As we discussed earlier, Udayana‚Äôs solution to [[P1381]] all these scholastic entanglements is to propose that the coherency of ritual‚Äîboth what it means and how it works‚Äîis a property of the one who enjoins it (niyokt·πõ), which in many cases is in fact the creator deity himself.

For Udayana, all the arguments we have just been exploring are leading up to a proof of the dependence of Indic ritual as a coherent system on God‚Äôs existence, one which in its particulars overlap in many places with HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own positions as put forth in the Kaivalayadƒ´pikƒÅ. And yet from the perspective of a social historian, utterly unqualified to stake lofty claims on such matters, this very same line of argumentation can also be read as arriving at a parallel observation, namely, that ritual becomes meaningful and actionable only when it is mediated by particular social relationships, whether between specific humans or between humans and nonhumans. As advocated by Udayana, this socialization of ritual knowledge‚Äî quite specifically of the types of ritual knowledge that Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ had claimed as its own exclusive purview‚Äîrepresents a shift in orientation, not only in degree but of kind. It offers an intellectual change in perspective akin to the trading off of logical positivism for phenomenology, with Udayana and those, like HemƒÅdri, who are influenced by him, playing the role of a Wittgenstein, who takes methods from the first system and deploys them in the service of an entirely different sort of worldview. Once you make this transition, as HemƒÅdri has clearly done, even when you are reading the exact same texts and applying shared exegetical techniques, you will end up doing different things with your sources because now and forever, ritual is inseparable from its social implications and the intentionalities of those authorities (human and otherwise) who enjoin its practice. In other words, everything you have to do is now inseparable from a certain given worldview.

As our exploration of the exegetical method that underlies the unpublished Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça comes to a close‚Äîa method that we have now seen in our author‚Äôs own [[P1382]] understanding is inseparable from a whole host of theological as well as methodological presuppositions‚Äîthe last task on our agenda is demonstrating concretely that HemƒÅdri‚Äôs theoretical work and choice of materials in the lost Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, though sui generis in their own time, had a lasting impact on the subsequent history of Hindu religion, especially in terms of the SmƒÅrta discourses of Early modernity. This is a vast and mostly uncharted field, where most of the key texts remain either unpublished or unstudied. Rather than attempting to historicize HemƒÅdri‚Äôs impact, we will have to make do with a single example of overwhelming direct textual influence. The text we will turn to as emblematic of a much larger story is Richard Salomon‚Äôs edition and translation of the opening division of the sixteenth-century Bridge of the Three Holy Cities (Tristhalƒ´setu) of the De≈õastha BrƒÅhma·πáa NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a.91 Though the influential polymath and public intellectual NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a was himself active in Varanasi, as Sheldon Pollock, Rosland O‚ÄôHanlon, and Christopher Minkowski have all discussed at some length, the Bha·π≠·π≠a line from which he and his descendants among early modernity‚Äôs new intellectuals sprang had its origins in central Maharashtra, with the progenitor of the line, RƒÅme≈õvara Bha·π≠·π≠a, being active primarily within the Seu·πáade≈õa in the generation after HemƒÅdri.92

[[P1383]]

It is interesting in this regard to note that though Salomon seems ignorant of the existence of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, the introduction to his edition repeatedly seems to be inferring not only the existence of an unknown intermediary between this work and the K·πõtyakalpataru but assigning to this lost source an array of seemingly discrete topical concerns and allusions, nearly all of which are found in our Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça. As Salomon writes:

The general section of the TSS is by no means an entirely original work. This general arrangement of the text, and in many cases the specific wording, are derived from a tradition developed in the earlier texts on the subject of tƒ´rtha. In a number of cases, NB borrows passages virtually verbatim from earlier tƒ´rtha and dharma≈õƒÅstra texts such as the Tƒ´rthacintƒÅma·πái, Sm·πõtyarthasƒÅra, and MadanapƒÅrijƒÅta, without acknowledging the source. . . . It is not until the time of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara (early 12th century A.D.) that we know of any independent dharma≈õƒÅstra text devoted entirely to these subjects. . . . The next important tƒ´rtha text which is now available is the Tƒ´rthacintƒÅma·πái (Tƒ´Ci) of VƒÅcaspati Mi≈õra of MithilƒÅ. . . . This text is quite advanced in comparison with that of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, and since it refers to points of controversy not yet raised in the TVK there must have been at least one, if not several texts written on the subject between the time of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara and that of VƒÅcaspati. One of these texts must have been by Ga·πáe≈õvara Mi≈õra, whom VƒÅcaspati cites occasionally. . . but unfortunately the works of Ga·πáe≈õvara are not extant.93

Turning to the Tristhalƒ´setu itself, starting from the very title of this division of the text‚ÄîsƒÅmƒÅnyapragha·π≠·π≠aka‚ÄîNƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a pursues an intellectual project, namely, to present ‚Äúfirst, the general rules for tƒ´rthas‚Äù which are to be ‚Äúproperly described,‚Äù and then to offer ‚Äúthe rules for the three tƒ´rthas, beginning with PrayƒÅga,‚Äù unimaginable without the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça. As we are about to see, though he never mentions our work by name, even if we only take into account the tiny portion of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça we have been exploring, his text is saturated with examples and glosses drawn from our text. Where NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a diverges from our work, in fact, is primarily in offering a more fluid sort of discourse that is even less dependent on the specific proof texts that he invokes for its structure. In other words, [[P1384]] he is much better at organizing his sources into a single cohesive argument that does not get derailed by long tangents.

As the Tristhalƒ´setu begins, for most of the opening chapter on ‚Äúthe praise of the purposes of men,‚Äù the points of commonality are not so evident except at a purely conceptual level.94 As the title announces, NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a‚Äôs focus here initially is theorizing how ritual serves human ends, what specific ends those might be (such as happiness and the avoiding of suffering) as well as how we might reconcile or prioritize soteriological concerns with our quotidian aims. Right away he is telling us that ritual is about people and not about the internal coherency of scholastic symbolic systems whose purpose is in actuality to support human aims. It is only as the chapter closes that NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a turns to the proof text from the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata with which Lak·π£mƒ´dhara and HemƒÅdri both began. It is only when we see how he glosses key terms in this passage that it becomes clear he is following HemƒÅdri:

MBH: Even by those who have just their own body [lit. with a single body] (ekƒÅtmabhi·∏•), are separated from their companions (avaganai·∏•) and who are devoid of assistance (asamhatai·∏•), the ancestors are made to cross over and the grandfathers [are extracted] from the hells.

HemƒÅdri: Where the texts says ‚Äúwho have just their own body‚Äù (ekƒÅtmabhi·∏•), [what is meant is] who is without a wife (patnƒ´rahitai·∏•). Where the text says, ‚Äúwho are not well equipped,‚Äù [what is meant is] those who lack a ·πögvedic and SƒÅmavedic priest and so forth.

NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a (trans. Salomon): Single men (ekƒÅtmabhi·∏•) means unmarried men (apatnƒ´kai·∏•). Unaccompanied men (asamhatai·∏•) means men without the company of ·πõtvijs and other priests (·πõtvigƒÅdisa·πÉghƒÅta≈õ≈´nyai·∏•).95

Just as we have seen in our other studies of formalized textual production, based on divergences in word choice and syntax, it is clear here that NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a is not merely [[P1385]] blindly copying glosses from the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, but is instead reworking the material again and again to fit his own idiom and style, a state of affairs which if anything suggests an even greater fundamental continuity at the points where the two texts overlap because we can tell it is deliberate and selective. For the next four chapters‚Äîthe general praise of tƒ´rthas (sƒÅmƒÅnyatƒ´rthapra≈õa·πÉsƒÅ), the nature of tƒ´rthas (tƒ´rthasvar≈´pa), and secondary tƒ´rthas (gau·πáatƒ´rthas), and those authorized to make pilgrimage (tƒ´rthayƒÅtrƒÅdhikƒÅri·πáa·∏•)‚Äîpoints of overlap, at least with the text we have been examining, are much more sparse. At each step along the way, NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a‚Äôs main objective seems to be a focused form of clarification and delimiting of the topics under discussion. Thus, for example, the reason we get a subsection on secondary tƒ´rthas is that one of his sources starts its discussion by listing actual places and then transitions into talking about such metaphorical ‚Äútƒ´rthas‚Äù as truthfulness and philanthropy, resulting in our author feeling the need to pause and sort out the distinction.96 In a major refinement of HemƒÅdri, though building on the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara‚Äôs own tendencies, such discussions are typically focused in a single portion of the text that forms a discrete unit from what logically came before it. But once we get past this new organizational apparatus and come to the points of topical overlap with the early sections of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, the parallels suddenly become overwhelming, far more than a case of a few stray shared glosses.

Fully half of the very short next chapter, ‚Äúthe causes of varying degrees of benefit from tƒ´rthas‚Äù is dedicated to exploring‚Äîagain, with exactly the same proof text and shared glosses‚Äîthe figure of the one who goes of tƒ´rtha on behalf of someone else‚Äîwhich NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a also understands as a pilgrim for hire. The section that immediately follows, ‚Äúvarying degrees of benefit caused by particular vehicles, ‚Äùas you might already anticipate from the title, [[P1386]] begins by offering, albeit with half a verse more of the proof text than we find in the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, just what will befall you if you set out for PrayƒÅga riding a bull.97 It then provides the same the rule‚Äîthat one should avoid vehicles‚Äîand the same modification of that rule cited by HemƒÅdri. The commentary focuses on the exact same terms of art and is again nearly verbatim and it builds to the overturning of the rule forbidding the use of vehicles, once again using the very same proof text and arguments we saw in HemƒÅdri.

Making an argument unknown to our Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, by quibbling over a point of grammar, NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a then responds to and refutes a new authority. At this point, in a more extended discussion than what we find in the earlier text, he resumes HemƒÅdri‚Äôs enumeration of the scales of the relative karmic merit yielded by using different vehicles. New topics then ensue, unknown to the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, such as what happens if you only use a vehicle part of the way, or how do ships figure into this calculus. In the next two chapters, these are followed by highly technical considerations of the scale of bodies of water and astrology, also not found in the older text. But again, once we are back on the familiar topical terrain of the way of going to tƒ´rthas (tƒ´rthagamana), it is HemƒÅdri‚Äôs text that sets the agenda.98 In other words, what we keep seeing again and again is evidence that HemƒÅdri‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, and not the works of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, provides the topical and conceptual basis for the conversations of the next several centuries.

Perhaps nowhere is this more evident than in the Tristhalƒ´setu‚Äôs ninth chapter. Here, NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a‚Äôs framework for the common vidhi for tƒ´rtha is unmistakably identical with the one that HemƒÅdri had ‚Äúderived‚Äù using Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ principles from a passage in the [[P1387]] BrahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça PurƒÅ·πáa. Indeed, the Tristhalƒ´setu not only quotes this same passage verbatim but it privileges and discounts the same elements selected by HemƒÅdri. Our ≈õƒÅstrin‚Äôs next major subject is also identical with what we find in the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, namely, a debate with an opponent over whether the common procedure applies only to people going to PrayƒÅga or is to be used during all pilgrimages.99 Though NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a assigns this objector‚Äôs view only to ‚Äúa certain someone,‚Äù the arguments being presented, such as the contestation over the role of shaving at the ≈õrƒÅddha and whether it is only to be done at GayƒÅ, are the very ones HemƒÅdri identified as belonging to Ga·πáe≈õvarami≈õra.100 NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa‚Äôs presentation, which simply singles out and then refutes Mi≈õra‚Äôs two objections, is both more terse and more clear. Along the way he even manages to integrate into the discussion some of the observations found earlier in HemƒÅdri about the role played by ghee as the mukhyadravya in the language of Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ and the difference between abhyudaya≈õrƒÅddha and pƒÅrva·πáa≈õrƒÅddha.101 In each of these discussions, things that in HemƒÅdri have to simply be inferred are stated outright. In HemƒÅdri, as we have seen, all of these arguments are building up to the introduction of the ekatra nƒ´r·πáita nyƒÅya, which teaches the functional interchangeability of sacred sites that have the same [[P1388]] specified fruit. But while this feature, retained by NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a, also serves as the culminating topic in this part of the Tristhalƒ´setu, an important divergence has emerged, as we can see when we compare HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own words with those of NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a. Parallels are marked in bold in the footnotes below.

NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a: Others, however say ‚ÄòSo be it; the journey is beneficial. But nevertheless, this same benefit which is proclaimed for a journey to GayƒÅ. ‚ÄòOne going to GayƒÅ gets at every step the benefit of an a≈õvamedha sacrifice.‚Äô—applies to all pilgrimages, according to the rule, ‚Äòthe meaning of a sacred text determined in one place (applies) elsewhere also.‚Äô

When the benefit of a pilgrimage is to be specified, it would be appropriate that this same benefit already mentioned be that benefit, as it is closely connected in that it is also a benefit of ‚Äògoing.‚Äô

Postulating the benefit to be heaven by invoking the rule of the Vi≈õvajit sacrifice would involve an excessive effort in bringing that rule to mind, because it is only remotely connected to this case. This too is sheer foolishness, for in various places the benefits of pilgrimages are mentioned.102

As we can see, NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a retains much of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs language but fundamentally misses the point of his argument. Perhaps this is because some of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs core premises, such as that every tƒ´rtha is worth going to and will yield some benefit, even if said benefits have not been carefully specified in a given proof text, have simply become common sense. As deployed by HemƒÅdri, the whole point of the ekatranƒ´r·πáita nyƒÅya is to arrive at the understanding that, in all cases where the benefits of different pilgrimages to various places are stated as having the same benefit, those places are functionally equivalent. Thus, in all such cases, one can use the same vidhƒÅna. From this perspective, the invocation of a so-called ‚ÄúGayƒÅ [[P1389]] principle‚Äù was never intended to promote the rather narrow idea that has been ascribed to the p≈´rvapak·π£in by NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a, namely, that for every pilgrimage where the result is not specified, ‚Äúevery step has the benefit of an a≈õvamedha sacrifice.‚Äù NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a, in contrast, is totally uninterested in justifying that pilgrimages to specific places have intrinsic merit and yield specific benefit. For him, that seems to be a fundamental feature of the discourse on pilgrimage. What he is invested in is developing a hitherto unknown calculus for determining as precisely as possible the comparative virtues of different modes of pilgrimage. Indeed, as he alludes to shortly in the above passage, as he understands it, interpretive tools such as the so-called ‚ÄúGayƒÅ principle‚Äù are problematic because they provide the pilgrim with loopholes that circumvent a now hegemonically established SmƒÅrta ritual world. After all, once you presume you only need one a≈õvamedha‚Äôs worth of merit, and you know you can get that already from taking a single step in a sacred space, why would bother spending the time and money taking part in the rest of the SmƒÅrta pilgrimage imaginary? In other words, NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa Bha·π≠·π≠a is defending a core part of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs siddhƒÅnta by using a bit of HemƒÅdri to refute another bit of a now problematically construed HemƒÅdri. One could hardly ask a greater confirmation of just how completely the words and ideas of the author of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái have saturated the discourse on pilgrimage among early modern SmƒÅrtas.

Exercises in Excising a ≈öƒÅkta Landscape: HemƒÅdri and the PrabhƒÅsak·π£etramƒÅhƒÅtmya

In its published form, much of which corresponds virtually verbatim with the citations found in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, the PrabhƒÅsak·π£etramƒÅhƒÅtmya makes up the concluding portion of the vulgate Skanda PurƒÅ·πáa. As its title conveys, the work is almost exclusively concerned with offering a guide for pilgrims to the history and ritual practices that lie behind the collection of sacred sites that surrounded the self-arising li·πÖga of SomanƒÅtha located in Saurashtra. The text [[P1390]] as it is transmitted within the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái is self-evidently a product of an era subsequent to the desecration by Mahmud of Ghazni of what was then at its core a wooden temple. Most plausibly, as it is entirely unknown to the early twelfth-century dharma literature, the mƒÅhƒÅtmya in its current form was composed in the middle of the twelfth century, sometime during the reign of the Sola·πÖkƒ´ CƒÅ·∏∑ukya ruler KumƒÅrapƒÅla (1143‚Äì1172 CE).103 Such a point of origin would coincide with the massive program of renovation and renewal that repopulated the landscape with stone structures, which is reflected in our text.104 In other words, the [[P1391]] ‚ÄúPurƒÅ·πáic lore‚Äù that the mid-thirteenth-century CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái has drawn upon as an authentic source of traditional knowledge was at the time itself in many cases probably less than a hundred years old.

While numerous textual connections exist between our text and the corpus associated with the Picumata and its successors, exploring these matters at any length would take us far afield from our purpose in the present chapter. For this reason, to substantiate the point at hand, [[P1392]] we will briefly turn to two such self-contained and hitherto undocumented instances. The first of these surfaces in the frame narrative that organizes the whole mƒÅhƒÅtmya, which introduces us to the rationale behind PrabhƒÅsak·π£etra. Most likely this is because the text as it comes down to us‚Äîquite close to what HemƒÅdri would have been using‚Äîis itself the product of a series of redactions, the dominant thread in which seeks to harmonize the materials it inherits with a more pointedly BrƒÅhma·πáa friendly ≈öaiva SiddhƒÅnta influenced theology and sensibility. In these more often polished portions of the origin story, the li·πÖga at Some≈õvara is connected with the brahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça, and the killing of BrƒÅhma·πáas on its grounds is treated as a terrible sin that nonetheless has preoccupied misguided pilgrims in previous ages. In another thread, however, Some≈õvara is unambiguously identified with Bhairava in the form of the fire that consumes the world at the end of time and the field he occupies is depicted as emanating yoginƒ´s and housing monuments to the PƒÅ≈õupatavrata and MahƒÅvrata practicing ≈öaiva sages of past ages who merged into the li·πÖga.105

It is to this earlier stratum that the following decidedly partial account, entitled by the editor of the translation of the vulgate ‚ÄúThe Garland of Skulls and Tattvas‚Äù most likely belongs. In relating yet again the glory of SomanƒÅtha, we have just been told that this li·πÖga was the source from which the Garu·∏ça and Bh≈´ta Tantras emanated and from which the khecarƒ´s first emerged.106 We are also informed that this is one of the main sites in the fields from which in each age the Siddhas have emerged to spread their doctrines. After reiterating the close association between the Some≈õvara li·πÖga and Bhairava in general, but especially the final Bhairava KƒÅlƒÅgnirudranƒÅtha who will consume this world when it ends, the goddess indulges in a seeming non-sequitur that points us in the direction of a much older register of religion. [[P1393]]

‚ÄúIf you are the great lord, the creator who is a treasure house without beginning or end, the cause of creator and dissolution, how is it that you [have] a garland of skulls?‚Äù Then, having laughed, the lord of the gods ≈öa·πÖkara spoke: ‚ÄúMy garland shines forth with crores of skulls beyond counting. It has been made by thousands of the heads of NƒÅrƒÅya·πáas and ten-thousands of BrahmƒÅs and thus it is without beginning or end. In every kalpa, another Vi·π£·πáu comes into being, and also another BrahmƒÅ. In every kalpa, I create Vi·π£·πáu and PrajƒÅpati. I alone am creator, O goddess. I am established in the field of PrabhƒÅsa at the root of the li·πÖga of kƒÅlƒÅgni, which is adorned with a garland of skulls.107

In the text as it comes down to us, the goddess next begins to praise the Lord with a stava. The lord is pleased, asks her is she wants a boon. In an obvious interpolation, the goddess uses this promise to abruptly shift the entire focus of the discussion entirely away from the li·πÖga and towards DvƒÅrakƒÅ, when she demands he recounts how and why did Lord Vi·π£·πáu, suddenly praised in exalted terms as the creator and annihilator of the universe and the source of the Vedas and the system of var·πáa, ‚Äúcast off his body at PrabhƒÅsa.‚Äù In yet further signs that the text here is a hodge-podge of influences, ≈öiva responds by largely ignoring her inquiry. ≈öiva corrects her attribution of higher order values to his lowly rival, and then launches into an extended delineation in sequence of the proximity to SomanƒÅtha of the sacred centers that make [[P1394]] up PrabhƒÅsak·π£etra. As we will see momentarily, this subject, which occupies the vast majority of the mƒÅhƒÅtmya, is precisely the body of materials that HemƒÅdri will draw upon extensively but selectively in the service of his own interpretive agenda.

Our second example, which is found much further into the text, occurs in the discussion of the main pƒ´·π≠ha associated with J√±ƒÅna≈õakti at PrabhƒÅsa,108 who in the current age is called AjƒÅ. In introducing this place, ≈öiva offhandedly remarks that this deity issued forth from his sixth mouth, which is perpetually worshipped by BrahmƒÅ.109 The astonished goddess then asks ƒ™≈õvara if there are any more heads he‚Äôs been hiding from her, and this is ≈öiva‚Äôs response:

O Goddess, this question asked by you is a good one. I shall describe to you what should be kept secret even from one‚Äôs own sons. It was originally arisen in an ƒÄgama that is not well known. Earlier, from the inception of the Universe, O goddess, I in fact had seven heads. Five are familiar, SadyojƒÅta and so forth. The sixth is remembered as AjƒÅ. The seventh was named Picu. Thus, I have seven faces. AjƒÅ was given to BrahmƒÅ, and the Picuvaktra to Vi·π£·πáu. In this way, O great goddess, now I have become five faced.110

As we can see, ≈öiva alludes to an even more complicated iteration of the Kaula cosmology presented by the ≈örƒ´ka·πá·π≠hƒ´yasa·πÉhitƒÅ. No sooner has this been introduced, directly credited to an obscure ƒÅgama (aprasiddhƒÅgamodita), than our text readily and somewhat nonsensically assigns such knowledge to the past. The Lord insists into his perplexed wife that after his crazy youth he gave away the other weird heads. Nowadays (adhunƒÅ), honey, he informs us, I have just got the five faces you learn about in school. This is but one of a myriad [[P1395]] of examples in which the PrabhƒÅsak·π£etramƒÅhƒÅtmya distances itself from the most ‚Äúdisreputable‚Äù aspects of its past.

The digression we have just been exploring immediately follows another story. In that account, we are instructed that in ancient times, at the ritual center associated with kriyƒÅ ≈õakti, a king incongruously named AjƒÅpƒÅla venerated Bhairavƒ´ to be rid of a debilitating illness. When the goddess appears before him, the king is rid of his ailment, which emerges from his body in the form of a herd of goats. The king dedicates himself to nurturing and protecting these animals until the end of his life, granting the center the name of AjƒÅpƒÅle≈õvarƒ´, the goddess who protects the goats. In other words, we are deep within in the sort of textual territory where the didactic programmatic approach of the PurƒÅ·πáas bumps up against hyper localized, likely vernacular, particularized traditions of storytelling, of the sort one finds among people like the Dhangar, where narratives do not always have a clear beginning, middle, and end. It is precisely at these seams in the text that we encounter, travelling in tandem in symbiotic relationship, the BhairavasiddhƒÅnta or VƒÅma traditions peeking out from behind a non- BrƒÅhma·πáa vernacular.

In the PrabhƒÅsak·π£etramƒÅhƒÅtmya itself, the persistence of this plurality of religious registers, where the rougher edges of the most transgressive forms of high ≈öƒÅkta and skull carrier traditions are smoothed over but not effaced entirely, becomes most apparent when the text interweaves among its catalogue of specific sacred spaces eccentric shaggy dog stories providing the background for individual tƒ´rthas, many of which conclude with extended discussions of specific practices. For the writers of the mƒÅhƒÅtmya, however, the incorporation of these materials seems to have amounted to a necessary evil, for much of the task that it undertakes is dedicated precisely to the relegation of these values to the status of cultural [[P1396]] curiosity in favor of mainstreaming a more ecumenical and public ≈öaivism. Part of the way this task is accomplished is through the integration of other mythological registers and resources. But, quite unlike what we will find in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs interpretive project, in the mƒÅhƒÅtmya, this task is hardly carried out in a systematically supercessionary fashion in the service of some simple domestication of the sacred landscape. Instead, what the mƒÅhƒÅtmya itself presents is a hodge-podge of eclectic influences, the most outlandish of which it seeks to temper. Somewhat counterintuitively, the best approach to arriving at a solid understanding of how HemƒÅdri programmatically intervenes into this landscape and reworks its imaginary is acquired by attending to an extended portion of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái where our ≈õrƒ´kara·πáe≈õvara dedicates a number of folios to citing from the mƒÅhƒÅtmya almost in sequence and virtually verbatim. In contrast to the in some ways more tantalizing sections of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça where HemƒÅdri draws upon PurƒÅ·πáic source materials that are quite distinct from our published texts or describes less familiar sacred sites of unknown provenance, our archive here enables us to differentiate between the interpretive work being enacted by the PurƒÅ·πáas themselves and conceptual project of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái. In short, it enables us to make solid judgements about what is new and different about our nibandha. As we shall see, there is a definite pattern in what our author elides and the rhetorical impact of these decisions. The section we will be briefly surveying (comprised of chapters 123‚Äì165 in the vulgate), deals mostly with minor sites on the outer orbit of the main li·πÖga. Over the course of this part of the mƒÅhƒÅtmya, forty-three sites are discussed, several of them in great detail.111 In [[P1397]] the vulgate, the chapters vary enormously in size, from three or four verses to a hundred verses in length. Almost every chapter commences with fixed formulas, most commonly ‚Äúthen, O great goddess, one should go to. . . (tato gacchen mahƒÅdevi)‚Äù followed by either the name of the site or the distance, measured in bow lengths from the previous site that has just been presented. In this same verse, we are then introduced to a dramatic persona, generally a Vedic sage, a supernatural creature, a king, or a god. We learn that this figure prayed or performed austerities at the site and then the rewards he or she received, and perhaps a short summary of site-specific practices, are then defined. For example, in the first stop on our itinerary,112 delineated in a mere four verses, we learn that at CitrƒÅ·πÖgadeve≈õvara, twenty bow lengths to the south-west of the previous site, a Gandharva named CitrƒÅ·πÖgada performed austerities before a li·πÖga. Those who follow in his footsteps will attain the world of the gandharvas. In [[P1398]] his Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, HemƒÅdri leaves virtually all of these postage stamp size itineraries virtually intact just as they are given. Such passages, from our authors perspective, in fact represent exempla for what a tƒ´rtha text should look and sound like, for as we shall see, HemƒÅdri takes the chapters in his sources that do not fit easily within this formula and heavily redacts them so that they assume a similar shape and texture.

HemƒÅdri in contrast discusses the following sites in sequence (bearing in mind the manuscript has been carelessly copied) under the following names, CitrƒÅ·πÖgade≈õvara, RƒÅvane≈õvara, Gaurƒ´saubhƒÅgyadƒÅyinƒ´, ≈öƒÅ·πá·∏çilye≈õvara, K·π£eme≈õvaram, Ak·π£amƒÅle≈õvara, PƒÅ≈õupate≈õvaram, Dhruve≈õvara, Vai·π£·πáavƒ´≈õakti, MahƒÅkƒÅlƒ´, Pu·π£karƒÅvarttaka, Gaurƒ´du·∏•khƒÅntakƒÅri·πáƒ´, CitrƒÅditye≈õvara, T·πõ·πáavidƒ´≈õvara, [manuscripts damage], Vighne≈õa, DharmarƒÅja, K≈´paku·πá·∏çale≈õvara, Bhairave≈õvara, NƒÅrade≈õvara, Hira·πáye≈õvara, Brahamku·πá·∏ça, Ratne≈õvara, Vainateya, SatyabhƒÅme≈õvara, Ratnaku·πá·∏çe≈õa, RƒÅjabha·π≠·π≠ƒÅraka Raivantaka, Vaivasvate≈õvara, Kuntƒ´≈õvara, MƒÅt·πõga·πáa- ekavallavƒ´rƒÅ.

Transcribed free from any corrections, here the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça reads: tato gacchen mahƒÅdevi li·πÖga·πÉ citrƒÅ·πÖgade≈õvaram / tasyaiva [bharai·π£yat] bhƒÅge dhanurvi·πÉ≈õƒÅtibhisthita·πÉ / citrƒÅ·πÖgadena deve≈õi gandharvapatinƒÅpriye k·π£etra·πÉ / pavitra·πÉ j√±ƒÅtvƒÅ vai li·πÖga·πÉ tatra prati·π£·π≠hitam k·πõtvƒÅ tapo mahƒÅghora·πÉ samƒÅrƒÅdhya mahe≈õvaram / atha yo bhƒÅvasa·πÉyuktas talli·πÖga·πÉ sa·πÉprap≈´jayet / gandharvalokam ƒÅpnoti gandharvair saha modate / caitra≈õuklacaturda≈õyƒÅ·πÉ sa·πÉsnƒÅpya vidhinƒÅ ≈õivam / p≈´jayed dvividhai·∏• pu·π£pai gandhadh≈´pair anukramƒÅt / sa·πÉprƒÅpnoty akhila·πÉ kƒÅmƒÅm manasƒÅ yady adƒ´k·π£ita·πÉ While some of these differences result from minor corruptions, two readings in the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·πá·∏ça, which offer a different date and specify a non-Tantric initiate are likely worth considering.

Thus, for example, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs fealty to the mƒÅhƒÅtmya‚Äôs treatment of CitrƒÅ·πÖgadeve≈õvara stands in considerable contrast to his vivisection of the chapter that immediately follows, which is dedicated to the tƒ´rtha center of RƒÅvane≈õvara.113 As its name already suggests, RƒÅva·πáe≈õvara [[P1399]] was remembered as a li·πÖga consecrated by the demon king RƒÅva·πáa. After the opening verse, the vulgate in this case breaks from the formula by telling an elaborate story. One day RƒÅva·πáa was out flying in his magical chariot when he encountered an invisible force field blocking his way. Astounded, RƒÅva·πáa descended from the heavens, where he encounters someone named Prahasta. This sage informs him about the greatness of Some≈õvara and that it cannot be passed over by anyone. Descending from his chariot, RƒÅva·πáa makes pilgrimage to Some≈õa and innocently sets out to worship the li·πÖga along with his RƒÅk·π£asa followers. The mere presence of all of these demons, however devoted they might have been, rather understandably scares away all the other pilgrims. As it so happens, a festival is approaching, when throngs of people are supposed to celebrate at SomanƒÅtha, but no one wants to come so long as the demons remain. To protect his other devotees, the voice of Some≈õa comes out of the li·πÖga. Thanking RƒÅva·πáa for his devotion, it suggests that he remove himself to a more obscure location for the festival season so that other bhaktas can come and pay the li·πÖga homage. RƒÅva·πáa agrees, retreats to a cave, and it is there that he installs the current li·πÖga that bears his name. At least, that is what happens in the root text. In HemƒÅdri‚Äôs rendition, in contrast, all but the first verse and last seven verses of this twenty-five-verse text have been excised. We lose the whole story and the whole discussion of appropriate practices. What we are left with, then, is only the introduction to the place, RƒÅva·πáa‚Äôs program of worship (which is not to be emulated), and its supposed benefits. In fact, we know for certain that HemƒÅdri‚Äôs source text looked quite bit like our vulgate, which is to say it travelled with the story, because his nibandha has incongruously retained a small portion of the text where the voice comes out of the li·πÖga. With the tale [[P1400]] excised, the li·πÖga, whose identity is nowhere made apparent, for no apparent reason speaks in the first person to RƒÅva·πáa and tells him to go to the cave and install another li·πÖga.

The text transcribed directly from the manuscript of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça reads as follows: tato gacchen mahƒÅdevi rƒÅva·πáe≈õvaram uttamam / tasmƒÅd dak·π£i·πáƒÅn·πõ·π£tyedhanu·π£ƒÅ·πÉ ·π£o·∏ça≈õisthitam // paulastyo rƒÅva·πáe devi rƒÅk·π£asas tu sadƒÅru·πáa·∏• / li·πÖga·πÉ ca sthƒÅpayƒÅmƒÅsa bhaktyƒÅ paramayƒÅ yuta·∏• // tatas tam niyato bh≈´tvƒÅ sarvais tairƒÅk·π£asaiar v·πõta·∏• / p≈´jayƒÅmƒÅsa deve≈õi upavƒÅsaparƒÅya·πáa·∏• // cakƒÅra puratas tasya gƒ´tavƒÅdyena jƒÅgaram / tato dvarƒÅtrasamaye vƒÅg uvƒÅca ≈õarƒ´ri·πáƒ´ // da≈õagrƒ´va mahƒÅbaho paritu·π£·π≠o smi te [nadya] / mama prabhƒÅtrailokye va≈õagante bhavi·π£yati // atra sannihito nitya·πÉ [sthƒÅsyamp] aham asa·πÉ≈õaya·∏• / yai·∏• caitap≈´jayi·π£yeti ≈õatr≈´·πáƒÅ·πÉ rƒÅk·π£ase≈õvara·∏• // yƒÅsyanti paramƒÅ·πÉ siddhi·πÉ matprasƒÅdƒÅd a≈õa·πÉ≈õaya·∏• / evam uktvƒÅ vararohe virarƒÅma v·πõ·π£abhadhvaja·∏• // rƒÅva·πáe ‚Äôpi sa sa·πÉtu·π£·π≠o bh≈´yo bh≈´yo mahe≈õvara·∏• / p≈´jayitvƒÅ tu talli·πÖga·πÉ samƒÅrudya tu pu·π£palaika·πÉ // trailokyavijayƒÅkƒÅ·πÖk·π£ƒ´ i·π£·π≠ade≈õa·πÉ jagƒÅmaha /
