O·πÉ. Today, to [fill in the blank gotra], and so forth, I present to you this black antelope skin, which is placed on top of a blanket that is placed on top of ku≈õa grass. I give you a heap of oil seeds, which are covered with a set of clothes. I give you a golden horn, a silver horse hoof. I give you a silver tooth, a string of pearls. A golden nƒÅbha adorned with five gems, accompanied by fragrance and flowers, along with a vessel made of brass, of whom ≈öiva is the deity. It is further endowed with a fourfold set of vessels, full of honey, yogurt, ghee, milk, which are situated in the four directions. I, having [fill in the blank desire], [declare]‚Äîit [this property] is not mine. Today, to the person [[P1444]] of [x gotra] and so forth, therefore, for the sake of establishing this gift of the black antelope skin, I bestow upon you this gold which is dak·π£inƒÅ. It is not mine.154

Much like the deeds from the Lekhapaddhati we had examined back in chapter two, on one level, what we have here is a concrete commemoration marking the transfer of property as well as resources along with explicit documentation of the surrender of rights of ownership and the exact moment and circumstances when those rights were conferred. In other words, the state is providing a common framework for regulating and documenting financial transactions, however paltry their contents might be. Such declarations also offer a public itemization of everything used in the rite. As we know from other sources, including inscriptions, temples and other institutions typically kept records of their own possessions as well as a catalog of the expected ritual expenditure for different festivals. At the hands of a newly interventionist state inserting itself with increased frequency into the oversight and management of land grants and institutions of all sorts, one might well imagine that such public proclamations offering periodic delineations of expenditures could also provide the transregional bureaucracy with information for auditing institutional budgets. For example, consistent discrepancies between the amount of resources deployed during ritual events and festivals and during the annual assessment might alert the authorities to indications of embezzlement and or the misrepresentation of assets during tax collection season. [[P1445]]

Recollecting our discussion in the introduction, we should remember that philanthropy (dƒÅna) is not merely a ritual but at once the organizing economic model of the day as well as an integral feature of the epistemic foundation of the medieval world. As YƒÅj√±avalkya has said, dƒÅna is the highest dharma, and as we have seen in our explorations of the undying land grants found throughout the region, philanthropic endowments and their periodic renewal formed the social and legal building blocks that made possible Tantric sociality. Moreover, it was this social institution of inaugurating and then reaffirming land grants that provided much of the framework that renewed the ties of these Tantric imaginaries to dynastic regional polities. In short, by mandating a prescriptive standardization and formalization of dƒÅna as rite as well as act of property transference, HemƒÅdri, as the de facto head of an empire, is intervening in the workings of the region‚Äôs economy, changing the rules governing who can and cannot participate in the social and economic order of the day.

At the same time that this new formulation renders the economic aspects of philanthropy transparent to outside observers, the same textual strategies make the exact parameters of the ritual accessible to the general public assembled in situ as well as to outside authorities. As the script makes clear, the ritual actor now effectively has to provide a running narration that accompanies each and every ritual act or manipulation of ritual materials. Thus, when he is transferring the deer skin blanket to its new owner, the donor not only declares to all the article in question, but makes explicit its physical position‚Äîplaced on top of a blanket that in turn is resting on ku≈õa grass‚Äîvis-√Ý-vis all the other items on the ritual grounds. Where the rite specifies the use of vessels of various kinds made of distinct materials and containing specific concoctions‚Äîthe sort of thing that observers of the ritual would be unlikely to directly perceive themselves‚Äî every dimension of the arrangement is publicly declaimed in a manner that effectively renders the clandestine interpolation of the types of transgressive ≈öƒÅkta rites that had previously resided at the heart of the means and methods of production all but [[P1446]] impossible. At the same time that pains are being taken to exclude such theologies as well as their promulgators, through the somewhat awkward integration of scholastic shorthand derived from Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ‚Äîsuch as the identification we see in this passage of the vessel being used in this rite as ‚Äúhaving ≈öiva for its deity (devatƒÅ)‚Äù‚ÄîHemƒÅdri is crafting a formalized ritual repertoire abounding in rhetorical features that systematically align a new approach to ritual with a new mode of ritual analysis, both of which can only be accessed through the study and mastery of his CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái.

That the values that animate these new techniques undermine the ≈öƒÅkta-inflected imaginary of CƒÅ·∏∑ukya Deccan in general, but even more specifically are effectively antithetical to the peculiarly Maharashtrian ≈öƒÅkta cosmopolitanism and emergent public culture so central to the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava court well into the thirteenth century, is hardly an accident. Indeed, though such an interpretation is uncharitable as it does not take seriously HemƒÅdri‚Äôs seemingly sincere commitment to the decidedly unorthodox theology we will encounter in detail in the next chapter, one is tempted at times to see HemƒÅdri as having reverse engineered his system with the deliberate intent of rendering the old paradigm outmoded and no longer functional. Perhaps most importantly for our purposes, keeping in mind this sociological backdrop helps makes explicable one of our text‚Äôs most significant, surprising, and unheralded innovations: HemƒÅdri‚Äôs reappraisal of the scope of BrƒÅhma·πáical philanthropy. As we shall see, breaking from a consensus as old as the Guptas, and by this time deeply entrenched in the commentarial tradition on the NƒÅrada and the YƒÅj√±avalkyasm·πõti, our author offers a vision of DƒÅna that explicitly inhibits royal and individual patronage and financial support for non-‚ÄúHindu‚Äù traditions and non-BrƒÅhma·πáa collectivities. [[P1447]]

Making sense of such developments demands a closer engagement with the framing of chapters 2 and 3 of the DƒÅnakha·πá·∏ça itself. As we have already seen in regard to the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, and as both Aiyangar and Kane have noted in passing, HemƒÅdri typically constructs the beginning chapters of each part of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái around a sizeable core of proof texts appropriated from the twelfth-century K·πõtyakalpataru of Lak·π£mƒ´dhara. In the case of the DƒÅnakha·πá·∏ça, HemƒÅdri even begins his own glosses by paraphrasing his predecessor, a state of affairs that has led the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, when it has been noticed at all by scholars, to be characterized as a derivative and unnecessary expansion upon an elegant original. What such a glib representation fails to recognize however, is that our author habitually deploys this inherited material in the service of new and radically distinctive conceptual ends. Sometimes, as we will see in a moment all it takes to instigate such a rectification of values is changing a single word. Just what is at stake and how our author engineers this shift will become clear to us when we place the largely parallel passages from the K·πõtyakalpataru and the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái side by side:

K·πõtyakalpataru:

Devala: And now, I will explain the rules of gifting. The bestowing of wealth upon a prescribed recipient with a spirit of generosity (≈õrƒÅddha) is designated as ‚Äúgifting.‚Äù An analysis of that will now be stated:

L: ‚ÄúPrescribed‚Äù means ‚Äútaught in the scriptures.‚Äù

D: Gifting is said to have two causes, six bases, six components, six effects, four kinds, three types, and three means of destruction.

L: [Causes of Gifting:] Whether small or large, the size of a gift does not bring about its benefits, but rather the spirit of generosity and capability associated with a gift‚Äîindeed, only these two things cause prosperity or ruin.155

[[P1448]]

CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái:

Now Devala [says]: What is called philanthropy is the bestowing of wealth, accompanied by ≈õraddhƒÅ, to the prescribed recipient.

H: The stated one means the one expounded in the ≈õƒÅstras. Namely, it is the bestowing of money unto a recipient‚Äîin other words, it is the relinquishing that culminates in the removal of one‚Äôs own ownership.

D: It is endowed with two causes, six bases, six components, and six effects. Philanthropy has four types, it is three-fold, and has three means of destruction.

H: By him himself was stated the elaboration on this: Whether small or large, the size of a gift does not bring about its benefits:

H: ≈öraddhƒÅ and bhakti are known to be the two producers of the flourishing or destruction of philanthropic activity. ≈öraddhƒÅ is the mindset of believers. Bhakti is meditation prefaced by emotional attachment.156

Unmistakably, both of our authors introduce the topic of dƒÅna with same quote from the Devalasm·πõti: ‚Äúphilanthropy is the bestowing of wealth accompanied by ≈õraddhƒÅ to the designated recipient.‚Äù That what we are looking at is direct textual dependence as opposed to a simple sharing of some canonical sources becomes inarguable when we attend to such details as the fact that both authors then open their commentary on this verse with an almost identical, and by no means self-evident, gloss on the past passive participle udita.157 In Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, [[P1449]] said gloss is the entire extent of his commentary on this portion of the text; he continues on to cite at length a long explication of the different factors that speciate philanthropy, presenting without interruption some of the very same quotes HemƒÅdri will later interweave into his work.

HemƒÅdri, in contrast, though addressing himself to the very same text, eschewing the privileging of textual exegesis as an end in itself, with pedagogy in mind breaks the materials up into digestible chunks and clearly states the purpose and nature of the philanthropic act. Since he is not a prisoner to the external demands of an exegete who is impelled to explain every unclear feature of the root text however tangential, he rapidly rattles off the traditional taxonomy of explicating dƒÅna in terms of two causes, six domains, and six ripenings‚Äîall offered with further explanation‚Äîbefore getting to his ‚Äúreal point,‚Äù which is that the success or failure of philanthropic activity is essentially determined by the giver having ≈õraddhƒÅ and bhakti.

Bhakti, you may notice, is actually absent from the common recensions of our proof text. Undeterred, HemƒÅdri simply adds it in, reducing the more common reading that we find in Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, ≈õakti, in the secular sense of capability, to a mere afterthought.158 Thus, with all the pieces now in place, our author has the materials on hand to harmonize his thinking in the realm of law with his wider theological program. In his superb dissertation, David Brick has noticed this peculiarity and proposed that HemƒÅdri‚Äôs decidedly strange gloss of ≈õraddhƒÅ as ƒÅstikyabuddhi·∏• intends to affirm that the giver must believe in the efficacy of the philanthropic process itself.159 As we will see momentarily, however, reading deeper into HemƒÅdri‚Äôs corpus [[P1450]] provides us with a simpler answer. Here, for example, is how our author addresses a similar conceptual concern in the opening section of his Kaivalyadƒ´pikƒÅ, the oldest substantial commentary on the BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa, to which we will turn at length in the last chapter:

The purpose is obtaining kaivalya. The subject matter is bhakti yoga. The qualification of the eligible person is purity (sattva) qualified by lack of self-interest; [adhikƒÅra is] not [dependent on] caste, stage of life, and so forth. Envy is not enduring the greatness of others. Purity is being a believer (ƒÅstikatva).160

Succinctly, for HemƒÅdri, notions of ‚Äúfaith,‚Äù eligibility for participation in religious life, and ontic purity of being are all but inseparable. If such purity is in part a matter of comporting oneself in accordance with what would later be called Vai·π£·πáava dietary and social habits, at the same time it demands a sort of ‚Äúmental hygiene,‚Äù what our author labels having the mindset of ‚Äúbeing a believer,‚Äù as opposed to a nƒÅstika. When we turn back to the DƒÅnakha·πá·∏ça, we find unambiguous confirmation for this interpretation of the term in the following chapter in its discussion of unsuitable recipients. There we are told, ‚ÄúA knower of dharma should not offer [philanthropy], even once, to an unbeliever (nƒÅstika) or a logician, or to any of the heretics (pƒÅ·π£a·πá·∏ça), or to someone who does not know the Vedas.‚Äù161 HemƒÅdri‚Äôs gloss on these passages, which tells us that a nƒÅstika is one who is empty of the traces of the other or supreme world and that a logician is one who, on the basis of argumentation, challenges the existence of such a state, make explicit that his rejection of such social actors is fundamentally a matter of beliefs and values. Succinctly, though such notions are entirely absent from his sources, our author is imposing an ideological litmus test over what constitutes the boundaries of acceptable religion at the very same moment that the very same conceptual resources are being deployed in the service of excluding those social agents and religious modalities that fall outside the purview of his worldview from having any access to substantive social capital, resources, or disciplinary power. [[P1451]]

As once again the raw materials on top of which HemƒÅdri installs his interpretive project are inherited from Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, it is worth looking at how the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái diverges from its sources. As Brick has shown us, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara dedicates most of the third chapter of the K·πõtyakalpataru to compiling what are mostly pretty prosaic praises of BrƒÅhma·πáas, speciating them at great length in terms of their individual attainments and forms of merit. While Lak·π£mƒ´dhara is quite clear in communicating that as a class, BrƒÅhma·πáas are the best recipients (satpƒÅtra) for dƒÅna, from his perspective, such statements are by no means intended to exclude non-BrƒÅhma·πáas from being fit vessels for philanthropy. It is perfectly acceptable to offer most kinds of things to ≈ö≈´dras and other such folks, though you do not yield quite as much merit from doing so. In this spirit, he liberally cites a range of proof texts in favor of this position, a representative sampling of which we will quote here:

Yama states: A gift to a non-Brahmin yields an equal reward; a gift to one who is a Brahmin in name only yields twice that; a gift to one who is learned yields one-hundred-thousand-times that; and a gift to one who has mastered the Vedas is infinite. . . .

B·πõhaspati (14.10, 11‚Äì12) states: According to tradition, a gift to a ≈ö≈´dra yields an equal reward and a gift to a Vai≈õya twice that. A gift to a K·π£atriya, they say, yields three- times the reward, while tradition holds that a gift to a Brahmin becomes six-fold. . . .

Yama says: It is said that a gift to a ≈ö≈´dra yields an equal reward and a gift to a Vai≈õya three-times that. They say a gift to a K·π£atriya yields six-times the reward, while tradition holds that a gift to a Brahmin becomes ten-fold.

Dak·π£a states: A man should give to any person who seeks him out and begs for the sake of a calamity, a disaster, or a debt or for the sake of his family‚Äîthis is the prescribed rule for all gifts. . . . Whatever is given to a parent, elder, friend, gentleman, benefactor, indigent, pauper, or distinguished individual is fruitful. Indeed, one who desires prosperity should give gifts to indigent, poor, and distinguished individuals, for those who do not give gifts to such people are reborn as those who must live off others‚Äô good fortune.162

[[P1452]]

Though indeed privileging BrƒÅhma·πáas, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara and his sources thus embrace a sort of catholic vision of philanthropy where, at least in regard to most types of philanthropic rites, being a ‚Äúproper vessel‚Äù is not determined by and large by one‚Äôs caste, religious commitments, or ideological positions. The only types of people whom he specifically identifies as not serving as fit vessels for philanthropy, quite pointedly, are thieves, slaves, quack doctors, gamblers, frauds, as well as BrƒÅhma·πáas who teach ≈ö≈´dras the scriptures for a living (but not ≈ö≈´dras themselves) or anyone who habitually displays a lack of integrity. In other words, he is making a qualitative and not a normative judgement. Indeed, while he shows little interest in scrutinizing the claims of ≈ö≈´dras, who are after all both fit vessels for dƒÅna as well as appropriate donors, provided they do not use Vedic mantras in their rituals, and even less concerned with the lower ranks of twice-borns, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara is quite invested in exploring the intricacies of the intra-BrƒÅhma·πáa pecking order. Towards this end, he directs the reader‚Äôs attention to a curious passage, ascribed to the ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapasm·πõti,163 that identifies six-classes of ‚Äúso-called BrƒÅhma·πáas‚Äù: 1) the BrƒÅhma·πáa who takes his wages from the king, 2) the one who is a professional trader, 3) the one who performs yaj√±as on behalf of many (in other words, who sells his Vedic rites), 4) the one who sacrifices on behalf of a village, 5) the one on the [[P1453]] payroll of a village or the payroll of a city, 6) the twice-born who does not observe the sandhyƒÅ rites at the three junctures of the day. All of these, it is said, should be regarded as the equivalent of ≈ö≈´dras, who, once again, are still fit if not ideal vessels for receiving philanthropy. Without more context it is difficult to determine for certain what this passage originally was actually about, but one plausible reading is that represents the continuation of an ancient inter-Vedic quarrel expressing the hostility and anxiety of Yajur Vedic communities who champion a ≈öratua sacrifice oriented a model of the ‚Äútriple Veda‚Äù over and against the largely Atharva Vedic advocacy for the purohita as Vedic ritualist par excellence. Such a reading explains why most of the so-called BrƒÅhma·πáas identified in our passage are deemed suspect simply for performing large scale rituals on behalf of royal authorities or civic formations.

Regardless of such niceties, for Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, the cited passage offers a transition into consideration of non-BrƒÅhma·πáas as suitable vessels for philanthropy, albeit on a graded scale. The passage‚Äôs use of the term ‚Äúnon-BrƒÅhma·πáa‚Äù as a term of art specifically referring to the six- fold classes of so-called BrƒÅhma·πáas also enables Lak·π£mƒ´dhara to reconcile some contradictions in the calculus of force multiplying the merit derived from donations based on the statuses of the recipient. The problem runs as follows. Three key Dharma≈õƒÅstra proof texts say that by giving to a non-BrƒÅhma·πáa, one achieves merit equal to the base line amount of pu·πáya stipulated by a scripture. So, if, for example, a specific philanthropic act is said to provide the donor with the merit from one hundred horse sacrifices, giving to a non-BrƒÅhma·πáa would provide one hundred horse sacrifices worth of merit. The same text then goes onto to assert that, in cases where giving to a BrƒÅhma·πáa is not stipulated by the text as a component of the ritual, one gets double that merit‚Äîtwo hundred horse sacrifices worth of pu·πáya instead the measly one hundred‚Äîfor giving to a BrƒÅhma·πáa. So far, so good. Where the trouble arises is that a greater [[P1454]] variety of sources offer a more classic pattern of caste-specific escalation that substantively diverges from this norm. In these texts, where the donor type is not specified, the philanthropy to ≈ö≈´dras produces merit equal to whatever is stipulated in the base form of a rite, philanthropy to Vai≈õyas doubles that merit, and giving to K·π£atriyas triples it. If the recipient is a BrƒÅhma·πáa, these sources say, you get six times the merit for your buck. The contradiction here is quite obvious‚Äînamely, does giving to a BrƒÅhma·πáa merely double your merit or does it multiply it six-fold? Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs solution to this not terribly pressing problem is to stipulate that where the first text is contrasting the relative merits of giving to a non-BrƒÅhma·πáa versus a BrƒÅhma·πáa and asserting that you only get double the merit, the text is not speaking of any non-BrƒÅhma·πáa person, but exclusively the six-classes of ‚Äúso-called BrƒÅhma·πáas‚Äù identified by ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapa. In other words, rather than having to pick between these two seemingly conflicting standards for calculating karmic merit, he sets out to prove that there is no contradiction at all.164

HemƒÅdri, as we should by now anticipate, takes this same set of proof texts, augmented with a few more of his own, and deploys the same rhetorical trick, taking the term ‚Äúso-called BrƒÅhma·πáa‚Äù out of ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapa and equating it with the other references to the ‚Äúnon-BrƒÅhma·πáa‚Äù found in his other sources.165 Departing markedly from Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, however, he equates the concept of the ‚Äúnon-BrƒÅhma·πáa‚Äù (abrƒÅhma·πáa), which is to be systematically equated with ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapa‚Äôs ‚Äúso-called BrƒÅhma·πáas‚Äù wherever that lexeme appears, not merely as linked to one discrete lexical item but as functioning as a conceptual unit with a plurality of synonyms. In fact, what HemƒÅdri seems to be asserting is that, since as he will establish, for most acts of [[P1455]] philanthropy only BrƒÅhma·πáas are the appropriate vessels, wherever in the proof texts a ‚Äúnon- BrƒÅhma·πáa‚Äù of any sort is treated as a fit vessel for philanthropy‚Äîeven when the referent used is one of the other var·πáas or jƒÅtis‚Äîwe need to understand the passage as solely referring to ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapa‚Äôs six-fold canon of ‚Äúso-called BrƒÅhma·πáas.‚Äô In other words, when a passage says that ‚Äú≈ö≈´dras get the same merit and a Vai≈õya twice that, but a gift to a K·π£atriya. . . yields three times the reward,‚Äù the words ≈ö≈´dra, Vai≈õya, and K·π£atriya are all being used in a metaphorical sense. The actual topic under discussion is the relative virtues of donating to the different types of ‚Äúso-called‚Äù BrƒÅhma·πáas. It is these strategies that rather counterintuitively enable HemƒÅdri to frame analysis of a compilation of passages whose plain sense conveys the message that ≈ö≈´dras and the other var·πáas are appropriate recipients for philanthropy in a manner that aims at radically circumscribing the participation of non-BrƒÅhma·πáas in the real economy of medieval India.

In these passages, when it comes to the non-vessels who are claiming BrƒÅhma·πáa status for non-BrƒÅhma·πáas, the designation of ‚Äúbeing a vessel‚Äù has as its subject forms of dƒÅna that are distinct from the dƒÅna of giving cows accompanied by mantras and so forth: ‚ÄúWhen dƒÅna is given to a ‚Äònon-vessel‚Äô preceded by [Vedic] mantras, then having, first severed the hand of the donor, he should cut off the tongue of the recipient‚Äù. . . . Because there is a prohibition on the conferring of the designation ‚Äúvessel‚Äù in cases where cows and so forth are given preceded by the mantras, the representation of ‚Äúbeing fit vessels‚Äù when it is applied to ≈ö≈´dras and the like has as its scope of applicability [solely] philanthropy that entails the giving of food.166

[[P1456]]

It is important we begin to recognize that the framework that HemƒÅdri is proposing here is no mere abstract aspirational scholastic exercise in proposing preferences but, as we shall soon see, is actively enforced by disciplinary power that has the backing of the state. This model effectively curtails ≈ö≈´dras and other non-BrƒÅhma·πáa agents from participating in the most expedient means from accumulating non-perishable property, just as further stipulations, which we will not be examining in detail, all but prevent most of these same social agents from functioning as the primary class of philanthropic donors. As we have seen time and again throughout this study, non-BrƒÅhma·πáa social formations under the guise of KƒÅlamukhas and MahƒÅpƒÅ≈õupatas of various sorts, as well, of course, as Jains, were the dominant property- owning communities as well key makers of institutions, institutional cultures, and infrastructure throughout the medieval Deccan. Non-BrƒÅhma·πáas were also largely responsible for funding these institutions and generating the lion‚Äôs share of religious merit. Thus, what HemƒÅdri is proposing amounts to a radical redistribution of privilege, wealth, and resources that effectively dismantles the economies of his day.

Now at first glance, one might be inclined to assume that what HemƒÅdri is asserting here is nothing more than a reversion to a hardline affirmation of intrinsic BrƒÅhma·πáical privilege. Something decidedly more interesting is going on, however, for HemƒÅdri‚Äôs characterization of the ‚Äúproper recipient,‚Äù the BrƒÅhma·πáa, takes a rather unexpected form, offering a denaturalization of BrƒÅhma·πáical privilege and that the very same moment that it is transferring disciplinary power and agency to a very particular type of BrƒÅhma·πáa. Thus, just after offering the usual platitudes‚Äîthere is no god equal to a BrƒÅhma·πáa, there is no guru equal to a BrƒÅhma·πáa, there is no injunction more authoritative than a BrƒÅhma·πáa‚Äîour author starts invoking sources that drastically restrict the very nature of what ‚Äúbeing BrƒÅhma·πáa‚Äù means:

‚ÄúNeither caste (jƒÅti), nor family (kula), nor the study of scripture, nor the ≈õruti are the causes of ‚ÄúBeing BrƒÅhma·πáa,‚Äù O king. Conduct alone (v·πõttam eva) is the cause of ‚ÄúBeing BrƒÅhma·πáa.‚Äù What is the benefit of lineage to a corrupt soul devoid of conduct? Do worms not arise in flowers that smell good? O king, there is not a single textual reading that is to be taken up exclusively! O dear one, seek out conduct‚Äî[after all,] even among the rƒÅk·π£asas there is great erudition! [[P1457]]

What is the use of a lot of rehearsal as if one were a bad-minded actor? It [the Veda] is studied or heard by [any] one who practices the ritual [regardless of their character]. Just as, if one tastes milk or water that is inside a skull bowl, it is degraded on account of the fault [deriving] from [the] place [that it occupies] (sthƒÅnado·π£ena), it is the same for scripture that is devoid of conduct (v·πõttahƒ´na).

Therefore, O king, know conduct to be the defining characteristic of a BrƒÅhma·πáa. One who knows the four Vedas but has bad conduct (durv·πõtta) is remembered to be lower than a ≈ö≈´dra. Truth, restraint, austerity, philanthropy, non-violence, restraining the senses, in whom these are seen, O Indra among kings‚Äîhe is remembered to be a BrƒÅhma·πáa.‚Äù167

Throughout this study, we have seen that across a whole host of social positions and levels of articulation, for at least half a millennium the dominant paradigm in operation throughout the medieval Deccan had embraced an episteme organized around context- and site-specific forms of knowledge and praxis. On a juridical level, these commitments had been translated into something akin to what the Anglo-American legalist tradition would be inclined to represent as a positivist vision of law, where what constitutes law is largely determined by the social facts on the ground as practiced by actual communities‚Äîand a pluralist vision of the nature of authority. When framed in terms of the philosophy of ethics, the sort of stances we keep encountering bear a family resemblance to what the philosopher and intellectual historian Isaiah Berlin has famously referred to as ‚Äúvalue pluralism: ‚Äúthe conception that there are many different ends that men may seek and still be fully rational (for rational we might read logically internally coherent), fully men, capable of understanding each other and sympathizing and [[P1458]] deriving light from each other.‚Äù168 Such pluralism denies there is any single all pervasive source of reasons either motivating or vitiating action. In doing so, it also necessarily rejects the existence of a single dominant value and standard for evaluation. Much like the evidentiary model we have seen at work in the evaluation of the claims staked by individual sam≈´has within the Indic legal system, these positivist visions of law assume that sources of value and internal standards for valuation are not absolute and universally true, but emerge within specific social and institutional bodies at documentable moments in sacred or secular time and that they are grounded in reasons that are best understood by people from within that community who have objectively mastered its canons of value. It is this confluence of ideals that renders internally coherent conceptual frameworks such as B·πõhaspati‚Äôs seminal invocation of a de≈õƒÅcƒÅra where, ‚ÄúIn the east, [people] are fish eaters and women are devoted to infidelity. In the north, the women drink liquor and they are to be touched by men while menstruating. . . . according to this conduct, these things are not demanding of punishment or expiation.‚Äù

Within the tradition of Western political legal history, legal positivism and its various medieval antecedents find their counterpart in the principle of lex naturalis‚Äînatural law, a conceptual framework that views that legality is not a matter of observing and the enforcing the actual codes of conduct practiced by communities, but in making normative judgements based on abstract standards. As the humanist political philosopher John Locke wrote in 1620, effectively distilling several thousand years of Western discourse on lex naturalis: [[P1459]]

Natural law is a fixed and permanent rule of morals which reason itself pronounces and which persists. . . . firmly rooted in the soil of human nature. Hence human nature must needs be changed before this law can either be altered or annulled. . . . Since all men are by nature rational, and since there is harmony between this law and rational nature, all men in the world are morally bound by this law. . . . In fact it seems to me to follow that. . . if he is a man he is bound. . . to fulfill the things appropriate to rational nature in the same way that it follows. . . that a triangle. . . [must have] three angles equal to two right angles. . . . Furthermore, this natural duty will never be abolished; for human beings cannot alter this law because they are subject to it, and it is not the business of subjects to abrogate laws to their liking. . . . For, since God has made man such that these duties of his necessarily follow from his very nature, He surely would not alter what has been made and create a new race of men, who would have another law and moral rule.169

Just as it did for many of his intellectual predecessors, for Locke, that idea that ‚Äúthere is a fixed and permanent rule of morals,‚Äù ‚Äúfirmly rooted in the soil of human nature‚Äù by which human beings are obligated to abide forms an integral component of his theory of legitimate authority. As we see in this passage, the existence of a universal and rational canon of values that orders our existence makes it incumbent upon each and every person that they have a ‚Äúnatural duty‚Äù to submit to the sovereign authority that defends and enforces these intrinsic norms. Just as the permanent rule of morals in Locke‚Äôs representation is singular and unchanging, so too is sovereign authority, whether we are talking about God in his heaven or the king and his state upon the earth, unitary and without any ‚Äúother.‚Äù [[P1460]]

Succinctly, what HemƒÅdri has done is to reintroduce a normative theory of the nature of law into the world of Dharma≈õƒÅstra. But unlike his predecessors, he grounds this prescriptive vision not in the self-justifying authority of the Veda, but in a universal theory of ethics that makes moral comportment‚Äîgood conduct‚Äîthe determining factor in deciding whether any discourse, agent, or human activity has intrinsic value and should be treated as authoritative. As we will see at some length in chapter 11, as befits a theory of natural law, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs discourse on ethical comportment is virtually inseparable from his ontology of purity (sattva) so as to render ‚Äúbeing ethical or good‚Äù effectively the same thing as being legitimate or even [[P1461]] real. What this essentially means is that any divergence from what are now being proposed as all-encompassing ethical norms, what the positivist tradition had inclined to see as ‚Äúsite- or context-specific‚Äù emendations to the general rules‚Äîlike when a Bengali is permitted his quaint customs of open adultery‚Äîis to be seen not as a difference that the authorities one must respect and protect, but as an abrogation of intrinsic duty nullifying an agent‚Äôs rights, authority, and privilege, regardless of his reputation or cultural patrimony, Vedic or otherwise.

Apart from its vivid imagery and intrinsic interest, part of what is so astonishing about the otherwise unattested passage we have just been exploring is that the conduct (v·πõtta) it has presented as the defining feature that makes someone a BrƒÅhma·πáa has strong parallels with the ethicalized reimagining of dharma as a universal discourse‚Äîequally applicable to all four castes‚Äîfor which, as we shall see when we turn to the next chapter, our author has so elegantly and forcefully advocated in the opening section of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái. In fact, the two lists diverge only in that the universal dharma expected of everyone actually entails a greater more demanding range of virtues than are ascribed here to BrƒÅhma·πáa-hood. What is being proposed here then, is something like a republic of virtue, in which the universalizing standards of a universal sƒÅmƒÅnya dharma overturns all particularized domain-specific logics, including the various community-specific samayas and the pluralistic theory of property rights and sovereignty that made them possible, that animated an older CƒÅ·∏∑ukya inflected world order. Thus, at the very same time that HemƒÅdri sets out to drastically curtail the ability of ≈ö≈´dras and other such folk to occupy a pivotal role in networks of circulation and exchange, he is also establishing an ideological litmus test for what constitutes a legitimate BrƒÅhma·πáa whose authority should be respected. Unstained by any sustained contact with systems of knowledge that, by advocating untoward conduct degrade the intellectual and moral integrity of those who [[P1462]] participate in them, the BrƒÅhma·πáas in question have found a new source of guidance that helps them to lead upright moral and ritual lives. The source of guidance in question, of course, is none other than the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái itself, which (especially in its original extended form) provides to its imagined reader a wealth of ready to use templates and resources that streamlines the degree of skill and knowledge necessary to execute or oversee a wide repertoire of socially and economically significant rituals. In essence, it offers itself as a guide for professionalizing BrƒÅhma·πáas, on the spot, to assume the role of a new kind of SmƒÅrta dharma authority, ready to assume a host of functions‚Äîfor which there were minimal precedents in the world of Dharma≈õƒÅstra‚Äîpreviously occupied by the very demographics they have displaced with assurance that they will have the complete backing of the Seu·πáa state, morally, politically, and economically.

As any vulture capitalist can testify, displacing entire classes of people from the key positions that they have traditionally held within the networks of circulation governing the transfer and movement of resources, ideas, people, and money, and dismantling long standing institutions liquifies previously frozen forms of capital, enabling its transfer to new communities and sites of accumulation. As we saw in the last chapter, well into the thirteenth century, far from being dominated by a BrƒÅhma·πáical ecumene, the Seu·πáa domains in Maharashtra as well as the borderlands comprising what are now parts of Karnataka and Telangana were a world where ‚Äúpakka‚Äù BrƒÅhma·πáas had to carve out an often relatively humble place for themselves in the shadow of KƒÅlamukhas and other ≈öƒÅktas who were intimately linked with royal and military power centers. To be BrƒÅhma·πáa in this world‚Äîas opposed to being an initiated vƒ´ra‚Äîgranted a person access to no private clubs and held no promise of privilege or substantial financial compensation for one‚Äôs activities. As this chapter has begun [[P1463]] to make clear, in the middle of the thirteenth century, emerging from the heart of the Seu·πáa court and the pen of a man who was often the kingdom‚Äôs de facto ruler, we see a radical reorientation of values and priorities that seeks to reestablish SmƒÅrta BrƒÅhma·πáas as disciplinary agents, as authorities, and as the pillars of their communities at the expense of other demographics. A key part of what makes this utterly ambitious and socially disruptive interpretive project feasible in practical terms is that it enables a radical redistribution of resources, out of the hands of institutions and agents embedded within older ≈öƒÅkta-inflected ecumenes and into the hands of the expanding ranks of upwardly mobile ‚Äúprofessionalized‚Äù BrƒÅhma·πáas. That this sea change in values coincides with a staggering redistribution of real- world resources is made evident in many of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own verses, which celebrate his efforts as acting as a veritable mountain of gold to the SmƒÅrta community. Let us look at just two examples.

By him, every day, 36,000 BrƒÅhma·πáas, learned in the Veda and its a·πÖgas, were fed with rice, [rice which served] as the abode [housing] different types [of food,] stuff to be licked, sucked and savored, accompanied by a broth and endowed with relish and sweets, all of which are placed in an array according to the propensities of their various qualities. And he led them in an instant to supreme joy with a whole lot of gold strewn all about.170

By him, money was given beyond counting‚Äî[so much money] such that it made the courtyards belonging to whole clans of BrƒÅhma·πáas glimmer from the wonderous rays [from the gold] that he had redirected away from taking entry into the treasury of the King. Praiseworthy for his lovely deeds, his good conduct and discrimination, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs glory is made even greater by the thickness of his love for the beloved of Nanda (K·πõ·π£·πáa), who is pleased by his hosts of intense vows.171

[[P1464]]

In order to be viable, social revolutions have to enlist a demographically sizeable social base comprised of a class of people who are dissatisfied with their place in the current social hierarchy and possess socially significant skills. To the BrƒÅhma·πáas of Maharashtra who were not revered on the basis of their initiations into ever more rarefied bands esoterica within the MantramƒÅrga, used to be being also-rans once they stepped outside of their agrahƒÅras, HemƒÅdri held out the promise of real wealth and real influence. Clearly, he delivered. If, setting aside much poetic hyperbole, we take the concrete claims offered in his verses basically at face value, by sometime in the 1250s, it would seem that the YƒÅdava state was feeding 36,000 BrƒÅhma·πáas a sumptuous feast every day and then showering them in gold. To help us orient ourselves to the scale of this endeavor, thinking comparatively, in the thirteenth century the city of Paris was the largest urban center in Europe. It had a total population of about 80,000 people. In other words, every day, at HemƒÅdri‚Äôs direction, the Seu·πáa empire set out to feed and publicly reward a community of BrƒÅhma·πáas just a bit shy of half of the population of Paris. Or if you prefer an Indic standard of measurement, albeit one that we can thus far not identify as extant in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own lifetime, PurƒÅ·πáic chronicles that focus on the Ko·πÖka·πáa, when speaking of the great deeds of the cultural hero of coastal BrƒÅhma·πáas, Para≈õurarƒÅma, celebrated as unparalleled his accomplishment of settling 36,000 BrƒÅhma·πáas along the coast of the western Deccan.172 Such tales treat these quasi-mythical progenitors as the sires of virtually all of the subsequent lines of BrƒÅhma·πáas in the region. Succinctly, whether we take his claim literally or otherwise, HemƒÅdri presents himself and the state he represents as the most [[P1465]] generous of patrons, eager to recruit a vast vanguard of the under-represented to carry out his cultural revolution.

Hemadpanthi: The ‚ÄúReformation‚Äù of Temple Culture under the Law

In comparison to the many other things we do not know about the highly schismatic dynasty that called themselves the ≈öilƒÅhƒÅras‚Äîsuch as where precisely they came from, what language was spoken at their courts, or why they chose to call themselves ‚Äúmeat offering on a rock‚Äù‚Äî the peculiar rhetorical choices made by the king of Thane that he should live on in the memory of his people and its institutions almost exclusively in terms of the story of how his enemies effectively annihilated his kingdom without taking him hostage is, at best, a minor sort of mystery. Nevertheless, in the handful of copper plate inscriptions produced during the relatively long reign of AparƒÅditya I, erstwhile king of the northern Ko·πÖka·πáa, as well as in the records produced his largely insignificant heirs, once one peels away the usual cacophony of rhetorically inflated birudas, the only concrete accomplishment assigned to this ruler by subsequent ≈öilƒÅhƒÅras is that ‚Äúhe was the king who lived.‚Äù As the Va·∏çavalƒ´ plates, found on the outskirts of what is now the city of Vasai tell the story:

There was a certain demon of the Andhaka [i.e., YƒÅdava] dynasty by the name of Chittukka.173 So that he could shatter the world, he dispersed/obliterated the assembled circle of the vasal lords [beholden to AparƒÅjita]. When the gurus had ‚Äúdeparted,‚Äù when the wealth of dharma was ruined, when the radiant abode [i.e., the palace at SthƒÅnaka] was obliterated, when the entourage of the people at the ruined city had perished, along with the prosperity of the kingdom, it was him [just AparƒÅditya I] all alone‚Äîwith one horse, and the pair of his arms, and a sword.

Looking up from within the frenzied battle, [Chittukka] saw him. He [AparƒÅjita] quickly caused that opponent to take flight. It was not known by [Chittukka] clearly, [[P1466]] how to fight or how to flee. From fear of him [AparƒÅjita], having extracted his army [from the battlefield], [Chittukka] went to rest in the domain of the Barbarians.174

But for its substitution of the sword for the more typical machine gun and the absence of various large things exploding, the picture being painted for us here resembles nothing so much as an action movie from the 1980s. King AparƒÅjita assumes the role that in the American century would be assign to a Bruce Willis, Sylvester Stallone, or Jean Claude van Damme. The plot is simple. Our hero, his family and friends eliminated, the stability of his whole world destroyed, looks down upon the smoking ruin of the battlefield. As the sound of the symphony introduces the soon to be incessantly recurrent theme, tonally marking our protagonist‚Äôs vendetta, looking up from the battlefield, the eyes of his arch enemy meets those of our hero. The bad guy glowers. Our hero grimaces. The enemy gives a signal, turns away, and he and his hoodlums escape into the night. Sitting in the audience, given the hyperformalized conventions of the genre, we know these very same bad guys will be encountered later on as the film draws to its close. In keeping with the simplistic moral universe of Reagan‚Äôs America, we know for certain that, after wiping out his rival‚Äôs forces, our hero will track down his nemesis. He will confront him. Then, after avenging the deaths of his friends and family, he will kill the wicked man, perhaps by throwing him off a building or beating him bloody with his cold bare hands. The details are immaterial, for the narrative structure is predetermined and the message it conveys is that the moral arc of the universe bends towards justice, so long as justice arrives armed to the teeth. [[P1467]]

Things worked out somewhat differently for poor king AparƒÅjita, more commonly called AparƒÅditya I. It is true that over the next few decades, he managed to cobble together much of the territory that traditionally made up his ancestral domain, and by the end of his reign, though they were not entirely contiguous pockets of land paying him allegiance extended from the heartland of his kingdom‚Äîin and around what is now Mumbai‚Äîas far south as the caves at Panhale Ganj just a few hours from Kolhapur. As far as we can tell, however, he never again encountered, let alone conquered, the ferocious Chittukka who had laid waste to his world. Instead, sometime after the reoccupation of the ≈öilƒÅhƒÅra capital city of SthƒÅnaka, located in what is now the city of Thane, the singular AparƒÅjita‚Äîor as he called himself, ‚Äúthe Second Sun‚Äù‚Äîamidst the ruins of his palace, began to compose the Sanskrit treatise for which he is best remembered, an extended and often very acerbic commentary on the YƒÅj√±avalkyasm·πõti entitled the AparƒÅrka. Referring to the very passages that will soon occupy our attention, namely, the commentary on verse 1.7, which is concerned with determining what sort of authorities have validity, Alexis Sanderson has described the AparƒÅrka as taking a ‚Äúconservative stance,‚Äù and characterized AparƒÅditya as one ‚Äúwho devoted much learned effort to resisting the drift into acceptance of the initiated ≈öaivas‚Äù into polite society.175 Based on such a representation, which arises organically from the rhetoric of the treatise itself, in the absence of any further historical details, a reader would naturally assume that the ‚Äúconservative‚Äù AparƒÅditya I embodied the efforts of the social establishment to restore a well- worn status quo, newly infringed upon by upstart social climbers holding noticeable ≈öaiva [[P1468]] commitments. As we are beginning to see, however, our documentary records overwhelmingly speak to a very different sort of dynamic, one in which AparƒÅditya I and the values he champions stand at the geographical and conceptual periphery. Far from representing the status quo, as modern scholarship has supposed, AparƒÅditya‚Äôs hyper-BrƒÅhma·πáism reads as a marginal force in the political and social realities of a western Deccan thoroughly dominated by the very sort of ‚Äúinitiated ≈öaivas‚Äù that he lambastes so vituperatively, and impotently, in his writings, among whose ranks one can indisputably include the enemies who levelled his kingdom.

As for the ‚Äúdemon‚Äù ChittukkarƒÅja, though history knows him primarily by another very different name, he went on to what at the time would have seemed a much more illustrious political and military career. Having laid waste to the heartland of the kingdom of this minor northern rival, after taking everything of value one could carry away, including many slaves and prisoners, the person historians typically refer to as the Kadamba king Jayake≈õin II‚Äîson- law of the KalyƒÅ·πái CƒÅ·∏∑ukya king VikrƒÅmaditya VI‚Äîwould have boarded his vast fleet of war ships and began the long journey home down India‚Äôs western coast.176 Most likely, as AparƒÅditya I himself suggests, Jayake≈õin and his armies would have first laid anchor eighty miles to the south of Thane at the port city of Chaul in modern Raigad district. Just as our inscription speaks of a domain of the barbarians (mlecchƒÅ≈õraya), at this time Chaul was under the control of an Arab merchant colony, whose mercenaries the Kadambas would often employ against their enemies. His armies fed and the storehouses of his ships refurbished, booty in hand, Jayake≈õin and his army would have set a course for the Kadamba capital of Candrapuri, modern day Chandor in the state of Goa. It is here that our story gets interesting, for, as Mark Dyczkowski has shown at great length in his multivolume introduction to the KumƒÅrikƒÅ [[P1469]] Kha·πá·∏ça of the ManthƒÅnabhairava Tantra, not only do the ≈öƒÅkta tantras of the Western transmission treat Candrapuri in the Ko·πÖka·πáa as the last dwelling place (ve≈õmƒÅn) in the age of kali of the goddess KubjikƒÅ in her form as the mother of the Ko·πÖka·πáa (Ko·πÖka·πáƒÅvva), but they identify the Kadamba kings themselves as numbering among the ≈öƒÅkta siddhas responsible for the transmission of her teachings throughout the Deccan.177

Now, we know of two Kadamba kings with this very same name. The first of them, ≈öa·π£·π≠hadeva I, is known to us only from certain Kadamba va·πÉ≈õƒÅvalis, though after the dynasty becomes established, many of these begin to omit him entirely, posing the possibility this king might well be mythical. In any case, this ≈öa·π£·π≠hadeva allegedly ruled from 998‚Äì1013 CE, a somewhat problematic proposition as these same dates would encompass in their entirety the period of rule ascribed to his son and successor Nagavarma as well as Nagavarma‚Äôs son and successor G≈´valadeva I (c. 980‚Äì1005 CE), the first Kabamba King for whom we have proper documentary records. It is G≈´valadeva, incidentally, who established the dynasty‚Äôs connection with the Arab merchants at Chaul, for it seems they rescued him after his ship, which was making pilgrimage to SomanƒÅtha, was lost at sea. G≈´valadeva‚Äôs own heir was ≈öa·π£·π≠hadeva II, famed for his piety, whose long rule and considerable influence render him a more plausible candidate for the ≈öa·π£·π≠hanƒÅtha of the KubjikƒÅ Tantras. In his inscriptions, ≈öa·π£·π≠hadeva, the great-grand uncle of Jayake≈õin II, is celebrated for making a joint pilgrimage to SomanƒÅtha in Saurashtra and then Kolhapur in the southern Ko·πÖka·πáa, with explicit intent that he would pay homage first to the god MahƒÅkƒÅle≈õvara and then to his consort MahƒÅlak·π£mƒ´. In other words, even without the additional corroboratory evidence provided by the ≈öƒÅkta texts of the Western transmission, we have ample reason to accept that the Kadambas in the eleventh and twelfth centuries had aligned their dynasty with the authority of an elite Kaula tradition into which the ruling monarchs would have been at the very least nominally initiated and that this is taking place at the very same moments when the Kadambas have established strong ties to the major source of political authority in the western Deccan, the KalyƒÅ·πái CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas. See S. L. Shantakumari, The Kadambas of Goa and their Inscriptions, as well as S. G. Kadamb, Sources of History of the Kadambas of Goa Inscriptions (Panjim: Broadway Publishing House, 2013).

It is against this very different backdrop that we must reassess the expected elocutionary effect of a minor regional ruler‚Äîone moreover who has recently lost most of his kingdom‚Äîpenning a diatribe questioning the legitimacy of Tantric scripture, Tantric personhood, and the construction and maintenance by Tantric practitioners of Tantric temples and institutions. What this wider historical context renders transparent is that AparƒÅrka‚Äôs voice [[P1470]] speaks to us from a position of vulnerability instead of strength, expressing the views of a heterodox minority and not the ‚Äúmainstream.‚Äù This is a state of affairs that diverges radically from the position HemƒÅdri‚Äôs CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, its prescriptive vision wedded to a powerful state, would assert for itself a mere century later.

The ostensible occasion for AparƒÅrka‚Äôs commentary, which runs dozens of pages, is a single verse in the YƒÅj√±avalkyasm·πõti that sets out to delineate ‚Äúthe foundation of dharma.‚Äù As we shall see in the next chapter, this is very same textual locus upon which HemƒÅdri will anchor a key part of his own in many ways very different hegemonic assertion of a new BrƒÅhma·πáical dharma. For this reason, it is worth taking a moment to orient ourselves to the issues at hand. Against Gautama, who spoke solely of vedam≈´latva as dharma‚Äôs foundation, YƒÅj√±avalkya proposes that dharma can be founded on the 1) ≈õruti, 2) sm·πõti, 3) sadƒÅcƒÅra, as well as 4) that which is pleasing to one‚Äôs own self (svasya priyam ƒÅtmana·∏•). In the MitƒÅk·π£arƒÅ of Vij√±ƒÅne≈õvara, composed, we should remember, in the court of the father-in-law of AparƒÅditya I‚Äôs arch enemy, the verse in occasion is of minimal interest, warranting just a few lines of prose, two supplementary proof texts, and a series of short inherited glosses. Vij√±ƒÅne≈õvara treats ≈õruti as meaning the Veda (≈õrutir veda·∏•) and Sm·πõti as referring to the Dharma≈õƒÅstra, interpretations he justifies with a well-known citation from the MƒÅnavadharma≈õƒÅstra (≈õrutis tu vedo vij√±eyo dharma≈õƒÅstra·πÉ tu vai sm·πõti·∏•).178 Somewhat less straightforwardly but still none too unexpectedly, the third component‚ÄîsadƒÅcƒÅra‚ÄîVij√±ƒÅne≈õvara interprets as the ƒÅcƒÅra practiced by the ≈õi·π£·π≠as (satƒÅ·πÉ ≈õi·π£·π≠ƒÅnƒÅm ƒÅcƒÅro ‚Äònu·π£·π≠hƒÅnam). For our purposes, it is this last interpretation that is the most important, for it stakes a position that essentially legitimizes any form of social practice so long as it is well known that it is practiced by highly respected [[P1471]] BrƒÅhma·πáa authorities. As we have seen time and again, upon such foundations of clever exegetical strategies was the Tantric social imaginary built. It is precisely this position that AparƒÅrka sets out to refute vigorously and at great length. The last element, originating with Manu, carves out a space for individual acts of the human will to be seen as bearing merit, a position that Vij√±ƒÅne≈õvara basically accepts and AparƒÅrka, as we are about to see, contorts to mean something entirely different. With this in mind we can begin to turn to AparƒÅditya I‚Äôs commentary.

On that matter, when it comes to the agnihotra and so forth, ≈öruti is the root of certainty. Sm·πõti [is the root of certainty for] var·πáƒÅ≈õramadharma and so forth, [and] good conduct (sadƒÅcƒÅra) for the holƒÅka [festival] and so forth. An action is said to have ‚Äúsatisfaction of the mind‚Äù when it is enjoyed as delimited by satisfaction of the mind. As, for instance: ‚ÄúWhen a certain action is done, there would be a heaviness of his mind, regarding that [set of circumstances], one should perform austerity until it causes satisfaction.‚Äù179

In redefining the canon of a nascent ‚ÄúBrƒÅhma·πáical‚Äù religion as comprised not of texts open to reinterpretation but of discrete practices, ideological stances, and events, AparƒÅrka is already departing liberally both from contemporary interpreters and from the inherited tradition. Since the authority of the Veda is unchallengeable and yet at the same time much of what the Veda contains and prescribes departs substantively from an emerging vision of what it meant to be BrƒÅhma·πáa in the early centuries of the next millennium, the scope of the Vedic must be confined to a recognizable set of ritual performances. In a similar spirit, as the canon of ‚Äúaccepted‚Äù Sm·πõtis has over the centuries expanded to incorporate textual authorities, such as the ≈öivadharma≈õƒÅstra, that advocate for values and practices that provide legitimacy to the very religious modalities AparƒÅditya I is about to condemn, the category of ‚Äúremembered [[P1472]] tradition‚Äù (sm·πõti) must be taken to as referring not to a class of texts, but to specific doctrinal stances, epitomized by the acceptance of var·πáƒÅ≈õramadharma, amiable to our author’s perspective. Perhaps most importantly, instead of reading sadƒÅcƒÅra as synonym for the conduct of the ≈õi·π£·π≠as, drawing upon arguments in ≈öabara‚Äôs BhƒÅ·π£ya on the Jaimini Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ S≈´tras, AparƒÅditya equates good conduct with a hyper-delimited set of ‚Äúpopular‚Äù festival events that find mention in the Vedic corpus but whose means of performance is never outlined in these works. For the Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ tradition, as epitomized by holƒÅka, the predecessor of the modern festival of Holi, the specific delineation of a discrete number of’ ‚Äúacceptable‚Äù non- Vedic festivals that find mention in the Veda has as its more fundamental intended meaning a blanket prohibition on the celebration of other sorts of ‚Äúlocal‚Äù religious festival by pious BrƒÅhma·πáa authorities.

Reinscribing sadƒÅcƒÅra with this hyper-delimited meaning has two other consequences that will prove absolutely essential to the AparƒÅrka‚Äôs attempts at delegitimizing the Tantric world. The choice effectively removes from consideration the textual foundations for the tradition of legal pluralism, which treats community- and site-specific codes of conduct (ƒÅcƒÅra) as legitimate sources of dharma, and devolves deliberative authority over those varied ƒÅcƒÅras to a range of community-specific agents, many of whom are non-BrƒÅhma·πáas. Just as significantly, AparƒÅditya is seeking to erase one of the mainstays of the classical Dharmas≈´tras, namely, the very notion that dharma can be discerned through observing the conduct and actual practices‚Äîthe ≈õi·π£·π≠ƒÅcƒÅra‚Äîof learned and respected BrƒÅhma·πáa authorities operating in the real world. For AparƒÅditya, this conceptual choice is absolutely indispensable, for ‚Äúthe real world‚Äù he lives in abounds in illustrious lines of BrƒÅhma·πáa authorities of impeccable pedigree who are also initiates in the ≈öƒÅkta and ≈öaiva Tantras. [[P1473]]

Now that he has redefined the legitimate sources for knowing dharma, AparƒÅditya I is ready to turn to his main task in this part of his commentary, the delegitimization of Tantra.

VyƒÅsa says: ‚ÄúBy those who are desiring to obtain purification by/of dharma, nothing other than the Vedas is needed. The cause of dharma is pure, everything else is called mixed.‚Äù

A: ‚ÄúPurity‚Äù means the state of not being mixed. ‚ÄúDharma‚Äù means certainty about dharma. ‚ÄúPure‚Äù means not flawed. ‚ÄúMixed‚Äù means that in which flaws have been brought into being.180

‚ÄúNow, that is the supreme dharma, which is learned from the Vedas. That which is remembered in the PurƒÅ·πáas and so forth is known as the lower [dharma]. One should practice with effort the ritual action that was remembered long by the sages, the best of the knowers of the meaning of the Vedas, but avoid what is forbidden by them. For, one should not deliberate about the dharma that was shown, out of compassion for the welfare of the worlds, by those knowers of the truth of the meaning of the Vedas. Even if there is ignorance about the meaning of the Vedas when one cognizes it for oneself, what doubt can men have when it is certified by the sages? Whatever would denote dharma other than that, know from a good distance such a thing is understood to have delusion as its foundation.‚Äù181

[[P1474]]

AparƒÅditya I‚Äôs doctrinal stance here is as transparent as it is self-serving. In the hierarchy of sources for knowing about dharma, the knowledge we get from the Vedas about ritual performance resides at the very top and our access to it is entirely mediated through the knowers of meaning of the Veda, namely, Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsakas and perhaps VedƒÅntins. This entire body of knowledge not only overrides all other sources for knowing dharma, but it itself cannot be questioned or made the subject of ‚Äúdeliberation.‚Äù Following his proof text, AparƒÅditya I concludes by asserting that all of the other things that call themselves sources for knowledge about dharma‚Äîwhich is to say a good portion of even what the textual mainstream of ≈õƒÅstra has considered to be sources for dharma knowledge‚Äîare illegitimate and must be entirely discounted. It is on this doctrinal foundation that our author now seeks to comprehensively demonstrate, through what he imagines to be rigorous logical argumentation in the manner of a philosopher, that the traditions of the AtimƒÅrga and MantramƒÅrga must neither by studied nor practiced by any human agent. Towards this end, he proposes, entertains, and then discards a series of distinctive p≈´rvapak·π£as that set out to defend the legitimacy of Tantric knowledge as having sanction in orthoprax canons delineating ritual obligations. AparƒÅditya I is not a particularly brilliant stylist nor is he a well-organized polemicist; indeed, at times it simply seems like he is jotting down a whole range of potential objections to his opponents‚Äô positions and has not even bothered to place them coherently in sequence so as to make a convincing argument. These are features of his discourse that may well suggest that the AparƒÅrka actually was composed by the king himself and not by some pandits on his behalf. For this reason, our present purposes will be best served distilling his argument down to a few key points. [[P1475]]

AparƒÅditya I really hits his stride as a polemicist when he turns to dismantling quite specifically the arguments in support of the authoritativeness of what he calls ‚Äú≈öaiva‚Äù knowledge. In essence, the AparƒÅrka identifies, in order of priority, three major ways in which Tantric knowledge diverges from the norms preferred by the vituperatively vocal but decidedly minority BrƒÅhma·πáa viewpoint he represents, and his construal has much to tell us about the social perception of Tantric knowledge systems and their practitioners by outsiders. First and foremost, our author objects to the core claim that tantric initiation‚Äîdƒ´k·π£ƒÅ‚Äîconfers a change in caste status that has real-world social consequences. His lengthy attempts at refuting this idea, and with it the very idea of ≈öaiva identity as legitimate, offer what is one of the richest intellectual moments in the whole treatise. Second, our author sees the Tantras as perpetuating and normalizing to an unprecedented degree acts of ritual violence, especially through sorcery, as well as the ritual use of transgressive substances, which he asserts is forbidden in the kali yuga. Finally, our author seeks to query the claims that because some of the doctrinal and practice elements found in these texts correspond to what we find in the Veda, we can readily accept whatever ‚Äúinnovations‚Äù these revealed texts might offer that do not explicitly conflict with Vedic knowledge. For our purposes, we will confine ourselves to engaging with the first of these arguments. In this spirit, let us begin with our author’s arguments regarding initiation and caste:

A: When it comes to the worshipping of the gods and so forth, only the procedure that is well known from the Narasi·πÉha PurƒÅ·πáa and so forth should be taken up, and not others. Thus, this is to be understood regarding dƒ´k·π£ƒÅ as well. For, in the dƒ´k·π£ƒÅ that is well known in the PurƒÅ·πáas, there is no purification of caste. However, in the ≈öaivƒÅgamas, [it is said:]

‚ÄúFirst is the stationary class, then are the things creep. The third is the class of birds, and the fourth is the class of hunted animals. The fifth is the class of domesticated animals. The sixth is the Untouchables. The seventh is the class of ≈ö≈´dras. The eighth is the class of Vai≈õyas. The ninth is the class of K·π£atriyas and the tenth are known of as BrƒÅhma·πáas. These classes are purified by ≈öiva, the Lord of jƒÅtis.‚Äù

A: By this statement and so forth, by the demonstration that there is purification [of caste], there is as a consequence non-eligibility [of the purified ritual agent] for ≈örauta ritual. ‚ÄúThe BrƒÅhma·πáa should establish the fires,‚Äù ‚Äúthe king should sacrifice with the rƒÅjas≈´ya,‚Äù ‚Äúthe Vai≈õya should sacrifice with the Vai≈õyastoma‚Äù‚Äîby these and so forth, Vedic ritual operates only in regard to someone possessing a class (jƒÅti).182

[[P1476]]

Confirming what we have been arguing throughout the present work, namely, that the teachings of the ≈öivadharma≈õƒÅstra, as well as the receiving of dƒ´k·π£ƒÅ were quite regularly construed as having a concrete real-world impact that rendered those who were governed by these strictures no longer subject to the regime of var·πáƒÅ≈õramadharma, AparƒÅditya I seeks to contrast the acceptable modes of initiation taught in other, often Vai·π£·πáava sources, with this disruptive ‚ÄúTantric‚Äù innovation. Whereas the Tantras themselves, in teaching that ‚Äúdƒ´k·π£ƒÅ liberates,‚Äù ontologically as well as sociologically, for through it ≈öiva either makes one become a BrƒÅhma·πáa or purifies one of caste identity entirely, saw such intervention as emancipatory, AparƒÅditya asserts that while such initiations do have real-world consequences, their actual impact is to render the initiate socially illegible and to inhibit his participation in Vedic ritual of any sort. Since, so he suggests, all Vedic texts specify specific marked identities of caste and stage of life as being governed by their ritual injunctions, the second one is ‚Äúpurified‚Äù by ≈öiva‚Äôs touch or glance, even if he started out a BrƒÅhma·πáa, a person is stripped of the very identities that were the foundation of their original eligibility for performing the rituals.

In keeping with our author‚Äôs rejection of the positivistic legal pluralism upheld by the mainstream of the Dharma≈õƒÅstra traditions most evident in the Deccan, including the YƒÅj√±avalkyasm·πõti itself, in which a multiplicity of ƒÅcƒÅra can serve as meaningful source for knowledge about dharma, the other key portion of our author‚Äôs polemic about the implications of Tantric ideas about caste sets out to refute the very idea that one can meaningfully designate any group of people as ‚Äú≈öaivas,‚Äù which is to say that there exists any social demographic for whom the practice and study of Tantric knowledge might be appropriate. [[P1477]]

[PP] Now one might object, just as it is the case that the Vedas and the other ≈õƒÅstras operate in regard to some certain limited group of people, the ≈öaiva and other similar ≈õƒÅstras should be taken in that same way as pertaining to those possessing a certain caste‚Äî≈öaivas and so forth..

A: [To that we say] no. [For, it is said:]

‚ÄúThat one of whom the injunction that has arisen, by means of the mantras, beginning with the anointing and ending with the cremation ground, of him there is eligibility in regard to this ≈õƒÅstra and not of any other.‚Äù

A: Thus, by this statement and so forth it is shown by Manu and others that only twice- borns are eligible in regard to their own ≈õƒÅstras, and not so for ≈öaiva BrƒÅhma·πáas with regard to the ≈õƒÅstras of the ≈öaivas and so forth.

[PP] Let it be the case then, that a ≈ö≈´dra is eligible with regard to the ≈öaiva and other ≈õƒÅstras.

A: No. Because this is not understood to be the case with regard to Sm·πõtis and other ≈õƒÅstras. And furthermore, there is no such thing as a ≈öaiva by caste towards whom might be addressed a certain ≈öaiva ≈õƒÅstra. However, according to PƒÅ·πáini‚Äôs s≈´tra (4.3101), by applying the suffix a·πá, ‚Äú≈öaiva‚Äù means the ≈õƒÅstra that has been spoken by ≈öiva. And from the same base word, ‚Äú≈öaiva,‚Äù one reapplies the suffix a·πá, thus ‚Äú≈öaiva‚Äù means ‚Äúone who studies that [i.e., the ≈õƒÅstra of ≈öiva]. Then, by applying luk [which erases the previous function only in this particular case], one who knows or studies the ≈öaiva ≈õƒÅstra is a ‚Äú≈öaiva.‚Äù Thus, we have the PƒÅ≈õupatas and others.

Since, thus, the ≈öaiva and other such ≈õƒÅstras do not by nature operate with regard to a certain delimited group of people, acquiring [this knowledge] is like seeing pictures in the clouds. [In other words, it is not a real form of knowledge acquisition]. That being the case, it is to be abandoned with regard to practice.183

Clever scholastic maneuvering aside, again it is important for us to recognize that AparƒÅditya I‚Äôs argument is founded upon claims that in the medieval Deccan most authorities would have considered rather questionable. As we saw in chapters two and three, the ideas that disputes should be adjudicated by people who belong to the communities under discussion and not by outsiders, and that collective social formations, whether defined by theology, caste, or profession, should be governed according to the principles outlined in ‚Äútheir own ≈õƒÅstras,‚Äù [[P1478]] were widely accepted textual norms held in common both by the living traditions of BrƒÅhma·πáical Dharma≈õƒÅstra descending through YƒÅj√±avalkya as well as Tantric and other ‚Äúheretical‚Äù ecumenes. Even more importantly, as we have seen many times throughout this study, these idealized textual prescriptions largely match up with the state of affairs reflected in our documentary records and material cultural resources. In contrast our author‚Äôs preferred notion that only twice-borns have a right to consult and be governed by ‚Äútheir own ≈õƒÅstras‚Äù as late as the twelfth century seems at best to have represented a minority position confined to a specific textual corpus and largely absent from the documentary record. Ironically, the p≈´rvapak·π£in‚Äôs second suggestion, also deflected by our author, that the Tantra as knowledge system is the religion of ≈ö≈´dras, while only occasionally represented in our documentary record, will actually become the preferred SiddhƒÅnta of the more ‚Äúinclusive‚Äù dharma traditions of early modern north India, such as the ≈ö≈´drakamalƒÅkara of the Maharashtrian BrƒÅhma·πáa KamalƒÅkara Bha·π≠·π≠a. In contrast, outside of a minority of Vai·π£·πáava sectarian theologians, AparƒÅditya I‚Äôs preferred stance‚Äîthat no one is eligible to become a ≈öaiva‚Äîwould continue to find few adherents among the emerging tradition of SmƒÅrta Dharma≈õƒÅstrin ritualists that would dominate elite early modern knowledge production, especially in Sanskrit, on the subcontinent.

If being ≈öaiva, our author suggests, is intrinsically illegitimate, the sort of practices these dubious texts put into circulation are at least equally disconcerting. From the perspective of an outsider looking inward, the MantramƒÅrga seems to be offering two concrete products that influence the lives of non-participants in these knowledge systems. The first of these is black magic,184 and the second the production of material culture. Drawing upon a range of [[P1479]] classic textual resources, including the ≈öabarabhƒÅ·π£ya and Manu, the AparƒÅrka sets to establish that in the present age, the use of transgressive materials in ritual is prohibited and deploying of ritual magic against one‚Äôs enemies brings about a fall into untouchability. But while the lurid details he has culled out of various lost Tantras offers us a candid glimpse of why outsiders to the Tantric knowledge systems would have been quite justified in holding the practitioners of these sorts of rites in abject horror, taken as a polemic here again, AparƒÅditya I has offered up a rather stale argument that any reasonably talented rival could readily rebut simply using passages from the Tantras themselves. Thus, for example, the very same grounds for discounting magic acts found the Tantras would prove equally applicable to the Atharva Veda in either of its recensions, both of which abound in almost exactly the same sort of offensive magical rites explicitly directed at parallel ends.

Just how unpopular the AparƒÅrka‚Äôs agenda must have been is laid bare when we turn to the second dimension of our author‚Äôs searing critique of the social consequences of Tantric normativities, namely, his discussion of the dominance of the world of material cultural production, especially the construction industry, by those pernicious ≈öaivas and their systems. In this case, however, faced with what seems to be a cold hard reality that the very technology that makes possible the production of material culture is largely derived from texts reflecting [[P1480]] with ≈öƒÅkta commitments, against the even more extreme disavowals attributed to an even more stringently orthoprax imagined opponent, our author seeks out something of a middle path, allowing for the use of the technical materials found in these texts provided that their doctrinal elements are discarded.

PP2: Thus, in regard to ritual installation (prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ) as well, only the procedure stated in the PurƒÅ·πáas and so forth is to be accepted, not anything else. Because they [the PurƒÅ·πáas] alone are accepted in the Bhavi·π£yat PurƒÅ·πáa as being authorities regarding ‚Äúmixed dharmas.‚Äù

And thus, in the Bhavi·π£yat PurƒÅ·πáa‚Äî‚ÄúThe Eighteen PurƒÅ·πáas, and likewise the story of RƒÅma, the Vi·π£·πáudharma≈õƒÅstra and so forth, and the ≈öivadharmas, O BhƒÅrata, the KƒÅr·π£·πáa, which is the fifth Veda, which is known to be the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata, the Sauras, O best of kings of dharma, those stated by the MƒÅnava, O lord of the earth, men say that victory is the name of these ones. . . .185

PP: But also, in the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa [it is said:]

The one who is the knower of the VƒÅma and Dak·π£i·πáa paths, who has gone to the far shore of the knowledge of the mothers, would be the best of ritual installers (sthƒÅpaka) of the goddesses and with regard to the mothers. He is skilled in the meaning of the Pa√±carƒÅtra. He is skilled in the system of the mothers. He possesses the house of Vi·π£·πáu. He is always praised as a brahmacƒÅrin and giver of pacification. He is a knower of the ≈õivadharma [≈õiva≈õƒÅsana]. The one who knows the purpose of the ga·πáas, the mothers and the grahas, being a householder or chaste student, is renowned as a ritual installer. . . .

PP1: This is also not an indication that the practice [of this system] is that of the ‚Äúoutsider‚Äù ƒÄgamas, [which are condemned].

PP2: [However this is to be rejected], because its practice is forbidden in the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa. As it is said in the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa:

‚ÄúO twice-borns, listen to the characterization in its entirety of the sthƒÅpaka. He is skilled in the mantras of the Vedas, endowed with all of the limbs. He is a knower of the PurƒÅ·πáas and is a knower of dharma. He is devoid of arrogance and greed. He arises in the country where the black deer goes about. He has an auspicious form; he is always [[P1481]] intent on the conduct of purity. He is not in contact with the lineages of the heretics‚Äù. . . .[^14??]

326]

A: Nor is it to be said that an installer of images who works with supplementary materials derived from the Tantras that constitute his own dharma‚Äîfor example, ritual installation‚Äîhas knowledge of [such] VƒÅma and Dak·π£i·πáa transmissions [i.e., a special capacity as sthƒÅpaka] strictly on the basis of having taken initiations stated in those [Tantras].

Because, this [idea] is not accepted in the Sm·πõtis and other ≈õƒÅstras‚Äîbecause, it is not established that it is the VƒÅma and Dak·π£i·πáa transmissions as stated that delimit one‚Äôs being an installer and so forth. Because, given that upon one‚Äôs own examinations, the Left and Right transmissions, preceded by the demonstration of their own exegesis, have their doctrines supplemented in a dependent manner with the supplementary materials of [knowledge derived from] various vidyƒÅsthƒÅnas, and therefore, the Left and Right transmissions merely convey supplementary assistance from one‚Äôs own Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ Tantra by [offering] purely easeful and complete instruction.‚Äù186

[[P1482]]

In this section, the AparƒÅrka introduces two distinctive p≈´rvapak·π£ins. The first of these insists that when establishing a structure or installing an image, only PurƒÅ·πáic authorities can be consulted, for these are the sole resources prescribed for engaging with the so-called ‚Äúmixed dharmas,‚Äù mostly pertaining to quotidian human needs and desires. At the same time, this stance prescribes that the ritualist who is to conduct the installation must be a-twice-born learned in the Veda and PurƒÅ·πáas who was born in ƒÄryavƒÅrta and has had no contact with heretics and their doctrines. For a Deccani king seeking the support of local BrƒÅhma·πáa authorities who resides in the Ko·πÖka·πáa, it is easy to see why such a definition would have [[P1483]] proven too stringent and narrow. Living in the ≈öaiva Age, complete non-contact with the heretics‚Äîin other words with the ideological and theological systems in evidence in most courts, was basically non-tenable for anyone who ever left their agrahƒÅra. Even more importantly, for someone based in the twelfth-century Deccan, the Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa‚Äôs emphatic insistence that the only person who can oversee construction sites and install images is someone born in what was by now a narrow belt in north India increasingly under the control of ‚Äúforeign invaders‚Äù was highly impractical. Such a standard not only would have entailed that the overseers for all temple building and construction projects would have been culturally and linguistically distinct from the crews they deployed, which in practical terms would have been a disaster, but it excluded from consideration any of the BrƒÅhma·πáa communities residing in the king‚Äôs own domain, the very people who would have represented the most logical allies for his BrƒÅhma·πáizing project.

It is the second opponent‚Äôs stance, invoking the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa, however, that proves most relevant to our own interests, for what it asserts is that the ideal sthƒÅpaka is none other than a ≈öƒÅkta Kaula following the VƒÅma path or BhairavasiddhƒÅntin adhering to the Dak·π£i·πáa transmission. This is to say, in the context of the medieval Deccan, that the sorts of authorities that the sthƒÅpaka should consult are texts like the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata and the type of person who should be in charge of installing images in the first place must belong to the very same demographics as the kƒÅrukas and their skull carrying KƒÅlamukhas guru overseers. Moreover, the text makes plain that such a person has mastered the ≈öivadharma≈õƒÅstra and is employed to perform the ≈öƒÅntyadhyƒÅya to pacify bad circumstances and malignant entities. In his terse prose commentary, immediately following this quote, the imagined opponent sets out to establish that far from representing an outsider (bƒÅhya) order of values, the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa is [[P1484]] perpetuating ‚Äúmainstream‚Äù values, and in a certain sense, as we have seen throughout our own investigations, he is basically correct.

AparƒÅrka begins by acknowledging what to him must have seemed a sad state of affairs, namely, that it is logistically impractical to dispense with a methodology that thoroughly dominates the existing market. In other words, unlike BallƒÅlasena, he is unwilling to simply declare that the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa, which transparently advocates for the ≈öƒÅkta Tantras as pramƒÅ·πáas, and its ilk are fraudulent sources that solely transmit delusion.
