How he seeks to circumvent this dynamic, which seemingly entirely undercuts his claims that the acquisition of ≈öaiva knowledge, like looking at the clouds in the sky and comparing them to different objects or animals, serves no concrete human end, is by asserting that the all of the actionable knowledge found in the texts of VƒÅma and Dak·π£i·πáa transmissions does not originate in these transmissions themselves nor is it causally conditioned by their doctrinal or theological tenets. Instead, he claims, everything of value in these texts actually is derived from the BrƒÅhma·πáical traditions of the vidyƒÅsthƒÅnas. Accessing this knowledge through the medium of ≈öƒÅkta Tantras is merely a matter of convenience for the users, since these sources offer particularly detailed and well-organized discussions of the relevant source knowledge. As we saw repeatedly in chapter 6, such a perspective of course is entirely in conflict with the emic understanding of the ≈öƒÅkta sthƒÅpakas themselves, for they believed the capacity to craft and enliven images and empower structures that are so integral to temple design and construction emerge directly and organically from an esoteric awakening. When the craftsman receives the transmission from their teacher, or a teacher from their own guru, as a byproduct of ≈õaktipƒÅta, there emerges a new capacity to align with, transfer, and manipulate different registers of energy, ≈õakti. By adopting the bhƒÅvas of the different gods as they work, the craftsman becomes able to replicate [[P1485]] their presence in permanent form within animate objects and spaces. In short, professionalization in this model is fundamentally dependent on the ability to cultivate at will the states of altered of consciousness or experiences of possession that are defining features of Kaula discourse and are most certainly not a regular part of the repertoire of classical vidyƒÅsthƒÅna pedagogy.

By attending to the interpretive choices that were made by AparƒÅjita I‚Äôs near contemporary, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, in the relevant portions of his own K·πõtyakalpataru, we get a much clearer sense of the eccentric nature of AparƒÅrka‚Äôs compromise position even within the ‚ÄúBrƒÅhma·πáical‚Äù discourse of the day.187 Lak·π£mƒ´dhara invokes nearly exactly the same corpus188 as AparƒÅrka, namely, the Narasi·πÉha PurƒÅ·πáa, Matsya PurƒÅ·πáa, Bhavi·π£ya PurƒÅ·πáa, and Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa. But in contrast to his counterpart, who, as we have seen, while not rejecting the text outright is noticeably embarrassed by much of what it advocates, for Lak·π£mƒ´dhara, it is the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa that provides the most important conceptual resources that he will use in structuring his treatment of prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ. In fact, when it comes time to define the characteristics of the sthƒÅpaka, the K·πõtyakalpataru embraces the very same passage that had caused AparƒÅditya I such consternation. Not only does Lak·π£mƒ´dhara cite from the text, offering a much more extended version of the passage that does much to clarify its actual intent, but the very passage in question actually supplies the guidelines he will use throughout his treatise in determining just how one should approach the consecration of the different types of deities.

L: Now the installers (sthƒÅpaka):

It is said in the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa: [[P1486]]

‚ÄúBrahmƒÅ spoke: In the ≈öaiva system, within the SiddhƒÅnta of ≈öiva belonging to ≈öiva, the entrance [into the work of installation] is to be done. Whether a householder or a brahmacƒÅrin, the installer (sthƒÅpaka) serves the cause of liberation. The one who is a knower of the VƒÅma and the Dak·π£i·πáa who has gone to the far shore of the knowledge of the mothers would be the best installer of the goddesses and even of the Mother. The one who is skilled in the meaning of the Pa√±carƒÅtra, the one who knows the nature of the mothers, [installs] on behalf of Vi·π£·πáu. He is a knower of the mantras, a householder, and a brahmacƒÅrin who keeps the fires. The one who is a knower of the ≈õivadharma, who knows the purpose of the ga·πáas and the mothers of the house, the one who knows the meaning of the solar deity system, is an installer of S≈´rya, who bestows auspiciousness.

The twice-born ·πõtvij BrƒÅhma·πás who knows the nature of the meaning of the Veda and the Veda, and with regard to the other [deities], the installers who are knowers of their own ≈õƒÅstras who do auspicious worship: [all these are acceptable]. The ≈öaivas who are householders and best of twice-borns [can do installation] with regard to all [ritual systems]. [[P1487]]

The text continues, offering additional specifications for esoteric ≈öƒÅkta systems, before concluding by specifying what types of social agents are not eligible for performing prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ and serving as sthƒÅpakas.

Now in regard to the GuruvetƒÅla Tantra and in the Bh≈´ta Tantras in the Dak·π£i·πáa system, in regard to the KƒÅlikƒÅ in the VƒÅma Tantras the one who knows [the installation in all those systems] is a conveyor of noble auspiciousness. . . . Having known the form [image] that is [for] the installation of the goddesses‚Äî[that is the] entry way into the work [of ritual installation]. Otherwise, he would bring about an agitated quarrel, death, and great terror.

In [the context of] the kingdom, in regard to a yajamƒÅna, a king, or installer who is without the ƒÄgama, those contrary entrants [into the work of prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ] will be cooked in the Avici hell and so forth [and likewise] those BrƒÅhma·πáa sthƒÅpakas who are devoid of the procedures (vidhi) belonging to Vi·π£·πáu or ≈öiva. . . . [[P1488]]

Now that he has provided a whole range of options for different types of sthƒÅpakas as befit different purposes, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara next turns to the question of who exactly has adhikƒÅra for performing ritual installations. Again, it is the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa that provides him with his answers.

L: Now, the ones who are eligible. The Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa says:

The gods should be installed according to the division of var·πáa and ƒÅ≈õrama and not otherwise. BrahmƒÅ is to be installed by BrƒÅhma·πáas, he being auspicious and accompanied by the GƒÅyatrƒ´s. Now, Vi·π£·πáu is established by the four var·πáas, having been caused to be installed by the ones desiring happiness. But Bhairava, however, [is installed] by the four var·πáas and by the untouchables, according to their doctrines.191 All the people install the Mothers who are the best of the gods.

Having caused a li·πÖga to be installed, a householder or even an ascetic should worship it always. ≈öiva being installed bestows health, a kingdom, and likewise, a son. However, a Lord of Yoga (yoge≈õa) who is endowed with yoga should be installed by those who know yoga. Otherwise, a loud substantial commotion will arise in the city.192

[[P1489]]

In comparison to what we saw prescribed in the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, in a certain sense, the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa is here advocating a socially ‚Äúconservative‚Äù position within which only specific agents can engage in particular activities in a manner conditioned by caste identity, and within which there are potentially dire consequences for transgressing such norms. What is conspicuously absent from such a disciplinary order, however, is any effort at creating a systematic privileging of the authority of BrƒÅhma·πáas. Instead, the ordering principle here is that pantheons and their associated regimes are essentially community-specific. While BrƒÅhma·πáas have exclusive sovereignty over the installation of the images of the god BrahmƒÅ, they possess this right for precisely the same reason that professional yogins are given a monopoly over the installation of ‚Äúyogic deities,‚Äù a logic of sympathetic magic in which like aligns with like.

Before setting these materials aside and returning to our account of how HemƒÅdri remade this world, we might also make note that the religious landscape that the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa formulates and that Lak·π£mƒ´dhara proceeds to formalize draws a fascinating and somewhat expected distinction between two kinds of social inclusivism in the domain of religion, one that should render plain that even in regard to the early medieval world, histories of caste are often entirely different and distinct from histories of untouchability. As the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa understands things, Vai·π£·πáava traditions acknowledge the adhikƒÅra of ‚Äúall four var·πáas‚Äù‚Äîin other words BrƒÅhma·πáas, K·π£atriyas, Vai≈õyas, as well as ≈ö≈´dras‚Äîto act as religious authorities, at least in the domain of installing images. This is substantive inclusivism that does indeed go beyond typical post Vedic-era Vaidika visions of who is eligible to act in the religious domain. As such, it would be easy to overlook that such a system deliberately skips over any mention of the people we now call Dalits, were it not for the fact that the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa deliberately juxtaposes this delimited inclusivism with the ethos of the Bhairava tantras, which, from an etic perspective explicitly treats people from an antyajƒÅti background as religious authorities. It is only in some of the ≈öƒÅkta sources, building upon the archaic AtimƒÅrgic legacy of the ≈öivadharma≈õƒÅstra where, at least in some domains, allusions to caste simply fall away. One [[P1490]] order of preferentiality is then replaced by a different hierarchical order. This founded, in theory, on initiations and esoteric attainment, but in practice quite likely was powered by networked social connections that linked people from disparate backgrounds on the basis of shared commensality in a ritual setting.

As we have seen in the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata and the inscriptional record, in the world of the late medieval Deccan, one of the primary occasions for experiencing such ritual commensality was precisely the festivities surrounding acts of prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ as well as the subsequent annual celebration of such occasions. This was when kings, warrior chieftains, the local big man, or other analogous political authorities, drawn from a range of castes, the religious professionals responsible for the management of sacred domains falling outside of the purview of the state, by this period largely KƒÅlamukhas, also from a range of castes, acting as the primary transmitters of the ≈öƒÅkta Tantras and Bhairava SiddhƒÅnta, and the ostensibly ≈ö≈´dra or Dalit professional artisan and artist builder and performance classes who made the lived reality and composed the soundtrack that ordered much of lived experience, came together and shared space and affective emotional experiences while supping on the transgressive witches brew that marked them as brothers. As we have seen throughout this study, it was these moments and these spaces that formed key bonds that ordered an otherwise almost overwhelmingly pluralistic world whose differentiated domains rendered much of influence and social capital site and praxis specific. Instituting a new world organized around different values would necessarily therefore demand disrupting this existing order and undercutting the mode of social relations that it fostered.

At Kukkunur, in the courtyard that stands before the Navali·πÖge≈õvara temple, the stone themselves speak of such continuities. There, on single sheet of stone, in varied hands spanning two centuries, five inscriptions from the CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas, the KƒÅlachuris, and the Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas [[P1491]] express their intentions to protect the long established siddhapƒ´·π≠ha in accordance with ‚Äúits own dharma‚Äù (ƒ´ dharmmama·πÉ svadharmmadi·πÉ sƒÅsirvvaru pratipƒÅ·∏∑isuvaru).193 The chief focus of each of these records is an affirmation of and investment in the oldest site at Kukkunur, corresponding to the original place of its founding (m≈´lasthƒÅna). A few kilometers away from the Jye·π£·π≠hƒÅ temple, we find the site is marked by a now heavily renovated RƒÅ·π£·π≠rak≈´·π≠a structure, somewhat older even than Navali·πÖge≈õvara, that is dedicated to ≈öiva.194

Figure 24: Ka·∏∑lƒì≈õvara temple, Kukkunur as it appeared before the recent renovations. Published in Gerard Foekema, The CƒÅ·∏∑ukya Architecture. Vol II. p. 642.195 [[P1492]]

For this reason it is hardly surprising that in the documentary portions of the ≈õƒÅsanas under discussion, the bulk of which were produced during the floruit of Ka·∏∑ƒì≈õvara‚Äôs predecessors and successors, alongside consistently offering homage to these sacerdotal authorities, acknowledged with varying degrees of intimacy as gurudƒìvaru, svƒÅmiga·∏∑u, and p≈´jƒÅri, we find evidence of financial support to the BrƒÅhma·πáas at the local brahmapurƒ´, variously characterized either as ·πög Vedic or (somewhat implausibly given the register of expression in evidence) those who have crossed over the farthest shore of the four Vedas (cartvƒìdapƒÅragarappa [sic]) but also of oja/ojja artisans, garland makers, and makers of song (hƒÅ·∏çuvargam), and of ascetics, possibly committed to the worship of Kha·πá·∏çobƒÅ/MailƒÅri, engaged in austerities (ekk≈ç·π≠i tap≈çdhanaru). Though each of these texts is pretty terse, there is [[P1493]] considerable consistency across the corpus not only in the sort of social agents and institutions to whom the donations are addressed, but even in the circumstances under which the endowments are being made.

Figure 25: The ma·πá·∏çapa that abuts the front of the Ka·∏∑ƒì≈õvara temple at Kukkunur. The now heavily whitewashed images on top seem to be older than the interior, which has many Vijayanagar period modifications.

Four of the five donations were made during solar, lunar, or combined eclipses, with two of them being made during eclipses during the new moon (amƒÅvƒÅsya) typically one of the least auspicious days of the Indian calendar.196 All five of the inscriptions, it almost goes with saying, understand Kukkunur as an undying land-grant, to be managed in perpetuity by the overseers of its temple complexes, figures who explicitly and implicitly are recognized as having juridical authority over the management of the space and whose affairs fall outside the purview of state intervention. This dynamic, in fact, which simply takes the sacred autonomy [[P1494]] of the siddhak·π£etra for granted, holds true throughout the entire inscriptional corpus without a single exception, independent of how aligned the donor is with the institutional values of the site. Thus, for example, a copper plate from 1183 CE issued by a Kalachuri ruler speaks of how:

The King of Kings. . . renewed the endowment of the great divine field known of as Kukkunur, which is the domain of the mother of the world, the illustrious Goddess Jye·π£·π≠hƒÅ, which is contained within the 300 charter of Beluvala, having villages endowed in perpetuity with well-known limits, along with its gardens, stones, water, buried treasure, deposited wealth. . . . endowed with governing authority has mastery over the eight enjoyments. . . . It is endowed with [exclusive rights concerning] all the collections of substances, taxes, duties, customs, correctional authority, juridical authority. . . [jurisdiction over] artisans, garland vendors, and worshipping officiants, and is entitled in perpetuity not to be ‚Äúpointed at‚Äù [repossessed] by the king.197

[[P1495]]

In a similar vein, less than a decade beforehand, the inscription dedicated to celebrating the siddha Ka·∏∑ƒì≈õvara concludes by rather emphatically declaiming: ‚ÄúTo the one who protects the unperishing dharma, there will be a destruction of all sin. May it be so! [But,] for the person who even thinks about the ruin of this place, a complete destruction of their family line will ensue.‚Äù198

The very last inscription produced at Kukkunur during its heyday is scrawled on the bottom of a floor to ceiling stone plate that still stands in the courtyard before the Navali·πÖge≈õvara temple.199 Rather significantly, it was issued in the twenty-first year of the [[P1496]] reign of Si·πÖgha·πáadeva II, the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava king. It is unclear whether it was the king himself or one of his representatives who made the pilgrimage to Kukkunur to perform worship of the honorable feet of the ƒÅcƒÅrya of the gods. By this time, it seems, Ka·∏∑ƒì≈õvara, either deceased or retired, had been succeeded by a certain Vir≈´pƒÅk·π£asvƒÅmi, and it was this authority that the Seu·πáa power implored to perform rites directed at removal of ‚Äúobstacles,‚Äù both sacred and secular. As part of the bargain, it almost goes without saying, the Seu·πáa kingdom affirmed the sovereignty of Kukkunur and its KƒÅlamukhas in perpetuity. The year was 1231 CE. Hardly anyone could have imagined that two decades later, the ≈öƒÅkta power center at the heart of Kukkunur would be wiped off the face of the earth let alone that this would take place under the explicit direction of the Seu·πáa king‚Äôs own son.

[[P1497]]

At Devagiri, in the assembly of the king, it was announced by the king ≈örƒ´ K·πõ·π£·πáa himself that Kukkunur [was to be made] a garbhag·πõha for all the gods (sarvadevatƒÅgarbhag·πõham). He [the commander, Cau·πá·∏çarƒÅya], being commanded by the king ‚Äúdo it, for the sake of the flourishing of our kingdom,‚Äù had obtained the seal of the king for that purpose.

Given that there was a transgression of dharma (dharmaprasa·πÖga), according to the statement ‚Äúwhoever owns the earth at which time owns the fruit,‚Äù the general welfare indicated that the great agrahƒÅra of Kukkunur, which is the crest jewel of the fields of all the siddhas, had to be taken over [lit., released (mukta)]. In the seventh year of the reign of ≈örƒ´ KƒÅnharadeva, who is the wheel turner proud with splendor with the power of the arms of NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa of the YƒÅdava clan, Kukkunur was made an agrahƒÅra with fixed taxes (niyatakara), to be paid with gold dinar (ni·π£ka). . . .

Due to truth, the sun illuminates the world; the deities have arisen from truth. From truth, the earth bears fruit. In truth, everything is established. Thus, being addressed by them, ≈örƒ´ Cau·πá·∏çarƒÅja, with supreme bhakti, preceeded by the washing of the feet, gave Kukkunur to those great BrƒÅhma·πáas of various gotras along with showers of gold.

Regarding the protection of this dharma, the fruit is the earth, which has been enjoyed by many kings beginning at creation. To whomever it belongs at a certain time, his is the fruit. . . . Whoever disregards [this] in action, thought, or speech will become a Ca·πá·∏çƒÅla at that very moment, cast outside of all dharma.

The seat of all dharmas, the breath of all embodied beings, the instruction of future kings: by him [KandharƒÅya], this order has been given. Let this be made free from [[P1498]] deficiencies, with no defects, by those who are knowers of faults. May dharma be eternal, and embodied beings be happy.200

As we seen throughout this chapter, the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava King Si·πÖgha·πáa II was at once the perpetuator of the old ethos of the KalyƒÅ·πái CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas and the promoter of newly emerging popular ≈öƒÅkta ecumene specific to Maharashtra. Rather unexpectedly, his death almost at once sparked a sea change in values. With the ascent to the throne of his son KandharƒÅya (c. 1247‚Äì 1259/60), a new sort of centralized policies emanated from the YƒÅdava royal court and down into the real world, leading to the rejection of an older order of values founded on a different vision of the comity between religion and law. In this copper plate issued in 1255 CE, a mere five years before the release of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, an undying land-grant that for centuries had been under the direction of non-BrƒÅhma·πáa ≈öƒÅkta-≈öaiva religious authorities tending to a pantheon of potent and even somewhat terrible esoteric deities is summarily terminated and its custodians are evicted‚Äîor in the bitterly ironic idiom of the Dharma≈õƒÅstra, they are forcibly granted mukti. [[P1499]]

Placed under new management and reinvented as an adjunct of the state subject to fixed taxes, much like we saw in the Marathi language inscription at Unkeshwar, the new Kukkunur is designated as a sacred center that is to house a pantheon comprised of ‚Äúall the gods.‚Äù This is to say, the existing focuses of worship‚ÄîKƒÅlƒ´, Jye·π£·π≠hƒÅ/Siddhayoge≈õvarƒ´, and the Picumata BrahmayƒÅmala‚Äôs Bhairava‚Äî are replaced by a decidedly non-popular and non-Tantric Vedic pantheon. In contradistinction with everything we have seen from our earlier sources, which addressed the merchant and artisan devotees of the KƒÅlamukha gurus as the primary local authorities, oversight for their worship as well as all local affairs are placed in the hands of a collectivity of BrƒÅhma·πáas. Indeed, instead of offering a hymn to a deity, such as the now ousted Jye·π£·π≠hƒÅ, the KandharƒÅya copper plate addresses its praises at tedious length precisely at these BrƒÅhma·πáas themselves‚Äîall one thousand and two hundred of them. It is these gods on earth, four faced on account of their study of the four Vedas, singularly intent solely on sattvic things‚Äîand them alone‚Äîthat the document sees as providing the source of sacral authority that makes Kukkunur great. And instead of being privileged for their great learning, the text celebrates our BrƒÅhma·πáas primarily for their purity and their piety‚Äîin other words, for their conduct. The inscription concludes with emphatic affirmation of the necessity of protecting and enforcing dharma. It is dharma itself, and not the institution at Kukkunur, that is deemed eternal. [[P1500]]

Indeed, radically departing from the formulations as we find in the grants of the CƒÅ·∏∑ukyas, Kalachuris, Hoysalas and prior YƒÅdava kings, our text ascribes the locus of dharma not to some particularized samaya or svadharma that must be protected or to the authority of an enumerated sam≈´ha, but rather to the body of the king himself. It is KandharƒÅya, and by extension agents such as his chief minister and military commander (mahƒÅpradhƒÅna da·πá·∏çanƒÅyaka) Cau·πá·∏çarƒÅja, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs immediate predecessor in the most powerful office in the land, that is ‚Äúthe seat of all dharmas, the respiration of all bodies, the instruction of future kings.‚Äù In place of site-specific pluralism, inflected by variations in time and context, now and forever, it is the state as embodied in the acting sovereign that is source of all legitimate authority and that sets the standard for what constitutes all acceptable human action.

In the realm of geopolitics, the reign of King KandharƒÅya was marked militarily by a series of rebellions. For reasons that are unclear, and which may well have to do with the sudden rejection of longstanding norms in the realms of governance and religion, virtually all of the Seu·πáa vassal powers residing in Karnataka, for example the Guttas, the Sindhas, and the Rattas, attempted to break away from Seu·πáa oversight, and each of them was brutally suppressed. In contrast, on the home front, the period saw an unusual degree of comity among the Seu·πáa aristocrats themselves. Rather than offing his siblings, a very common strategic approach in these circles for consolidating power, KandharƒÅya entrusted them with key duties in helping to manage the Seu·πáa realm. Most importantly, he embraced his younger half- brother, MahƒÅdeva, first appointing him as the prince in waiting and designated successor (yuvarƒÅja) and then actually sharing the throne with him in the last years of his reign, a dynamic that in the inscriptional records leads to a repeated representation of KandharƒÅya and MahƒÅdeva as a second RƒÅma and Lak·π£ma·πáa or even Yuddhi·π£·π≠hira and Arjuna, in the kali age. Thus, already, during the lifetime of his predecessor, certainly by 1251 CE, when he is no younger yuvarƒÅja, MahƒÅdeva seems to have begun to assemble his own personal court of commanders and advisors with the express blessings of his brother the king. [[P1501]]

In the absence of the publication and comprehensive study of the complete Seu·πáa YƒÅdava documentary records as they have been uncovered by various Indian institutions, especially the ASI, we are not yet in a position to accurately reconstruct the officials that would have made up either of these Seu·πáa cabinets, which is to say who were the exact people who would have stood witness in the assembly of the king at Devagiri while King KandharƒÅya announced that to ensure the stability of his realm, Kukkunur as it currently existed would have to be destroyed. There is little reason to doubt, however, that a young HemƒÅdri, already working within the chancellery and the treasury and working hard on what would eventually become his CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, would have been among them. And while access to such resources may well serve to clarify once and for all the direction in which such influence is flowing‚Äîin other words, whether HemƒÅdri is simply entextualizing an newly emerging Seu·πáa imperial ethos already in evidence in the early days of his professional life or whether, as his writings assert, he himself was a key architect of this conceptual project, one thing that is certain is that the considerable conceptual and rhetorical overlap between documents like our Kukkunur copper plates and the prescriptions of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái can hardly be an accident. In fact, as we are about to see, it is the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái itself that provides the interpretive context for making sense of how seemingly incidental efforts at remedying individual ‚Äúbreaches in dharma‚Äù could usher in a new world order.

As was alluded to at some length earlier in this chapter, the published edition of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái leaves much to be desired, for it prints a text that is disordered and in many places very incomplete. As we do not, at least as of yet, possess a copy of the Mok·π£akha·πá·∏ça, and the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái was unknown to its editors, in its current state the text as printed transitions from the division on dƒÅna, a proper kha·πá·∏ça, right to the Pari≈õe·π£a, or supplementary portion. In Bharatacandra Siromani‚Äôs edition, this Pari≈õe·π£a section begins with the two volumes containing the ≈örƒÅddhapaddhati and concludes with the astrological guidelines provided by the kƒÅlanir·πáaya. One has only to read HemƒÅdri‚Äôs [[P1502]] own preface to the ≈örƒÅddhapaddhati, however, to recognize that the text itself presumes that the ≈örƒÅddhapaddhati was to have followed at least two additional topical divisions within the Pari≈õe·π£a:

And on that matter in the first [prakara·πáa of the Pari≈õe·π£a], brilliant HemƒÅdri who has an auspicious heart, speaks the ≈õrƒÅddha injunction joined with a praise of the result.

There is stated in the beginning, during the installation pertaining to li·πÖgas and images and so forth, [the procedures] for worship of such deities as ≈öiva, Vi·π£·πáu, Ga·πáe≈õa, and so forth. Immediately after [in the text], during the section on the installation of the cows, there was stated the procedures for the ƒÅrƒÅdhana of Harihara and so forth, as well as the wish-fulfilling cows who are Nanda and so forth, [all of whom] have the form of deities. Now, regarding the installation of those having the forms of BrƒÅhma·πáas and so forth. . . is stated the worship of deities who have the form of ancestors (pit·πõ). . . .201

Thus far at least, the second, and potentially quite pertinent part of this paddhati, which would have told us in detail about what HemƒÅdri envisioned regarding the worship of Harihara‚ÄîVi·π≠·π≠hal at Pandharpur‚Äîas well as how Nanda and the other companions of K·πõ·π£·πáa fit into this part of our story, has yet to surface. What we do have on hand, however, almost entirely intact, is the opening division of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs unpublished and thus far undocumented Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati, which we will read from the manuscript currently in the possession of the Royal Asiatic Society of Bombay. Here is how it begins:

There was a king known of as MahƒÅdeva who was the ornament of the Lineage of the Moon, of whom there was a multitude of gracious [lit., autumnal] qualities. . . . Of him there was a lord of all the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáas named HemƒÅdri. . . . Of that lord of the ≈örƒ´kara·πáas, there was a paddhati pertaining to all the gods (sƒÅrvadaivikƒ´), which was exceptionally clever in showing the path of the ≈örauta and SmƒÅrta rituals. In that paddhati, this is the first ≈õloka:

Having approached the Pa√±carƒÅtras, all the PurƒÅ·πáas, and the entirety of the ≈öruti and Sm·πõti, and having ‚Äúexamined‚Äù the ƒÄgamas, the Karmapaddhati is laid out for the benefit of sacrificers.

By HemƒÅdri is stated the dyadic i·π£·π≠ƒÅp≈´rta, which are the sanƒÅtana dharmas of all twice- borns, conveying the rituals of the ≈örauta and SmƒÅrtas [and] who [abide by the] sadƒÅcƒÅra.202

[[P1503]]

Eerily foreshadowing the idiom of early colonial and modern Hindu apologetics, HemƒÅdri states that the purpose of the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati is to teach one dimension of the sanƒÅtana dharmas to his preferred demographic, namely, all twice-borns of unimpeachable conduct. On the surface, the religious modality he is promoting is made up of a two-part synthesis of ≈örauta‚Äîor ‚ÄúVaidika‚Äù‚Äîand SmƒÅrta elements, with the latter term in this context referring to a heady brew of Dharma≈õƒÅstra, PurƒÅ·πáa, and repurposed extracts from both the ≈öaiva and Vai·π£·πáava traditions of the MantramƒÅrga. In actuality, however, in the sources we will be examining, the ≈örauta contribution is mostly confined to some mantras and the liturgical use of Vedic hymns, often in strikingly new ritual settings, as well as to the rhetoric of BrƒÅhma·πáical normativity. Almost all HemƒÅdri‚Äôs key conceptual resources are drawn from an expanding canon of SmƒÅrta religious resources, which have themselves been extracted from rather disparate points of origin. We might notice as well that in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs formulation, ‚Äúthe ƒÄgamas‚Äù are set apart rhetorically and syntactically from the other elements, even being governed by a different verb (instead of being ‚Äúattained‚Äù (samƒÅsƒÅdya), they are ‚Äúexamined‚Äù (nirƒ´k·π£ya)), a state of affairs in alignment with the subordinate incorporation of this discourse that will be on display throughout our text. While many of these elements are themselves hardly new, when the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái was composed, one could have found precious [[P1504]] few precedents that were more than a century or two old for treating these practices and values as forming complementary parts of single system, or for labelling that system ‚ÄúSmƒÅrta,‚Äù let alone for proclaiming, as HemƒÅdri is about to do, that this SmƒÅrta canon serves superior religious ends than ≈örauta knowledge and praxis. In other words, our author is seeking to identify as constitutive of what is orthoprax and orthodox a body of knowledge that in fact, among learned BrƒÅhma·πáas, has only just barely begun to pass into the realm of social acceptability.

As is often the case, before moving onto the HemƒÅdri‚Äôs use of the term i·π£·π≠ƒÅp≈´rta as comprising the union of ≈örauta and SmƒÅrta dharmas, with the SmƒÅrta elements taking conceptual as well as rhetorical priority over the ≈örauta, it is instructive to look to Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs K·πõtyakalptaru as a rhetorical foil. Lak·π£mƒ´dhara raises the matter of i·π£·π≠ƒÅp≈´rta in an extremely brief manner towards the beginning of his BrahmacƒÅrikƒÅ·πá·∏ça,203 in the context of the wider consideration of the different dimensions of var·πáƒÅ≈õramadharma that opens his nibandha. Citing the KƒÅlikƒÅ PurƒÅ·πáa, we are told that i·π£·π≠ƒÅp≈´rta is a dvandva compound made up of the Sm·πõta (and not SmƒÅrta) and ≈örauta dharmas as accepted by the ≈õi·π£·π≠as (≈õi·π£·π≠asammata). I·π£·π≠a is comprised of yaj√±a and so forth, while p≈´rta is made up of things like prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ, a term Lak·π£mƒ´dhara helpfully clarifies he understands as referring to the installation of the gods through methods such as jalƒÅ≈õayotsarga and so forth (devatƒÅprati·π£·π≠hƒÅjalƒÅ≈õayotsargƒÅdi). I·π£·π≠ƒÅp≈´rta, the text tells us, bestows both enjoyment and liberation (bhuktimukti), though it is [[P1505]] somewhat unclear whether these two outcomes are native to both domains, or if the p≈´rta gives ‚Äúworldly enjoyment‚Äù while the i·π£·π≠a results in heaven. Lak·π£mƒ´dhara next introduces his last proof text on this subject, drawn from the MahƒÅbhƒÅrata, which seeks to further clarify the practices we should group under the two terms. I·π£·π≠a, we learn, entails the observance of the agnihotra, the keeping of the fires within the homes, and dƒÅna. P≈´rta, in contrast, is comprised of setting up wells, step wells, and tanks, the building of temples to the gods, the giving of food, as well as the making of gardens. Rather than representing the foundational elements that make up ‚Äúpure‚Äù BrƒÅhma·πáical observances, the purpose of these passages in situ is merely establish in passing that these practices are accepted‚Äîand acceptable‚Äîamong twice-borns. Just as importantly, no attempt is made by our commentator or by his sources to align these two elements with specific canons of textual authorities, and this is in spite of the fact that the K·πõtyakalpataru, for the first time in Dharma≈õƒÅstra history, makes liberal use of PurƒÅ·πáic textual resources. Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs real objective here, as is made clear by the following citations from Manu and several other dharma works, help clarify the relationship between Var·πáƒÅ≈õramadharma as a system in the abstract and the specific dharmas allotted to particular var·πáa subsets, a topic that occupies him for quite a bit of time. With this in mind, we can return to considering how HemƒÅdri will make use of the very same sorts of raw materials.

H: Among those two, the i·π£·π≠a bestows the fruit of heaven and the p≈´rta is known to bestow mok·π£a. The i·π£·π≠a is defined to be the agnihotra, austerity, truth, and the recitation of all the Vedas, the honoring of guests and the vi≈õvadeva sacrifice. Wells, step wells, tanks and so forth, and the temples of the gods, bestowing food, and sweet [speech] are called p≈´rta. [Thus it is said:]

‚ÄúOne who makes eastern gardens or reservoirs, and likewise, one who makes trees grow, one who bestows women, builds bridges‚Äîhe obtains heaven, there is no doubt. Of whom there are tanks and so forth, and reservoirs resplendent with wells, and gardens, and shelters, the bestowing of food and who has lovely speech, there is wealth and the higher world. Those dedicated to building bridges and those dedicated to purifying tƒ´rthas, the makers of the wells and step wells, are released from the great [[P1506]] danger, and one who performs the improvement (sa·πÉskƒÅra) in the step wells, the gardens, and the tanks, and in the temples obtains the fruit belonging to original founders [of a settlement].‚Äù204

The first thing that should be obvious here is that there is a gaping theological divide between the ideas espoused by HemƒÅdri in his prose preface and his chosen proof texts.205 In the prose, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái seeks to align the p≈´rta practices of making ‚Äúwells, step wells, tanks and so forth, and the temples of the gods, and funding (people/endowments)‚Äù‚Äî what it defines as the SmƒÅrta dharmas‚Äîwith the obtaining of the higher order goal of liberation (mok·π£a). There is imagined to be a contrast here with i·π£·π≠a practices of ≈örauta religion, which at best merely lead to a temporary rebirth in one of the heavens. The problem here is that as HemƒÅdri‚Äôs very first proof text itself proclaims, ‚Äúhe obtains heaven, there is no doubt‚Äù (svargam ƒÅpnoty asa·πÉ≈õaya·∏•). In other words, his sources themselves draw no such distinction, for they treat the p≈´rta practices as simply, at best, another way to win heaven (svarga) or the other world (para·πÉ loka·πÉ) and in fact mostly commonly align them with the gratification of mundane human desires.

[[P1507]]

If we want then to understand why HemƒÅdri has chosen to foreground these precise proof texts, despite the fact that they explicitly undercut one of his major assertions, we have to look at the other rhetorical features evident in these particular passages that are hardly ubiquitous in this sort of discourse. And what we find, not merely in the verses we have just examined, but also in the remainder of the text that has not been provided up to here, is an atypical emphasis on ‚Äúpurifying‚Äù or ‚Äúrenovating‚Äù existing material culture, whether we are talking about water sources or ritual centers, as producing the same exact karmic benefits as building something new, a stance that travels together with the conspicuous absence of the usual invocation that the protection and preservation of existing endowments and structures on their own terms by political and religious actors is the highest form of dharma. In other words, these proof texts offer sanction for a massive project to refurbish ‚Äúpublic works‚Äù in a manner that brings existing institutions into closer alignment with the best practices and values of the people doing the ‚Äúrenovation.‚Äù Such an aim, which invokes that it is better to do things according to the right set of rules, provides a logical bridge to the next major focus in the paddhati, which seeks to align material institutional renewal with the instituting of new rule governed norms:

A man becomes devoid of greed by not being intoxicated/impassioned and by means of proper rules. Having given to twice-borns by whom the Veda is caused to flourish [lit., who are made venerable by the Vedas], he is released from sin. The one whose food, given with a pleased mind, is consumed by hundreds and hundreds of BrƒÅhma·πáas does not become an animal. By the giving of food, a man does not follow a bad course. Therefore, food is to be given in a manner that does not violate the rules. . . .

H: Thus, by informing about the p≈´rta, he continues to relate the installation (prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ) of the god in context.206

[[P1508]]

Essentially what HemƒÅdri is doing here is proposing that the procedures for establishing institutions are dependent on a new and necessary context. This context has as its constant point of reference norms that benefit or elevate BrƒÅhma·πáas directly as well as norms concerning what is ‚Äúproper‚Äù that BrƒÅhma·πáas alone are in a position to adjudicate. As we have seen in earlier chapters, this represents a marked departure not only from influential prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ texts such as the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, which recognize no such normativity, but also from the canon of PurƒÅ·πáic materials invoked by earlier Dharma≈õƒÅstrins such as Lak·π£mƒ´dhara. Ad hoc though they may be, these standards serve to harmonize these broader categories comprising different types of installation practices with HemƒÅdri‚Äôs reinvention of the philanthropic procedures in general as an almost exclusively BrƒÅhma·πáa-centric domain.

Since the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati has never been discussed anywhere in print, it is perhaps worthwhile to reflect a little on the structure and rhetorical strategies being deployed at least in the opening portions of the text, especially as much of what we see is less than transparent. Immediately following the theorization of i·π£·π≠ƒÅp≈´rta we have just been exploring, the text offers a small section on determining the timing for installing different types of deities (tatra prati·π£·π≠hƒÅkƒÅla·∏•). All of this material is HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own prose. The text then proceeds to offering a series of what it calls ‚Äúpaddhatis,‚Äù offering detailed accounts of the particular ritual sequences to be used for the different deities. Again, within these individual paddhatis, the material seems to be composed just for the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái. Stylistically, these portions of the text look very much like the later portions of the DƒÅnakha·πá·∏ça we looked at earlier, in which long run-on sentences organized around a chain of gerunds in order sequencing a set of activities are interspersed with fully scripted and frequently quite lengthy ritual pronouncements, outlining to the public everything that is going on in the ritual. Where these sub-paddhatis touch upon an issue of general relevance to all the procedures, either concerning the nature of a specific ritual agent or of the acts or objects he is making use of, however, the compositions interrupt the flow of the step-by-step instructions to offer topical treatments of the particular issue. In these cases, HemƒÅdri consistently supplies us with his understanding of the defining characteristics of the ritual actor and the meaning of the technical terms of art, an approach that often demands the insertion of proof texts from other sources. [[P1509]]

One very useful way to think about this repeated alteration between these long hyper formalized stretches of prose and the introduction of some of the authorities they lean on for support is as an act of code switching. Just as we saw when looking at the world of transactional quotidian Sanskrit in chapter two, an attentive reading of these stock preliminary summaries of long ritual sequences reveals that through them the author is repeatedly instantly rendering legible the key details that a professional ritualist is trained to extract and identify from his sources. Just as significantly, at the same time as this is being accomplished, these elements are being translated into a conceptual vocabulary shared with P≈´rva Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ. The rhetorical impact of this approach is to demonstrate over and over again that prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ ritual comes entirely embedded within and is inseparable from both a learned BrƒÅhma·πáa and specifically SmƒÅrta framework and ethos, and that this is the case even in places where for dozens of folios the text is offering his reader cosmological visualizations and reams of mantras extracted from different corpuses within the logically unrelated tradition of the MantramƒÅrga. For, as we are about to see, at the same time that a SmƒÅrta framework is being presupposed as indispensable, our Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati deliberately elides the tradition-specific theologies and methodologies pertaining to the veneration of the individual deities, such that, in the end, a functional equivalence is simply presumed between Vi·π£·πáu, ≈öiva, the Goddess, and S≈´rya in a manner that prefigures early modern pa√±cƒÅyatana p≈´jƒÅ. As we have seen in many places within the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, we can recognize the intention behind such efforts as an act of deliberate obfuscation, because sometimes HemƒÅdri‚Äôs redactions sloppily leave behind fragments from older value systems. With this background in mind, we are ready to turn to the text. [[P1510]]

Now there is the procedure for the ritual application regarding the installation of Vi·π£·πáu.207

The general (sƒÅmƒÅnya) rituals apply to all the gods (sarvadevƒÅnƒÅm), the particular (vi≈õe·π£a) is described here.208 [Now, the prayoga:]

Now, in the region (de≈õe) to the northeast of the ritual space (ƒÅgƒÅra) belonging to the yajamƒÅna, which is smeared with pure stuff [i.e., cow dung] and accompanied by a rangoli, he performs ritual sipping (ƒÅcamƒÅna) and is well ornamented with white garments and ointment with gold, silver, ku≈õa grass, water. . . . Having pronounced the occasion, on the astral conjunction that was indicated by the astrologer, for the sake of the completion of the intended ritual, which involves the installation of the gods or so forth, which is desired to be commenced without obstacles, and for the sake of satisfying wonder-dispensing Ga·πáe≈õa who is acting as a necessary subordinate component [utilized] at the time of the pacification of all the obstacles regarding all undertakings; [Then,] having offered gold, clothes, candana, pan, and fruit, having done the p≈´jƒÅ of the sun, having bestowed a tilak on the swami, having offered p≈´jƒÅ to MahƒÅga·πáapati, success will be obtained; [As per the quotation‚Äù]

‚ÄúFor a man who is desiring to do the prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ ritual of the gods, having produced the needed implements with regard to all the rituals of the yoginƒ´s. Haivng taken recourse to the five nights, the four nights, three nights, or even one night, such that on the final pak·π£a the karmasiddhi will take place, having considered the right day, directly, according to the ≈õƒÅstras, for the installation of the gods, having made offerings on the seven day from the day of the installation of the gods and so forth. . . he should practice the offering of the sprouts of all seeds of karma. Having approached the charming ≈õƒÅlƒÅ, having satisfied the ≈õilpin, having obtained image and indeed the li·πÖga of the ≈õilpins, having examined the goods/materials‚Äîone should mount it on the chariot and adorn it. . . .‚Äù Then, according to the vidhi, one should practice the incubating in water of the god.

On the day of the beginning of the prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ, having worshipped Ga·πáe≈õa, one should articulate the conditions for the accomplishment of the ritual. And, having worshipped the BrƒÅhma·πáas, having formed an intention for the ritual installation, one should practice the warding off.209

[[P1511]]

Deploying a terminology strongly reminiscent of the inscriptional record, HemƒÅdri begins by suggesting that while the core format for conducting a ritual installation is to be applied to ‚Äúall the gods,‚Äù a specialized or particular modification of these procedures is to be employed when the object of worship is Vi·π£·πáu. Having introduced this meta-rule, HemƒÅdri then proceeds on to offering a summary of the ritual activities that preface a ritual installation. Every element of his presentation translates these rituals‚Äîall of which originate outside any of the ritual realms that Vedic exegetical traditions would have recognized as legitimate‚Äîinto a Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ idiom. It is for this reason that at every step in the process, we are repeatedly reminded which ritual agent holds authority over which dimension of the ritual procedure, that the key rituals are labelled using technical terms, like karman, even when such designations would seem superfluous, and that we are constantly being explicitly told what purpose (artha) individual steps in the ritual will serve. The most elegant illustration of how Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsaka models are shaping this discourse is almost certainly its representation of the role of that Ga·πáe≈õa plays in the ritual proceedings, for HemƒÅdri takes great pains to demonstrate that he is not serving as ‚Äúa devatƒÅ‚Äù in the Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ sense, but is only acting as ‚Äúa necessary subordinate component (antara·πÖgabh≈´ta)‚Äù whose scope of influence is confined to the opening pacification rites. [[P1512]]

When we turn to the unspecified textual sources from which HemƒÅdri has analytically derived this ritual procedure, almost immediately, we see that the materials in use, though full of the same general details, have nothing to do with the ‚Äúspecial ritual‚Äù adaptation only to be used when installing Vi·π£·πáu. In fact, they seem to have been extracted from an explicitly ≈öƒÅkta ritual milieu that made use of non-standard Sanskrit, and within which rites for appeasing yoginƒ´s formed an integral component of prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ. Just as importantly, quite unlike HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own ritual program, this unknown source does not specify the nature and scope of authority of each of the kinds of ritual actors nor is it invested in imposing a hierarchy of significance among the different ritual segments, a central concern for the Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ exegete. In this alternatively theorized realm, ritual is just one damn thing after another, all of which are equally important.

The Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati is very long and much of it is concerned with ritual procedures whose ≈öƒÅkta analogues we have already discussed at some length in chapter 6, or which would be familiar to anyone who has spent time with prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ literature in general. So, much like good Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsakas, we are going to jump ahead to the mukhyadravya‚Äîor main ritual details‚Äî that matter to us. We have skipped ahead through all the ritual preliminaries and are now at the point where in any prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ rite, the ritual patron takes his seat, offers homage to the presiding religious authority, pronounces his ritual intention, and begins to act under the direction of the prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpanƒÅcƒÅrya.

H: Then, upon the bhadrapƒ´·π≠hƒÅ to the east of the ritual space (ƒÅgƒÅra) upon the darbha grass, the yajamƒÅna along with his wife, having entered facing east and having done three rounds of prƒÅ·πáƒÅyƒÅma accompanied by the wife, the one who has begun the sequence should make an intention:

‚ÄúToday, proceeded by the destruction of sin of myself, of my father, of my mother, and of twenty-one other people, including myself, and of the ten generations before and of those after, I will do the installation of Vi·π£·πáu, in so far as it is possible, according to place, time and so forth.‚Äù

H: It [the ritual procedure and announcement] is then done the same way regarding another god, and [also] with regards to dredging of the wells, in deploying the various [[P1513]] words and so forth, it is to be known [as being the correct procedure in all such cases].210

If we go by our documentary records, the medieval Deccan had a mostly generic notion of what constitutes a patron. In practice, this social space tended to be occupied perhaps most often not by kings or BrƒÅhma·πáa ritualists but by merchants, military commanders, and big men from a range of caste backgrounds. In HemƒÅdri‚Äôs paddhati, in contrast, the patron is not only identified rhetorically with the Vedic role of the yajamƒÅna, but he is required to assume an affect and ritual role explicitly derived from that of the twice-born sponsor of a Vedic sacrifice, right down to being accompanied in all his efforts by his wife. The mere inclusion of a wife as a necessary contributor to the ritual in and of itself deliberately narrows the scope of who can be a patron, as it effectively eliminates ascetics of any sort and young warriors on the make‚Äî to say nothing of women themselves acting as agents‚Äîthree demographics that surface with considerable frequency in earlier sources. Furthermore, once we learn that the paradigm under discussion applies to all acts of institutionalization, even digging wells or setting up aqueducts, the representation of the yajamƒÅna as ‚Äútwice-born‚Äù effectively shuts down one of the primary means that ≈õilpins/ojjas had of leaving their individual mark upon the social landscape through sponsoring small scale public works projects. As HemƒÅdri is about to show us, these consequences are by no means unintended, but are part and parcel of efforts to inscribe a new form of credentialization process that is designed to exclude the previous officials who would have played these roles, from the very heart of the ritual. It is for this reason that the forming [[P1514]] of the ritual intention is now to be followed by ‚Äúthe choosing‚Äù of the ƒÅcƒÅryas, a process that within our text involves laying out the standards for their selection, and in ritual itself demands the public pronouncement of their credentials.

H: He should choose the ƒÅcƒÅryas and so forth according to the forms as will be described. [As per the citation:]

‚ÄúAn intelligent one with a good form, who is skilled, who is pure, who has good characteristics who knows the Vedas, the hot·πõ should begin the hotra enjoined by the Vedas. The BrƒÅhma·πáa who possesses ≈öruti‚Äîand even one who is solely intent on adjudicating the Vedas‚Äîone who is established in ritual, established in tapas, and likewise established in gnosis; one who is not attached, one who has offspring, one without wounds or disease; he is not violent, he is not ego driven, he is intent on the practice of rituals always. He has arisen ‚Äúfrom‚Äù ƒÄryavƒÅrta, and likewise from the land of the black deer.

The ·πõtvijs are endowed with all these characteristics. They are skilled in the rituals for prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ and subordinate to the ƒÅcƒÅryas. The ·πõgvedic priest, the adhvaryu, the singer of the SƒÅma Veda, and the even the Atharvan are [examples of] the ·πõtvijs, knowers of the Tantras and skilled in the prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ rituals.‚Äù

H: These two [the ·πõtvij and ƒÅcƒÅrya] are endowed with the characteristics: they are in the BrƒÅhma·πáa assembly [i.e., the council], and they are skilled in all rituals.211

[[P1515]]

In place of having a prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpanƒÅcƒÅrya who directs the ≈õilpins and serves as their guru, HemƒÅdri envisions the installation rituals as being governed by a two-tier managerial system. The majority of the ritual action, we are told, is the responsibility of figures called ·πõtvijs, who are legally subordinated (adhƒ´na) to a governing ƒÅcƒÅrya, in the same manner a master has sovereignty over a servant. Typically speaking, the word ·πõtvij is a term of art first found in the early Veda that refers to a ritualist who has mastered the ·πög Veda. For HemƒÅdri, however, the term is expanded to include those trained as SƒÅma, Yajur, and even Atharva Vedic ritualists, as well as those who merely ‚Äúadjudicate the Veda,‚Äù in other words, P≈´rva and Uttara Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsakas, even if they do not themselves practice the rituals. Instead of themselves being born in ƒÄryavƒÅrta, as HemƒÅdri clarifies elsewhere, the ritualists need merely to be able to claim that they arise from people who had their origins there. At the same time, just as we saw with the yajamƒÅna, the imposition of the requirement that ritualists be married householders with children works to exclude other classes of professional religious authorities, including the yogins who earlier sources had been viewed as playing an integral role in many sorts of installations. And since prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ refers not merely to installing deities or even building temples but to virtually all forms of institution building‚Äîright down to making gardens and planting trees‚Äîthe impact of these changes, just in and of themselves, is to substitute a world full of things made by diverse social communities for a world where most things are made by or under the direction of BrƒÅhma·πáas. But in fact HemƒÅdri takes things a step further, for in a manner that will be instantly familiar to anyone who has spent time reading Dharma≈õƒÅstra literature, even just Manu, after outlining a whole bunch of best case scenarios for what one should hope for in a prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ ritualist, in prose crafted by our author himself seemingly supported by no particular proof texts, the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati then offers a bare bones standard for what qualifies one to be a ·πõtvij or ƒÅcƒÅrya. The credentials that really matter are rather telling, for they are simply that the person is skilled in all the rituals, which earlier we learned entailed them ‚Äúknowing the Tantras‚Äù while also being members in good standing ‚Äúwithin the BrƒÅhma·πáa assembly‚Äù (brahmasadas). In other words, in cases where other types of BrƒÅhma·πáas are not available, institution building is to be overseen as well executed by the same social elite that make up the council of juridical authorities. The same dynamic is even more in evidence when [[P1516]] it comes to the replacement for the prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpanƒÅcƒÅrya, now called the sadasya, whose mode of self-presentation instantly conveys his extreme degree of privilege.

Then, he should choose the sadasya and so forth with finger rings, hand ornaments and necklaces, gold earrings. . . . Then facing north, seated on his own ƒÅsana, the one who knows VedƒÅnta, arising from ƒÄryavƒÅrta, with a shaved head, devoted to the PurƒÅ·πáas, he is extremely skilled in the main rituals; he is smiling, and he has deep knowledge.

Having adorned the sadasya, who has conquered the senses, who knows the mantras, and has white clothing, with pearl ornaments, having worshipped, with effort, the sadasya who knows the ≈õƒÅstras with clothes with ornaments and with smearing with good smelling things and garlands.212

[[P1517]]

Thoroughly decked out in the gold HemƒÅdri celebrates doling out en masse to his supporters, here the sadasya seems very much the representative of the nouveau riche elite that he most certainly would have been. The term sadasya itself had previously referred to the Vedic ritualist presiding over a sattra sacrifice. Here, in contrast, the sadasya is celebrated not, as we would anticipate, as an embodiment of the Vedic a·πÖgas or of ≈õƒÅstra, but simply for his mastery of VedƒÅnta and devotion to the PurƒÅ·πáas. As we will explore in the next chapter at some length, even as late as the twelfth century‚Äîeven within Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs K·πõtyakalpataru‚Äî these are perhaps the two least prestigious bodies of knowledge in South Asia so far as the traditions of classical ≈õƒÅstra and classical Tantra were concerned. There is thus every indication that people credentialing themselves by claiming mastery over two corpuses of knowledge that most traditional pandits and ritualists viewed as somewhere between irrelevant and stupid need to be understood as emerging from a different BrƒÅhma·πáical demographic than that of the esteemed old families. In fact, despite the almost over the top peppering of lofty sounding Vedic terms of art found throughout the prose of our paddhati, the actual contents of the ritual procedures prescribes reveals such rhetoric to be a form of neo classicism, spoken by people who were no more living inside of a Vaidika mindset than Renaissance era princes who obsessively studied the rhetoric of Cicero from childhood onward can be characterized as authentically embodying the ethos of Republican and Imperial Rome. When this veneer is peeled back and you look at the actual content of the rites themselves, they are thoroughly saturated with what are unmistakably ≈öƒÅkta and ≈öaiva conceptual models. [[P1518]]

Having bowed to [the sadasya], according to injunction, one must perform the offering of the self to him.

‚ÄúYou are our guru, you are mother and father, you are the Lord, you are the recitation! By your grace, O ·πõ·π£i among BrƒÅhma·πáas, who are unwavering, may everything be achieved by the mind [lit., gone to my mind]. For the sake of release from calamity, do the ritual action called the karuyaj√±a with the ·πõtvijs who are pure, disciplined, and well equipped!‚Äù With regard to the ritual called the vi·π£·πáuprati·π£·π≠hƒÅ, to have accomplished the ritual installation of you people, I choose you of x-gotra of insert x-≈õarman, with these clothes, ornaments, betel, and so forth [to be] sadasya!

The reply is: ‚ÄúI am chosen!‚Äù Furthermore:

In this ritual, in order to do the prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ ritual (karma) I choose you as ƒÅcƒÅrya with the clothes, ornaments, betel and so forth. You, sir, are the embodiment of the form of the mantra here on earth, which is the destroyer of the flood of sa·πÉsƒÅra and so forth.213

[[P1519]]

In this passage, we catch a glimpse of the incongruous appropriation of the dominant mode of sociality within the medieval ≈õivapura by a newly arisen SmƒÅrta BrƒÅhma·πáa professional class. In order to make sense of what sort of rhetorical work this appropriation accomplishes, it is worthwhile to first briefly reflect on the paradigm that it is supplanting. Within the realm of the ≈õivapura, we need to remember, professionals such as artisans and performers, whose livelihoods were effectively inextricable from their inculcation within Tantric knowledge systems, as well as Tantric adepts, had typically undergone ritual initiations that rendered them subject (adhƒ´na) to the initiating guru. This teacher was from thenceforth to be viewed as ≈öiva himself in human form and became the final juridical and spiritual authority in their lives. On both theological and pragmatic grounds, in emic terms, this role was seen as carrying with it substantial responsibilities, for the teacher was frequently seen as taking on both the literal and karmic debts of his disciples. In short, every element in this formulation emphasizes that what was being established is a lasting bond that fundamentally changed the initiate‚Äôs status. Depending on which tradition within the MantramƒÅrga we are discussing, at the time of initiation, the guru either was to extract the soul of the initiate and infuse them with the mantra of lineage or, in the case of some Kaula traditions, he was enter into the body of disciple and transform him or her from within by leaving a seed representation of himself inside the channels and subtle body. Within the ≈öƒÅkta transmissions especially, of the sort that were codependent on the artisan and performance traditions so relevant to the present context, disciples were to venerate and visualize their initiatory teachers and their lineages on a daily basis as part of acts of routine worship. As the KumƒÅrikƒÅ Kha·πá·∏ça of the ManthƒÅna Bhairava makes plain, this insertion of the veneration of the teacher as a structuring element even extended to the celebration of the birthdays of the lineage heads as a major calendrical festival. In other words, in very concrete ways, ‚Äúthe surrender‚Äù of the soul to a teacher was rooted in a lasting relationship, whether real or imagined, instantiated through sustained and repeated practice. [[P1520]]

Despite the fact that our text is mining what conceptually speaking are MantramƒÅrga resources, as imagined by the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati the social role of the sadasya diverges radically from these older expectations. As HemƒÅdri envisions him, instead of having a preexisting personal or ritual relationship with the patron sponsoring an act of institution building or with the various professionals who will execute the task, the sadasya is simply an elite member of the BrƒÅhma·πáical jurist class whose services have been procured temporarily for the occasion. For that matter, despite the fact that during the ritual they are deemed subordinated (adhƒ´na) to the sadasya, the ·πõtvij ƒÅcƒÅryas are also merely hired help with no preexisting connection to either their temporary master, the artisans, or the community. In other words, what our text is encouraging is essentially a ritualized surrender of personal and political authority to a stranger, quite likely serving in the local sabhƒÅ, without any pretense even of an imagined reciprocity. Indeed, in the formalized script provided by HemƒÅdri, the patron is to begin the act of surrendering to this figure‚Äîwho serves as a stand in for the Vedic ·πõ·π£is‚Äî even before he has given his assent and chosen the sadasya as the designated authority. As we can see, this choice itself is executed in an idiom akin to a contractual establishment of a patron client relationship drawn from the world of ephemeral quotidian Sanskrit textuality. As the formalized passage makes plain, the sadasya‚Äôs authority is purely a function of his office, not the product of personal charisma, ascetic accomplishments, or even active sustained participation within the knowledge systems of the MantramƒÅrga. In other words, not only do the sadasya and his ƒÅcƒÅryas potentially lack any meaningful relationships with the other social agents involved in the prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ ritual, but they also even lack a meaningful relationship with the Tantras themselves. In other words, they are precisely the sort of sthƒÅpaka the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa had anticipated with such agitation, as bringing terror and ruin, one who is ‚Äúwithout the [[P1521]] ƒÅgama,‚Äù and who seeks to organize rites using ‚Äúa common procedure,‚Äù instead of entering into each individual ritual occasion aligned with what is prescribed by ‚Äúthe different doctrines.‚Äù Nonetheless, in the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati, these agents, who by traditional standards would be utterly lacking in adhikƒÅra, for they have not immersed themselves in the modes of praxis that produce such a habitus, have acquired for themselves the quintessentially MantramƒÅrga derived status of being like ‚Äúgod himself,‚Äù or ‚Äúthe embodiments of the form of mantra here on earth.‚Äù Without engaging in any efforts to actually bestow their grace or concrete favor upon the assembled public‚Äîfor rewards in this system are reserved almost exclusively for BrƒÅhma·πáas‚Äî the SmƒÅrta jurist has usurped the position of the KƒÅlamukha sthƒÅpanƒÅcƒÅrya carrying out the ‚Äúkaruyaj√±a‚Äù with all its rhetorical trappings. Deploying what is fundamentally originally a Tantric or even AtimƒÅrgic repertoire to rather new even antithetical ends, the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati transmutes each act of institution building from an instantiation of sovereignty within a pluralistic world order to an affirmation of the subordination all religious activities to the state and its representatives.

The considerable irony is that at the very same time that Tantric ritual and conceptual materials are operating as the engine that powers all of its rituals, the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati seeks to subordinate these elements both logically and rhetorically under a mountain of signifiers that while functionally still deriving from the Tantras are marked as performatively SmƒÅrta. As an illustration of how this works, let us skip past the rest of the ritual preliminaries and turn to the moment, immediately preceding the installation rituals themselves, when the sadasya and his ƒÅcƒÅryas invoke the ma·πá·∏çala and prepare themselves to enter into the ritual space. When it comes to the ma·πá·∏çala itself, the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati offers us two options, both of which are treated as functional equivalents. If the deity and its pantheon is aligned with Vai·π£·πáava [[P1522]] elements (vai·π£·πáave), one is to construct a vƒÅru·πáama·πá·∏çala, while in cases where ≈öaiva imagery prevails (≈õaive) one is to construct a sarvatobhadrama·πá·∏çala.214 After the text explains how each of these two basic formats can be executed at three different scales and degrees of elaboration, much like we saw in the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, there is a brief worship of the stylus (kha·π≠ikƒÅ) and measuring thread (s≈´tra).215 With the tools of his craft prepared, the ritualists then turn to making their own bodies suitable to the task at hand. As HemƒÅdri explains:

H: Thus, having constructed the auspicious ma·πá·∏çala according to the emanation (s·πõ·π£·π≠i) and/or dissolution (sa·πÉhƒÅra) sequences, having constructed the divinity of one‚Äôs own body (tanudaivƒ´) by means of the mat·πõka nyƒÅsa and so forth, one should begin the p≈´jƒÅ, nyƒÅsa, and so forth:

‚ÄúOne should sustain (dhƒÅrayet) the concentration exercise (dhƒÅra·πáƒÅ) directly, which destroys the sins of the self. The yau bƒ´ja is situated in the cakra of the navel. It consists of smoke and heat and wind [or smoke and fierce wind]. Through meditation, one should separate all sin from the body. Established in the midst of the lotus of the heart, one should recollect the one who has a form made of splendor (tejor≈´pam). Above and below and horizontal from the navel, one should incinerate impurity by means of the rays.

One should meditate on the form of the moon‚Äî an ocean of nectar established in the sky‚Äîwhich is the syllable vau. The intelligent one should cause the entire body to delight with the winds(?) (conj. from vyƒÅdhibhir, ‚Äúdiseases‚Äù)216 of the heart lotus, which are creeping about all the nƒÅ·∏çƒ´s by the path within the interior of the su·π£umnƒÅ. Thus, having purified his own body, the one who knows the supreme reality, by means of the supreme bƒ´ja, [understands/meditates upon] himself as having the nature of the universe, born from the sequence of the sprouts and so forth. Having meditated upon himself as having this nature, then he should commence the nyƒÅsa.‚Äù217

[[P1523]]

HemƒÅdri here has prescribed that the sadasya and his ƒÅcƒÅryas, who we must remember are not initiates in a Tantric knowledge system, are to perform what is instantly recognizable as a form of bh≈´ta≈õ≈´ddhi ritual that has as its express purpose the quintessentially Tantric divinization of the body.218 The practice is supposed to commence with a series of focused visualizations practices (dhƒÅra·πáƒÅ) directed at different points in the body, beginning with the smoke within navel and culminating within the ‚Äúsky‚Äù or the center of the head. Derived from the traditions of ≈öaiva yoga, these linked visualizations serve two purposes. On the one hand, as HemƒÅdri tells us, they separate the body from ‚Äúsin.‚Äù Just like we find in the ≈öaiva Tantras, as opposed to being framed in terms of accumulated karma, HemƒÅdri here treats sin (pƒÅpa) as if it is a substance, akin to the notion of an ontic stain (mala) that has to be forcibly burned out of the body. While this purification process, engendered by one type of dhƒÅra·πáƒÅ, is unfolding, our text simultaneously prescribes a second order of visualization practice. Commencing with an initial ritual identification of the practitioner with the divine splendor (tejor≈´pƒ´) that resides within the center of the heart (this does not seem to be connected to a specific bƒ´ja), the practitioner is then to meditate on the moon within his forehead which has taken on the form of the Tantric seed syllable vau. While the text is not clear about what happens next (though based on parallels in other discourses, we would anticipate the body is flooded with nectar), apparently through the manipulation of an unspecified ‚Äúsupreme bƒ´ja,‚Äù the sadasasya and his ƒÅcƒÅryas identify themselves with the universe in its entirety. This stage of the practice then concludes, as the practitioner makes the essentially Kaula-informed conceptual move of reversing the polarity between subject and object by taking this identification with the universe in its entirety as the object of his meditative focus. [[P1524]]

Before we can turn to examining how HemƒÅdri appropriates and subverts the traditional methods of the MantramƒÅrga that accomplish the divinization of the body, it is useful to orient the reader to just what such methods entailed and how they would have deployed in a more typical setting. Now that he has replaced his ordinary human body with a loosely characterized divinized body, this is the point in the practice that we would anticipate that‚Äîat least within a Tantric sƒÅdhanƒÅ‚Äîthat the practitioner will begin to reinscribe his form and subjectivity with content specific to his initiatory tradition. Just as will happen in the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati, this task is accomplished through the practice of ritual imposition (nyƒÅsa), which typically involves the projection of visualized images or seed syllables onto the different parts of the human hand or body. While in some cases, as in the construction of the ‚Äúhand of ≈öiva,‚Äù where bƒ´jas are installed on the five fingers, these nyƒÅsa practices can be localized and brief, especially in the later classical ≈öƒÅkta Tantras aligned with KubjikƒÅ, and the emergent tradition of ≈örƒ´vidyƒÅ, there is a tendency to ‚Äústack‚Äù independent nyƒÅsa sequences, often derived originally from different traditions, one on top of one another.

The nyƒÅsa procedures HemƒÅdri teaches in the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati are considerably more elaborate than similar practices one finds in contemporary Tantric sources. Thus, while the KumƒÅrika Kha·πá·∏ça of KubjikƒÅ traditions, for instance, limits itself to a six-phase nyƒÅsa, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái in fact prescribes a sixteen-fold nyƒÅsa.219 Introduced just before the Sadasya, the ƒÅcƒÅryas and the crew make their initial contact with the image that is to be installed, the practice contains the following components: 1) pra·πáava-nyƒÅsa, 2) vyƒÅh·πõti-nyƒÅsa, 3) mƒÅt·πõkƒÅ-nyƒÅsa, 4) kƒÅla-nyƒÅsa, 5) var·πáa-nyƒÅsa, 6) toya-nyƒÅsa, 7) veda-nyƒÅsa, 8) vairƒÅj- nyƒÅsa, 9) devayonƒ´-nyƒÅsa, 10) dvƒÅda≈õadevatƒÅ-nyƒÅsa, 11) kratu-nyƒÅsa, 12) gu·πáa-nyƒÅsa, 13) [[P1525]] ƒÅyudhƒÅ-nyƒÅsa, 14) ≈õakti-nyƒÅsa, 15) mantra[mƒÅrga]nyƒÅsa, 16) jƒ´va-nyƒÅsa. We will explore some of these in more detail in a minute. As you may already be noticing just based on the names, large portions of this practice incorporate what were originally entire AtimƒÅrga and MantramƒÅrga ritual systems. But, these older practices are embedded alongside and often beneath ‚Äúa new revelation,‚Äù to which our author gives conceptual priority. And as we are about to see, that tradition, much of which seems to have invented just for this occasion, celebrates the twofold division of the sanƒÅtanadharmas that proclaims its allegiance to a fusion of ≈örauta and especially SmƒÅrta tradition.

Now, given their understanding of how mantras work, this SmƒÅrta appropriation of the practice of nyƒÅsa would seem to pose a serious conceptual problem. NyƒÅsa, on the simplest level, involves the manipulation of bƒ´jas as well as mantra sequences as means of imbuing the practitioner‚Äôs body and subjectivity with the powers inherent in those syllables. The practice makes sense precisely because it is predicated on believing that the mantras are themselves the deities. But this complete identification of deities and principles with non-semantic Sanskrit bƒ´jas is essentially a Tantric idea for which the newly codified SmƒÅrta system, which as we have seen throughout this chapter, grounds its theory of mantra in resources it borrows at least in part from the Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsakas, has no equivalent. In Mƒ´mƒÅ·πÉsƒÅ, in contrast, the meaning of mantras is mostly irrelevant, for while the individual words within a mantra are supposed to all have meaning, even if some of them have been lost over time, taken as a unit they serve an indicative function, pointing the ritualist towards identifying the deity governing the ritual. As we are going to see, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs SmƒÅrta nyƒÅsas simply repeatedly center litanies of proper names and nouns‚Äîonly some of which refer to deities‚Äîbut all of which are indicative of the same interpretive context, namely, the world of the sanƒÅtanadharmas. Succinctly, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs [[P1526]] sixteen-fold nyƒÅsa envisions that the practitioner is to travel about in a vehicle that, while powered by the technology of the MantramƒÅrga, has its every surface thoroughly encompassed by SmƒÅrta advertising.

In our exploration of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs sixteen-fold nyƒÅsa, we will primarily attend to seams in the text indicative of its heterogenous nature and diverse points of origin. Thus, for example, the second nyƒÅsa, or vyƒÅh·πõti-nyƒÅsa, prescribes the imposition of the different subcomponents of the quintessentially BrƒÅhma·πáical Savitra GƒÅyatrƒ´ mantra, on the feet, heart, and in the cavity of the skull. Despite the plausibly ≈öƒÅkta connotations of the name mƒÅ·π≠rkƒÅ-nyƒÅsa, the third stage in the practice simply involves the installation of the letters of the Sanskrit alphabet onto the body, beginning with the vowels and culminating with consonant clusters arranged in terms of varga. It is here that things become increasingly strange. In the kƒÅla-nyƒÅsa, first the planets, constellations, other important astrological elements, and the months of the year are mapped onto the human body, and then the process is repeated using the different yugas and other time frames. Apart from the absence of any bƒ´jas, this could almost be a cousin of the much- understudied kƒÅlacakra practices taught in many of the ≈öƒÅkta Tantras. The same, however, cannot be said for the var·πáa-nyƒÅsa that follows immediately afterwards, for here the meaning of var·πáa is quite simply the identities of BrƒÅhma·πáa, K·π£atriya, and so forth, all of which are to be mapped onto the body. Not only do we see here a literal grafting of a part of the social order onto the divinized body that is supposed to be identical with the universe, a practice of course as old as the Puru·π£as≈´kta, but the text even introduces post-Vedic conceptual components integral to policing caste relations as key elements of the practice. Thus, caste mixing in general (sa·πÖkara) as well as anuloma marriages‚Äîwhere the male is higher caste than his partner‚Äîare ritually projected onto soles of the practitioner‚Äôs right and left feet, assigned inauspicious [[P1527]] places right alongside the animals of the village (grƒÅmapa≈õu) and those of the wilderness (ara·πáyapa≈õu) in what is essence a representation of the great chain of being. Following a brief projection of forms of water (toya-nyƒÅsa), that descends from the clouds (megha) down to the ocean (samudrƒÅ), the text next introduces a ‚Äúveda-nyƒÅsa.‚Äù As we might by now expect, here HemƒÅdri teaches that the eighth imposition involves superimposing the four Vedas, the vidyƒÅsthƒÅnas, and the disciplines of ≈õƒÅstra, beginning from the ·πög Veda and concluding with Nƒ´ti and Va≈õya≈õƒÅstra (perhaps referring to indrajƒÅla?) onto the divinized body, again suggesting an implicit argument that an emerging vision of BrƒÅhma·πáical normativity is integral to all viable forms of ritual power.220

The eleventh stage in the practice, the so-called kratu-nyƒÅsa, makes such a very un- Vedic use of ostensibly Vaidika materials that it reads like a modern exercise in cultural appropriation, every bit as incongruous‚Äîeven outrageous‚Äîto a ‚Äúreal ≈örauta ritualist‚Äù as when yoga studios put an m≈´rti of Ga·πáe≈õa right above the toilet or an attendee at Bhakti Fest gets the GƒÅyatrƒ´ tattooed above her belly button. [[P1528]]

On the forehead, [place] the horse sacrifice. On the upper palate, place the human sacrifice. On the face, the royal sacrifice; on the throat, the cow sacrifice. . . . In the navel, the ahƒ´nas (sattra); on the knees the sarvajits; on the penis, the agni·π£·π≠oma; on the other side of the penis, the atirƒÅtra; the aptoryƒÅma on the two thighs; the ·π£o·∏ça≈õins go on the flanks; the utkthya on the left shank; the vƒÅjapeya on the right shank; the atyagni·π£·π≠oma on the right arm; the caturmƒÅsyas on the left; the sautrƒÅma·πái on the hands; the par≈õva-i·π£·π≠is on the fingers; the dar≈õap≈´r·πáamƒÅsas on the eyes; the sarva-i·π£·π≠is in the pores; the syllables svƒÅhƒÅ, and va·π£at on the two arms; the pa√±camahƒÅyaj√±as beneath the soles of the feet; and, the ƒÅhavanƒ´ya fire on the head; and the dak·π£i·πáƒÅgni in the heart; and the gƒÅrhapati fire on the arms; and the vedƒ´ in the heart; the pravargya on the ornaments. . . . darbha grass upon the hair. . . .221

[[P1529]]

HemƒÅdri here appears to have taken several previously discrete canons of Vedic sacrifices, inclusive of many exceptionally arcane and rarely practiced forms of Soma sacrifices, and strung them all together, filling in the gaps with stray elements used in sacrifice, such as the darbha grass, much in the same manner as one would decorate a Christmas tree. And just as holiday ornaments may have spent many years in the attic gathering dust, only to be taken out for special occasions, much the same is the case with HemƒÅdri‚Äôs ≈örautism, which amounts to a miscellany of very different things haphazardly assembled together on account of the fact that they all look decoratively Vedic. Though on the surface, the correlation of sacrifices to specific parts of the body appears evocative of the bandhu logic of the BrƒÅhma·πáas, when one looks at the actual contents of these supposed parallels individually, they seem neither to be the product of careful reflection on points of commonality nor a continuation of older formations from the BrƒÅhma·πáa literature.
