Indeed, the randomness and carelessness of many of these selections‚Äîwhy select the generic ahƒ´na without representing the generic sattra [[P1530]] sacrifice?‚Äîwhy invoke the virtually nonexistent term sarvajit as opposed to the standard vi≈õvajit?‚Äîall suggest a certain superficiality in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs command of ≈örauta knowledge.

This shallowness in our author‚Äôs command over traditional ‚ÄúBrƒÅhma·πáical‚Äù materials is starkly in contrast to the intimate understanding and precision that orders the sequences in the nyƒÅsa that immediately follow it, wherein the materials being used are derived from the worlds of the AtimƒÅrga and MantramƒÅrga, both of whose canons HemƒÅdri has surreptitiously mined and cited without attribution throughout the paddhati. During the next five nyƒÅsas, each of which are given in both ≈öaiva and Vai·π£·πáava variants, the practitioner invokes the parivƒÅra, weapons, and attributes of the supreme deity (either SadƒÅ≈õiva or VƒÅsudeva) and constructs the throne upon which the deity sits, which is perched on top of the hierarchy of worlds. Here, for example, is a small selection from the nyƒÅsa of the gu·πáas, the preliminary act in the sequence, offered with occasional ≈öaiva and Vai·π£·πáava variants:

o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ prak·πõtitattvƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ prak·πõtitattvƒÅdhipataye pitƒÅmahƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ hƒÅ[·πÉ] puru·π£atattvƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ puru·π£atattvƒÅdhipataye vi·π£·πáave nama·∏• /

o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ sadƒÅ≈õivatattvƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ sadƒÅ≈õivatattvƒÅdhipataye aje≈õƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ kƒÅlatattvƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ kƒÅlatattvƒÅdhipataye ·πõtudhvajƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ vidyƒÅtattvƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ vidyƒÅtattvƒÅdhipataye vi·π£·πáave nama·∏• /

o·πÉ hƒÅ·πÉ cakrƒÅdyƒÅyudhacihnebhyo nama·∏• /

o·πÉ sa·πÉ sattvƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ ra·πÉ rajase nama·∏• / o·πÉ ta·πÉ tamase nama·∏• / o·πÉ ra·πÉ vahnima·πá·∏çalƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ a·πÉ arkama·πá·∏çalƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ va·πÉ somama·πá·∏çalƒÅya nama·∏• /

≈õaive a·π£·π≠atri·πÉ≈õat[t]attvebhyo nama·∏• / a·π£·π≠atri[·πÉ]≈õat[t]attvƒÅdhipebhyo nama·∏• /222

[[P1531]]

After having installed the elements and the tanmƒÅtras, which, apart from occasional divergences on who is the presiding deity, are treated as identical in the two systems, as we ascend up to the veneration of more subtle ontic levels, the text begins to offer us two sets of options, both of them within a Tantric framework. Rather atypically, once we get past puru·π£a and prak·πõti, the text places sadƒÅ≈õivattatva as subordinated to kƒÅla and vidyƒÅ, each of which in turn is subordinated to Vi·π£·πáu and his weapons. In this shared fused cosmology, however, the two founts of theistic religion themselves take second place to the elements, and the three domains of the fire, the sun, and the moon, so central to post-classical ≈öƒÅkta traditions. The section concludes by indicating that ≈öaivas can emend this visualization (≈õaive‚Äî a·π£·π≠atri·πÉ≈õatattvebhyo nama·∏•) to incorporate some more elements of their traditions. In doing so, however it invokes, not the expected thirty-six but thirty-eight tattvas, which are to be superimposed ‚Äúon top‚Äù of the already invoked sƒÅmƒÅnya Sa·πÉkhyƒÅ-derived map of the cosmos. At our current state of knowledge, identifying HemƒÅdri‚Äôs exact sources is not yet possible, but it is telling that Abhinavagupta, who also knows of this variation in the enumeration of ontic levels, identifies tattvas 37 and 38, respectively with Bhairavatattva and KƒÅlasa·πÉkar·π£i·πáƒ´tattva, strongly suggesting this system‚Äôs point of origin in the scriptures of the pre-philosophical KƒÅlƒ´ Krama, perhaps among materials that make their way into the later divisions of the JayadrathayƒÅmala.

Very much taking the opposite stance of tradition specific sectarianism, when HemƒÅdri turns to the question of envisioning the presiding deities, he reframes them in a manner that emphasizes points of commonality. For example, he suggests that the core theological principles that support each version of the throne‚Äîdharma, j√±ƒÅna, vairƒÅgya, and ai≈õvarya‚Äî are identical for ≈öaivas and Vai·π£·πáavas. He even suggests that, despite divergences in the names and imagery the weapons and the entourages associated with the two pantheons, as they are made up of an equal number of components, we should understand them as operating as tradition specific optionalities that are functionally equivalent.223 In both cases, again, our author has rearranged these elements to maximize the appearance of apparent commonalities. Within such a framework, the text is implicitly arguing being Vai·π£·πáava or being ≈öaiva is primarily evocative not of distinctive worldviews, but different approaches to ritual style. [[P1533]]

Just as this delimitation of pantheons‚Äîjust the two options with minimal internal variation‚Äî offers us functionally analogous parallel sets of preferences with minor variations, so too are the mantra systems deployed by the practitioner during the sixteen-fold nyƒÅsa upon his own body largely mirrored in the ritual mechanics that the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati prescribes for preparation of bases and the installation of images. As the final stage of the sixteen-part nyƒÅsa, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái teaches what it calls the jƒ´va-nyƒÅsa‚Äîthe ritual installation of the soul. The same procedure in an expanded form also is deployed during the act most commonly called prƒÅ·πáaprati·π£·π≠hƒÅ, when the deity is made to take residence in the image, what our text calls pratimƒÅjƒ´vanyƒÅsa. In a substantial terminological departure from what we typically find in the classical Tantras, repudiating one of the core theological claims of a ≈öƒÅkta worldview of reality pervaded by powers (≈õakti) in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati, the sadasya, ƒÅcƒÅrya, [[P1534]] yajamƒÅna, and ≈õilpins are never depicted manipulating or embodying ‚Äúpowers.‚Äù Instead, as they work their craft, they fuse their subjectivities with atom-like ‚Äúsouls‚Äù (jƒ´vas). As the text tells us, these souls come to us accompanied by adjuncts or impositions; the word often used is upƒÅdhi, a term of art borrowed from VedƒÅnta that, while not integral to the nature of souls themselves, result in the manifestation of specific qualities and ontic modalities (bhƒÅvas). The manner in which one works with these principles is the same regardless of whether the site into which they will be made to enter is the human body or an object, and is also independent of the apparent nature of the deity being installed. Through a preliminary practice of prƒÅ·πáƒÅyƒÅma, the ritualists cause different breaths into enter into distinctive channels.224 Using the pra·πáava mantras, the practitioner brings the unmarked puru·π£a who is a pervader (vyƒÅpin) to rest in the cavity within the eight-petalled lotus in the heart. Through the use of a whole array of mantras, the puru·π£a is then ‚Äúmade‚Äù into a jƒ´va through the superimposition of series of qualities, including the tanmƒÅtras and the elements, the jƒ´vƒÅtman, and paramƒÅtman. The ritualist joins (sa·πÉyojya) his own self to the image, and the pu·πá·∏çarƒ´kƒÅtman is then invoked from his heart into the heart of the image. The ritualist now makes himself identical in his bhƒÅva with the witness of all things (sarvasƒÅk·π£i·πáa·πÉ bhƒÅvayitvƒÅ).225 This enables the installation of something called the sarvƒÅtman, followed by the puru·π£ƒÅtman, an act that is accompanied by the recitation of the passage in the ≈övetƒÅ≈õvatara Upani·π£ad that speaks of the supreme reality as having faces everywhere (sarvatomukha). Finally, the grace bestowing nature (anugrahƒÅtman), the nature of making up everything (sarvabh≈´tƒÅman), and the three functions of creation, maintenance, and dissolution are infused into the deity, at which point either the practitioner or the image awakens to its true nature. This nature‚Äîthe real identity of both person and deity‚Äîis expressed [[P1535]] in a language that is deliberately crafted so as to be devoid of either explicitly ≈öaiva or Vai·π£·πáava technical terms of art. The implicit message is that while at lower levels of articulation, different circumstances may call for the use of different mantra systems, ultimately the ‚Äúone who sees everything‚Äù transcends all dualities. At least within the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái itself, for HemƒÅdri‚Äôs later writings will express a different point of view, this supreme deity is neither ≈öiva nor Vi·π£·πáu but something that incorporates or transcends both of them. Elsewhere in the Pari≈õe·π£akha·πá·∏ça, where the matter comes up in passing, HemƒÅdri directs his readers to the explanations he had offered in the Mok·π£akha·πá·∏ça as well as in the second division on prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ, both parts of our text that have yet to surface in manuscript. Though we will not pursue the matter further, there is good reason to believe that HemƒÅdri himself identified this supreme reality with the Harihara principle, which in the present age has assumed the form of the patron deity of the later Seu·πáa kings, Vi·π≠·π≠hal at Pandharpur.

With this new presumption of an exoteric undifferentiated universal nature as the undifferentiated principle that animates reality comes a new commitment to transparency in ritual. If all images are functionally the same thing and the ritual installer is always the same ‚Äúestablishment‚Äù person who in his daily life is at once responsible for the management of a var·πáƒÅ≈õramadharma oriented social order and answers to an increasingly centralized surveillance state, it logically follows that there is no need for any secrets at all, let alone a hidden esoteric regiment of practices offering an alternative theory of sovereignty that falls outside the state. From the perspective of the emerging new social order, both those responsible for the preservation and transmission of those secrets and those secrets themselves are utterly superfluous, and that is before one begins to take into consideration that this obsolete demographic is sitting on top of much of the wealth and property of the Deccan. Thus, for both [[P1536]] political as well as theological reasons, the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati reimagines almost every stage of ritual to minimize potential opportunities for the old guard to keep peddling their outmoded wares. This reinvention begins with a complete spatial reorientation of the workings of ritual. Succinctly, the vast majority of activities that were previously conducted either off site or inside of a private space within the temple such as the garbhag·πõha are now performed in public, either outside or alongside the temple or in ma·πá·∏çapas and other pavilions, and before a public. Even at places in the ritual where it is essentially unavoidable that certain activities have to be performed inside of a closed structure, the sadasya and the artisans are never left alone to themselves, but are always accompanied by Vedic ritualists who are not part of their team but paid on spec. It is probably not an accident that it is precisely at the junctures in the ritual where the older order, represented for example by the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, would have imbued public acts with secret private meanings or clandestinely conducted parallel events for initiates, that the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati deploys the ·πõtvijs to go about reciting Vedic ·πõcs, fumigating the space with the air of the new orthopraxy.

Here, for example, is how the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati treats the period while the image of the deity is incubating. This activity now happens underneath an open-air pavilion‚Äîa pavilion that is in fact built using ritual procedures that HemƒÅdri himself identifies in the Vratakha·πá·∏ça as being derived from the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata itself! In the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, this event marks the occasion for staging public utsavas for the public during the day and gatherings where vƒ´radravya is consumed by initiates at night.226 Needless to say, HemƒÅdri proposes a rather different approach: [[P1537]]

This is the procedure for the incubation of the god and temple and so forth.

Then, having passed the night in the awakening (jƒÅgara·πáe), [the celebration is to be done] with the recitations of PurƒÅ·πáa, instrumental music, and Veda. In the morning, outside, the teacher, having bathed, having done the occasional rituals, is accompanied by the m≈´rtipas. Having gone to the yƒÅgama·πá·∏çapa, having enjoined the worship for the eastern door and so forth, having done the p≈´jƒÅ of the banners, pots, vastuma·πá·∏çala, and ritual site, in each respective pit, with the m≈´lamantra, having oblated a hundred and eight times with the ≈õƒÅnti substances to each deity with the mantras of the image, the m≈´rtipati, and the lords of the world, having offered the p≈´r·πáƒÅhuti, having given to the m≈´rtidhara the brahma stone in the form of a turtle and the pi·πá·∏çikƒÅ that are to be incubated along with it, and having utilized the protector with the Indra mantra, accompanied by the sounds of pacification, it is led counterclockwise to the prƒÅsƒÅda.

Having sprinkled the garbhag·πõha in a couterclockwise fashion with pacification water and [water purified by] the astra mantra to ward off obstacles, with a stream of flowers and water. . . . using the [Vedic] mantra that begins ‚ÄúmahƒÅ·πÉ indro vajra bahur,‚Äù having inscribed [the space] with that mantra, again having sprinkled all around with water purified by the astra mantra, [they enter] into the middle of the womb of the door [to the garbhag·πõha].227

[[P1538]]

Prior to recovering the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati, on the basis of what could be inferred from other sections of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái,228 I had assumed that the domesticated model of temple construction HemƒÅdri teaches was largely indebted to the late classical tradition of the ≈öaiva SiddhƒÅnta, which from the eleventh century onward increasingly acclimated itself to emerging neo-BrƒÅhma·πáical norms. Through its continued incorporation at key points in the ritual of elements and theological claims utterly alien to ≈öaiva SiddhƒÅnta discourse, such as the thirty-eight-level tattva system, however, the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati itself unexpectedly and repeatedly disproves this hypothesis. In light of our close reading in chapter 6 of the centrality of the worship of Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara both to a posited archaic artisan religiosity among the kƒÅrukas and to the ≈öƒÅkta traditions maintained by the KƒÅlamukhas into which this tradition was absorbed, it is fitting that we close our exploration of the ritual systems propounded in the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati by looking at how what is essentially a SmƒÅrta ritual manual appropriated and preserved key components of kƒÅruka vi≈õvakarman religion. In fact, throughout the text as a whole, especially in passages that HemƒÅdri attributes to ‚Äúthe Vi≈õvakarmamata,‚Äù we intermittently keep encountering Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara, often paired with Sarasvatƒ´ as his ≈öakti, woven into mantra sequences and visualizations in rhetorically significant places. While as one would anticipate, given our author‚Äôs theological commitments, during the installation process itself there is no trace of the ‚Äúsecret‚Äù as a material object nor is there a complementary clandestine ritual system being enacted alongside the installation of the public deity, as we reach the final day of the ritual process, uncanny commonalities begin to reassert themselves.

[[P1539]]

Through by making the sadasya simply an elite member of the community hired just for the occasion, HemƒÅdri has effectively elided the role of the sthƒÅpanƒÅcƒÅrya as at once a ritual professional and the actual initiatory guru from the ritual process. Just as we saw in the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, as the ritual comes to an end, this conceptual choice poses a methodological problem, for one of the roles assigned to the sthƒÅpanƒÅcƒÅrya is to use his powers and his command to ‚Äúmake the ritual complete.‚Äù229

[[P1540]]

HemƒÅdri‚Äôs unexpected solution is to assign this role not to a person but to a deity. As it so happens, that deity is Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara:

Having meditated thus, with the stated mantras, having performed the upacƒÅrap≈´jƒÅ, one should petition: ‚ÄúWhether from knowledge or ignorance, whatever is deficient or extraneous, let all that be complete, O Ca·πá·∏çanƒÅtha, by your command! So long as the li·πÖga stands, the god of gods, Mahe≈õvara, within it, for that much time, O Ca·πá·∏ça, may you remain in the presence of ≈öiva.230

[[P1541]]

The Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati next turns to describing Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara‚Äôs ritual function. At first glance, it would seem that Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara is simply a servant of the Supreme deity set up as the designated consumer of ritual refuse (nirmƒÅlya), just as in parallel ≈öaiva SiddhƒÅnta sources. And then, out of the blue, having first indicated the general rule, the text itself introduces a stipulation that for its intended audience would have effectively ruled out such a possibility.

‚ÄúFruits, roots, food, and drink and so forth, tamb≈´la, garlands and ointment‚Äîthe consuming of nirmƒÅlya is given to you, indeed, by the command of ≈öiva.‚Äù

Having bowed, he should give the first bali offering.

‚ÄúCa·πá·∏ça is not authorized [however], with regard to a bƒÅ·πáali·πÖga, an iron li·πÖga, a siddhili·πÖga, or a self-arisen one, and in regard to all images.‚Äù231

[[P1542]]

As an attentive reader may have noticed, back in chapter 6, we have already encountered substantial scholastic reflection by Tantric authors working within the tradition of the ≈öaiva SiddhƒÅnta on this very same issue. In his own writings on Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara, for example, Dominic Goodall directs our attention to a passage in the thirteenth-century SaiddhƒÅntika J√±ƒÅna≈õiva‚Äôs thirteenth century J√±ƒÅnaratnƒÅvalƒ´. And as we can see below, J√±ƒÅna≈õiva, almost a direct contemporary of HemƒÅdri, is unambiguous in his assertion that any text that excludes ca·πá·∏çe≈õvarap≈´jƒÅ from forming an essential component of the rituals associated with bƒÅ·πáali·πÖgas, siddhili·πÖgas, svayambh≈´va li·πÖgas, or m≈´rtis by definition cannot belong to the tradition of the ≈öaiva SiddhƒÅnta:

Surely [PP]

In the case of a bƒÅ·πáali·πÖga, a portable one, ones that have risen [from rivers], one established by a Siddha, a spontaneously arisen one and in the case of all [representational] images, Ca·πá·∏ça has no authority. [So too] in the case of one in which there is visualization of a non-dual [deity such as Bhairava or Tumburu]. And also in the case of rites for the Lord [installed] on the ground.

Others, however, [opine:]

No worship of Ca·πá·∏ça [is to be performed] when the [image of] the Lord has been crafted either.

How can [such a view as] that [be maintained]? True. [But] this is [in fact] a prohibition of the installation of Ca·πá·∏ça and not a prohibition of his worship. This is advanced as somebody else‚Äôs doctrine. In the ≈öaiva SiddhƒÅnta, however, the rule is that he should always and in every case be worshipped. And this is expressed in the KƒÅlottara:

Whether the li·πÖga is stable or portable, or made of precious stone, clay, wood, rock, iron, or is represented in a picture, or is a bƒÅ·πáali·πÖga, [the worship of] Ca·πá·∏ça remains determined by rule (niyƒÅmaka·∏•) in the SiddhƒÅnta, but not in other tantric traditions: neither in the VƒÅmasrotas nor in the Dak·π£i·πáasrotas.232

[[P1543]]

Following the KƒÅlottara, J√±ƒÅna≈õiva assigns the methodological stance that certain focuses of worship do not fall under the jurisdiction of Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara, and thus by implication that the things offered to them do not become nirmƒÅlya, to either the VƒÅma or Dak·π£i·πáa transmissions. This is to say, he associates it with the domains that, as we have seen, the Devƒ´ PurƒÅ·πáa associates with ‚Äúthe best among the sthƒÅpakas,‚Äù which by our period have come to be occupied by the KƒÅlamukhas and their ≈õilpin or kƒÅruka disciples. Just as we saw in the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, a work that the ≈örƒ´ka·πá·π≠hƒ´yasa·πÉhitƒÅ had assigned to the VƒÅmasrotas alongside the Mata·πÖgapƒÅrame≈õvara, which also preserves these features, the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati has also retained strong indications that it has inherited an older system of values in which Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara stands in for ≈öiva as the Lord of the universe.

On the fourth day after the installation of the li·πÖga or the day after that, outside of the base of the temple (jagatƒ´) in the ƒ´≈õƒÅna direction, according to half the measure of the garbhag·πõha of the temple, in house of moon, having practiced the pƒ´·π≠hanyƒÅsa and so forth, [one is to do the following mantras:]

o·πÉ ca·πá·∏çanƒÅya nama·∏• / o·πÉ ca·πá·∏çe≈õvarƒÅya h≈´·πÉ pha·π≠ svƒÅhƒÅ / Thus, having summoned [him, one recites:] o·πÉ ca·πá·∏çƒÅya h·πõdƒÅya h≈´·πÉ pha·π≠ nama·∏• o·πÉ ca·πá·∏çakavacƒÅya hu[·πÉ] pha·π≠ nama·∏• / o·πÉ ca·πá·∏çƒÅstrƒÅya hu[·πÉ] pha·π≠ nama·∏• /

o·πÉ ca·πá·∏çasadyojƒÅtƒÅya hum pha·π≠ nama·∏• / o·πÉ ri·πÉ [ca·πá·∏ça]233vƒÅmadevƒÅya hu·πÉ pha·π≠ nama·∏• / o·πÉ ra·∏• [ca·πá·∏çƒÅ]ghorƒÅya hu·πÉ phat nama·∏• / o·πÉ [ca·πá·∏ça]tatpuru·π£ƒÅya [hu·πÉ pha·π≠ nama·∏•] / o·πÉ cƒÅ·πÉ [ca·πá·∏ça][ƒ´]≈õƒÅnƒÅya [hu·πÉ pha·π≠ nama·∏•] /234

May there be homage to you Ca·πá·∏çanƒÅyaka the fierce lord, who is the origin of the fire of Rudra, and terrifying. He bears a ≈õ≈´la and a da·πá·∏ça and has four faces and four arms. He has twelve red eyes and a great fire that spews forth from his mouth. He is adorned with a moon, a crown, and dread locks, and a small bracelet/earring. He has a serpent for his sacred thread, and ascetic‚Äôs pot along with a mƒÅlƒÅ. He is seated on a white ƒÅsana. He destroys afflictions through devotion and humility. . . . He is the inconceivable one, the lord of Vi≈õvar≈´pa. Homage to you, with genuflection out of devotion.235

[[P1544]]

In the prose ritual instructions that precede the mantra system and the visualization, which have been composed by HemƒÅdri himself, though even here we see some continuities, Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara‚Äôs role is much diminished. Though the old association with ‚Äúthe place of the moon‚Äù has been retained, instead of residing in a closed structure of equal dimensions to the garbhag·πõha of the main focus of worship in the temple, Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara is now installed in a permanent open pavilion outside of the temple in a space that has half the dimension of the main focus of worship. Like so many other features of the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati, every element of this realignment serves to undercut the older ritual system in which the ‚Äúreal‚Äù esoteric god, who instantiated a radical rejection of the very idea of ritual purity through distributing his offerings as blessings to everyone, resided in the place the moon.236

[[P1545]]

When we turn from HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own prose to the unattributed ‚Äútraditional sources‚Äù that he has retained for liturgical purposes, however, a very different dynamic is in evidence, beginning with the mantras themselves. As Goodall has observed in other contexts, while one would anticipate that a mere servant of the deity would be assigned a single seed syllable for his mantra, here Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara is given the five-part mantra nyƒÅsa of a sort that is otherwise reserved for ≈öiva and the goddess. Even more tellingly, beginning with the mantra ‚Äúo·πÉ ca·πá·∏çasadyojƒÅtƒÅya hum pha·π≠ nama·∏•,‚Äù this five-part nyƒÅsa then appears to be followed by the unprecedented invocation of the five faces of ≈öiva as extensions of Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara himself. The dhyƒÅna that immediately follows maintains this identification throughout, invoking Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara as the terrifying lord of the world in the form of a four faced AtimƒÅrgic deity. The iconography in evidence, even many of the terms of art, largely overlaps with the visualizations given in the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata.237 Instead of being identified with ‚Äúthe wrath of god,‚Äù here Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara is understood as the ‚Äúorigin of the fire of Rudra,‚Äù in other words, the source of the cosmic fire that destroys the universe at the end of the kalpa. And while these cosmic affiliations abound, conspicuously absent from these passages are any allusions to Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara as a disciplinary force, responsible for example for inflicting punishment on those who break samaya codes, of the sort one would expect to find in SiddhƒÅnta aligned practice systems.

HemƒÅdri‚Äôs deliberate retention of these formal elements in the liturgy coincide with considerable efforts to undercut the ≈öƒÅkta theological vision of the tradition of Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara, especially its radical inclusivism and outright rejection of notions of purity and impurity. For before, during, and after the god is installed, the *Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati performatively seeks to overwrite the ≈öƒÅkta approaches to the redistribution of food, goods, and value as well as its more transgressive and sanguinary dimensions. In this new ritual system, once the deity is installed inside of the temple‚Äîthe only major activity conducted inside of the temple out of the view of the public‚Äîthe ƒÅcƒÅrya and his ·πõtvijs, but noticeably not the artisans, offer a series of ≈õƒÅnti rites in which a vegetarian and non-transgressive form of caru‚Äîquite unlike the concoction contained inside ‚Äúthe secret‚Äù‚Äîis fed into the fires to the accompaniment of Vadika mantras. Once this act is completed, the ritual refuse produced during the installation of the deity is gathered up and brought before Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara, while at the same time, the ƒÅcƒÅrya‚Äîwho does not share with anyone‚Äîis fed the wholesome caru.238 [[P1546]]

HemƒÅdri, CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati, ‚ÄúOne should give [to the guru] yogurt, ak·π£ata grain, the tips of ku≈õa grass and milk or honey, barley, along with fruits and flowers for the attainment of desires. . . . The installation of ‚Äúall the gods‚Äù applies to a garden of trees, a pond, or in regard to images, with regard to rituals of improvement and renovation, or with regard to replacement that has to be done, and with regard to temples, pots, and so forth. All of this is the common system, which is stated in the ≈öruti, Sm·πõti, and ƒÄgamas. Fol. 102 verso: sarvadevaprati·π£·π≠hƒÅ suv·πõk·π£odyƒÅne jalƒÅ≈õaye / mahopadyƒÅte vƒÅrcƒÅsu jƒ´r·πáasa·πÉskƒÅrakarma·πá[i] // jƒ´r·πáoddhƒÅre ca kartavye prƒÅsƒÅdakala≈õƒÅdi·π£u / eta[t] sƒÅdhƒÅra·πáa·πÉ ta[n]tra·πÉ ≈õrutism·πõtyƒÅgamoditam // Fol. 98 recto - verso: dadhyak·π£ataku≈õƒÅgrak·π£ƒ´ra[·πÉ] vƒÅ madhuyava[·πÉ] s[id]dh[ya]rthaka[·πÉ] pu·π£paphalƒÅnvitam a[rgh]apƒÅtram ƒÅsƒÅdya /

Once the sadasya has been fed, accompanied by the yajamƒÅna, bearing the ritual refuse, he goes before Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara so that the rite can be completed. In place of the sharing of the joy (anumodana), which in the Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata the guru distributed to everyone assembled at the end of rite, here the sthƒÅpaka simply pleads to the deity on behalf of the community. Once it is clear the deity has accepted this offering, Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara receives a series of baths designed to ensure that he is clean.

‚ÄúO lord, for the sake of the pacification of the flood of sins, may the p≈´jƒÅ of this yajamƒÅna, the bathing and giving of enjoyments be accepted‚Äîit [this result] is brought near239 by me‚Äù. . . . One should begin the bath at the place situated on the right side, preceded by the statements of hail along with sounds of trumpet, the conch, the recitation of auspicious things, the Devas≈´kta, and other such sounds.240 [[P1547]]

The ritual that follows, which incorporates the use of powdered marijuana and entails the worship of the previously ≈öƒÅkta aligned sandals of a ≈öƒÅkta siddha, a procedure that has been Vedicized and domesticated, is of considerable interest. Thus, despite numerous issues with the text, it has presented below in a preliminary fashion. ‚ÄúHaving set down the pƒÅdukas on the footstool, having dressed it with bathing clothes, having worshipped along with lights, incense, flowers, and scents, having anointed it with oil and fragrance, having made a powder. . . there is the bathing in the purified unction water. . . with acacia flower (≈õirƒ´·π£a), indigo, and sunflower, along with water full of powder that contains ku≈õa grass and sida cordifolia (sahadevƒ´), there is the ‚Äúwiping‚Äù of the body. With the words ‚Äú≈õa[·πÉ] no devƒ´‚Äù. . . with the mantra ‚Äúagnim m≈´rdhƒÅnam [divo aprati·π£kutam tamƒ´mahe namasƒÅ vƒÅjinam b·πõhat],‚Äù there is the offering of the murƒÅ plant (myrrh), the ku·π£·π≠a (dolomiaea costus), a coconut, the fragrant root of the u≈õira plant (vetiver), fragrant flowers, vƒÅlaka plants (fragrant swamp mallow), the chaste tree, re·πáukƒÅ (chaste tree), turmeric, and haridrƒÅdvaya (turmeric extract), for the sake of siddhi. And, the head [is bathed] with water suffused with powered marijuana (vijayƒÅ) and so forth.‚Äù pƒÅduk[au] pƒÅdapƒ´·π≠ha·πÉ tv agre nidhƒÅya snƒÅnavastra·πÉ paridhƒÅya gandhap≈´·π£padh≈´padƒ´pƒÅdyair abhyarcya gandhatailenƒÅbhyajya drupadƒÅdi vety udvartya ≈õuddhodakasnƒÅna·πÉ. . . . saptabhadrƒÅ≈õirƒ´·π£arajanƒ´s≈´ryƒÅvartinƒ´sah[a]devƒ´ku≈õac≈´r·πáodakair dehamƒÅrjanam / ≈õano devƒ´r iti haridrƒÅdvaya-murƒÅku·π£·π≠avacƒÅbhƒÅsƒ´bƒÅlakako·π≠aramurƒÅ≈õaileyac≈´r·πáajalasnƒÅnam / m≈´rdhƒÅna·πÉ diva iti surƒÅku·π£·π≠hanƒÅrikela-padmo≈õirasugandhabƒÅlakare·πáukƒÅharidrƒÅdvayasiddh[ya]rtha·πÉ dravƒÅbalƒÅjayantivijayƒÅc≈´r·πáajalai·∏• ≈õira·∏•sthƒÅnam /

Notice the use of the so-called Atharvavedic Sailila hymn 1.6. Interestingly, the Kau≈õuikas≈´tra, which offers ritual applications for Atharvan mantras, prescribes the hymn is to be recited for ≈õƒÅntyudaka as well as in several other contexts related to pacification rites.

Now housed in an outdoor pavilion, the antithesis of his traditional abode, the ancient one Ca·πá·∏çe≈õvara‚Äîthe god who gave things to everyone and for whom everything was considered pure‚Äîis bathed again and again in the service of purification to the sounds of pacification mantras drawn from ·πög and Atharva Vedas. In a similar spirit, the conclusion of the ritual incorporates two conceptual elements very much at odds with the milieu from which the practices had sprung. On the one hand, we have an act of sin-eating, which benefits only the patron. On the other, there is the offering of a coconut coated in turmeric and steeped in extracts from a cornucopia of flowers and intoxicants in lieu of ‚Äúthe best of sacrifices‚Äù‚Äîat a moment where either an animal or a human being was to be decapitated and placed upon the pƒÅdukas. Succinctly, while on the surface HemƒÅdri has preserved many of the formal dimensions found in his sources, his knife-like mind has set itself to the task of trimming out the core theology that animates these texts with the critical intelligence and deftness of a surgeon. In a systematic manner, what takes the place of this old order of values is the promotion of a theological-political model promoting the universality of dharma that consistently selects against practices suggestive of the episteme it has just dethroned and those orders of society that are demographically likely to attempt to clandestinely perpetuate it.

This seems an opportune moment to introduce the reader to what from a modern perspective is one of the most unsavory dimensions of our Dharma≈õƒÅstra, namely, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs robust discourses on the moral necessity of excluding and expelling from the realm of ritual any ‚Äúsubversive‚Äù elements clinging to the now deposed paradigms of the early medieval Deccan. In attending to this task, we will begin with examining the [[P1548]] prescriptions offered by the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati at its conclusion, which warn of the moral danger of permitting the occurrence of deviant practice in the realm of institution building before concluding with a closely related discourse in the ≈örƒÅddhakalpa on policing the demographics among ritual attendees at all public acts of commemoration and institution building. Here, then, in HemƒÅdri‚Äôs own words, is how the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati concludes:

The installation of all the gods (sarvadevƒÅnƒÅ·πÉ) is stated by the ≈öruti, the Sm·πõti, and the ƒÄgamas. Have rummaged through the PurƒÅ·πáas and other things, it is related by HemƒÅdri Suri.

Whatever else is discerned in accordance with what is contained within another system, that also is to be employed here [but only] in so far as it is not contradictory [with the structures governing] place, time, and so forth [as taught within our system]. . . .

One is not authorized to perform the installation ritual merely having had recourse to the ≈õƒÅstra. One who knows the Vedas and who knows the principles of ritual practice is worthy to do it.

A person of little knowledge (alpaj√±a), who, out of ignorance or greed, desires to do the ritual installation of the gods, should be forcibly restrained by the kings.

For, by the king, who acts according to dharma and desires the increase of dharma, all of the rituals in his kingdom must be done, directly, in accordance with the ≈õƒÅstra. . . .

For, there are many people who want to do it [prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ] who do not know the mantras (amantraj√±ƒÅ·∏•). The suppression (nigraha) of [such people] is to be done by the kings out of fear for the destruction of the kingdom.

The lowest of men does not correctly know the procedures for nyƒÅsa and homa. He is not to be considered an ƒÅcƒÅrya. He is to be seized like a thief. . . .

Thus, having seen that the triple worlds were impoverished in the recitation of dharma, due to the power of the kali [age] by him, acting out of concern for the assistance of the triple worlds, the lovely ‚ÄúWish-Fulfilling Gem‚Äù was manifested. ≈örƒ´ HemƒÅdri, the lord of all the ≈õrƒ´kara·πáas who are connected to MahƒÅdeva, king of kings, is skilled in all the vidyƒÅs. Contained within the Pari≈õe·π£a Kha·πá·∏ça of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, in which he has composed the elaboration on the i·π£·π≠ƒÅp≈´rtadharma, that paddhati concerning the installation of all the gods is completed‚ÄîOffering to Vi·π≠·π≠hal.241

[[P1549]]

As this division of his work draws to a close, HemƒÅdri takes great pains to drive home a quintessential dimension of his interpretive project that was perhaps in danger of getting lost amidst a sea of ritual details. Quite simply, the comprehensiveness of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái is implicitly an argument for its self-sufficiency. In other words, HemƒÅdri is presenting his own work as constituting in and of itself an entire knowledge system, whose strictures anyone else‚Äôs interventions into these same domains will have to obey and whose standards they will have to match. As we will see in much more detail in the next chapter, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái offers itself to its intended readers as both a canon of content and a method. As HemƒÅdri suggests here in the second verse, while the former is amiable to further expansion‚Äîsuch that, for example, one might add to a list of observances (vrata) by drawing on further PurƒÅ·πáas or integrate a set of procedures for building a well or temple with different shapes or proportions‚Äîthe latter is fixed and inflexible. The terms of these restrictions are at once demographic and ideological. While having access to ‚Äúthe ≈õƒÅstra‚Äù is absolutely necessary if one wishes to perform any act of institution building (prati·π£·π≠hƒÅ), however humble, in and of itself it is hardly sufficient, for one must also know the Veda. What might not be immediately apparent is that by ‚Äúthe ≈õƒÅstra,‚Äù HemƒÅdri refers not to scholarly learning in general but, quite pointedly, to his own text. In other words, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái is telling us, over and over, that if you want to do anything ‚Äúthe right way,‚Äù you will have to rely on the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái. [[P1550]]

By extension, everyone who does not take the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái as their methodological and ideological lodestone is by definition a person of little knowledge (alpaj√±a) and a person who does not know the mantras (amantraj√±a). And, since doing things ‚Äúthe right way‚Äù is more important in this paradigm than doing things ‚Äúeffectively,‚Äù such wrong modes of doing and being, which inculcate a divergent model of the social order grounded in divergent networks of sentiment and solidarity, can hardly be tolerated. They threaten the ‚Äúharmony of the world,‚Äù at least if one believes that the world is harmonious in so much as it matches up to the idealized models laid out in the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái. Indeed, in a certain sense, HemƒÅdri is entirely right. Whatever their background, anyone who has not been trained under his direction is virtually guaranteed not to know the ‚Äúright procedures‚Äù and the ‚Äúright mantras.‚Äù The very simple reason why this necessarily would have been the case is that, though individual elements in the rites he prescribed have their own earlier histories, as pre- systematized ritual units, the methods and mantra sequences taught in the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati had no prior discursive life outside this text itself.

In other words, in crafting his Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati, HemƒÅdri has deliberately and summarily set out to dismiss and decredentialize an entire industry and everyone who works in it. Their expertise deemed obsolete, the very pool of prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpanƒÅcƒÅryas and their crews who made the institutional worlds of the Deccan and had for centuries been acclaimed as among the most learned people on the subcontinent are rebranded. Instead of being celebrated as tantravi≈õƒÅrada in every sense of these multifaceted terms, now they are ‚Äúmen of little knowledge‚Äù who act ‚Äúout of greed or ignorance.‚Äù Because acts of ritual and institution building are above all else performances of values that make experientially real the norms that inform them for those who participate within their frames, the activities of these people are deemed a [[P1551]] threat to the health and power of the state. Just like what we saw happening at Kukkunur, where after its ‚Äúliberation‚Äù the KƒÅlamukhas were expunged from the historical record and the esoteric deities of the MantramƒÅrga and VidyƒÅpƒ´·π≠ha whom they tended were summarily replaced by a pantheon comprised of ‚Äúall the gods,‚Äù the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati demands that ‚Äúthe lowest of men who does not correctly know the procedures for nyƒÅsa and homa‚Äù‚Äîand indeed how could they, for HemƒÅdri has invented both of these ex nihilo‚Äî“is not considered an ƒÅcƒÅrya.‚Äù Instead of the kings of the Deccan stepping outside of their own domains and going before such figures as supplicants who are seeking wisdom, power, and council, HemƒÅdri proposes a new royal program. From henceforth, it is incumbent upon any king to treat such people as thieves. If the kingdom is to survive, let alone flourish, they must be quickly identified and then forcibly suppressed.

The term here that HemƒÅdri repeatedly deploys in describing this disciplinary regime, quite interestingly, is nigraha. Depending on context, this is a word that can mean suppression, restraint, imprisonment, or even destruction, and it likely that here most if not all of these applications are intended. As a term of art, however, nigraha has special additional rhetorical significance, for it is situated at the heart of the emic theory of just what it means to be a king. Vij√±ƒÅne≈õvara, for example, tells us at the beginning of his commentary on YƒÅj√±avalkya‚Äôs division on vyavahƒÅra, ‚Äúthe supreme dharma (paramo dharma·∏•) of the king is the protection of the people (prajƒÅpƒÅlanam), and that is not possible apart from the suppression of bad [people] (du·π£·π≠anigraha).‚Äù242 As we have seen again and again throughout this work, in the [[P1552]] medieval Deccan, one of the key components of the responsibility of the king to protect his people had been understood as entailing the protection of dharma through the preservation in perpetuity of the individuated sovereignty of the undying land grants and their administrators. The king‚Äôs role as protector against ‚Äúbad people,‚Äù in contrast, is treated as a conceptually subsidiary duty, largely homologized with the preservation of the rule of law, and given considerably less rhetorical emphasis. Far from being restricted to simply the king himself, this same dictum was viewed as being relevant for any local big-man who wields disciplinary power. This is why both aspects of its implementation, protecting the people through protecting the dharma embodied in the land along with protecting the people from enemy raiders and brigands with his mighty bow, could feature prominently in the professional self-representation of a ≈öƒÅkta ≈ö≈´dra gangster such as Habbƒïya NƒÅyaka operating in remote regions in the shadows of the mountains.

For a big man like Habbƒïya just as much as for a king, however, the ‚Äúdu·π£·π≠ajana‚Äù under consideration were simply understood as people who preyed upon the population, such as thieves, murderers, and brigands. Vij√±ƒÅne≈õvara243 himself also seems to adhere to a similar standard, for though he councils that in regard to a bad person, disciplinary power (da·πá·∏ça) must be deployed directly and immediately (du·π£·π≠e samyagda·πá·∏ça·∏• prayoktavya·∏•) his catalog of what makes something bad, whether we are talking about thoughts and impulses that must [[P1553]] be suppressed (also called nigraha) through self-discipline, or thieves, liars, frauds, violent criminals, and murderers, is in no way framed in doctrinally specific terms. As we have seen in chapter 3, in this earlier discourse, ‚Äúdifference‚Äù (bheda) in ritual or conduct is not only never labelled du·π£·π≠a by the authorities, but the king is explicitly instructed that within its respective domain it is to be protected using the full resources and authority at his disposal. Thus, in this context, the suppression of people is about punishing material crimes as defined under the law and not about summarily punishing ‚Äúideological deviancy,‚Äù wherever and whenever it might occur. Though not entirely lacking in historical precedent, especially earlier in history and elsewhere on the subcontinent, in terms of the norms that animate the prescriptive and documentary records in use throughout the medieval Deccan, what HemƒÅdri is proposing amounts to a stark break from tradition. Even when compared with their peers, the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava rulers of earlier generations were unusually eloquent and vocal advocates for what can only be described as something more than mere religious tolerance.244 From henceforth, under the Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas, at least in theory, through the newly trained cadres of BrƒÅhma·πáa authorities it has recruited for just such a purpose, the state is supposed to closely monitor every act of institution building. Where their services are not employed and CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái-derived ‚Äúbest practices‚Äù are not followed, the institution is to be disrupted and its managers expelled, imprisoned, or even killed.

‚ÄúOutcasting ‚ÄúDifference‚Äù: The Making of the Tantric Subaltern

Though monitoring acts of institution building and renewal, and intervening when they conflict with the ‚Äúbest practices‚Äô‚Äù of the new professional class, represent a much higher priority for [[P1554]] the securing of a new YƒÅdava ecumene grounded in the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs vision, an analogous dynamic is also at work in the realm of occasional rituals, with considerable attention being devoted to policing who is to be included and excluded from meaningful social moments. Once we recognize that the very heart of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs reimagining of what it means to ‚Äúbe BrƒÅhma·πáa‚Äù entails the recasting of the Dharma≈õƒÅstrin primarily as the administrator and overseer of the quotidian religious lives of the population, the rationale for this focus on governing and monitoring rituals of very different sorts becomes immediately clear. It is certainly no accident that, at least in the portion of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái that has been edited and published, the greatest care and attention that HemƒÅdri directs to thinking through in painstaking details the ins and outs of any ritual system, including the for us exceptionally pertinent matter of who is to be expelled from the sacrifice, is directed towards the topic of ≈õrƒÅddha rites.

The choice is clearly highly strategic. As Matthew Sayers has demonstrated245 convincingly and at some length, such rituals form a logical conceptual bridge between classical ≈örauta, pre-dharmanibandha SmƒÅrta, and dharmanibandha style religious praxis. In selecting ≈õrƒÅddha as the test case for thinking through the workings and boundaries of ritual, HemƒÅdri is able to make a convincing case for the continuities between the pi·πá·∏çapit·πõyaj√±a of the Vedas proper, the four-fold ≈õrƒÅddha ritual of the G·πõhyas≈´tras, and the at least twelve types [[P1555]] of ‚ÄúPurƒÅ·πáic‚Äù ≈õrƒÅddha recognized by the dharmanibandha SmƒÅrtas, and thus, by extension, for the canonicity of the new SmƒÅrta mode of religion. Just as importantly, however, though they had originated as a ritual honoring the ancestors on specific delimited occasions, governed by calendrical concerns, or marking the death of the sacrificer‚Äôs parents, over time, the scope of ≈õrƒÅddha ritual expanded so far beyond this ritual function that they had effectively become a standard approach to sanctifying pretty much any kind of special occasion, such as celebration of victory, a marriage, the birth of a son, starting out on pilgrimage, commencing or concluding medical treatment, or performing public forms of theistic worship.246 Nor was the influence of this ritual form restricted to domains where Vaidika BrƒÅhma·πáas were the only religious authorities. By the eighth century, Tantric variations of ≈õrƒÅddha247 have been adapted into ≈öaiva and ≈öƒÅkta revelations and codifications [[P1556]] of the ritual are prescribed in the ninth- and eleventh-century paddhatis of Brahma≈õambhu and Soma≈õambhu of the ≈öaivasiddhƒÅnta as well as in the twenty-fifth chapter of the tenth-century TantrƒÅloka of Abhinavagupta.[^1556_1389] Succinctly, even before we take into account the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs own efforts to add to the instances where ≈õrƒÅddha rites are performed, by the thirteenth century, this ritual form would have effectively punctuated most of the important moments in many people‚Äôs lives.

For the next chapter, we will examine in some detail how HemƒÅdri‚Äôs treatment of the ≈õrƒÅddha ritual provides his readers with a hermeneutical skeleton key to understanding both the theology and method that underlies his vision of the universalization of Hindu dharma. For the moment, however, let us restrict our focus to the much narrower task of examining the disciplinary dimensions of the ≈õrƒÅddha ritual as a means of policing who is to be included and excluded from meaningful social moments. Like so many other modalities we have examined throughout our investigations, within the various sources that nibandhas draw upon, the rules for who is invited to sponsor, to attend, and to be fed at ≈õrƒÅddha rites not only vary in substantive and significant ways from text to text, but even within a single text are highly dependent upon the specific type of ≈õrƒÅddha rite under consideration. For example, if a specific type of ≈õrƒÅddha rite involves an offering of liquor and meat to the bh≈´tas and pi≈õƒÅcas, with the remainder from that offering being fed to the attendees at the conclusion of the rite, ascetics who have taken vows that prohibit the consumption of such substances under any circumstances are prohibited from participating. In the nibandha literature, however‚Äîfor this is a case where, though he provides an order of magnitude more detail, HemƒÅdri is building upon some precedents set by Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äîthe bare effect of bringing together these disparate strictures and compiling them in a single framework where they are treated as complementary [[P1557]] elements of single system markedly changes the nature of the conversation. In the ≈örƒÅddhakalpa of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, the section on who is to be forbidden from being fed after the ≈õrƒÅddha rite and who is to be driven away from a site where ≈õrƒÅddha rituals are taking place runs more than a hundred pages. By the time all of the types of social agents who cannot participate have been delineated, which HemƒÅdri does with great precision in his commentary on the sources, much of society has been excluded. For HemƒÅdri, however (seemingly in distinction from his source), one of the key objectives in these sections is to lay a foundation for the unambiguous exclusion of the ruling authorities of the ≈öaiva Age from every significant aspect of social and religious life.

Let us take, for example, the manner in which the text frames the general rules for feeding ascetics at the ≈õrƒÅddha.

H: ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapa·∏• says, ‚ÄúOne should feed the ascetics (yati).‚Äù And with regard to that, a qualification is stated. As is stated in the Brahmavaivarta:

‚ÄúOne should drive away (varjjayet) bald-headed ones (mu·πá·∏ças), those with dread locks, and those with red robes. You should pay off (pradƒÅpayet) the ones with scalp tufts, those reddened with dhƒÅtu marks, and those with triple notched staves. However, the ones who are always established in their vows, are knowledgeable, and are meditators, are devoted to the gods, and are great souls, they, being all those things, cause purification by mere sight alone.‚Äù

H: Mu·πá·∏ças means ones with bald heads along with a top knot. The ja·π≠ilas means ≈öaivas, PƒÅ≈õupatas, KƒÅlamukhas, and so forth. The robed ones means those who have for their clothes red and brown robes, such as figgies?(ƒÅ≈õvatthas).248

[[P1558]]

As you will by now anticipate, faced with the core conceptual problem that the Sm·πõti texts do not actually make the arguments that he wants them to make, HemƒÅdri qualifies the teachings of Dharma≈õƒÅstra with statements from an unrelated source, the Brahmavaivarta PurƒÅ·πáa, which in its current published iteration is virtually an addendum to the BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa. Though the RƒÅdhƒÅ-centric iteration of this work now available to us is almost certainly quite different from the version available to HemƒÅdri in the thirteenth century, given the rest of his use of this text, the recension from which this extract was drawn expressed roughly analogous and unambiguously Vai·π£·πáava commitments that informed its exclusion of its ≈öaiva rivals. Even so, the text as written does not really speak very clearly to his exact concerns, and so he must flesh out his intended message through his commentary. In each case, he tweaks the construal of the term present in the root text considerably. The end result is to exclude from the ≈õrƒÅddha all of the major competing types of religious authorities.

In place of people with shaved heads, an accoutrement that could arise from a range of communities and circumstances, HemƒÅdri excludes perhaps the most orthodox-presenting form of BrƒÅhma·πáa, known especially from south India, whose head is adorned solely by a top knot. People with dreadlocks again represents an underspecified category that in fact does not exclude all the social agents that defined the ≈öaiva Age, since some of them did not wear dreadlocks. Just as problematically, the category could also incorporate slightly more acceptable varieties of philosopher ascetics, like PƒÅta√±jalian yogins, of whom HemƒÅdri approves. As HemƒÅdri‚Äôs predecessor in the field of Dharma≈õƒÅstra, MedhƒÅtithi would have it, jƒÅ·π≠ila might even simply refer to BrƒÅhma·πáa students who have not completed their studies and thus do not have the adult privilege of participating in ≈õrƒÅddhas. For this reason, our author spells out explicitly that what the text intends is that one is to drive away ≈öaivas, PƒÅ≈õupatas, KƒÅlamukhas, and so forth. In other words, the whole panoply of ritual agents who might have been attending and conducting ≈õrƒÅddha rites in lieu of SmƒÅrta BrƒÅhma·πáa Dharma≈õƒÅstrins are [[P1559]] now to be expelled. Finally, because the category of robed ones could potentially be interpreted as demanding that HemƒÅdri‚Äôs precious VedƒÅntins, the future Da≈õanƒÅmƒ´s, might be excluded from the very roles for which they are being cultivated, the prohibition is adapted to specify only brown and red robes, perhaps intending the deselection of Buddhists. Saffron, in contrast, is deemed perfectly fine.

The passage we have just examined introduces a substantial section exploring the different rules and restrictions for including yatis in the ≈õrƒÅddha, such as what do you do with ascetics who have taken a vow to eat nothing or only one food. Most of these are largely irrelevant to our concerns. The interesting point is that HemƒÅdri has chosen this particular focus as his framing mechanism in organizing his text. He will return to it several times within the discourse, including at the end. Rhetorically, HemƒÅdri is explicit that the passage we have just examined is to be treated as the benchmark against which all other statements about yogins must be tested and interpreted. As he writes, ‚Äútherefore, by this [above statement], it is known that the entirety of the statements about yogins [in our text] have precisely the subject of yatis who are endowed with these characteristics.‚Äù249 And thus, after treating such topics as the importance of seeking out and inviting to the feeding lines after the ≈õrƒÅddha BrƒÅhma·πáas who are hungry and living in poverty, but are too proud and concerned about their reputation to seek out assistance, HemƒÅdri concludes this topic, which is labelled under the heading ‚Äúthose outside the ƒÅ≈õramas (ƒÅ≈õramabƒÅhyƒÅ·∏•),‚Äù seemingly for both good and ill, by reasserting in no uncertain terms that certain categories of ascetics are to be summarily driven away from the ≈õrƒÅddha, as for example in this extract from the Brahmavaivarta which reads, ‚ÄúBut the bald headed ones, those wearing dreadlocks, and clothed in red robes must be driven away from the ≈õrƒÅddha.‚Äù Once again, his preferred authorities are collections of PurƒÅ·πáic proof texts mostly drawn from Vai·π£·πáava leaning sources, since these are the texts most likely to match up to his intended message, but these have been interspersed with clipped extracts from other, more ≈öaiva leaning PurƒÅ·πáic sources that are not as self-evidently treating the same topic but that have been edited such a way so that the reader assumes doctrinal harmonization.

[[P1560]]

The next thirty pages of the ≈örƒÅddhakalpa are devoted to two deeply interconnected topics, a detailed catalog of the professional and religious identities who are not permitted a banner, and those viewed as ‚Äúdespoilers‚Äù of the purity of the ≈õrƒÅddha offering, and who are thus are not fit for the receiving lines. In the present context, we can barely do justice to the sociological richness of the resulting text‚Äôs representation of life-worlds of non-elite people in the medieval Deccan. Eschewing the usual stereotyped imagery we expect from kƒÅvya, or antiquated formulas one finds in the Dharma≈õƒÅstra, HemƒÅdri approaches the topic with a devotion to verisimilitude‚Äîmaking sure we know exactly who these people are and what they do‚Äîthat previously in this study we have only seen in Some≈õvara III‚Äôs gadyakƒÅvya, the VikramƒÅ·πÖkƒÅbhyudaya. The key difference, of course, is that whereas the CƒÅ·∏∑ukya king shared with us the social diversity of his kingdom because he believed it to be part of what made KalyƒÅ·πáa great, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs intention is to ensure that professional BrƒÅhma·πáa ritualists throughout the Seu·πáa kingdom have all the tools they need to weed out unwanted classes of people. The particularity and the sheer variety of the social world that CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái sets out to domesticate blows open our comfortable presumptions about what medieval India must have been like. Thus, in the lesser manner of those forbidden a banner (ketanƒÅnarhƒÅ·∏•), to cite just a few example (pg. 477‚Äì79) the ranks include devalakas‚Äîas HemƒÅdri explains, [[P1561]] these are those who worship deities for the sake of obtaining money250‚Äîas well hypocrites (dambhikƒÅs), which HemƒÅdri tells us is a technical term for those who observe restrictive injunctions (niyama) that cannot possibly ever yield results.251 Our text mentions transgender people and soma sellers‚Äîas HemƒÅdri specifies, the latter are those who sell the soma vine.252 It singles out various different subclasses of bards that attend a royal court, subdivided into such categories as the bodhakas, who are vaitƒÅlikas, and the cara·πáas, who keep the company of female dancers called plavakas. Apparently there was a recognizable class of people who taught ≈ö≈´dras Sanskrit ≈õƒÅstra, again with a special subcategory for those who specialized in teaching ≈ö≈´dras Sanskrit grammar, and there were also those who taught ≈õƒÅstra to those who would end up teaching ≈ö≈´dras.253 HemƒÅdri mentions several sorts of those who, for all intents and purposes, ascend in caste by marrying up, again with a special category for those who marry the daughters of BrƒÅhma·πáas.254 He speaks of those who cast out pretas for a fee, apparently called m·πõtaniryyƒÅtakas, figures whose inclusion in the ranks of those who do not get a banner but are not despoilers of the line apparently differentiates them as somewhat less despised than several other categories of exorcists who appear in the other section.255 Our author lists several different subcategories of financial debtors and the indebted, including those who charge exorbitant interest256 as well as the so called ‚Äúdebt maker,‚Äù ‚Äúone who obtains [[P1562]] a debt for the purpose of producing ‚Äòunnecessary income.‚Äô‚Äù257 People who make arrows sit alongside BrƒÅhma·πáas who never even observe their rituals, who in turn are lumped next to the foreigners (yavanas).258

One of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs longer and rather revealing discussions, for it is very suggestive of a new kind of state coming to terms with documenting and cataloging the population, involves the different subcategories of ‚Äúpeople who have not been previously recognized.‚Äù259 As our author explains, these are people who either themselves or even their ancestors are not mentioned in recorded sources, along with those whose foundational line are only listed in relation to a ga·πáa or a sa·πÖgha, archaic terms that may well still point to the survival of vestigial pre-monarchical modes of governance into the thirteenth century. As will be clarified in the next division, on those who ‚Äúdespoil the line,‚Äù one sub-type of people who descend from a ga·πáa are in fact ≈örauta ritualists who apparently remain within their own communities all their lives, having no further ties with the rest of society, a social arrangement that HemƒÅdri treats as just as bad as being nƒÅstika.

When it comes to the ranks of those who ‚Äúdespoil the line,‚Äù HemƒÅdri synthesizes together a range of different sources, each of which on its own privileges different criteria for determining exclusion from ritual settings.260 Encompassing at once concerns we would label as legal, purely ‚Äúeconomic,‚Äù the regulation of professions, and the policing of social behavior, HemƒÅdri‚Äôs approach to ritual governmentality amounts to a series of interrelated approaches for regulating different modes of transactionality and exchange. Part of the point here is that someone like a usurer, a counterfeit or a gambler, a defrauder of the village, someone who [[P1564]] manipulates the scales, which is to say someone who cheats the system ‚Äúeconomically,‚Äù finds their immediate counterpart in the realm of religious life in the hypocrite ‚Äúwho practices dharma on a pretext for the sake of pleasing the world,‚Äù which is, of course a near paraphrase of the more conservative MantramƒÅrga position about why one should continue to observe worldly dharma, and in the domestic world in a married man who is impotent. By defying the role assigned to them by the order of things, all of them are effectively committing fraud in precisely the same manner. HemƒÅdri is equally keen on targeting professions and ways of life that step over and outside of his vision of a singular shared social normativity, in a large part because they represent autonomous sites of power and agency. Poison sellers and alchemists belong alongside exorcists for hire in that they offer or manipulate power substances on behalf of anyone of means as a paid service. People with ‚Äúdeviant ƒÅcƒÅras‚Äù as well as pre·π£yas, the designated voices of the village council and the state, are problematic because they represent the older pluralistic paradigm HemƒÅdri seeks to displace. Just like travelling troupes of performers, and nomads who wander in and out of urban and village areas selling the products from their cows, goats, and buffalo, contract labor exists outside of permanent social relations governed by logics of uninterrupted dependency. In other words, literally in the first case but figuratively in the second, these are people who do not ‚Äúknow their place‚Äù within the social order and who spend much of their time effectively not subject to a singular and consistent guiding authority. [[P1565]]

[In response to Manu,] A pre·π£ya is one who is doing the command of a king or village council. . . . The cow protector is the one who protects cows for the sake of subsistence. . . . But a contract laborer (bh·πõtaka) is someone who takes wages according to the speech, ‚ÄúI will do this for so much money‚Äù. . . . A grinder of oils and so forth is a tailaka. A k≈´·π≠akƒÅraka is someone who speaks non-truth in the court while acting as witness. Otherwise, the term refers to the act of the falsification of impressed coins and so forth. As for the mad or ones whose minds are unsteady, [this can be] on account of flaws of the vƒÅta and so forth, or because of being seized by pi≈õƒÅcas and so forth. . . . A g·πõhasa·πÉve≈õaka is one who subsists on the discipline of vƒÅstu, such a sthapitas and s≈´tradhƒÅras and so forth. . . . A klƒ´ba is someone of whom the ‚Äúexcitement has been broken off‚Äù in regard to ‚Äúduties that have to be done.‚Äù ‚ÄúOne who is always asking‚Äù is someone who always irritates the world by asking all the time. Here, the ka suffix is used in the original sense to indicate blameworthiness and so forth. . . . AurƒÅbhrikas are those of whom the vendible items are rams or sheep; thus is also the case with the [seller] of buffalos. . . . The pretaniryƒÅpaka is one who, for a price, casts out or conveys away pretas. The one who has a reviled ƒÅcƒÅra is one of whom the social practice is censured. The ajapƒÅla, a goat herder, is someone who protects goats for the sake of subsistence. As for the grƒÅmak≈´·π≠aka he is someone who cheats the village. The sense is he causes to be consumed the wealth of the village by many sorts of fraudulent businesses. But a tulƒÅk≈´·π≠a, scale fraud, is a deceiver [who operates] by means of having a business that uses fraudulent scales.

CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, ≈örƒÅddhakalpa, pg. 479‚Äì480: *athƒÅpƒÅ·πÖkteyƒÅ bhavanti. . . . satya·πÉ yasmin jƒ´vikopƒÅye dhƒÅnyav·πõddhijƒ´vana·∏• vƒÅrddhu·π£ika·∏• / m≈´lyena devatƒÅp≈´jako devadravyabhoktƒÅ vƒÅ devalaka·∏• / krayavikrayavavahƒÅropajƒ´vƒ´ ba·πáik. . . . dhanƒÅrtha·πÉ rujƒÅ·πÉ pratikarttƒÅ cikitsaka·∏• / dharmƒÅrthinas tu tasya pra≈õastatvƒÅd. . . . g·πõhaprƒÅsƒÅdƒÅdinirmƒÅ·πáav·πõtti·∏• ≈õilpopajƒ´vƒ´ /. . . . samprayoktƒÅ dy≈´takƒÅrƒ´ / chadmaprayoktƒÅ kitava·∏• /. . . . lokaprƒ´tyartha·πÉ chadmanƒÅ dharmƒÅnu·π£·π≠hƒÅtƒÅ dƒÅmbhika·∏•. . . . pg. 483: grƒÅmasya rƒÅj√±o vƒÅ pre·π£ya·∏• ƒÅj√±ƒÅkara·∏•. . . . ƒÅjƒ´vanƒÅrtha·πÉ ya·∏• pa≈õ≈´n rak·π£ati sa pa≈õupƒÅla·∏• / pg. 484: vandƒ´ stutipƒÅ·π≠haka·∏• / tilƒÅdƒ´nƒÅ·πÉ pe·π£·π≠ƒÅ tailaka·∏• / sƒÅk·π£itve ‚Äôn·πõtavƒÅdƒ´ k≈´·π≠akƒÅraka·∏•, k≈´·π≠ƒÅnƒÅm nƒÅ·πáakƒÅdƒ´nƒÅ·πÉ karttƒÅ vƒÅ / pg. 485: iyatƒÅ dhaneneda·πÉ karomƒ´ti vacanavyavasthayƒÅ vetanagrƒÅhƒ´ bh·πõtaka·∏•. . . . vƒÅtƒÅdido·π£e·πáa pi≈õƒÅcƒÅdig·πõhƒ´tatvena vƒÅ anavasthitacitta·∏• ‚Äúunmatta·∏•‚Äù. . . . g·πõhasa·πÉve≈õaka·∏• vƒÅstuvidyopajƒ´vƒ´ sthapatis≈´tradhƒÅrƒÅdi·∏• / pg. 486: kartavye·π£u bha·πÖgotsƒÅha·∏• klƒ´ba·∏• / sadaiva yƒÅcanayƒÅ lokƒÅnudvejayati sa·∏• nityayƒÅcanaka·∏• / nindƒÅdibhya·∏• svƒÅrthe kap /. . . . urabhrƒÅ·∏• me·π£ƒÅ·∏• pa·πáya·πÉ yasya ca aurƒÅbhrika·∏• / eva·πÉ mƒÅhi·π£ika·∏•. . . . yo m≈´lyena pretƒÅn niryƒÅpayati vahati sa pretaniryƒÅpaka·∏• / vigarhitƒÅcƒÅrƒÅ·∏• ninditƒÅcƒÅrƒÅ·∏• / pg. 487: ƒÅjƒ´vanƒÅrtham ajƒÅ·∏• pƒÅlayati ajapƒÅla·∏• / HemƒÅdri (pg. 490) is here commenting on terms of art found in a passage assigned to the Yamasm·πõti: grƒÅma·πÉ k≈´·π≠ayati chalayatƒ´ti grƒÅmak≈´·π≠aka·∏• / bahubhir alƒ´kavyavahƒÅrair grƒÅmadravya·πÉ bhak·π£ayatƒ´ty artha·∏• / alƒ´katulƒÅvyavahƒÅre·πáa va√±cayitƒÅ tulƒÅk≈´·π≠a·∏• /

Finally, there are ritually indispensable but low status communities like the oil pressers and the oil vendors, who, we should remember, figure frequently in the early medieval Kannada inscriptional record especially as both the patrons and recipients of usually small- scale grants affiliated with a temple complex. As we have seen repeatedly throughout this [[P1566]] study, many of these demographics who are to be shunned, ‚Äúservice classes,‚Äù musicians and performers, artisans, healers and exorcists, nomads, as well as oil makers, also tend to have cultural and initiatory ties with MantramƒÅrga ritual practitioners and ‚Äúspecialized‚Äù institutions. That being said, it is difficult to say whether it is the internal logic that animates these life-worlds themselves or their doctrinal allegiances that would have proven the bigger point of contention, for both represent value systems that are effectively antithetical to HemƒÅdri‚Äôs interpretive project and for precisely the same sort of reasons.

As you may have noticed, among the ranks of those who despoil the line, HemƒÅdri has already incorporated ≈õilpins as well as more general contractor builders (g·πõhasa·πÉve≈õaka). This portion of our text is not organized thematically but is structured around HemƒÅdri‚Äôs responses to a range of proof texts that themselves often simply enumerate different classes of rejected persons in accordance with the exigencies of meter as opposed to with the aim of cataloging examples according to coherent principles. Even so, more so than with any other demographic, HemƒÅdri choses to return again and again to tackling comprehensively all the possible different terms of art that might be used to refer to the different groupings of artisans, focusing especially on goldmakers, with the express purpose of making sure that none of them slip through the cracks of his attempts at excluding them from commensality.

[In response to the Yamasm·πõti], ≈õre·πáƒ´s are original inhabitants (prak·πõti) [of a village] such as goldmakers and so forth. The sacrificer on behalf them is called a ≈õre·πáƒ´ sacrificer. One who subsists by attaining an increase in his store of gold through [the practice of] art is kalopajƒ´va. He is one who makes a livelihood by means of art such as fashioning gold and so forth. Or, he is someone who lives by the sixty-four arts. One who is a great offender is a person of whom punishment has been done by the king, through the marking of the feet and so forth; he is [also] said to be a ‚Äúpunishment banner.‚Äù A honey killer is one who kills honey bees for the purpose of taking their honey. . . . Samayas are differential [legal] arrangements made by a king and so forth. . . .261 A sacrificer of the p≈´ga: a p≈´ga is a collectivity of people who have the same [[P1567]] occupation, who are also of the same caste. One who is a sacrificer of that p≈´ga is a p≈´ga sacrificer. . . .262 A bh·πõtya-teacher is one who has taught hired laborers or who is a teacher of hired laborers. Thus also [he] is a ≈ö≈´dra-teacher. . . .263

One who subsists by the activity of writing books is a paustika. Or, a book writer may be one who sells books for money. A storyteller is one who is a teller of stories, such as the B·πõhadkathƒÅ, for the delight of the court parasite (vƒ´·π≠a). A machine maker is someone who constructs machines for the sake of an assembly in forts and so forth, or for the sake of hunting. A kƒÅ·πá·∏çap·πõ·π£·π≠ha is someone who subsists on weapon craft. . . . One who is vƒÅditram plays a musical instrument that is struck. N·πõtya means one who dances. Gƒ´tƒÅ is song. TƒÅla rhythms are [dancing rhythms] such as ca√±catp≈´ta and so forth. The focus is one who subsists by his skill at these vidyƒÅs. A m≈´lyasƒÅ·πÉvatsarika is someone who points out the constellations and so forth out of his desire to obtain money.

As is stated by Manu: ‚ÄúOne should not speak the tithi of another person, or one should not point out the nak·π£atras.‚Äù A great traveler is someone who travels on the ocean or is always going on the road. The a≈õmaku·π≠·π≠aka is a fashioner of stone for the purpose of building temples and so forth. . . .264

[[P1568]]

In much the same way that the Prati·π£·π≠hƒÅpaddhati sets out to dismantle piece by piece the ritual systems and ethos that animated the professional lives of the makers of the world, here in the ≈örƒÅddhakalpa, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái sets about to disassemble the variety of social and juridical forms that made possible their largely autonomous way of life. In doing so, our text provides welcome confirmation that the social realities on ground in the Maharashtra of his day that HemƒÅdri sets about regulating out of existence bear a close affinity to those that we saw outlined in chapter three in VaradarƒÅja‚Äôs VyahƒÅranir·πáaya, right down to the use of parallel terms of art. The world of the arts and artisans, in other words, was comprehensively ordered around the differential arrangement of the law (vyavasthƒÅ) as established by kings and other ruling authorities, which gave sanction to samaya codes of differentiated law. This in turn enabled social forms like the p≈´ga, self-governing collectivities in which people of single profession share a single caste background, but also created spaces for a myriad of types of non-BrƒÅhma·πáa religious and technical authorities, such a mathematicians and astrologers, who were responsible for the ritual lives of these communities and who also provided various forms of mass education, both in technical disciplines and in the realm of cultural production.

[[P1569]]

Particularly in light of the almost normative historiographical assumptions that the BrƒÅhma·πáa as landlord-farmer was one of the defining pillars of a perennial BrƒÅhma·πáa led social order often characterized as ‚Äúfeudal,‚Äù the amount of attention that HemƒÅdri invests in delegitimizing this practice is also worthy of mention. Since the age that they live in does not entail the adoption of the dharmas associated with a time of trials, any involvement in agriculture, we are told, renders a BrƒÅhma·πáa ineligible as a ritual participant, with an important stipulation. Overriding the context-specific legal pluralism we have encountered so often, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái offers a universal injunction governing all agricultural production that removes the practical equivalent of ritual fault. Presumably taken out of a single year‚Äôs harvest, the producer is to give 1/6 of his yield to the king, 1/20 to the gods, and 1/18 to BrƒÅhma·πáas, and so long as he does so, his transgressions will not be punished. This arrangement at once points to the preexistence of a substantial number of caste inappropriate tenant farmers who have to somehow be accommodated as well as alternative financial means whereby BrƒÅhma·πáas [[P1570]] who are not themselves farmers can passively accrue income from the land merely by agreeing to overlook the transgressions of their neighbors. At the same time, much like we saw in the inscription from Unkeshwar,265 it points to a new consensus that all modes of production should both directly enrich the state and help to fund the state supported project of reimagining the religious landscape, regardless of whether the ‚Äúdonees‚Äù themselves have any devotional, social, or personal ties to the religious institutions in question.

With the multitudes of people who merely despoil the line dispensed with, the worst of the worst‚Äîthose who must be violently driven away if they even approach the area where a ≈õrƒÅddha is taking place are reserved for last. In contrast to what we have seen up until now, this category is defined explicitly in doctrinal and theological terms.

As described by HemƒÅdri, of good intellect, whose jocular conversation is an ocean of nectar, a veritable sun whose radiance is wisdom, who is a lovely treasury of discrimination of truth from falsity, who is the king of the waves of compassion, the highest gold mine of auspiciousness‚Äîhear now the ones who have come forth from bad living creatures, who are to be driven away from the ground in the vicinity of the ≈õrƒÅddha [sacrifice]. . . .266

As is stated in the BrahmƒÅ·πá·∏ça PurƒÅ·πáa: Naked people and so forth should not ever see the ≈õrƒÅddha. . . .

H: Naked (nagna) means those who have abandoned the Vedas. By the word ƒÅdi (‚Äúand so forth‚Äù) are understood those who have renounced the practice of Vedic ritual of those sorts. And likewise, on that very subject it is stated:

‚ÄúSince the three Vedas is the covering of all beings, those indeed who abandon it, out of delusion, are known indeed as the naked.‚Äù

H: The ‚Äúthree‚Äù means the Veda. Sa·πÉvara·πáa means covering or garment. For just as a garment protects people from cold, wind and so forth, in the same way, the Vedas also [[P1571]] protects from suffering in that it establishes the practice of one‚Äôs own dharma. Thus, it is similar to a garment.

‚ÄúThose who follow the dharmas of the Buddhists, ≈örƒÅvakas, the Text-less, ≈öƒÅktas, Jƒ´vakas, KƒÅpilas are indeed folks that are naked.‚Äù

H: Buddhists means Buddhists (saugata), ≈örƒÅvakas means ≈övetƒÅmbara Jains. The Text- less are the Digambara Jains. ≈öƒÅktas are Kaulas. Jƒ´vakas are BƒÅrhaspatyas‚Äîthe sense is the CƒÅrvƒÅkas. Kapila is one who resides in the LokƒÅyata country. KƒÅpilas are those who are led by him.267

[[P1572]]

In the root text on which HemƒÅdri‚Äôs is relying, the people who are to be completely forbidden fall under the category of ‚Äúthe naked‚Äù (nagna). In the original source, this is likely a mundane pragmatic restriction. We are probably simply talking about people who do not wear any clothes, who must put clothes on before attending the ritual. And yet, through intertextual exegesis, HemƒÅdri is able to shape this passage into something that provides a capstone to this part of his interpretive project. In this regard he clearly again is leaning on a specifically Vai·π£·πáava precedent, for all of the sources that offer this somewhat fanciful allegorical reading of the term nagna seem to originate in such a domain.268 What our author [[P1573]] has done in a sense is that, without invoking Vai·π£·πáava signifiers or making plain that this idea is product of interreligious polemic, he has taken entextualized Vai·π£·πáava specific norms and theological frameworks and equated them with the shared worldview of a new kind of SmƒÅrtas. Indeed, as HemƒÅdri explains in his commentary, the function of the Veda is not that it provides the core conceptual resources for this new tradition, but rather that it ‚Äúprotects from suffering‚Äù by ‚Äúestablishing the practice of one‚Äôs own dharma (svadharma).‚Äù In other words, its main virtue is that it provides checks that seek to override a pluralism of values and a pluralism in law. For HemƒÅdri, the figures that most thoroughly embody such abhorrent normativities are not the agents of the MantramƒÅrga as whole, but a curious rouges gallery of what we would call ≈örama·πáa religions‚ÄîBuddhists, ≈övetƒÅmbara and Digambara Jains, the survivors of the old materialisms, along with what this study has taken to be our author‚Äôs main intended opponents, the ≈öƒÅktas, whom HemƒÅdri quite accurately identifies as Kaulas. Intriguingly, by and large, one of the defining features of the heterodoxy of all of these communities is that, in addition to being socially dependent for their survival on a site- and community-specific differentiation of norms, at least by this time period, they have adopted nominalist-like epistemologies that treat individual things and ‚Äúexperience‚Äù as sources of authority. In essence then, whether or not such agents themselves adhere to the expected norms of Var·πáƒÅ≈õramadharma, they represent everything written on the slate that is our particularized world that the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái seeks to wipe clean away. This section then concludes with a series of warnings, emphasizing once again that in a world where legal and moral judgements are both normative and universal, there exist no exceptions to universalized ritual and legal norms, even for the ruling powers. Perhaps most pertinently, HemƒÅdri directs his readers to the following seemingly cursory statement: [[P1574]]

And this is the prohibition regarding the eating of rice of a king who has deviated from his own dharma. Thus is to be seen: ‚ÄúThe food is never to be eaten of kings who are devoid of protecting the people, who are deviating from the law books. One who is eating it should go to hell.‚Äù269

[[P1575]]

Given what we have learned about the dominant normativities that animate the medieval Deccan, and which continued to hold sway in places where the Seu·πáas did not rule and the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái had not been embraced as the law of the land, if taken seriously, this small statement amounts to the shattering of any hopes of commensality between the Seu·πáas and other royal courts. This is not the place to engage with the rather substantial multilingual corpus of texts and documentary records produced in the courts of the Seu·πáa‚Äôs major regional rivals, the Hoysalas to the east in Karnataka and the Sola·πÖkƒ´ Ca·∏∑ukyas of Gujarat, but suffice it to say that in both domains, but especially among the Hoysalas and their vassals, right alongside an increasingly influential and decidedly Vai·π£·πáava normativity, KalyƒÅ·πái Ca·∏∑ukya social ‚Äúbest practices,‚Äù including the use of non-BrƒÅhma·πáa ƒÅcƒÅryas overseeing artisans in construction and the initiation of generals and sometimes the kings themselves into ≈öƒÅkta samaya codes continued into the fourteenth century. Were they actually to follow the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, even to parley or arrange and celebrate a marriage, the Seu·πáas would have no longer been willing to share the food and trust of their relatives and rivals. Succinctly, the program of social reform that the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái promotes in almost every aspect aims not at the restoration of long held social norms but at a revolution in [[P1576]] the practice and understanding of dharma that irrevocably breaks away from an almost thousand-year consensus.

In preparation for our upcoming encounter with HemƒÅdri‚Äôs vision as a theologian, it is worth considering some the sources that have inspired this drastic break from the familiar. As we will see in our final chapter, though HemƒÅdri‚Äôs universal dharma is profoundly influenced by a range of Vai·π£·πáava scriptures, no single work has more an impact than the BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa, and this true even in regard to the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, where it is quoted sparingly.
