It is thus fitting that we close out our own exploration of the disciplinary dimensions of HemƒÅdri‚Äôs interpretive project, especially as they pertain to the ‚Äúcuration‚Äù of individual participation upon the sacrificial grounds, by turning to the BhƒÅgavata‚Äôs own reflections upon such matters, which until now have been entirely overlooked by modern Western scholarship.

The fourth division of the BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa270 begins with a retelling of the story of the destruction of Dak·π£a‚Äôs sacrifice, a tale that for many contemporary ≈öaivas in the thirteenth century was emblematic of both the demographic inclusivism of their tradition as well as of the necessity of ferociously defending the honor of their god.271 Needless to say, the BhƒÅgavata construes this story in a rather different manner. Succinctly, as we are about to see, it will reimagine this story as a charter myth justifying the exclusion of initiated ≈öaivas from social life and the sacrificial grounds. [[P1577]]

At first things proceed in the usual fashion, with Dak·π£a denouncing his daughter Satƒ´‚Äôs relationship with the ill-mannered skull wearing lord who dwells in the cremation grounds.272 In a pointed language that resonates with the rhetoric of the text we have just read, Dak·π£a laments that by ≈öiva and his followers the path (panthƒÅ) practiced by good people (sadbhir) had been defiled (d≈´·π£ita). Though ≈öiva took his daughter‚Äôs hand in marriage in front of sacred fire in the presence of BrƒÅhma·πáas (viprƒÅgnimukhata·∏•) as if he was a good person (iva sƒÅdhuvat), from Dak·π£a‚Äôs perspective, at least, the god failed to do his father-in-law proper homage after the marriage. For this reason, his new father-in-law has come to resent him immensely, lamenting that bestowing his daughter in marriage was ‚Äúlike [giving] beautiful speech to a ≈õ≈´dra (≈õ≈´drƒÅyevo≈õatƒ´·πÉ giram).‚Äù Dak·π£a then continues on in the usual fashion, denouncing the impurity and inauspiciousness of the so-called auspicious lord, as well as his motley filthy retinue of followers, until at last he declares that there is going to be implemented a new restrictive injunction (ni·π£idhyamƒÅna·∏•). Henceforth, in the Vedic sacrifice, the god Bhava will no longer receive any offering whatsoever. This far, the story is indistinguishable from any of its other tellings. Of course, by end of the tale, ≈öiva‚Äôs rightful share will be restored to him and we will be taught that ≈öiva and Vi·π£·πáu are one and the same supreme principle and neither is to be disrespected. [[P1578]]

Then, suddenly and unexpectedly, the BhƒÅgavata swerves off onto a very different course that aims at conveying a contrarian message. To accomplish this task, in a subplot leading up to the actual performance of Dak·π£a‚Äôs sacrifice, it introduces into the story two new protagonists, who usually play no meaningful role in the myth. The first of these figures is none other than Nandike≈õvara, the leader of ≈öiva‚Äôs ga·πáas and the revealer of the ≈öivadharma≈õƒÅstra. The second is the Vedic sage Bh·πõgu, forefather of the often-ferocious line of the warrior BrƒÅhma·πáa BhƒÅrgavas. What happens next, according to the BhƒÅgavata, is that Nandƒ´ke≈õvara makes a mistake. Defiled by the garment that is anger (ro·π£aka·π£ƒÅyad≈´·π£ita·∏•), he places a curse on the head of Dak·π£a and his followers.273 Dak·π£a, he declares, entrapped by the net of karma, will have his mind turned away from the Vedic dharma. Because he acted like a beast (pa≈õu), that clever one will become goat -aced (bastamukha·∏•) and will become obsessed with desire for women. Dak·π£a and his followers, the ga·πáe≈õvara proclaims, will be reborn again and again in this world. Directing his attention to Dak·π£a‚Äôs BrƒÅhma·πáa followers, Nandike≈õvara proclaims:

May those haters of Hara whose selves are agitated be deluded by the abundant kindling wood that is the sweet smell of the flowery scriptural language! For the sake of their livelihood [and not out of piety] may the twice-borns eat everything, bear vows, austerity, and wisdom here in this world! May they be beggars for whom the [only] wealth is the pleasure gardens of the bodily senses.274

[[P1579]]

As the BhƒÅgavata teaches, while Nandike≈õvara was well within his rights in cursing the misguided Dak·π£a, in directing his ire to his BrƒÅhma·πáa followers the lord of the ga·πáas made a pivotal mistake with long lasting repercussions. In defense of the BrƒÅhma·πáas, Bh·πõgu now steps forward and offers his own counter curse.

These ones who bear vows to Bhava, and those ones who are entirely attached to them‚Äîmay they be heretics, obstructing the true ≈õƒÅstra! Their purity destroyed, their minds deluded, bearing bones, ash, and matted locks, may they enter into ≈öaiva initiation (≈õivadƒ´k·π£ƒÅyƒÅ·πÉ) in which liquor is divine. Since you all blaspheme against BrahmƒÅ and the BrƒÅhma·πáas, who are the bridge that is the support of men, thus, you have taken recourse to heresy! For this alone is the auspicious (≈õiva) eternal path of all the worlds/people (lokƒÅnƒÅ·πÉ), which previously they followed, of which the veridical authority (pramƒÅ·πáa) is JanƒÅrdana. Having reviled that brahman, the Supreme, Pure [one], eternal, the abode of the good, go to heresy (pƒÅ·π£a·πá·∏ça), where your divinity is king of bh≈´tas!275

[[P1580]]

In this manner, the BhƒÅgavata condemns to the darkness the knowledge system of the MantramƒÅrga and all its followers, not because they ‚Äúdisrupted‚Äù the sacrifice, but because they had the audacity to criticize BrƒÅhma·πáas. The message here quite simply is ≈õivadƒ´k·π£ƒÅ is the foremost sign of heresy and that it is inextricably linked with transgressive practices. As the story progresses, ≈öiva himself and his worship, provided it is part of a multifaceted system of practice addressing other non ≈öaiva gods, will be redeemed. And though he is humbled, his head replaced with that of a goat, Bh·πõgu‚Äôs curse is never lifted nor does the text ever condemn it. Indeed, it subtly asserts that it has established a new status quo where those who exclusively worship ≈öiva and imitate his actions are rendered radically impure and forever more unfit to participate in society. The story resumes in the usual fashion, with Satƒ´ denouncing her father and committing ritual suicide and an enraged Vƒ´rabhadra, acting on behalf of ≈öiva and accompanied by his ga·πáas, descending upon Dak·π£a‚Äôs sacrifice and destroying it. In order to appease the frenzied throng of ≈öaiva devotees, BrahmƒÅ and the other goods seek out lord ≈öiva and ask him to intervene, promising that his rightful share in the sacrifice will be restored to him. The decapitated Dak·π£a is given the head of a goat, at which point he realizes the essential truth that BrahmƒÅ, ≈öiva, and Vi·π£·πáu are the same supreme power‚Äîin other words, the core theology that animates the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái. Dak·π£a proclaims that this supreme god‚Äôs role is to serve as the protector and corrector of BrƒÅhma·πáas, and ≈öiva is pleased. Everyone then assembles to attend the ‚Äúvai·π£·πáava yajna,‚Äù which is to commence with an offering made to Vi·π£·πáu in three vessels. First, however, in order to ensure both the sanctity and continuity of the interrupted rite, the malign influence of ≈öiva‚Äôs ‚Äúdisruptors‚Äù‚Äîthe pramathaga·πáas‚Äîmust be expunged. As the text pointedly states, the puro·∏ça≈õa is performed ‚Äúto purify [the sacrifice] from the touch (sa·πÉsarga) of the vƒ´ras (vƒ´rasa·πÉsarga≈õuddhaye).‚Äù276 [[P1581]]

Once this has been accomplished and the taint of the followers of the MantramƒÅrga has been expunged, all at once‚Äîto the astonishment of the crowd‚ÄîVi·π£·πáu makes himself manifest before the sacrifice. One by one, the gods and the sages begin to praise him, and of course ≈öiva is no exception. Strangely enough, however, the words that ≈öiva speaks are only incidentally addressed to his other half, for in reality, they target his followers.

If some ignorant person promotes me [lit., repeats me] as having bad conduct (apaviddha)‚ÄîI whose mind is fixed on your lotus feet, which, even though they [grant] all aims here in this world by means of [your] benediction, ought to be honored by dispassionate sages with respect‚Äîbecause of your supreme grace, I do not take that into account.277

[[P1582]]

Though these words are quite oblique, the core message is quite simple. Though some ignorant people may have disseminated (japati glossed by ≈örƒ´dhara as jalpati) the message that ≈öiva promotes a degraded mode of social praxis (ƒÅcƒÅrabhra·π£·π≠am in ≈örƒ´dhara‚Äôs understanding of apaviddham), ≈öiva says, ‚ÄúI pay (ga·πáaye) them no heed,‚Äù for in actuality ≈öiva himself clings to the grace of the supreme lord and repudiates such toxic nonsense. In other words, the price ≈öiva has paid for his own inclusion in respectable society is the exclusion of his most devout devotees. The gist of the matter is that ≈öiva is great, but ≈öaivism is not, and anyone who thinks otherwise needs to get with the program and start reading and following real ≈õƒÅstras. The BhƒÅgavata, for instance. Or, the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái…. [[P1583]]

  16. While the Old Marathi Unakadeva inscription we have been analyzing was issued in 1278 CE, the Sanskrit copper plate found in Thane district outside of Mumbai that offers the key textual parallel was issued in 1289 CE. As we shall see, we have documentary evidence for HemƒÅdri holding the multifaceted position of chief minister and de facto ruler of the Seu·πáa YƒÅdava Kingdom from circa 1260, when the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái was completed during the reign of King MahƒÅdeva, until 1290 CE, when he last appears in our records. We know for certain that he was dead or least no longer in office in 1297 CE, for that is when a certain Joideva is depicted as having replaced him in his office. Given the enormous length of the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái, which proclaims HemƒÅdri‚Äôs high office in every single one of its divisions, as well as documentary evidence for the implementation of policies first outlined in the CVC as early as 1245, it is plausible that the completion of the text may have taken as long as a decade, beginning during the reign of KandharƒÅya, during most if not all of which HemƒÅdri held high office. Thus, we can conservatively suggest that HemƒÅdri was a dominant influence in the court for thirty to forty years. I had originally intended to incorporate herein an exhaustive survey of the documentary records pertaining to HemƒÅdri S≈´ri, a project that was largely outlined as early as 2014. In the time of Covid, however, it has become exceptionally difficult to reconsult all of the inscriptional evidence across all of the various different volumes in which it is contained to cross check notes and verify readings, as much of it is for in library use only. As a result, this project will have to wait for another occasion. ↩︎

Barnett, the editor and translator of this inscription, gives the conjectural reading ‚Äúsubhagam-bhƒÅvuke bhƒÅvake,‚Äù which clearly contains some sort of play on words, upon which he comments: ‚ÄúThis is corrupt. Apparently, the sense demands something like ¬∞subhagatva-bhƒÅvuka-bhƒÅvake, and I have ventured to translate accordingly.‚Äù His editor, F.W.T, then adds in brackets: ‚Äú[But subhaga·πÉ bhƒÅvuka would be correct]. See Epigraphia Indica, Vol. XIII, pg. 202, ftn. 3. This later correction loses the deliberate play on words intended, which we might reconstruct as something like, ‚Äú[HemƒÅdri] is a literary connoisseur (bhƒÅvaka) in regard to language full of emotion/rasa (bhƒÅvuka) the good fortune from which is an expression of his own qualities.‚Äù While for anyone else, this sort of over-the-top bravado would be suspect and suggest a poor construal of the text, as we shall see in this chapter, for HemƒÅdri, this mode of expression is entirely unexceptional. ↩︎

  19. In the Unkeshwar inscription, one possibility is that bhƒÅvaka almost seems to be serving as a marker of an appositional compound. The alternative, namely, that it means ‚Äúbringing into being the master of the elephants,‚Äù does not make much sense. Another perhaps even stronger reading is that in one or both inscriptions, bhƒÅvaka has a dual intended meaning, and what is being suggested is the technical term of art from Ala·πÉkƒÅra≈õƒÅstra, where a bhƒÅvaka is the highest type of literary critic responsible for assessing the worth of poems and plays. As we will see in the next chapter, in the context of our study of the Kaivalyadƒ´pikƒÅ, within the domain of aesthetics, the term bhƒÅvaka is first popularized within the Da≈õar≈´paka of Dhana√±jaya and its commentary, the ƒÄloka, by Dhanika, two authors HemƒÅdri knows intimately and draws upon extensively. The role of bhƒÅvaka as master aesthete is one we could easily imagine HemƒÅdri enacting, though its exact rhetorical function within the present inscriptions remains less than transparent. ↩︎

  27. It is an understatement to say that HemƒÅdri has been ill-served by both Western and Indian secondary literature, within which his work is almost exclusively discussed either in the service of mining his PurƒÅ·πáic citations or with the intention of using the CaturvargacintƒÅma·πái‚Äôs rƒÅjapra≈õasti (not included in our editions but printed separately by Bhandarkar) in the service of triangulating dynastic histories in the early medieval Deccan. The only full- length study dedicated to HemƒÅdri in any language is Kesava Appa Padhye, HemƒÅdri urpha HemƒÅdpanta yƒÅ√±ce Caritra, composed in 1931. Though a work of considerable erudition, for unlike everyone else he has clearly read HemƒÅdri‚Äôs work at length and with care, it is afflicted by what to the modern Western reader seems an almost unimaginable credulousness on the part of its author. The opening sentence should be enough to give the interested reader a taste of what is at hand:‚ÄúhemƒÅdripa·πá·∏çitƒÅsƒÅrakhƒÅ vidvƒÅn, vibhava≈õƒÅlƒ´, kƒ´rttimƒÅn, dƒÅna≈õ≈´ra, rƒÅjyakƒÅryadhara·πÉdhara, sadƒÅcƒÅranƒ´ va dharmƒÅbhimƒÅnƒ´ asƒÅ dusarƒÅ puru·π£a p≈´rvƒ´cyƒÅ kƒÅlƒÅ·πÉta jhƒÅlƒÅ nƒÅhƒ´·πÉ va pu·∏çhe·πÉ ho·πáƒÅra nƒÅhƒ´,‚Äù (bhƒÅga pahilƒÅ, page 1). Succinctly, Padhye takes at face value everything ever said by or about HemƒÅdri in sources ancient and modern. He also assigns to our subject a host of works that are clearly by other authors, resulting in a composite picture of HemƒÅdri as an intellectual. More constructively, Padhye is privy to oral traditions concerning HemƒÅdri that are apparently now lost, including a memory among certain artisan communities of a HemƒÅdrƒ´ya ≈õilpa≈õƒÅstra tradition, for which we will find partial confirmation later on in the present chapter. He has also drawn upon a number of MahƒÅnubhƒÅvika sources, published and unpublished, both contemporary to the Seu·πáa YƒÅdavas and from subsequent centuries, that offer representations, of varying reliability, of the Seu·πáa court and HemƒÅdri‚Äôs role within it. ↩︎

As the literature is quite extensive and well discussed elsewhere, what follows is a selective introduction to representative works in the discourses on bhakti rasa concerning largely Sanskritic Gau·∏çƒ´ya traditions on the one hand and the vernacular deployments of bhakti rasa in Braj Bhasa and various registers of Rajasthani. Among this vast and varied corpus, only a handful of works reflect explicitly on the indebtedness of the largely north Indian Vai·π£·πáava rasa traditions to HemƒÅdri and Bopadeva‚Äôs intellectual project. Among traditional authorities, it is only Jƒ´vagosvƒÅmin in his ·π¢a·π≠sandarbha who acknowledges his debt to his predecessors, especially in the Tattvasandarbha, where he cites the MuktƒÅphala by name several times. Even here, his dependence on HemƒÅdri‚Äôs arguments and rhetoric, which is far more extensive than he acknowledges, is heavily occluded. Secondary literature that reflects on this dynamic includes: Stuart Mark Elkman, Jƒ´va GosvƒÅmin’s Tattvasandarbha: A Study on the Philosophical and Sectarian Development of the Gau·∏çƒ´ya Vai·π£·πáava Movement (Delhi: Motilal Banarsidass, 1986); Ravi M. Gupta, The Caitanya Vai·π£·πáava VedƒÅnta of Jƒ´va GosvƒÅmƒ´: When Knowledge Meets Devotion (London: Taylor & Francis, 2007).   31. A major shortcoming of the present study, unavoidable under the current world circumstances, is that it does not contain an excavation of the specific lineage of KƒÅlamukhas, distantly related to the Balligave ≈öaktipari·π£ad, that, at least from the time of Bhillama V, had a special relationship with the Seu·πáa royal family. Prior to the lineage‚Äôs relocation of their ritual center to the Seu·πáa constructed city of Munavalli in the greater Dharwad region, the center of this particular community of KƒÅlamukhas from the time of the RƒÅ·π£·π≠rak≈´·π≠as was in the greater Bijapur region. Key sites I have yet to document include SƒÅlo·π≠agi, Tamba, Go·π≠·π≠aga·∏çi, and Kuppa·π≠≈´r. We also know of branch ma·π≠has at Nƒìsarige, G≈çkƒÅge (modern Gokak), Ko·π≠·π≠umbage, and Go·∏∑iyaha·∏∑·∏∑i. Many of these places seem to preserve relevant material culture. There is also a large corpus of multilingual inscriptions (many unpublished) that need careful study. ↩︎

  43. This early colonial context also accounts for the rather unfortunate choice of fonts. As I have spent many a night lamenting, all six thousand pages of the work are transmitted in a Bengali-inflected variant on Devanagari. Intrinsically already more difficult to read, as the font does not clearly differentiate between many of the vowel markers, such that long ƒ´ and o are often hard to distinguish, over the course of numerous reprintings, the visual quality of the font has further degraded, resulting in a frequent and annoying blurring of characters and loss of anusvƒÅras. ↩︎

Throughout the present edition in progress of select passages from the theoretical portions of the Tƒ´rthakha·πá·∏ça, references in the apparatus to ‚ÄúGV‚Äù refer to the Ga·πÖgƒÅvƒÅkyƒÅvalƒ´ of Vi≈õvƒÅsadevƒ´, a work by a female scholar of Dharma≈õƒÅstra of the fifteenth century who was also a part of the royal family of Mithila.   73. As presented in the grantha pra≈õasti contained within the BrahmacƒÅrikƒÅ·πá·∏ça of K·πõtyakalpataru, the works listed run as follows: The oldest work is the Kalpav·πõk·π£a. Lak·π£mƒ´dhara then speaks in sequence of the MahƒÅr·πáava, the KƒÅmadhenu, and the RatnamƒÅlƒÅ, all of whose authors he does not mention. See the BrahmacƒÅrikƒÅ·πá·∏ça, page 3. The Japanese scholar Ryoshu Kouda has located two fragmentary but substantial manuscripts containing portions of the K·πõtyakƒÅmadhenu of GopƒÅla. These are related to the DƒÅna and ≈örƒÅddha divisions, with the former topic being handled quite tersely. On the basis of the text preserved therein, which he has been able to determine that this work was also composed in Varanasi for Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs patron Govindacandra. The two scholars, whose works both reflect the casual Vai·π£·πáavism of the court in which they served, thus seem to have been contemporaries. This dynamic suggests that the RatnamƒÅlƒÅ, which Lak·π£mƒ´dhara understands as a response to the KƒÅmadhenu, was also composed around the same time in a similar milieu. This leaves us with potentially two dharmanibandha texts, the MahƒÅr·πáava and Kalpav·πõk·π£a, which could possibly have been produced before the twelfth century and outside of the eastern portion of the Indo-Gangetic Plain. Though Kouda‚Äôs work is published in Japanese, a language I do not read, his articles reproduce useful facsimiles of the folios while also transcribing colophons and key portions. See, for example: Ryoshu Kouda, (2015) ‚ÄúGopƒÅla cho K·πõtyakƒÅmadhenu no baiy≈ç shahon (kami),‚Äù Hasegawa bukky≈ç bunka kenkyujo nenp≈ç 39 (2015): 1‚Äì61 (230‚Äì170). ↩︎

In Salomon‚Äôs translation: ‚ÄúThe ≈õrƒÅddha (done before leaving), like the ≈õrƒÅddha at the tƒ´rtha, should be a pƒÅrva·πáa-≈õrƒÅddha, with six, nine, or twelve deities, according to different opinions. If it were combined with other substances, that would cancel the sense of the instrumental case of ‚Äòwith ghee,‚Äô which denotes an independent means, as in the phrase ‚ÄòOne should sacrifice with grains of rice.‚Äô This is not so, because the literal statement (‚Äòwith ghee‚Äô) must be restricted in order to avoid a meaningless statement; for ghee alone cannot be the (only) material for a ≈õrƒÅddha, because it cannot be eaten by itself. Thus others say: ‚Äò(The ≈õrƒÅddha-offering consists) mainly of ghee.‚Äô This is also not so; because the instrumental case is not used to prescribe ‚Äòplentitude‚Äô; and because this would constitute indirect expression (lak·π£a·πáƒÅ).   104. As I hope to demonstrate in a future study, the PrabhƒÅsak·π£etramƒÅhƒÅtmya displays numerous signs of representing a mid-twelfth-century redaction. It is intent upon, as it tells us, bringing existing traditions into alignment with the Vaidika norms (vedasammata). Yet at the same time, it has actively drawn upon a range of older Tantric sources, which are often only partially digested. This state of affairs explains the ubiquitous casual allusions throughout the text to Tantric trade secrets such as the thirty-six tattvas and the conquest of the six adhvans. Indeed, in its second chapter, which candidly alludes to some of the mƒÅhƒÅtmya‚Äôs sources, the work actually refers to many such kalpas by name, associated with MahƒÅlak·π£mƒ´, the Garu·∏ça Tantras, and so forth, aligning them with specific subsections within the text. Often the redactional process, which imposes a proto- SmƒÅrta-≈öaiva veneer, is extremely transparent. Consistently, what lies underneath, right down to the retention of ai≈õa modes of compounding and idiom, (such as an eccentric use of x-vivarjita in the sense of transcending-x as opposed to devoid of x, resembling early KƒÅlƒ´ Krama scriptures) is a heavily epistemologized and vaguely non- dual form of ≈öƒÅkta Tantra. Thus, Some≈õa himself, for example, in the sixth chapter, is identified in passing as originally being known of as NirvƒÅ·πáabhairava, who is beyond duality (dvaitavarjita·πÉ), whose state is devoid of the elaboration of language (vƒÅkprapa√±cƒÅdirahitam) and is established in the observation of knowledge and the means of knowing (j√±ƒÅnaj√±eyƒÅvalokasthita·πÉ) and yet beyond the mere appearance of causality (hetvƒÅbhƒÅsavivarjitam) (PrabhƒÅsak·π£etramƒÅhƒÅtmya 5.10‚Äì11). The text discusses at some length how these Bhairavamata traditions have in the present generation been replaced by the recitation of the ≈öatarudrƒ´ya and other Vedicizing ≈öaiva texts. As for some of the original demographics involved in making pilgrimage to the region, the third chapter in this regard is particularly telling. After first introducing the practice of pilgrimage in the most general terms, it then obsesses at some length with remarkable specificity about the ‚Äúwrong motivations‚Äù and wrong understanding for going on pilgrimage, alluding, for example to a tradition of rasa siddhas whose activities are in opposition to var·πáƒÅ≈õramadharma. PrabhƒÅsak·π£etramƒÅhƒÅtmya, pg. 5, vs. 3.119‚Äì122: evam ukta·πÉ mahƒÅdevi prabhƒÅsa·πÉ k·π£etra·πÉ uttama·πÉ / d·πõ·π£·π≠vƒÅ sa·πÉskƒÅrarahitƒÅ·∏• kalau pƒÅpena mohitƒÅ·∏• // rƒÅjasƒÅs tƒÅmasƒÅ≈õ caiva pƒÅpohatacetasa·∏• / paradƒÅraparadravyaparahi·πÉsƒÅratƒÅ narƒÅ·∏• // udvega·πÉ ca para·πÉ yƒÅti pratapyanti yas tata·∏• / ƒÅtmasa·πÉbhƒÅvitƒÅ m≈´·∏çƒÅ mithyƒÅj√±ƒÅnena mohitƒÅ·∏• // var·πáƒÅ≈õramaviruddha·πÉ tu tƒ´rtha·πÉ kurvanti ye‚ÄôdhamƒÅ·∏• / ↩︎

  163. David Brick, Brahminical Theories of the Gift, 82. In actuality, Lak·π£mƒ´dhara‚Äôs ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapasm·πõti shares its title but not content with the published work under that name, for the ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapasm·πõti available to us is utterly dissimilar in tone and focus. Like many of the almost entirely unstudied upadharma≈õƒÅstras, it is a rather strange text, primarily concerned with the future karmic consequences of committing the mahƒÅpƒÅtakas and upapƒÅtakas. Much of the work consists simply of a charming laundry list of hyper specific ‚Äúif, then‚Äù formulations, matching consequence to karmic result. To cite just a few compelling examples, a person who passes off someone else‚Äôs words as their own will be reborn dumb, and the remedy for this in a future life will be to give another scholar copies of works of NyƒÅya and ItihƒÅsa in manuscript. While a person who steals wool in this life will be reborn really hairy, a state of affairs that can be remedied by making a golden image of Agni and giving it to a BrƒÅhma·πáa, in contrast, a person who steals silk will be reborn with an unmanly lack of hair, which is fixed for future births by giving a BrƒÅhma·πáa a cow. If you have incestuous sex with your mother, next life you will be born with a vestigial penis. However, if you merely have licit sex in your teacher‚Äôs bed, in your next life, it will hurt when you pee. Sleeping with female ascetics causes kidney stones. Sleeping with your maternal aunt causes ulcers on the left side, incest with your paternal aunt makes them manifest on the left. On and on the text goes, without at any point expressing even a passing interested in the examination and evaluation of different classes of BrƒÅhma·πáas attested in the dharmanibandha literature. For the published edition of the ≈öƒÅtƒÅtapasm·πõti, see Manmatha Nath Dutt, ed., The Dharam Shastra: Hindu Religious Codes (New Delhi: Cosmo, 1978‚Äì1979). ↩︎

  220. In a similar spirit, the vairƒÅja or devayonƒ´ mantra maps a hodge-podge of Vedic deities and PurƒÅ·πáic figures, many of which, such as Hira·πáyagarbha, NƒÅrada and PrahlƒÅda, are associated with the traditions of devotional VedƒÅnta, onto a body, rounding out its lists with canonical categories of types of wombs, yak·π£a, gandharva, and the like, in which a person may achieve rebirth. HemƒÅdri next begins to incorporate devotional theistic religion into this field of discourse. This will ultimately prove to be a rather involved and multistep process. Elsewhere its intended end is that Vai·π£·πáava and ≈öaiva mythology, treated as unified field, are to be understood as functionally equivalent optionalities that are theologically identical and closely linked with Vedic knowledge. At this point in the text, however, no such optionality is invoked, and we are simply provided with a single format for what the text calls a m≈´rti-nyƒÅsa. M≈´rti-nyƒÅsa prescribes the imposition on the body of a variation on the incarnations of Vi·π£·πáu, in which the typical ten avatƒÅras are supplemented with an additional list comprised of Ke≈õava, NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa, MƒÅdhva, Govinda, Vai·π£·πáava, Madhus≈´dana, Trivikrama, VƒÅmana [sic], ≈örƒ´dhara, H·πõ·π£ƒ´ke≈õa, PadmanƒÅbha, and Damodara. As scholars of Vai·π£·πáavism will probably have noticed, these additional names are the first ten of a set of twenty-four epithets frequently recited in Vai·π£·πáava ritual communities, with our list terminating right before the introduction of the vy≈´has of the Pa√±carƒÅtra system. It is very likely that HemƒÅdri has himself joined these together with the preceding list of da≈õƒÅvatƒÅra, for this formulation conspicuously repeats the figure VƒÅmana, the dwarf, who now appears in both lists. What is fascinating about this inclusion is that unlike most of the other theistic materials in the text, the extended list of incarnations of Vi·π£·πáu are not treated as forms of knowledge that are tradition-specific to Vai·π£·πáavas but instead are understood as part of the common cultural heritage of anyone participating in temple life. Clearly, we have come a long way from the late-eighth- or early-ninth-century Pi·πÖgalƒÅmata, where, so that the artisans could craft appropriate images, the goddess had to inquire with the god what that whole and decidedly ‚Äúforeign‚Äù incarnation thing was all about. ↩︎

  245. Matthew Sayers, Feeding the Dead: Ancestor Worship in Ancient India (New York: Oxford University Press, 2013). Sayers offers a painstaking historicization of the procedural and rhetorical continuities and discontinuities among ancestor rituals as they developed in the Vedic corpus proper, within the G·πõhyas≈´tras, and the emergent codifications of ≈õrƒÅddha reflected within the Dharmas≈´tras. The scope of his study sadly does not extend even to the world of early Dharma≈õƒÅstra. Much of his conceptual apparatus is derived from an early study by Knipe, and the historical work he offers is nicely complemented by Knipe‚Äôs more wide-ranging and acutely theorized engagements with ancestor rites in South Asia, which are deeply informed by anthropological work conducted in numerous parts of the subcontinent and among very different demographics. Knipe‚Äôs work has been recently anthologized as: David Knipe, The Hindu Rite of Entry into Heaven: Essays on Death and Ancestors in Hinduism (Delhi: Motilal Banarsidass, 2019). ↩︎

  247. Tantric materials on ≈õrƒÅddha, both in terms of the classical period and in the quite textually rich world of the early modern tantranibandha, which especially in Kaula sources often have elaborate discussions of lineage- specific ≈õrƒÅddha rituals, have until recently received virtually no attention. A welcome foray into correcting this oversight, though somewhat constrained by its near exclusive focus on the traditions of the ≈öaiva SiddhƒÅnta, is found in the fifth chapter of Nina Mirnig‚Äôs recent monograph on ≈öaiva death rituals, which argues for a relatively late accommodation of the ritual within ≈öaiva domains. Nina Mirnig, Liberating the Liberated: Early ≈öaiva Tantric Death Rites (Vienna; Austrian Academy of Sciences, 2018). It is this pioneering works which informs our discussion of the ≈öaiva sources. Based on admittedly impressionistic engagements with the ≈öƒÅkta materials, suggestions emerge of a parallel dynamic either of late adoption or a post-hoc attempt to homologize what was originally an independent system of praxis within a recognizably ≈õrƒÅddha paradigm. This would explain Jayaratha‚Äôs framing of Abhinavagupta‚Äôs comments at the beginning of the TantrƒÅloka‚Äôs twenty fifth chapter that some people have asked skeptically where in the Trika system is the name ≈õrƒÅddhavidhi uttered. Abhinavagupta‚Äôs explanation is that within the Siddha Tantras, while the term is nowhere in evidence, the same practice is referred to when the scriptures speak of the necessity for feeding the vƒ´ras at the time of a death rite. Even so, the primary sources that both authors will lean upon in the remainder of the chapter are mostly derived from the ≈öaiva SiddiddhƒÅnta. BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa 4.7.16‚Äì17: k·π£amƒÅpyaiva·πÉ sa mƒ´·∏çhvƒÅ·πÉsa·πÉ brahma·πáƒÅ cƒÅnumantrita·∏• / karma santƒÅnayƒÅm ƒÅsa sopƒÅdhyƒÅyartvigƒÅdibhi·∏• // vai·π£·πáava·πÉ yaj√±asantatyai trikapƒÅla·πÉ dvijottamƒÅ·∏• / puro·∏çƒÅ≈õa·πÉ niravapan vƒ´rasa·πÉsarga≈õuddhaye // As ≈örƒ´dhara, whose commentary postdates HemƒÅdri‚Äôs floruit but whose glosses often mirror those offered by our author, understands things‚Äîthe vƒ´ras are the pramathas, and the flaw that must be purified is their having made contact with the sacrifice so that their impact will cease to be active (vƒ´rƒÅ·πáƒÅ·πÉ pramathƒÅdƒ´nƒÅ·πÉ sa·πÉsargak·πõtado·π£asya ≈õuddhaye niv·πõttayartham). ↩︎

  277. BhƒÅgavata PurƒÅ·πáa 4.7.29: tava varada varƒÅ·πÖghrƒÅv ƒÅ≈õi·π£ehƒÅkhilƒÅrthe hy api munibhir asaktair ƒÅdare·πáƒÅrha·πáƒ´ye / yadi racitadhiya·πÉ mƒÅvidyaloko ‚Äòpaviddha·πÉ japati na ga·πáaye tat tvatparƒÅnugrahe·πáa // This verse has many syntactic odd features which ≈örƒ´dhara‚Äôs commentary conspicuously avoids acknowledging. When it comes to its lexical peculiarities, ≈örƒ´dhara‚Äôs commentary reads out of the verse a specific theological message somewhat in excess of the bare semantics in evidence, much of which is founded on his understanding of the term apaviddha as referring to deviant conduct. Nevertheless, numerous textual parallels from across a range of time periods and genres, where the term is frequently glossed as paridu·π£·π≠a or ak·πõtya, support the general contours of his construal. The reading of japati as standing in for jalpati is more eccentric. Note also that mƒÅ here is the rarely seen enclitic for the first personal pronoun and not a negation lacking a corresponding aorist. ↩︎
